1. General

1.1. Safety, Ground Operations, and Servicing

1.1.1. Shop safety

  1. Electrical safety
    1. Physical safety
      1. wear/use proper safety equipment
      2. fear and/or overconfidence can cause problems; maintain a healthy level of respect for electricity
      3. it is important to understand how it works
    2. Fire safety
      1. electrical fires can happen in shops
      2. keep equipment/work areas clean
      3. take care not to kink or damage the wire
      4. monitor the condition of electrical equipment
  2. Compressed gasses
    1. keep tanks, hoses, etc in good condition
    2. never use compressed air to clean hands or clothing
    3. never spray air around other people
    4. aircraft tire-mounting can lead to accidents; use tire dollies
  3. Hazardous materials
    1. learn to read risk diamonds
      1. colors
        1. red=flammability
        2. yellow=reactivity
        3. blue=health
        4. white=special hazard
          1. RAD=radiation
          2. ALK=alkali materials
          3. Acid
          4. CARC= carcinogenic
          5. ~~W~~= highly reactive to water
      2. numbers
        1. 0=no hazard
        2. 4=very hazardous
    2. Safety Data Sheet (SDS) tells all chemical safety issues, including formulas and what to do in case of exposure
  4. Machine tools
    1. drill press
      1. eye protection
      2. secure all work
      3. use proper settings
      4. turn machine off to make adjustments
      5. clean area when finished
    2. lathe
      1. eye protection
      2. make sure the tools are sharp
      3. don't stop it with your hands
      4. check for defects beforehand
      5. don't put tools on the lathe
    3. milling machine
      1. eye protection
      2. always have a clean work bed
      3. secure all work
      4. use the correct tools
      5. don't change speed mid-job
      6. make sure it all fits under the arbor
    4. grinder
      1. eye protection
      2. inspect grinding wheel
      3. don't force the wheels onto the spindle
      4. check that wheel flanges/compression washer are 1/3 diameter of the wheel
      5. don't stand in the arc while it's spinning, in case it explodes
    5. welding stuff
    6. ideally done in a welding shop or outside, not in the hangar
      1. keep fuel away!
      2. have plenty of clean, open space
      3. only qualified workers
      4. keep extinguishers on hand, and keep watch for fire

1.1.2. Flight Line Safety

  1. Hearing Protection is very important
  2. Foreign Object Damage (FOD)
    1. any damage/injury caused by any loose object
    2. keep areas clean
    3. take care of tools, put them away
    4. keep tools away from turbine intake/exhaust
  3. Around airplanes
    1. be cautious, stay visible
    2. no smoking/open flames
    3. keep equipment, etc secure
  4. Around helicopters
    1. approach in view of pilot
    2. never approach from the rear
    3. give the rotors plenty of clearance
  5. Fire safety
    1. everyone must be aware and cautious
    2. training/drills are important

1.1.3. Fire Protection

  1. Prerequisites for fire
    1. fuel
    2. heat
    3. oxygen
  2. Classification
    1. Class A
    2. ordinary combustibles
    3. Class B
    4. petroleum products, greases, solvents
    5. Class C
    6. energized electrical wiring/equipment
  3. Types/Operation of extinguishers
    1. water fire extinguishers are only for class A
    2. carbon dioxide extinguishers are for classes A, B, and C
    3. halogenated hydrocarbon extinguishers are for all classes, best on B or C
  4. Inspecting extinguishers
    1. proper location
    2. unbroken safety seals
    3. clean
    4. gauge in operable range
    5. proper weight
    6. no nozzle obstruction
    7. no damage
  5. Identifying extinguishers
    1. markings are placed on front
    2. they must be large enough to be visible
  6. Using extinguishers
    1. check it's the right kind
    2. pull the pin
    3. stand back 8 ft
    4. aim at the base of the fire
    5. squeeze and sweep side to side

1.1.4. Tie-down procedures

  1. Prep aircraft
    1. facing into the wind
    2. plenty of space on either side
  2. Tie-downs for land planes
    1. light aircraft
      1. tie at tie-down rings
      2. leave ~1" of slack
      3. use a bowline or square knot
    2. heavy aircraft
  3. Tie-downs for sea planes
  4. Tie-downs for ski planes
  5. Tie-downs for helicopters
  6. Securing Weight-Shift-Control
  7. Securing Powered Parachutes

1.1.5. Ground Movement

  1. Engine starting/operation
  2. Reciprocating engines
  3. Hand crank engines
  4. Extinguishing engine fires
  5. Turboprop engines
  6. Starting a turboprop
  7. Turbofan engines
  8. Starting a turbofan
  9. Auxiliary power units (APUs)

1.2. DC Electronics

1.2.1. Basic definitions:

  1. conductors
    1. loosely bound electrons in valence shell
    2. silver, copper, gold, aluminum
  2. insulators
    1. valence shell is full or nearly full
    2. glass, plastic, rubber
  3. current
    1. rate of flow of electric charge
    2. measured in amperes
      1. 1A = 6.25* 1018 electrons (1C) past a point/sec
    3. I is current
  4. resistance
    1. opposition to current flow
    2. units ohms
    3. R is resistance
  5. voltage
    1. potential difference between two points in a circuit
    2. V or E is voltage
    3. unit is volts
  6. Ohm's Law
    1. V=IR

1.2.2. Series Circuits

  1. Characteristics
    1. current** is the same through all components
  2. total resistance is equal to the sum of the individual resistances
  3. total voltage is equal to the sum of the voltage drops across each resistor
  4. Kirchhoff's Voltage Law
  • Adding voltages
    1. series-aiding** voltages are connected so that each voltage source pushes current in the same direction
  • positive terminal of one is connected to the negative terminal of the next
  • add them up for the total circuit voltage
    1. series-opposing** voltages are the opposite
  • connected positive to positive or negative to negative
  • produces opposing current flow
  • when calculating total voltage, subtract voltage of series opposing parts
  • Troubleshooting
    1. opens/breaks
    2. a break in the circuit
      1. current flow is interrupted, so I=0
      2. only voltage drop is across the break, equal to total voltage
      3. eg Christmas lights
    3. shorts
      1. resistance is lost
      2. current increases
        1. could be dangerous

    • 1.2.3. Parallel Circuits

    1. Characteristics
      1. total current is equal to the sum of currents in each branch
      2. Kirchhoff's Current Law
        1. main line
          1. pair of leads connecting the branches to the voltage source terminals
          2. always has total current
          3. voltage** is the same in each branch, equal to circuit total
      3. total resistance is always less than the lowest branch resistance
        1. the opposite of total (equivalent) resistance is equal to the sum of the opposites of component resistances
    2. Benefits
      1. ensures that the same voltage is felt on each branch
        1. e.g. house wiring needs to have 120V at each plug
      2. you can have separate circuit breakers for each branch, so only part of the circuit would blow at a time
    3. Troubleshooting
      1. open/break
        1. in one branch
          1. it has no effect on the current in other branches
          2. total current of the whole circuit drops
        2. in main line
          1. all branches are affected, no current flows
      2. shorts
        1. excessive (basically infinite) current in the shorted branch
        2. it sucks all the current from the other branches
        3. battery will die

    1.2.4. Series-Parallel (Combination) Circuits

    1. more complex circuit with components of both types of circuit
      1. the same laws apply
      2. it helps to group components together when calculating resistance
    2. typically used when different voltage and current values are required from the same voltage source
    3. when there is a short circuit, total current increases
    4. with an open, total current decreases

    1.2.5. Power (watts)

    1.2.6. rate of energy use in a circuit

    1. P=IV**
  • P=energy/time (joules/sec=watts)
  • P=R*I2=V2/R
  • 1hp=746W
  • work and energy are basically the same, both measured in Joules (Newton meters)

    • 1.2.7. Overview of Laws

    1. Ohm's Law
      1. V=IR
    2. Kirchhoff's Voltage Law
      1. all voltages in a circuit loop add to total voltage
    3. Kirchhoff's Current Law
      1. all current in parallel branches adds to total current
    4. General Power Law
      1. P=IV

    1.2.8. Conductors

    1. material that offers low resistance to current flow
      1. best conductors
        1. usually metal (carbon and water work, too)
        2. have a few loosely bound valence electrons
      2. eg. silver, gold, copper, aluminum
        1. insulator** is a material that offers high resistance to current flow
      3. eg. rubber, ceramics, plastics
    2. factors affecting resistance
      1. type of material
        1. number of free valence electrons –> conductivity
      2. length of wire
        1. longer wire = greater resistance
      3. gauge of wire
        1. greater cross-sectional area = lower resistance
        2. too small of wire for given current could cause a fire
        3. _increasing__ gauge numbers mean decreasing diameters
        4. often measured in 'mils' meaning 1/1000th of an inch
      4. temperature of material
        1. higher temp = higher resistance

    1.2.9. Resistivity

    1. a measure of how strongly a material opposes electron flow
    2. expressed in units of ohm-meters
    3. differs from resistance in that it is a property of the material
    4. commonly represented by rho (ρ)
    5. most materials resistivity increases with temperature
    6. there are tables to find ρ at a given temp for a material
    7. How to calculate resistance of given wire
      1. R=ρ(L/A)**
    8. L=length, A=cross-sectional area

    1.2.10. Resistors

    1. Characteristics
      1. resistance (R)
        1. produces a drop in voltage if current is flowing through the resistor
      2. power rating (W)
        1. indicates amount of power the resistor can safely dissipate as heat
          1. otherwise they can catch on fire
        2. typically larger resistors can dissipate more heat, and have higher power ratings
        3. depends on the resistor's construction
          1. larger size means higher power rating
          2. higher wattage resistors can operate at high temps
      3. problems
        1. resistors can become open
        2. they can drift out of tolerance
        3. some variable resistors can become noisy or scratchy, indicating a dirty or worn-out resistance element or wiper blade
        4. a burned resistor is usually caused by a short circuit, exceeding the power rating
    2. Types
      1. wire-wound
        1. resistance wire is wrapped around an insulating core; great length makes resistance
        2. typically have low resistance values and are used to control large amounts of current and have high power ratings
      2. carbon-composition (used in class)
        1. made of carbon or graphite mixed with a powdered insulating material
        2. metal caps with tinned copper wire (called axial leads or pigtails) are joined to the ends for connection to a circuit
        3. becoming obsolete because of the development of carbon-film resistors
      3. film-type
        1. carbon film
          1. compared to carbon composition resistors, carbon-film resistors have
            1. tighter tolerances
            2. are less sensitive to temp changes and aging
            3. generate less noise
          2. they are constructed by depositing a resistive graphite material on a ceramic rod
        2. metal film
          1. very tight tolerances
          2. less sensitive to temp changes and aging
          3. less noisy
          4. made by depositing nickel chromium film on a ceramic rod
      4. surface-mount (chip)
        1. found in circuit boards
        2. temp stable and rugged
        3. end electrodes are soldered directly onto a circuit board
        4. much smaller than conventional resistors
      5. fusible resistors
        1. wire-wound resistors made to burn open easily when the power rating is exceeded
        2. dual function as both fuse and resistor
        3. identified by white band on one ends
      6. thermistors
        1. temp-sensitive resistors whose resistance value changes with changes in operating temp
        2. as temp increases, resistance decreases
        3. used in electronic circuits where temp measurement, control, and compensation are desired
    3. Ratings/Markings
      1. color codes used to represent numerical resistance values and tolerance
      2. coding is standardized by the Electronic Industries Alliance (EIA)
        1. brown
        2. red
        3. orange
        4. yellow
        5. green
        6. blue
        7. violet
        8. gray
        9. white
        10. black
      3. for resistors under 10ohms, the third band is either gold or silver
        1. gold: divide number by 10
        2. silver: divide number by 100
      4. five-band color code
        1. precision resistors add a third digit
        2. tolerance is the fifth stripe
          1. brown = 1%
          2. red = 2%
          3. green = 0.5%
          4. blue = 0.25%
          5. violet = 0.1%
      5. Ne mangez rien ou je vous battrai violement, grosse bête.
    4. Variable resistors
      1. variable resistors used to control the amount of current or voltage in a circuit
        1. rheostats**
      2. rheostats have two terminals
      3. they are connected in series with a movable bypass arm
      4. they control current in a circuit with a dial
    5. potentiometers
      1. they control voltage in a circuit
      2. potentiometers have three terminals
      3. ends connected across the voltage source
      4. load is wired into the wiper arm branch in parallel to the circuit

    1.2.11. Circuit protection and control

    1. fuses
      1. protect the circuit components from excessive current
      2. current melts the fuse element creating an open
        1. slow-blow fuses** are designed to open only on a continued overload (eg. short circuit) not a temporary surge
      3. when measured with an ohmmeter, a good fuse has practically no resistance
      4. when measured with a voltmeter in the circuit, a good fuse has zero volts
      5. looks like a squiggle on a schematic
    2. circuit breakers
      1. replace fuses in applications where re-setting is desired
        1. circuit breakers can be re-used, fuses must be replaced
      2. types:
        1. magnetic breaker: current generates a magnetic field trips an armature, opening a breaker
        2. thermal overload breaker: bimetallic strip heats up under current, bends away and opens the breaker
      3. most can be reset in the cockpit
      4. don't reset it if it keeps opening
        1. repeated tripping of a circuit breaker means there is a persistent short circuit that can overload the wiring
        2. in this case, try shedding loads to isolate the short
      5. a short circuit can lead to burned wires/components and possibly fire
      6. look like an arc in schematics
    3. switches
      1. turns a circuit on and off or redirect current flow
      2. all switches have a current rating and a voltage rating
      3. current rating indicates max current it can carry when closed
      4. voltage rating indicates max voltage that can be applied across it without it arcing when open
      5. terminology
        1. pole** number of completely isolated circuits that can be controlled by a switch
        2. throw** number of closed contact positions that exist per pole
      6. SPST: single-pole, single-throw (on/off)
        1. controls a single current path
        2. closes a single set of terminals
      7. DPDT: double-pole, double-throw
        1. controls two current paths
        2. closes one of two terminals in each circuit
      8. SPDT: single-pole, double-throw
        1. single current path
        2. closes one of two terminals
      9. DPST: double-pole, single-throw
        1. controls two current paths
        2. closes on a single terminal for each circuit
    4. types
      1. toggle switch: spring-loaded in the off position
      2. aka: momentary contact two-position switch
        1. locking toggle switch: operator must pull the switch out to move it to another position
        2. typically used to prevent inadvertent activation of components such as flaps
        3. normally open (NO) switch: stays open unless held closed
        4. normally closed (NC) switch: stays closed unless held open
      3. microswitches
        1. detect very small movement
        2. primarily used as limit switches for flaps, landing gear
        3. must typically be 'rigged' correctly
      4. relays
        1. electromagnetic device that operates by induction
        2. uses an electromagnet to open or close one or more sets of contacts
        3. they have poles and throws, can be NO or NC
        4. can control high power load with a low amount of input power
        5. in remote-control applications, relays can control high power loads long distances away more efficiently than can mechanical switches
        6. aka 'contactors'
          1. armature** is the moveable arm

    1.2.12. Magnetism

    1. magnetism is ability of object to attract/repel ferrous materials
      1. e.g.: iron, steel, alnico, etc
      2. nickel and cobalt are slightly magnetic
      3. strongest permanent magnets are called rare earth magnets
    2. magnetic force
      1. every magnet has two poles
      2. magnetic field is concentrated at the poles
      3. the field exists in all directions but weakens with distance from poles
      4. field lines are drawn north to south conventionally
      5. magnetic flux (ф)
        1. number of lines of force connecting a magnet's poles
        2. units
          1. 1 Maxwell (Mx) = 1 field line
            1. part of the CGS system (centimeter-gram-second) used for small values
          2. 1 Weber (Wb) = 1*108 field lines
            1. part of MKS system, aka standard SI units, (meter-kilogram-second) used for larger values
        3. lines of flux never cross
      6. flux density (B)
        1. B = ф/area
        2. density of flux lines of force; strength of magnetic field
        3. units
          1. Gauss (G) = 1 Mx/cm2
          2. Tesla (T) = 1 Wb/m2
      7. magnetic induction
        1. induction** is the effect of one object on another without contact
        2. magnetic induction** e.g.: ferrous materials become magnetized within a field
    3. magnetic permeability (μ)
      1. magnetic fields pass through some materials more easily than others
      2. ferromagnetic materials have high permeability
      3. magnetic shields are made with highly permeable metals; they offer a more attractive path for the lines of flux
      4. shielding can protect sensitive components from magnetic disruption
    4. magnetic materials
      1. ferromagnetic materials
        1. become strongly magnetized in the same direction as the magnetizing field, with high permeability
      2. paramagnetic materials
        1. become weakly magnetized in the direction of the magnetizing field
      3. diamagnetic materials
        1. become weakly magnetized in the opposite direction of the magnetizing field
        2. permeability <1
        3. gold, antimony, copper, bismuth, etc
      4. nonmagnetic materials
        1. permeability ~ 1
      5. magnets can be weakened by hitting or heat
    5. magnetic reluctance (R)
      1. measure of opposition to magnetic lines of force through a material
      2. opposite of permeability, similar to resistance
      3. similar rules as resistance when it comes to wire
      4. reluctance is not linear; in increases as the strength of the field increases
    6. Ohm's Law for magnetic circuits
      1. mmf = фR
      2. magnemotive force = flux * reluctance
      3. magnemotive force of 1 Gilbert will cause 1 Maxwell to be set up in a material when the reluctance of the material = 1
    7. Types of magnets
      1. permanent magnet
      2. electromagnet
        1. coils of wire with external source of current
        2. strength of magnetic directly related to current
    8. Electromagnetic induction
      1. current moving through a conductor creates a magnetic field
      2. when current is shut off, the field collapses
      3. strength of an electromagnet
        1. strength is proportional to current and loops of wire
        2. IxN
          1. I = current running through the wire
          2. N = # of turns
          3. units are Ampere-turns (A-t)
        3. type of iron core also affects it
      4. Generators
        1. principles
          1. current moving through a wire creates a magnetic field.
          2. so, moving a magnetic field past a conductor could induce current
            1. electromagnetic induction** discovered in 1831 by Faraday
        2. wire coil around iron cylinder, magnet moves through the cylinder
          1. relative motion is essential
          2. lines of flux must move perpendicular to the wire
          3. Lenz's Law**
        3. The current produced always flows in such a direction that the magnetic field it produces opposes the magnet's motion
        4. conservation of energy
        5. eddy currents
      5. Induced Electromotive force (EMF)
        1. generator left hand rule shows motion of conductor, flux, and emf
        2. generator converts mechanical energy to electricity
        3. motor converts electrical energy to mechanical
        4. factors affecting amount of EMF induced in a conductor:
          1. number of wires moving through the magnetic filed
          2. strength of the magnetic filed
          3. speed of motion though the magnetic field

    1.2.13. Multimeter Function

    1. Measurement principles
      1. meters measure current flow through an electromagnetic coil
      2. current creates a magnetic field, which moves the coil to align with the magnets inside, and deflects the needle
      3. needle moves against a return spring
      4. D'Arsonval Meter Movement
      5. resolution (precision) vs accuracy
      6. understand meter loading errors
        1. when meter significantly changes the circuit you're measuring
    2. Ammeter
      1. measures current
      2. insert in series
      3. 'current sensitivity' is current required to drive the meter to full scale (typically 1mA)
        1. shunt resistors** pass any additional current
      4. range selector switch diverts current through correct shunt resistor
  • Voltmeter
    1. measures voltage
    2. connect in parallel
    3. 'DC Volts Sensitivity' is total resistance of meter when it indicates full scale voltage
      1. on Simpson VOM it's 20kΩ/V
      2. very big resistance in voltmeters
        1. multiple resistors** drop excess current before it reaches the coil (they are added in series)
  • Ohmmeters
    1. must remove component from circuit to measure
    2. uses a variable resistor to adjust to zero ohms
      1. compensates for deteriorating internal battery
    3. uses shunt resistors for different ranges
  • Meter sensitivity
    1. current needed to push meter to full scale
    2. expressed in Ω/V
    3. represents input impedance
    4. higher is better

    • 1.3. Fire Protection & Digital Logic

    1.3.1. What is fire?

    1. rapid oxidation of any compound that will readily release energy when chemically combining with oxygen
    2. Fire triangle: requirements for fire
      1. fuel
        1. combines with oxygen in the presence of heat, releasing more heat
        2. reduces itself to other chemical compounds –> chemical chain reaction
        3. types of fuel:
          1. all organic compounds
          2. many metals
          3. several gasses
          4. some synthetic compounds
          5. more
      2. heat
        1. accelerates the combining of oxygen with fuel
        2. in turn releasing more heat
        3. ignition source that exceeds the flash point/flame point of surrounding materials
          1. flash point: temp at which vaporized combustible material, when mixed with oxygen, will ignite
            1. combustion may not be sustained
          2. flame point: temp at which fuel vapor will continue to burn for at least 5sec after ignition by open flame
            1. normally higher than flash point
              1. autoignition temp**: temp that results in spontaneous autoignition
        4. Combustion points
          1. 4 factors that determine when ignition occurs:
            1. material and material state
            2. temperature
            3. oxygen content
            4. ambient pressure
        5. flammable vs combustible liquids
          1. flammable liquids: flash point <100F
            1. ignite easier
            2. eg, propane, naptha, gasoline, acetone, Avgas
            3. low flash point solvents:
              1. not to be used for cleaning or refurbishing!
          2. combustible liquids
            1. flash point >100F
            2. vapors burn
            3. eg. jet A, diesel, paints
            4. high flash point solvents:
              1. specially refined petroleum products
              2. aka 'Stoddard solvents', mineral spirits
              3. properties similar to gasoline
              4. fire hazard similar to kerosene
              5. most have flash point 100-140F with comparatively low toxicity
          3. mixed liquids
            1. some commercial solvents are mixtures of liquids with differing rates of evaporation
              1. eg, napthas and a chlorinated material
              2. different rates of evaporation present toxicity and fire hazard problems
              3. must be stored and handled with full knowledge of these hazards and appropriate precautions taken
        6. Ignition sources
          1. high temp engine areas
          2. friction
          3. high electrical current
          4. human induced flame source
      3. oxygen
        1. combines chemically with another substance: oxidation
        2. Oxygen's role in combustion
          1. rapid oxidation, with noticeable release of heat and light, is called combustion/burning
          2. gaseous oxygen is chemically stable and nonflammable
          3. combustible materials ignite more rapidly and burn with greater intensity in an oxygen-rich atmosphere
          4. oxygen combines with oil, grease, or bituminous material (eg, coal) to form a highly explosive mixture sensitive to compression or impact
        3. only qualified and authorized people should service oxygen systems
        4. liquid oxygen is a dangerous substance
          1. very low temp liquid
          2. boils at -297F, will cause frostbite
      4. chemical chain reaction produced by all three
    3. Classes of fires
    4. established by National Fire Protection Association (NFPA)
      1. Class A
        1. ordinary combustible materials
          1. wood, cloth, paper, rubber, plastics, etc
      2. Class B
        1. flammable liquids
          1. petroleum, oils, grease, tar, paints, lacquers, solvents, alcohols, flammable gasses
      3. Class C
        1. energized electrical equipment
        2. must be extinguished with nonconductive stuff
      4. Class D
        1. combustible metals
          1. magnesium, titanium, zirconium, sodium, lithium, potassium
          2. not all metals are flammable
      5. Class K
        1. combustible cooking stuff

    1.3.2. Safety Data Sheet (SDS) labels

    1. blue: health hazard
    2. red: fire hazard
    3. yellow: reactivity
    4. white: specific hazard

    1.3.3. Airframe Fire Protection Systems

    1. system should include:
      1. fire detection
      2. fire extinguishing
        1. fixed vs portable fire extinguisher
    2. different needs based on 'fire zones'
      1. fire zones: areas of an aircraft designed by the manufacturer to require fire detection/extinguishing equipment and a high degree of inherent fire resistance
      2. typical zones on aircraft that have fixed firefighting systems:
        1. engines and APUs
        2. cargo/baggage compartments
        3. lavs
        4. electronics bays
        5. wheel wells
        6. bleed air ducts
      3. manned areas
        1. least need for detection
        2. temporary areas must have detection
          1. eg, lavs with smoke detectors
        3. contain all fire systems controls
          1. so crew can discharge system
      4. unmanned areas
        1. wheel wells or empty structure spaces
        2. potentially no detection or protection provided
          1. eg, NationAir flight 2120
            1. mis-serviced tire led to fire, fatal crash
        3. may have cooling ventilators in wheel wells
    3. FARs
      1. flame resistant**: will not propagate flame after ignition source is removed
    4. Title 14
      1. requirements:
        1. no false warnings
        2. rapid indication
        3. accurate location
        4. indication fire is out
        5. indication of re-ignition
        6. continuous indication while burning
        7. means to electrically test system from cockpit
        8. resistant to damage
        9. lightweight and adaptable
        10. operates directly from the battery
        11. minimal electrical current requirements when there's no fire
        12. cockpit light that illuminates and indicate location, with audible alarm
        13. separate detection system for each engine
      2. Part 23
      3. normal category aircraft
        1. 23.2325-23.2335
        2. overheat detectors
        3. rate-of-temperature rise detectors
        4. flame detectors
        5. observation by crew
      4. Part 25
      5. large transport category aircraft
        1. rate of temp change detectors
        2. observation by crew
        3. smoke detectors
        4. overheat detectors
        5. CO detectors
        6. optical detectors
        7. cargo/baggage fire detection requirements:
          1. must provide visual detection system
          2. must detect fire well below point at which aircraft fails
          3. press-to-test
          4. must be effective in all conditions
      6. Part 43
      7. Part 65
      8. Part 91
  • System parts
    1. thermal switch
      1. spot detectors
      2. bi-metallic switch (thermostat)
      3. closes a contact at specific temp
      4. in parallel with each other, in series with the lights
    2. thermocouples
      1. 2 dissimilar metals
        1. hot junction
        2. cold junction (reference junction)
      2. rate of temp rise detectors
        1. when one heats more than the other quickly –> voltage differential
        2. it produces a small current
      3. automatically resets
      4. requires no power
      5. test uses a small electric heater
    3. continuous-loop systems
      1. dual loop systems
        1. 2 complete systems
        2. both must signal a fire
          1. AND logic
        3. may dispatch with one system inop
        4. automatic self-interogation
      2. common in transport category aircraft
      3. continuous sensing tubes
      4. provide more coverage than spot type
      5. easily routed through whole aircraft
      6. self-resetting
      7. 2 types
        1. thermistor**
      8. _negative temperature coefficient variable resistors__
      9. inconel tube filled with thermistor ceramic and conductor center
        1. resistance varies inversely as it's heated
      10. needs external power supply
      11. needs FDCU
      12. major manufacturers
        1. Fenwal
          1. 1 nickel wire in eutectic salt packing
          2. conducts from sleeve to core
          3. salt packing is the variable resistor
        2. Kidde
          1. 2 wires
            1. 1 to ground
            2. 1 to FDCU
          2. overheat set point
            1. 10 sec delay
          3. fire set point
          4. rapid change = short circuit
          5. system check tests whole system
          6. can supply nacelle temp
          7. don't kink them!
      13. systems still works with an open circuit
        1. with multiple opens, only the section between them is out
        2. pneumatic**
      14. Systron-Donner/Whittaker/Meggitt
      15. consists of
        1. thin tube of helium
        2. core of titanium hydride
      16. one end sealed shut
      17. other end connects to pressure switches
        1. 1 for high pressure
          1. indicates fire
          2. averaging function
            1. heat of the whole tube increases
            2. titanium hydride releases more helium when heated
          3. discrete function
            1. localized overheating of a short section
            2. in out-gasses hydrogen, which increases the pressure, closing the pressure switch
        2. 1 for low/no pressure
          1. indicates failure
        3. after fire is extinguished, the area colls
          1. titanium hydride reabsorbs the hydrogen
          2. pressure decreases
          3. system returns to standby
      18. does not require FDCU
  • smoke detectors
    1. used in lavs, cargo
    2. sample small amount of air
    3. air flow through the detector
      1. small fans, or
      2. compartment pressurization airflow
    4. in-line water separator
    5. heater, well, heats the air
    6. types
      1. photoelectrical
        1. photodiode creates current when exposed to photons
        2. LED is shone perpendicular to detector
        3. if there's smoke, the particles reflect light into the cell
        4. monitor photodiode = continuous self-test
      2. ionization
        1. small radioactive source aimed at ion detector cell
        2. creates nitrogen and oxygen ions
          1. cause the cell to conduct current
        3. smoke interferes by attaching to ions
  • optical flame detectors
    1. 'fire eye'
    2. detects IR or UV radiation from fire
      1. IR- light turboprop & helicopter engines
    3. used in hangars, careful when
      1. welding
      2. starting engines
      3. grinding
  • CO detection
    1. chemical color change detectors
      1. spot detectors
      2. change tan –> black
      3. time-life limited (oxidation
      4. cheap
      5. non-reusable
      6. no audible alarm
    2. electronic CO detector
      1. shown on EFIS screen
  • INSERT NOTES FROM RECORDED LECTURE
  • fire suppression systems (read chapter 17
    1. cargo compartment classification
      1. 25.855: each cargo/baggage compartment must meet requirements of 25.857
        1. 25.857 Classes of compartments
          1. A: easily discovered by crew member and fought
          2. B: accessible in flight with portable extinguisher
          3. C: not accessible in flight, but has built-in extinguisher
          4. ~D: passive oxygen starvation~
          5. E: cargo only; control ventilation to control fire
          6. F: 'B' alternative, has built in system
    2. Halogenated Hydrocarbons (Halons)
      1. most commonly used flooding agent
      2. 5x heavier than air
      3. noncorrosive
      4. does not affect the material it touches
      5. requires no cleanup
      6. HCL-125 common replacement (because it's better for environment)
    3. CO2 extinguishing systems
      1. older aircraft with recips used CO2
      2. all newer aircrafts with turbines use Halon or equivalent extinguishing agent
    4. Fixed fire extinguishing systems
      1. designed to dilute the atmosphere with an inert agent that doesn't support combustion
      2. perforated tubing or discharge nozzles distribute the extinguishing agent
      3. discharge indicators
        1. immediate visual evidence of container discharge
        2. thermal discharge indicator
          1. red disc
          2. safety pressure release device
          3. indicates that the fire extinguisher container needs to be replaced before flight
        3. intentional discharge indicator
          1. yellow disc
          2. indicates that extinguishing system was activated by a crew member
          3. container needs to be replaced before flight
    5. Cargo compartment fire extinguishing system
      1. when the discharge switch is activated, the squib fires and a projectile breaks the diaphragm
      2. nitrogen pressure inside the bottle pushes the halon through the discharge port into the cargo compartment
      3. pressure switch activates a flight deck indication that a bottle has been discharged
      4. types:
        1. dump systems
          1. releases extinguishing agent directly when the cargo fire discharge switch is activated
          2. High Rate of Discharge (HRD)
            1. one shot, massize, short duration blast (2-3sec)
            2. electrically fired squib (cartridge)
              1. CAD/cartridge/squib
              2. explosive, blows a projectile
              3. life-limited components
                1. date stamp
                2. service life recommended by manufacturer (~5yrs)
              4. may be tested in place with special equipment
                1. do not use analog ohmmeter!!!
              5. CAD is matched to the bottle, no switching
            3. manual/solenoid-operated in some application
              1. sometime operated manually
            4. projectile penetrates the sealing disc to release Halon
            5. large diameter plumbing and perforated manifolds or spray nozzles distribute agent
            6. floods a contained area for short period
            7. systems may be single, dual, or cross-feedable
            8. second (follow-on) discharge
              1. possible with 2-bottle systems
              2. check valves to keep halon from going into other bottle
                1. 2-way check valve
                2. required in a 2-shot system to keep halon from reserve container from getting into empty main container
        2. metered system
          1. bottles discharge slowly at a controlled rate
          2. keeps the correct concentration of extinguishing agent in the cargo compartment to keep the fire extinguished for up to 180min
          3. used on planes going across the ocean
      5. Cargo smoke detection systems
        1. transport aircraft cargo bay will normally contain at least 3 ambient air smoke detectors
          1. any 2 will sound an alarm
          2. ventilation and heating systems will close
          3. flight crew deploys agents
        2. cockpit indications in case of smoke
          1. master warning lights come on
          2. fire warning aural
          3. cargo fire warning message
          4. cargo fire warning light
          5. (master warning lights and fire warning aural are disabled during part of takeoff)
    6. Lav fire extinguishers
      1. to extinguish fires in waste compartment
      2. pressurized Halon 1301 (or equivalent)
        1. "Halon hand grenades"
      3. when the temp in the waste compartment reaches ~170F, the solder that seals the nozzles melts and the halon is discharged automatically
      4. weighing the bottle is often the only way to see if it's full
      5. often has temp indicating strip
  • Fire detection system maintenance
    1. refer to manufacturer's instructions
    2. sensing elements of a continuous-loop system should be inspected regularly
    3. container pressure check
      1. prescribed min and max limits
      2. pressure depends on temp
      3. if the pressure doesn't fall w/i limits, replace container
  • Challenges
    1. cargo
      1. combustible materials are easy to control
      2. ignition materials are easier to control
      3. cargo is the exception
      4. eg. ValueJet 592
    2. batteries
      1. lithium batteries prohibited in cargo holds
        1. carry-on only
        2. minimal charge
        3. protected/padded
        4. check TSA/DOT requirements
        5. check airline requirements
      2. they easily ignite
      3. primary battery for consumer equipment
      4. recently began use in aircraft
        1. Boeing787 uses them
      5. contains flammable electrolyte and is pressurized
    3. combustible lines and hoses
      1. keep ignition and fuel separate
      2. protection must be provided
      3. static discharge is a danger
        1. empty fuel tanks are more likely to ignite; vapors
    4. seats
      1. coverings and panel materials must be approved
      2. should be certified with a stamp

    • 1.3.4. Emergency exit systems

    1. part of the fire protection system
    2. may be normal doors or extra exits
    3. systems to direct people to exits on large aircraft
    4. must function correctly
    5. any aircraft with >43 seats, must be able to evacuate within 90secs

    1.3.5. Powerplant Fire Protection Systems

    1. detection systems
      1. lots of the same stuff from airframe fire detection
      2. FARs
        1. 14 CFR Part 23 &25 requires detection on:
          1. multi-engine turbine
          2. multi-engine turbo recip
          3. engines not visible from cockpit
          4. all commuter and transport category
          5. APU compartment fo any aircraft
        2. not required on
          1. most single and twin GA aircraft
      3. ideal fire detection system
        1. no false alarms
        2. rapid indication
        3. accurate location
        4. accurate indication when it's out
        5. indication of reignition
        6. continuous indication through duration
        7. detector circuitry that operates directly form the aircraft power system without inverters
        8. min electrical current when not indicating
        9. each detector should turn on a cockpit light, show location with audible alarm
        10. separate system for each engine
      4. Spot indicators
        1. Thermal switch
          1. thermostats
          2. In parallel with each other, in series with the lights
          3. push to test lights
          4. test relay checks all wiring and bulbs
        2. Thermocouple
          1. detects rate of temp rise
          2. changing difference between hot junction and reference junction generates tiny voltage
          3. 4 milliamps closes sensitive relay, closes shave relay
          4. thermocouples–> relays–> lights
          5. connected in series with each other and the lights
        3. Optical flame detectors
          1. Fire eyes
          2. detects IR or UV radiation
          3. Used in light turboprops, helicopters, hangars
          4. welding or grinding could set it off
        4. Pneumatic detectors
          1. thin tube filled with helium
          2. core of titanium hydride
          3. one end sealed, the other has 2 pressure switches
            1. high= fire
            2. low= system failure
        5. thermistors
          1. variable resistor with negative temp coefficient
          2. iconel tube filled with thermistor ceramic
          3. needs external power supply (FDCU)
          4. automatically resets
          5. still works with an open; with multiple opens it fails between them
      5. Continuous loop systems
        1. transport category
        2. continuous sensing tubes
        3. better coverage
        4. easily routed through aircraft structure
        5. Fire detection control unit (FDCU)
          1. simple systems
            1. resistance monitoring and alarm output
          2. complex systems
            1. controls all fire protection functions
        6. Kidde fire detection loops
          1. 2 level response
            1. overheat warning
            2. Fire warning
          2. temp trend
            1. analog signal can display nacelle temp
          3. system test
            1. tests whole system
          4. fault indication
            1. short discriminator circuit detects a short on the sensing element loop
        7. Dual loop systems
          1. 2 complete systems
          2. AND logic; both must indicate
          3. automatic self-interrogation
          4. plane can fly with one inoperable
        8. Troubleshooting
          1. intermittent alarms
            1. intermittent short, can be caused by kinks and bends
          2. false alarms
            1. go through system to find fault
          3. failure to alarm
            1. defective test switch or control unit
            2. no power
            3. light inop
            4. open in sensing element or connecting wiring
    2. extinguishing/suppression system
      1. FARs
        1. Part 23
          1. aka normal category aircraft, level4
          2. commuter aircraft need at least one-shot fire extinguishing system
          3. max weight 19klbs
          4. max seats 19
          5. 23.2325
            1. on level 4, materials must be self-extinguishing
              1. cockpit
              2. cabin
                1. pilot, while seated, can easily extinguish fire
              3. baggage and cargo compartments
                1. located where fire is visible to pilots, or
                2. have means of detection, warning, extinguishing
            2. on levels 3-4, passengers should have fire extinguishers available
        2. Part 25 aircraft must have
          1. 2 discharges
          2. each produces adequate agent concentration
          3. 25.1195-25.1201
          4. must have 2-shot extinguishing system for each fire zone
            1. each shot must be adequate to extinguish the fire alone
            2. 2-way check valve required to prevent reserve from backing up into the empty main container
            3. some exceptions only need 1 shot
              1. APUs
              2. fuel-burning heaters
              3. combustion equipment
        3. extinguishing agents and containers
          1. must extinguish flames emanating from any combustible materials
          2. need thermal stability over reasonable temp range
          3. people need to be protected from any toxic extinguishing agents
          4. containers need pressure release valve that discharges without damaging the aircraft
            1. also need an indication that it has been discharged (thermal disk)
            2. Kidde system also has temp compensated pressure switch to track pressure variations
            3. may include a pressure gauge; otherwise you have to weigh it
          5. container temp must be maintained to keep pressure within acceptable range
            1. prescribed range, varies with temp
            2. if container is outside limits, replace it
          6. if container has a squib, it must be stored so it doesn't go off by accident
          7. components in the system can't make things worse and must be fireproof
          8. Halon 1301
            1. good firefighting, low toxicity
            2. noncorrosive, no effect on materials it contacts
            3. no clean-up required
            4. can only use recycled stuff; bad for ozone layer
            5. replacements are being developed
        4. powerplant fire suppression systems
          1. hazards particular to turbines
            1. thermodynamic
              1. mostly caused by ice, excess booked air leakage, or fault controls that permit the compressor to stall
              2. anything that upsets the proportion of cooling airflow
            2. mechanical
              1. fractured or throw blades
                1. much worse in the forward section
                2. can penetrate turbine case or tail cone, possible fire hazard
                3. can cut lines, spilling flammable fluids
          2. powerplant fire zones
            1. engine power section
            2. engine accessory section
            3. any powerplant compartment with no isolation between power section and accessory section (except recips)
            4. APU compartment
            5. fuel-burning heater
            6. turbine compressor
            7. turbine combustion section
          3. fire switch
            1. usually in overhead center panel or center console in cockpit
            2. when activated
              1. shuts off fuel, engine off
              2. isolates engine from other systems
              3. activates extinguishing system
                1. still not discharged
            3. fire switch is locked unless there's a fire, to prevent accidental discharge
              1. can be manually overridden
          4. APUs
            1. Often run without anyone in the cockpit
              1. "unattended mode"
              2. extinguisher discharged automatically
            2. if at least 1 engine is running, it is in "attended mode"
              1. must be manually discharged
            3. if fire is detected, fire switch
              1. warnings light up
              2. automatically stops APU
              3. isolates APU
              4. can discharge extinguisher
            4. shut down panel on landing gear for ground crew
          5. Turbine engines
            1. 2 loops in each engine
              1. only 1 needs to be working
              2. overheat or fire detection
              3. temp recording
              4. continuous fault monitoring
            2. built-in test equipment (BITE) tests detection system
              1. when it first gets power
              2. after power interruption
              3. every 5 min of operation
            3. if overheat is detected, info is sent to AIMS and warning electronics unit cockpit indications
              1. master caution light
              2. caution aural warning
              3. engine overheat caution message
            4. if engine fire is detected, cockpit indications
              1. master warning light
              2. fire aural warning
              3. engine fire warning message
              4. engine fire warning light
              5. fire control switch warning light
            5. fire switch functions
              1. engine fire indication
              2. isolates engine from other systems
                1. fuel
                2. hydraulics
                3. electrical
                4. air
              3. stops engine
              4. control engine fire extinguishing system
                1. pull and rotate to discharge
          6. Definitions
            1. fireproof
              1. withstands 2000F for 15 min as well as or better than steel
            2. fire resistant
              1. only 5 min

    1.3.6. Shop fire protection

    1. Extinguisher types
      1. water
        1. best for Class A fire
        2. smothers and cools
        3. not for Class B
        4. use with caution on Class C
          1. turn off power
          2. residual electricity may remain
        5. Never with Class D
        6. pressurized and discharged
      2. CO2
        1. UL toxicity rating of 5 (not very toxic)
          1. scale is 2-6, most to least toxic
        2. Used for classes A, B, C
        3. smothers and cools
        4. Never use on Class D!
        5. Generally self-expelling
        6. be careful using it in enclosed spaces
        7. can cause frostbite
      3. Halon
        1. Halogenated hydrocarbon
        2. can be used on Classes A, D, but less effective
        3. called freon
        4. limited by EPA, but can use reduced halon
      4. carbon tetrachloride
        1. Halon 104
        2. UL rating: 3 (when cold)
        3. banned, very toxic!
        4. produces hydrochloric acid vapor, chlorine, and phosgene gas when heated
        5. there are regulations for how to dispose of it
      5. methyl bromide
        1. Halon 1001
        2. toxicity rating: 2!
        3. liquefied gas
        4. corrosive to aluminum, magnesium, and zinc
        5. not recommended for aircraft
      6. Halon 1011
        1. chlorobromethane
        2. toxicity rating: 3
        3. not recommended for aircraft
      7. Halon 1202
        1. dibromodiflouromethane
        2. toxicity rating: 4
        3. not recommended for aircraft
      8. Halon 1211
        1. toxicity rating: 5
        2. colorless
        3. non-corrosive
        4. evaporates quickly, leaves no residue
        5. similar to CO2, but not dangerously cold
        6. doesn't harm aircraft materials
        7. blanketing mist blocks oxygen, and it interferes chemically with the prices of combustion
      9. Halon 1301
        1. bromotriflouromethane
        2. liquefied gas
        3. toxicity rating: 6
        4. all characteristics of 1211, but vapor spray is more difficult to detect
        5. the best we have
        6. bad for the environment
      10. dry powder
        1. used for Class D fire
          1. also works with classes B, C
        2. not recommended for aircraft use, except for metal fire
        3. leftover chemical residue, dust can damage electronic and other delicate equipment
        4. difficult to clean up
    2. Extinguisher inspection criteria
      1. proper location
      2. appropriate type of extinguisher
      3. unbroken safety seals
      4. clean, no dirt or rust
      5. gauge/indicator within range
      6. proper weight
      7. no nozzle obstruction
      8. no obvious damage
    3. Identifying extinguishers
      1. marked for Class of fire
      2. make sure appropriate type in proper location
      3. markings on extinguishers must be easily visible and identifiable from 3ft
      4. markings on wall panels must be identifiable from 25ft
      5. Class markings:
        1. A: green triangle
        2. B: red square
        3. C: blue circle
        4. D: brown star
    4. Using portable extinguishers
      1. Steps (PASS)
        1. Pull safety pin
        2. Aim at base, from 8ft away
        3. Squeeze handle
        4. Sweep side to side
      2. Keep extinguisher upright
    5. Flammable/combustible cleaning agents
      1. most solvents are flammable
      2. min flashpoint 105F
        1. to avoid explosions
      3. chlorinated solvents are nonflammable, but toxic
      4. avoid carbon tetrachloride
      5. always check the SDS
      6. High flash point agents
        1. Stoddard solvents
          1. flash point 100-140F
          2. similar to gasoline, but hazardous as kerosene
          3. low toxicity
          4. most common petroleum-based solvent
          5. can clean grease and dirt
      7. Low flashpoint agents
        1. flashpoint below 100F
        2. flammable liquids
        3. don't use for cleaning or refurbishing aircraft
        4. acetone
        5. AVGAS
        6. MEK
          1. for metal surfaces and paint stripper
          2. flashes at 24F
          3. toxic
          4. destroys plastics
        7. naptha
          1. aliphatic naphtha
            1. wipe down clean surfaces just before painting
            2. can clean acrylics and rubber
            3. flashes at 80F
          2. aromatic naphtha
            1. toxic
            2. attacks acrylic and rubber
            3. be careful use adequate controls
          3. safety solvent
            1. trichloroethane
            2. general cleaner
            3. nonflammable
        8. Toluol
        9. kerosene
          1. general cleaner
          2. will leave residue that must be cleaned off
        10. cleaning compound for oxygen systems
          1. anhydrous ethyl alcohol
          2. isopropyl alcohol
          3. don't use anything that leaves an oily film!
          4. used to clean components
        11. benzene, turpentine, mineral spirits, etc
      8. Mixed liquids
        1. Be careful, components may escalate at different rates, changing flammability characteristics
      9. Guidelines for cleaning
        1. adequate ventilation
        2. no open flames
        3. no spark-producing tools
        4. approved electrical equipment
        5. switches should remain in place and not worked on
        6. when using flammable liquids
        7. flashpoint <100F
          1. do it outside
          2. have 20-B or 4-A fire extinguisher (or better) available
          3. if dealing with aluminum corrosion, don't use all purpose extinguisher, ABC
          4. when working indoors, there should be automatic fire-protection equipment
      10. Extinguisher number ratings
        1. numbers indicate extinguishing effectiveness
        2. only for Class A and B fires
        3. proportional
    6. Aircraft fueling
      1. ground at both ends
      2. volatility
        1. ability of liquid to evaporate at low temp
      3. AVGAS is volatile
      4. static electricity is a hazard
      5. clean any spills on clothing or skin ASAP
      6. make sure electrical systems are off
      7. don't fuel with lightning
      8. don't fuel near ground radar station
      9. be careful, don't be stupid

    1.3.7. Digital logic

    1. binary
      1. very easy, use your brain
    2. types of diagrams
      1. Installation diagrams
        1. identifies each component and their location in the aircraft
      2. Schematic diagrams
        1. locates components with respect to each other within the system
      3. Block diagrams
        1. simplify a complex system, displayed as blocks
      4. Wiring diagrams
        1. show electrical wiring and circuitry
      5. Troubleshooting chart
        1. illustrates sequence or flow of events
      6. Logic Flowcharts
        1. logic gates [image]()
          1. AND** produces a 0 output unless both the X and Y inputs are both 1, when the output is 1.
          2. NAND** is the negation of AND, it produces a 1 output unless both the X and Y inputs are both 1, when the output is 0.
          3. OR** produces a 0 output only when both the X and Y inputs are 0, otherwise the output is 1.
          4. NOR** is the negation of OR and produces a 1 output when both inputs are 0, otherwise the output is 0.
          5. XOR** is an exclusive OR and outputs 1 if there is only a single 1 input between X and Y, inputs of two 0s or two 1s produce an output of 0.
          6. XNOR** is the inverse of XOR and inputs of two 0s and two 1s will produce an output of 1, it produces an output of 0 if there is a single 1 input between X and Y.
          7. NOT** is also called an inverter; if the input is 1, the output is 0, and if the input is a 0 the output is 1.
    3. LAMM Schematics
      1. developed standardize schematics
      2. before, they were component-oriented, not system-oriented
      3. depicts integration between hardware, software, electronics and flight controls, etc
      4. reduces maintenance time and cost
      5. quick and easy source for training and troubleshooting
      6. establishes relationship between system malfunction and defective component
      7. 3 levels
        1. system block diagram
          1. very simple overview
        2. simplified system schematic
          1. intermediate depth and scope
        3. system schematic
          1. detailed
          2. shows all LRUs, wiring and all functional interfaces between sub-systems

    2. Airframe

    2.1. Sheet Metal

    2.1.1. Materials and Processes

    1. sheet metal
      1. measured in 0.001" increments
      2. all have different strength properties and pair with different rivets
      3. thicknesses usually inked onto sheet– writing in direction of grain
      4. inking includes
        1. alloy number and heat treatment (eg 7075-T6)
        2. thickness measurement
    2. designations
      1. <0.006 is foil
      2. 0.006-0.249 is sheet
      3. >0.250 is plate
    3. Aluminum
      1. general info
        1. ~8% of earth's crust
        2. not found in metallic state
        3. lightweight (1/3 of steel)
        4. easy to fabricate
        5. good heat conduction
        6. resists corrosion
        7. available in many forms
        8. chemical affinity for other elements
      2. production
      3. weight halves at each stage
        1. begin with bauxite ore
        2. aluminum extraction gets aluminum oxide Al2O3
        3. electrolytic production gets pure aluminum
      4. properties
        1. low tensile strength (9K-13K psi)
        2. corrosion resistant
        3. exposure to air form aluminum oxide
        4. alloying (eg with copper) makes it more prone to corrosion
        5. very anodic with other metals
        6. aluminum oxide provides abrasion resistance and increases corrosion resistance
          1. increased elastic strength
        7. Al is a 'hot short' material
          1. it looses strength at high temperatures
      5. products
        1. wrought
          1. rolled, drawn, forged, or extruded
          2. sheet, plate, etc
        2. cast
          1. melted and poured in a mold
          2. crankcase, cylinder heads
        3. alloy series
          1. 1000: pure aluminum
          2. 2000: copper
            1. ductile, malleable, heat-treatable
          3. 3000: manganese
            1. resists wear and corrosion
          4. 4000: silicon
            1. low melting point, adds strength
            2. good for welding rod
          5. 5000: magnesium
            1. corrosion resistant
          6. 6000: silicon and magnesium
            1. heat-treatable** formable, and weldable
          7. 7000: zinc
            1. heat-treatable**, strong, stiff
        4. H marking
          1. H type of treatment-hardness in 1/8 increments
          2. eg H32 = 1/4 hard
          3. H1x: strain hardened
          4. H2x: strain hardened and partially annealed
          5. H3x: strain hardened and stabilized
      6. caustic soda test
        1. to see if Al alloy has copper in it, rub a 10% lye solution on the surface
        2. if there is copper, it will turn black
        3. neutralize it with a nitric acid solution
      7. Alclad
        1. ~5.5% thick layer of pure aluminum on alloy sheet
        2. to prevent corrosion; it oxidizes and blocks air
    4. Magnesium
      1. most widely distributed element
      2. 5th most abundant
      3. extracted from magnesite carnalite and dolomite ores, and seawater
      4. production by thermal and electrolytic processes
      5. commercially available in cast and wrought forms
      6. alloying/heat treatment is required for strength properties
      7. used in aircraft for landing gear, housings, small castings
      8. lightweight, not very strong
        1. very** anodic
      9. check for magnesium with a 1% silver nitrate solution, see if it turns black
    5. Steel
      1. refined iron with carbon
      2. SAE designation
        1. SAE A B C
          1. A: type of steel
            1. carbon
            2. nickel
            3. nickel and chromium
            4. molybdenum
            5. chromium
            6. chromium and vanadium
          2. B: percent of alloying element
          3. C: carbon content in 0.01% increments
            1. low
            2. 0.1-0.3%
            3. medium
            4. 0.3-0.6%
            5. high
            6. 0.6-2.2%
      3. stainless steel
        1. 2 times the pressure need to shear/punch
        2. high chromium and nickel content
        3. nonstructural=gas weldable
        4. structural=spot weld only
        5. low coefficient of heat conduction, good for firewalls
        6. high coefficient of expansion
        7. exhaust collectors, heat exchangers, manifolds
      4. chrome-moly
        1. SAE 4130
        2. molybdenum with 1%chromium, .25% molybdenum and 0.3% carbon
        3. common tubing used for aircraft structures
    6. Monel
      1. 2/3 nickel, 1/3 copper
      2. only hardened with cold working
      3. weldable
      4. good corrosion resistance
      5. rivets, carburetor needle valves, exhaust manifolds
    7. Annealing
      1. aluminum
      2. 1100, 3003, 5052
        1. heat to 900F and air cool
        2. difficult judging temp– no color change when heated
        3. coat with soot from candle or torch, then heat until soot disappears
        4. keep the torch moving
      3. brass and copper
        1. heat to 1200F (bright red heat) and quench in water
      4. steel
        1. heat to 1000-1200F and cool slowly

    2.1.2. Riveting

    1. Driven rivet dimensions
      1. Shop head dimensions
      2. for A, AD, B, and DD rivets
        1. 1:3 ratio on shop head
        2. ideal
          1. 1.5xdiameter wide
          2. .5xdiameter thick
        3. min
          1. 1.25xdia wide
          2. .66xdia thick
        4. max
          1. 1.66xdia wide
          2. .33xdia thick
      3. Need to compact it enough to coldwork it to necessary strength, but not so much that is is too thin
    2. Finding correct rivet dimensions
      1. instructions:
        1. diameter
          1. take thicker skin, multiply x3
          2. diameter cannot be less than 3x as thick as the thickest skin
          3. multiply that by 32
          4. increments of diameter are 1/32"
          5. round up
        2. length
          1. shank diameter (eg 3/32") x 1.5
          2. must stick out 1.5x diameter before bucking
          3. add thicknesses of both skins
          4. this is the grip length
          5. multiply x 16
          6. increments of length are 1/16"
          7. round up
      2. example 1:
        1. thicknesses: .015, .025
        2. find diameter
          1. .025x3=.075
          2. .075x32=2.4
          3. 2.4 rounds up to 3
          4. so, AN 470AD**3**-3
        3. find length
          1. 3/32 x 1.5=.140625
          2. .015+.025+.140625=.180625
          3. .180625x16=2.89
          4. round up to 3, so 3/16" length
          5. AN 470AD3-3
      3. example 2:
        1. .032 and.040 thickness skins
        2. 4/32" dia
        3. 9/16" length
        4. AN470AD4-4.5
      4. example 3:
        1. .040 and .051
        2. 5 dia
        3. 5.5 length
        4. AN470AD5-5.5
      5. example 4:
        1. .040 and .062 sheets
        2. AN470AD**6-6.5** rivets
      6. for tubing, rivet diameter must be at least 1/8 the OD of thickest tube
    3. Rivet layout
      1. distances are often expressed in terms of rivet diameters (D)
      2. row of rivets is defined by longest distance of rivet direction
        1. edge distance**
      3. distance from center of the rivet hole to the nearest edge of the sheet
        1. min edge distance for protruding head rivet = 2D (2x rivet diameters) from center of the hole
          1. rivet pitch (spacing)**
      4. distance from the center of the hole to the center of the next hole
        1. min pitch for protruding head rivets = 3D from center of holes
          1. transverse pitch (gauge)**
      5. perpendicular distance between centers of rivets in adjacent rows
        1. usually 75% of pitch
        2. min pitch = 2.5D
      6. stagger
      7. the offset between rivet rows
      8. abreast vs staggered rivet rows
        1. stagger holes when they line up with the grain of the sheet
          1. if they are abreast, the sheet is weakened along the grain
    4. Rivet installation
      1. Precision Reamers
        1. used for precision holes/close tolerances
        2. drill undersize hole first, then ream it out
        3. always turn it cutting direction
        4. make sure to keep it perpendicular to the surface!
      2. Clecos and pliers
        1. pin-type
          1. work well on thinner materials
        2. side clamp
          1. used for initial clamping before drilling holes
        3. wing-nut
          1. hold better
          2. more common
          3. good for thicker materials
          4. good for parts that need to be held tightly
          5. eg, parts under tension around a bend
          6. may be fastened with drill extension
        4. power
          1. installed with a Cleco Runner
            1. box end wrenches work, too
        5. colors indicate rivet size
          1. 3, silver
          2. 4, copper
          3. 5, black
          4. 10, brass/gold
      3. Procedure
        1. mark and center punch hole lightly
        2. drill with correct pilot drill size
        3. enlarge hole to correct ream size
        4. deburr the hole
        5. insert and drive the rivet
        6. inspect for defects
    5. Flush assembly methods
      1. Dimpling
        1. for thin materials
        2. both materials are drilled and dimpled
        3. pretty strong, because material is cold-worked
        4. most dimpling methods slightly enlarge the hole
        5. be careful not to use too long a rivet; clinched rivets are easy to get
        6. methods
          1. draw dimpling (coin pressing)
            1. rivet and draw dimple block are used to create a dimple
            2. make sure rivet is correct length before dimpling
            3. don't remove the rivet after dimpling
            4. use ball peen hammer
          2. radius dimpling
          3. like in the shop
            1. standard/'spring back' dies mounted in pneumatic/hand squeezers used to create a dimple
            2. materials don't nest very well, so better for just 2 thicknesses of sheet
            3. remove air source before adjusting the die
            4. punch and die must match!
            5. test on scrap first, make sure it's set properly
          4. coin dimpling
            1. superior dimples are created by special die sets
            2. interlocking ridges are formed, stronger
            3. good nesting of dimples, female die has ram driver in it
            4. cross-sectional thickness and conical shape that allows for a better fit of the rivet to the material
            5. good when there are multiple thicknesses of material
          5. thermo dimpling (hot dimpling)
            1. dimple is made by specially heated die sets
            2. process is used with materials that have low formability
            3. dies heated to 650°-800°
            4. stainless steel can't be thermo dimpled :(
            5. common materials
              1. 7075-T6
              2. 2024-T81
              3. Magnesium
              4. Titanium and its alloys
      2. Machine (standard cut) countersinking
        1. for thick materials; thickness of material must by greater than the thickness of the rivet head!
        2. top material is machine countersunk
        3. bottom material is drilled only
        4. good countersinking relies on good drilling and deburring
      3. Combination of dimpling and machine countersinking (sub-cut countersinking)
        1. for combinations of think and thin materials
        2. top material is dimpled
        3. bottom material is machine countersunk at ~120°
        4. use a draw dimple block for support when dimpling
      4. NACA Riveting method
      5. National Advisory Committee for Aeronautics
        1. formed head is driven into a countersunk depression and then shaved
        2. provides interior seal– liquid tight with aerodynamically smooth exterior
        3. basically, countersink the back sheet for the shop head to fill, then shave off the shop head so it's smooth
        4. can be 'double flush' so it's countersunk on both sides
      6. other flush fasteners
        1. countersunk screws
        2. structural or non-structural
          1. 100deg
          2. 82deg
        3. wing slugs
          1. common in double flush joints
          2. two formed heads driven into countersunk nests
          3. microshave it smooth afterward
        4. crown flush rivet
          1. slightly rounded head countersunk rivet, for better contact with rivet gun
        5. Briles rivets
          1. need special piloted counter-bore/countersink
          2. dual gauge used to check flush condition
          3. simultanteous rivet head and shank expansion

    2.1.3. Welding

    1. what is it?
      1. process of fusing metal when it is in a molten state to form an amalgamation of metal
      2. molecular union or fusion of two or more pieces of metal by heating to the melting point
      3. the most important characteristic of a sound weld is weld fusion
    2. types
      1. gas welding
      2. flammable gas mixed with oxygen
        1. oxy-acetylene
        2. oxygen mixed with acetylene
          1. acetylene
            1. man made, highly flammable, wide flammability range
            2. colorless and slightly lighter than air
            3. produced by the reaction of calcium carbide and water
            4. smells of garlic
            5. easier for beginners to observe fusion
            6. slower
            7. ~1/2 the heat of arc welding
            8. acetylene is dissolved in acetone to keep it stable
              1. watch out for a purple flame; that means you're burning acetone, not acetylene!
            9. unstable above 15psi
            10. reactive with copper, silver alloys, and mercury; produces potentially explosive compounds
            11. left hand threads on the fittings
          2. oxygen
            1. 99.5% pure
            2. production
              1. electrolysis
              2. separation of oxygen from water
              3. fractional distillation
                1. compress air into a liquid, boil off the components (different boiling points)
            3. usually painted green
            4. must be regularly hydrostatically tested (every 10 yrs)
            5. not flammable, but supports combustion
            6. never get petroleum products on it
            7. no open flames
            8. 1F change –> 5psi chang
            9. 11.2ft3/lb
            10. open valve slowly
            11. right hand threads on the fittings
          3. equipment
            1. pressurized gas cylinders (oxygen and acetylene)
            2. regulators
              1. high cylinder pressure
              2. low line pressure to torch
              3. types
                1. single stage
                2. two stage
              4. clockwise –> increase pressure!!!
              5. counterclockwise –> decrease pressure!!!
            3. hoses
            4. torch body
            5. torch tip
              1. injector type; not common
              2. balance type; two needles valves control gas flow
              3. size matters
              4. potential problems
                1. backfire
                  1. momentary backward flow of gasses at the torch tip, causes flame to extinguish
                2. flashback
                  1. burning of gasses within the torch, which causes the flame to be extinguished at the tip and produces a shrill hissing/squealing noise
                  2. Very dangerous!
                  3. possible damage to hose or regulators
                  4. turn off immediately
            6. fire brick
            7. reinforcing rod
              1. filler material which does not conduct a current, increases bead mass
              2. must be made of a compatible material
              3. strengthens weld and acts as heat sink
              4. should also add ductility and corrosion resistance
              5. welding turns wrought to cast, which is weaker; rod compensates for that
              6. diameter generally equal to material thickness
              7. steel rods are copper clad
          4. flame types
            1. neutral flame
              1. used for most welding
              2. complete combustion from a balanced mixture of oxygen and acetylene; by products just CO2 and water
              3. 5850F
              4. acetylene feather even with or barely visible befond well-rounded inner cone
              5. almost colorless/ blue gas envelop
            2. reducing (carburizing) flame
              1. overabundance of acetylene
              2. acetylene feather beyond inner cone
              3. brush-like gas envelope
              4. 5700F
              5. can harden the surface of steel
              6. slight carburizing flame sometimes used to weld stainless steel
            3. oxidizing flame
              1. excess oxygen
              2. short pointed inner core
              3. short gas envelope
              4. hissing sound of gas flowing
              5. 6300F
              6. can burn or cut the metal
              7. slight oxidizing flame sometimes used to weld copper alloys (brass or bronze)
          5. shut down
            1. close torch valves, acetylene, then oxygen
            2. close cylinder valves
            3. open both torch valves and make sure pressure gauges are at 0
            4. close torch valves
            5. turn adjusting screws on both regulators all the way out (off)
        3. hydrogen
          1. burns with colorless flame! hard to adjust the torch
        4. liquid propane
        5. MAPP
        6. methylacetylene-propadiene
      3. electric arc welding
      4. heat created by electric arc
        1. stick welding
        2. or shielded metal arc welding (SMAW)
          1. not commonly used in aviation; not good for precision work
          2. how it works
            1. consumable electrode (wire) which carries current and fuses into the surface of the metal
            2. arc digs into the surface, cratering it, and creates a lot of heat
            3. temperature of the arc is >10k degrees F
            4. electrode polarity affects penetration
              1. DC electrode positive (DCEP)
              2. reverse polarity
                1. deepest penetration
              3. DC electrode negative (DCSP)
              4. straight polarity*
                1. consumes electrode much quicker
              5. AC
                1. compromise between other two
          3. power source info
            1. duty cycle
              1. length of time that a welding machine can be used continually at its rated output in any 10 min period (usually ~60%)
            2. current setting
              1. constant current power supply is adjusted according to electrode diameter and material thickness
          4. CLAMS
            1. current
            2. length of arc
            3. angle of electrode
            4. manipulation
            5. speed of travel
          5. dangerous
            1. lots of sparking; requires lots of protective equipment
            2. light is dangerous to eyes and skin (infrared and ultraviolet)
          6. characteristics
            1. low voltage
            2. high current
            3. wire is covered in a flux-like material
              1. it gives off 'slag' after welding, making the weld cool more slowly
              2. prevents cracking, embrittlement
              3. prevents oxidation from affecting the weld
              4. the flux covering determines which polarity to select
              5. must be removed after completion
            4. when flux is consumed, it produces a shielding gas to protect the molten metal
            5. flux contains alloying elements, impurity scavengers, and de-oxidizing agents
          7. equipment
            1. AC or DC constant current welder
            2. electrode lead/holder
            3. work lead/clamp
            4. chipping hammer, wire brush
            5. bench with ventilation system!!!
            6. protective equipment
              1. helmet with #10 shade and plastic protectors
              2. leather jacket, gloves, chaps
        3. tungsten inert gas (TIG)
        4. or gas tungsten arc welding (GTAW)
          1. electrode heats the material, while an inert gas shields the metal from oxygen to prevent oxidation
          2. use a filler rod to reinforce weld (electrode not consumed)
          3. use tungsten electrode because of its high melting point
