          4. usually has a foot pedal to control current flow
          5. advantages
            1. highly visible weld pool
            2. no slag or spatter
            3. quiet
            4. no need for flux (or related problems)
              1. developed to get away from using flux (like in oxyacetylene welding) because the flux can get included in the weld, weakening it
              2. also better for specialty materials
            5. probably best welding style for aerospace
            6. better for metals that oxidize quickly (Aluminum, Magnesium, Titanium)
            7. very precise (good for turbine engines)
          6. equipment
            1. constant current power supply (like in stick welding)
            2. post-flow timer, gas continues to flow a bit after you stop (protects electrode/puddle while cooling)
            3. TIG torch assembly
            4. regulator and flow meter for shielding gas
            5. oscillator for high frequency AC
            6. coolant supply and lines for water-cooled torches
            7. safety equipment
              1. helmet with lens
              2. leather jacket and gloves
              3. dark clothing to reduce light reflection
              4. don't wear cotton
        5. metal inert gas (MIG)
        6. or gas metal arc welding (GMAW)
          1. very rarely used on aircraft
          2. consumable wire fed electrode short circuits the material, creating heat
          3. regulated shielding gas protects the pool (carbon dioxide or a mix with argon)
          4. constant voltage type power supply
          5. lower quality than TIG welding
      5. electric resistance welding
      6. heat created by electric resistance to current flow
        1. better with stainless steel, not so good with aluminum
        2. depressions left in the surface by electrodes
          1. nugget** in between sheets
        3. quality tested with a peel test
        4. types:
          1. spot welding
          2. or resistance spot welding (RSW)
            1. the electrical resistance of the material being welded heats it
          3. seam welding
    3. safety
      1. protective equipment
        1. goggles with shade
        2. helmet
      2. NO:
        1. horseplay
        2. petroleum products near oxygen equipment
        3. heating, brazing, welding of plated parts or hazardous materials
          1. cadmium
          2. nickel
          3. zinc
          4. lead
        4. smoking or open flames
        5. lighters in your pocket
        6. flammable/combustible materials or solvents permitted in welding area
          1. acetone
          2. gasoline
          3. solvents
          4. rags/paper
      3. NEVER:
        1. breathe fumes from soldering, brazing, or welding
        2. heat glass or any material resting on concrete surfaces
        3. pick up hot materials with leather gloves– use pliers
        4. rest a lit torch on the welding table
        5. never let acetylene cylinder exceed 15psi
      4. keep gas cylinders upright at all times
      5. acetylene usually has fusible plugs protect against overheating or overpressurization
      6. oxygen cylinders usually have a safety valve with rupture disk
      7. whenever moving/servicing cylinders, detach it and put the cap on
      8. always have fire extinguisher, class A and B
    4. uses
      1. engine mounts
      2. landing gear
      3. tanks
        1. fuel
        2. oil
        3. hydraulic systems, sometimes
      4. aircraft jigs and fixtures
    5. advantages
      1. rigidity
      2. simplicity
      3. lightweight
      4. high strength
    6. disadvantages
      1. some materials cannot be welded
        1. many alloyed/high carbon content steels
        2. heat-treated aluminum alloys
        3. parts that are strengthened by cold-working
        4. parts with close tolerances
      2. unacceptable problems
        1. heat-treatment (aluminum and steel alloys)
        2. heat dissipation– warpage of parts
        3. metal finishing
        4. location of the repair– may not be safe
    7. basic welding joints
      1. butt
      2. lap
      3. tee
      4. corner
      5. edge
    8. non-ferrous welding
      1. aluminum
        1. work-hardened alloys are weldable
          1. 1100, 5052, 3003
        2. heat-treated alloys are not weldable
          1. 2024, 7075
        3. melting point: 1100F
        4. heat doesn't change Al color
        5. flux is required to chemically combine with float oxides
        6. neutral flame generally used
        7. use a torch tip one size larger than for steel
        8. keep gas pressure much lower than for steel
        9. remove flux residue after welding
          1. clean with sulfuric acid
          2. wash off the acid, check that it's gone with 5% silver nitrate solution
        10. Flux categories:
          1. soldering
          2. brazing
          3. welding
        11. edge prep
          1. thin sheets are bent 90deg at the butt
          2. thickish sheets are edge notched
          3. thick sheets edge notched and beveled
      2. stainless steel
        1. have sufficient chromium and nickel to maintain an austenitic condition
          1. low heat conductivity
          2. high coefficient of expansion
        2. non-structural (not cold worked)
          1. excellent corrosion and heat resistant properties
          2. usually used in manufacture of engine exhaust collectors
          3. develop UTS of 80,000psi and elongation of 40%
          4. parts fabrication and repair by most welding processes
        3. structural (cold-worked for extra strength)
          1. develop UTS up to 300,000psi
          2. used in firewalls
          3. usually spot welding only
        4. use a slight reducing flame to prevent oxygen contamination
        5. use torch tip one size smaller than for normal steel
        6. use flux and remove after welding

    2.1.4. Brazing

    1. advantages over welding
      1. dissimilar metals can be joined
      2. no amalgamation of base material– gluing without fusion
      3. lower heat– less issues with warping
      4. less fuel and equipment needed
      5. good for assembling cast iron, copper, nickel, galvanized iron
    2. disadvantages
      1. loss of joint strength over 500F
    3. use borax flux and clean metal to prep
    4. brazing rods
      1. generally non-ferrous
      2. bronze, technically alloy of copper and tin
      3. brass, alloy of copper and zinc
      4. usually 60% copper, 40% zinc, typically some tin
    5. technique
      1. thoroughly clean the surfaces
      2. neutral or slightly carburizing flame
      3. heat rod to dull red with steady sweeping motion, allow material to flow
      4. do not quench, allow to cool slowly
      5. white flux residue afterward means overheated
      6. glassy surface means too much flux
    6. Silver brazing
      1. Almost the same technique
      2. Chloride compounds commonly used for flux
      3. Observe behavior of flux
        1. Flux will dry out as moisture boils away
        2. Turns milky at~600°F
        3. Clear liquid at ~1100°G; time to add filler material

    2.1.5. Soldering

    1. Like brazing, but material melts at <840°F
    2. Not common on aircraft
    3. Soldering copper is prepped by 'tinning' to remove oxides and all for hear transfer
    4. Metals must be very clean
    5. Use the correct copper, solder (tin and lead), and flux
      1. Lead gives more strength, tin makes it more free flowing
    6. Can use electric soldering iron
    7. 150-200W most common
    8. Tinning
      1. Heat until copper is yellow/light brown– don't overheat
      2. File the tip clean
      3. Rub flux on the surface and coat it with a bit of solder
      4. This allows for easy heat transfer
    9. If tip is too hot, solder will sputter and run, causes rapid tip oxidation
    10. Thin, pointy tip will cool too quickly
    11. Don't solder on a steel surface; it's a heat sink

    2.1.6. Bending

    1. Bend allowance
      1. used to develop a flat layout of a part so that after bending, the part will fit without alteration
      2. basis for formula is circumference of a circle
      3. important considerations:
        1. material thickness
          1. sharper bends possible with thin material
        2. material composition
          1. sharper bends possible with softer materals
        3. temper/hardness
          1. sharper bends possible with annealed materials
      4. when possible, bend sheet metal perpendicular to the grain to keep uniform strength throughout the bend
      5. Parts of a bend
        1. bend tangent lines
        2. radius
          1. depends on material
        3. neutral line/axis
          1. line not subject to tension or compression; shear web
          2. about in the middle of the metal
        4. mold point
          1. imaginary point where corner would be if it were a perfect 90deg bend with no allowance
      6. Finding bend allowance and setback
        1. variables
          1. R=desired bend radius
          2. T=thickness of the material
          3. N=number of degrees in the bend
        2. geometric method
          1. BA=2piN(R+1/2T)/360
        3. empirical method
          1. same as geometric method, except they are more precise with the neutral axis
          2. BA=2piN(R+.445T)/360
          3. ~or BA= ((.01743*R) + (.0078*T))N~
        4. bend allowance chart method
          1. just look it up in the chart
            1. setback** is distance b/w mold point and bend tangent line
        5. SB = K(R+T)
          1. for 90deg bends, K=1
        6. K factor = tan(N/2)
    2. Bending tools
      1. Cornice brake
        1. bending machine good for long bends
        2. not adjustable
      2. Box and pan (finger) brake
        1. more adjustable
        2. you can space the fingers out to make multiple bends in different directions
        3. finger pieces can be changed out for different radius
      3. Hydro-press forming
        1. hydraulic press pushes sheet onto a die set (mold)
          1. dies usually constructed with relief angle to account for spring-back
        2. good for making complex, compound curves
      4. Fluid forming
        1. similar to hydro-press, but using liquid instead of rubber
      5. Drop hammer forming (progressive die set)
        1. requires softer material, may require re-annealing because it gets cold worked and becomes progressively harder through the process
      6. Stretch press forming
        1. sometimes used for compound curvature of a cowling
        2. sheet metal is stretched across a mold
      7. Extrusion stretch forming
      8. Slip roll forming
        1. roll sheet between rollers, like pasta, to curve it
      9. Hand forming
        1. guidelines
          1. best formability with 1100-F, 3003-H14, 5052-H32/34 and 2024-O (annealed condition)
          2. re-annealing during forming may be required due to work hardening
          3. heat-treated alloys such as 2024 are usually worked in the annealed condition and the heat-treated to T3, T4, etc after forming
          4. 2024-T3 has poor formability, limited to minor shrinking and stretching
          5. 7075-T6 cannot withstand cold joggling, shrinking and stretching
        2. tools
          1. bench stakes
            1. fixed into a bench plate
            2. bend sheet over stakes to form it
            3. usually only use wooden or soft-faced mallets
            4. planish metals lightly with planishing hammer
          2. rotary machine and dies
            1. two dies fit together and sheet is rolled between them
            2. can use wiring rolls to wire an edge
          3. v-block
          4. shrinking block
          5. shrinking mallet
          6. guide block
          7. "Metal is like a woman; too much heat will harden her, and she won't bend, the right amount of heat and she will soften and bend easily."
        3. straight line hand forming
          1. radius block must have desired bend radius
          2. ruffle and trap shrinking
    3. Bends
      1. open angle: obtuse
      2. closed angle: acute

    2.1.7. Sheet metal repairs

    1. Standard practice
      1. Current aircraft maintenance manual or SRM
        1. Approved data and acceptable data
      2. ATA ch 51 covers standard practice
      3. Aircraft IPB (IPC)
        1. Exploded views
        2. Use on codes
        3. Hardware call-out, position
      4. Thorough inspection
        1. Cause of damage?
        2. Possible prevention?
      5. Classify all damage
      6. Check for other damage
        1. Oil canned skins
        2. Working rivets
        3. Corrosion
      7. Repair or replace?
        1. Salvage parts/damaged part use
      8. IA inspection
        1. Block 8 FAA firm 337 hidden repair
      9. Control surface repairs– check balance/rebalance
        1. make sure there's no flutter
      10. Aircraft support
        1. have correct jigs, fixtures, structural support
      11. Symmetry/alignment check
      12. Avoid FOD; tool count
    2. 337 form
    3. Logbook entry per FAR 43.9
    4. Concepts
      1. Patches must accept and transfer all loads
      2. Stop drill a crack
      3. Rounded corners
      4. Make sure there are no abrupt changes in cross section; smooth transition
      5. Rivets should have comparable shear strength to the skins (D=3T)
    5. Welding structural tubing
      1. general info
        1. repair normally permitted, but it is a major repair (337 with IA
        2. often use SAE 4130 seamless tubing – 80,000-90,000psi tensile strength
          1. usually normalized (condition N) stressed relieved condition
            1. normalizing** stress relief for iron based alloys; heat steel above critical temp (1600-1700F) and cooling in still air
          2. it only loses <10% of strength after welding
        3. tubing sized by OD and WT
        4. may be spliced or repaired between any station
        5. repair dimensions in OD
        6. coatings removed before and replaced
      2. types of welds
        1. rosette weld
          1. holds parts in alignment during repair
          2. holes drilled in outside tube only
          3. no larger than 1/4 OD of outside tube
        2. lap
          1. common in construction and repair
          2. scarf cut = 30deg
          3. fish-mouth cut = 60deg included angle
        3. butt
          1. not common in repair work
          2. limited surface area
      3. prep and follow up
        1. remove enough fabric to eliminate fire hazard (have fire extinguisher)
        2. cut heavy WH tubing with a miter box and hacksaw; thin wall with standard tubing cutter
        3. provide for tube venting during construction and repair with drilled vent holes
        4. interior hot linseed oil coating prevents corrosion
        5. vent and oil holes are sometimes closed with drive screws or plug welds
      4. Cessna particular info
        1. preheat SAE 4130 to 300-400F before welding
        2. bead width ~2x thickness of the tube wall
        3. allow ~1/16" spacing in joints to allow for penetration
        4. butt joints require 100% penetration
        5. fillet welds need min 15% penetration into parent materials
        6. bead should be smooth and uniform
        7. oxides from welding should not extend >1" from the bead
        8. porous or holey welds are unusable and must be removed
        9. welds shouldn't have: cracking, pitting, distortions, welding scale
        10. no filing or filling welds with solder/brazing; weakens the weld
        11. never weld over an existing weld
      5. Truss structures
        1. Pratt truss
          1. like a box truss with diagonal members across the bays carry tension/compression loads
        2. Warren truss
          1. longerons are connected with diagonal members only
      6. bent tubing
        1. can be bent back using a clamp and wooden blocks
      7. out of round tubing
        1. use a clamp and a wooden block with correct dimensions and squeeze it back into shape
    6. Types of repairs
      1. welded patch repairs
        1. diamond shaped patch bent around a damaged tube
        2. for dents <1/10 of tube OD
        3. coverage of max 1/4 tube circumference
        4. damage no longer than tube OD
      2. welded sleeve repairs
        1. sleeve can be opened longitudinally for installation
        2. often cut with scarf or fish-mouth cuts
      3. welded patch plate
        1. used for a dented cluster weld
        2. fingers (patch) only needed on one side of the cluster
      4. tube repair of cluster by outer sleeve method
        1. very involved, just get a new airplane
      5. splices
        1. splice by inner sleeve
        2. splice by outer sleeve
        3. splice using larger diameter tube

    2.1.8. Special fasteners

    1. What are special fasteners?
    2. -Fasteners other than standard AN and MS hardware (bolts, nuts, washers, standards solid shank rivets ,etc)
    3. Some special fasteners are :
      1. “Blind” (access from one side only)
      2. “Proprietary” (Peculiar to aircraft manufacturer)
    4. -Expensive!
    5. -May require special tools
    6. About blind rivet grip range and hole preparation :
    7. -Follow mfg. recommendations
    8. -Proper installation dependent upon correctly drilled and prepared holes and correct rivet grip range.
    9. -Locking collars of mechanically locked rivets must correctly seat if grip range is incorrect
    10. -Over size / damaged holes prevent blind rivets from developing full strength due to limited sleeve expansion during installation
      1. be very careful when removing rivet/fastener
    11. DuPont Explosive Rivets
    12. -Brazier or Countersunk Manufactured head
    13. -1/8” , 5/32” , 3/16” diameters with specific heating elements tips
    14. -2017-T3 aluminum alloy; low shear strength -partially hollow shank
    15. -Charge detonated by heat source in 1.5 seconds - 6 seconds
    16. -Corrosive explosive debris ; potentially dangerous - fuel and oil tanks
    17. -Color identifies the grip of the rivet
    18. DZUS (snoopy tool)u99Fasteners:
    19. -Major components parts
      1. Stud
      2. Grommet
      3. Spring (receptacle)

    2.2. Cabin Atmosphere

    2.2.1. Oxygen Systems

    1. Regs
    2. should probably read
      1. 91.211 Supplemental oxygen
        1. unpressurized aircraft
        2. pressurized aircraft
      2. 25.1443 transport aircraft
      3. mass flow
      4. supplemental
    3. Aviator's Breathing Oxygen (ABO)
      1. MIL-O-27210
        1. only contains 0.02 mg water vapor/liter
          1. sea level at 70F (21.1C)
          2. no freezing in system
        2. at least 99.5% pure
          1. no impurities to block the system
      2. odorless, colorless
      3. ~10% heavier than air
      4. non-explosive, non-flammable
      5. highly supports combustion
        1. dangerous around hydrocarbon materials and a source of ignition
        2. NEVER allow grease, oil, etc to come in contact with oxygen
        3. when servicing oxygen systems, always electrically
      6. hospital medical grade oxygen is NOT acceptable
        1. Type 1, Grade B, contains too much water (could freeze at altitude)
    4. 3 components on most oxygen systems, portable or installed:
      1. storage systems
      2. tanks, containers
        1. gaseous
          1. low pressure cylinders
            1. distinguished by light yellow bottle
            2. mostly used in older military aircraft
            3. pressure
              1. full
              2. 400-450psi
              3. 400-425 nominal
              4. operational empty
              5. 100psi
                1. refill
              6. maintenance empty
              7. 50psi
                1. remove/replace
            4. shatterproof or non-shatterable
              1. resists shattering by gunfire
              2. heat-treated steel alloy or metal bands welded to the outside surface of the cylinder
          2. high pressure cylinders
            1. Models:
              1. DOT 3AA-1800 cylinders
                1. standard weight steel cylinder
                2. green
                3. pressure
                  1. full
                  2. 1800psi at 70F
                  3. 1800-2000 nominal
                  4. operational empty
                  5. 200psi
                    1. refillable
                  6. maintenance empty
                  7. 100psi
                    1. remove/replace
                4. portable (carry-on) or fixed installation cylinder
                5. hydrostatic test required
                  1. every 5 years
                  2. date stamped near neck of bottle
                  3. hydrostatic test pressure= 5/3x1800psi
                6. no cylinder service life limit
                  1. conditional inspection for airworthiness
              2. DOT 3HT-1850 cylinders
                1. light weight steel cylinder
                2. full pressure
                3. 1850psi at 70F
                4. green
                5. fixed (permanent) installation cylinder only
                6. hydrostatic test required
                  1. every 3 years
                  2. test pressure = 5/3x1850psi
                7. service life limited
                8. 24 years or 4380 filling cycles
              3. DOT E-8162 cylinders
                1. composite cylinder
                  1. aluminum shell wrapped with Kevlar
                2. color coded green
                3. full pressure
                4. 1850psi at 70F
                5. fixed installation only
                6. hydrostatic test required
                  1. every 3 years if made before 2006
                  2. after 2006, every 5 years
                  3. test pressure =5/3x1850psi
                7. service life limit
                8. 15 years or 10,000 filling cycles
              4. DOT 3AL-2216 cylinders
                1. 6061-T6 seamless aluminum cylinder
                2. green
                3. service pressure
                4. 2216psi at 70F
                5. portable or fixed installation cylinder
                6. hydrostatic test required
                  1. every 5 years
                  2. test pressure = 5/3 x 2216psi
                7. no cylinder service life limit
                  1. conditional inspection for airworthiness
            2. Cylinder markings
            3. stamped in the cylinder, on or near the neck
              1. DOT specification number
              2. hydrostatic test date, mo/yr
              3. cylinder serial number
            4. Hydrostatic Testing
              1. cylinder is submerged in a pressure chamber
              2. it is pressurized to 5/3 max operating pressure and expands, water in the chamber is displaced
              3. amount of displaced water indicates strength/integrity of oxygen cylinder
              4. measured with a burette
              5. when pressure is released, the cylinder must return to its original shape
        2. liquid
        3. liquid oxygen (LOX) in converter
          1. typically a military oxygen system
          2. LOX boiling point = -182F
          3. conversion ratio= 862:1
        4. mechanical sieve
        5. oxygen concentrator
          1. how it works
            1. takes in surrounding air
            2. compresses it
              1. cooling systems prevent overheating
            3. removes nitrogen via an air filter and sieve beds
              1. zeolite beads
            4. delivery settings are via an electronic interface
            5. delivers purified oxygen via a nasal cannula or mask
        6. solid state
        7. 'candles'
          1. emergency chemical/solid state oxygen generator
          2. how it works
            1. mix of sodium chlorate and iron is ignited and burns
              1. by products:
                1. oxygen
                2. rust, salt, heat
              2. They get very hot!
            2. oxygen flow within 10-12 secs after activation
            3. each generator supplies a minimum average 4 liters/min for about 20 mins
          3. supplemental oxygen for crew and passengers
          4. 10 year shelf life from date of manufacture
      3. delivery system
      4. regulators, lines, hoses
        1. continuous flow
          1. simple system
            1. not efficient at conserving oxygen
            2. oxygen flows whether user is inhaling or not
              1. wasteful
            3. most efficient 28k ft and below
            4. flow indicator
              1. red spring loaded indicator disappears and shows green when oxygen is flowing to the mask
          2. fixed flow continuous, or
          3. altitude adjustable
            1. manual
              1. pilot adjusts flow output of regulator to match indicated altitude
                1. military A8A regulator
            2. automatic
              1. altitude compensated
                1. once ON, flow scheduling begins at 8-10k ft
        2. regulated demand flow
          1. gives the user oxygen on-demand (during inhalation) and stops the flow when the demand ceases (during exhalation) to conserve oxygen
          2. supplements oxygen with cabin air at lower altitudes below ~34k ft
          3. flow indicator
            1. blinker opens/closes to indicate flow
          4. types:
            1. diluter demand
            2. only in aircraft flying <40k ft
              1. oxygen is provided, breath normally
              2. can be panel-mounted or built into a mask
            3. pressure demand
            4. >40k ft
              1. air is forced into the lungs at positive pressure
                1. delivers oxygen at .5psi
              2. regulator like diluter-demand regulator
        3. components
          1. [regulator]()
            1. diaphragm opens demand valve on inhale
            2. aneroid bellows meters cabin air
              1. mixes cabin air with oxygen as needed
            3. 100% oxygen setting cuts off cabin air, used for:
              1. treatment of wounds or shock
              2. protection against exhaust gasses or other poisonous gasses in the aircraft
              3. avoid bends, chokes
              4. to correct for feeling of hypoxia
              5. night time or special use
              6. oxygen system check and test
            4. emergency setting bypasses the demand valve, works like continuous flow system
              1. steady stream of 100% oxygen
              2. malfunction of regulator
              3. hypoxia or unconsciousness at any altitude
              4. sudden serious leakage in mask or mask to regulator tubing (maybe?)
          2. oxygen overboard discharge
            1. blowout disk seals the over-pressure discharge vent line of the system
            2. if cylinders exceed 2600psi or 225F, an emergency relief valve opens
            3. as long as the disk is there, the system is not over-pressurized
          3. oxygen valves
            1. pressure relief valve
              1. prevents high pressure oxygen from entering the system in case of pressure reducer failure
              2. vented to the overboard discharge (green disk)
              3. in low pressure system, usually caused by overcharging
              4. in high pressure system, usually caused by overheating
            2. manual cylinder valve
              1. safety wired to the 'ON' position
              2. open valve slowly
            3. line valves
              1. control flow in distribution line
            4. check valves
              1. prevent reverse flows
            5. shutoff valve
          4. lines and fittings
            1. high pressure
              1. usually fabricated from 3/16"OD, .035" wall thickness stainless steel or seamless copper alloy tubing (moving to mostly CRES)
                1. copper gives off copper ions when exposed to oxygen
                2. copper work hardens under vibration
                3. copper tubing is usually coiled at connection to each cylinder; acts as vibration dampener designed to preserve the annealed condition of the tubing
            2. low pressure
              1. fabricated from 5/16"OD, .035" wall thickness seamless aluminum alloy or equivalent tubing
              2. commonly double-flared
      5. oxygen mask or nasal cannula
        1. mask care
          1. cleaning
            1. wash mask with mild soap solution and rinse with clean water
          2. sterilization
            1. swab mask thoroughly with a gauze or sponge soaked in a water/merthiolate solution or alcohol
              1. 1/5 teaspoon merthiolate/1 quart of water
            2. wipe mask with clean cloth and air dry
          3. inspection of masks/hoses, check:
            1. fabric for cracks and rough seals
            2. general sealing of the mask
              1. thumb over line connection, try to breath
            3. transparent plastic for cleanliness and condition
            4. hose for deterioration
            5. stowage compartment
            6. mask quick release system, if installed
            7. microphone
    5. leak testing
      1. listen for leaking gas
      2. bubble test
        1. there are special leak detector fluids for oxygen systems
        2. MIL-L-25567A
        3. soapy water (mild soap in low concentrations)
      3. maximum permissible leakage is 1% in 24hrs
      4. do not attempt to tighten any connections while the system is charged
    6. servicing (refilling) gaseous oxygen
      1. steps
        1. purge moisture, dirt and air from manifold and service hose before connecting to filler valve
          1. cap lines
        2. select service cylinder with lowest pressure, but above the aircraft cylinder pressure
        3. open cylinder shut-off and service valves slowly
      2. oxygen booster
        1. compresses source oxygen
        2. increases serving efficiency
      3. servicing valve
        1. check charging pressure requirements
      4. temperature requirements
        1. when it is hot, oxygen cylinders are filled to a higher pressure than the standard maximum pressure
        2. when it is cold, cylinders are filled to a lower pressure than the standard maximum pressure
        3. filling cylinders to temperature compensated pressure values ensures a full supply of oxygen is available when needed
        4. strict adherence to the temperature/pressure compensation chart values is mandatory to ensure safe storage of aircraft oxygen
        5. standard temp is 70F
        6. filling the cylinders too quickly can cause them to heat up
    7. inspection of oxygen systems
      1. cylinder inspection
        1. legible marking for:
          1. hydrostatic test date
          2. DOT number
          3. serial number
        2. exterior surfaces
          1. dents, bulges, chafing
        3. neck for cracks, distortion and damaged threads
        4. mounting bracket, straps, hardware for condition and security
        5. any chafing or interference with other equipment
      2. all parts for cracks, nicks, damaged threads
      3. actuate regulator controls and check operation
      4. gauges for pressure indication and condition
      5. lines for chafing corrosion, flats spots, kinks, and security
      6. fittings for corrosion around threads
      7. pressurize system and check for leaks

    2.2.2. Air Cycle Systems

    1. Theory
      1. 1st Law of Thermodynamics: heat is energy
        1. thermodynamic processes are therefore subject to the principle of conservation of energy
        2. Potential Energy
        3. Stored energy
        4. Kinetic Energy
        5. Energy of motion
      2. Compressed air has a large quantity of stored energy in the form of heat and pressure
      3. air is processed to desired pressure and temperature
        1. we release/decompress air to extract the heat energy
        2. transform the heat energy into mechanical energy to do work
    2. Basic requirements
      1. compressed air source
      2. heat exchangers
      3. expansion turbine
    3. open system which consists of:
      1. source of high pressure air to provide a pressurized atmosphere in the cabin
        1. turbine compressor
        2. roots blower
      2. ducting and valves
        1. direct and control air flow
      3. flow limiters and check valves
        1. regulate flow and prevent air loss due to a system failure
      4. heat exchangers
        1. cool the compressed air to workable temperature
      5. air cycle machine
        1. compressor
        2. expansion turbine
      6. water separator/condenser
        1. dry the air and prevent icing
      7. mixer/mix valves
        1. combine hot and cold air to maintain desired cabin temperature
    4. How it works
      1. engine bleed air enters the system through a flow control valve/pack valve
        1. regulates the flow for air conditioning and cabin pressurization demands
        2. regulated through Wheatstone bridge circuits
        3. temperature sensitive variable resistor
      2. engine bleed air passes through the primary heat exchanger
        1. flate/fin type– aluminum alloy
        2. located in a ram air duct
      3. ram air passes across the primary heat exchanger
        1. ram air acts as a coolant
          1. partially cools bleed air (to ~300F)
          2. heat is transferred by convection from bleed air to ambient air
        2. increases efficiency of the ACM
          1. lower temp, denser air goes into compressor, so less work is required to compress the air mass
            1. energy required to compress a by a given ratio rises as the temperature of the incoming gas rises
      4. air is ducted to the compressor section of the ACM
      5. compressor compresses the bleed air again
        1. increases pressure and temperature
      6. compressed bleed air passes through the secondary heat exchanger
        1. plate/fin type– aluminum alloy
        2. located in a ram air duct
        3. temp is decreased
      7. air goes to the expansion turbine
        1. rapid expansion and energy extraction across the turbine causes rapid cooling
        2. heat extraction energy is used to drive the ACM compressor
          1. shaft speed is ~40-50k rpm
      8. condensed water is collected by a water separator/extractor
        1. moisture in warm air condenses out below dew point
        2. Cyclonic Water Separator
          1. water is extracted to prevent ice from forming in the system
          2. swirling moisture laden air flows through water separator
          3. water particles condense on a sock and are collected and drained clear of the aircraft
          4. provides dry air for cabin pressurization
          5. bypass valve
            1. opens if the condenser freezes
            2. sends unconditioned cold air to cabin (snowing)
        3. sometimes water is fed to spray nozzles in the ram air duct on the ground
          1. improves cooling efficiency of the heat exchangers
        4. water separator temp control
          1. 35F control valve
          2. prevents ice formations
          3. automatic thermostatic control
            1. vernatherm/thermostat
            2. ports bleed air to the separator
      9. mixer/mix valves control temp of air going to cabin
        1. warm bleed air may then be added to the conditioned air to bring it to the desired cabin air temperature
        2. modulated by complex wheatstone bridge circuit
          1. circuit with 4 resistors (2 fixed, 2 variable) one controlled by pilot, one varies with temperature
            1. thermistors, anticipators
          2. relative resistance controls amount and direction of current through the middle leg (the bridge) of the circuit

    2.2.3. Aircraft Pressurization

    1. creates the proper environment for prevention of hypoxia
    2. permits operation at high altitudes
    3. pressurization subjects the airframe to tension stress
      1. not** designed for negative pressure differentials
    4. air enters the cabin at a controlled rate and exits the cabin at a variable rate
      1. comes from air cycle system
      2. flow control valve sets the rate
      3. leaves through the outflow valve
      4. max cabin altitude is 8000ft
      5. max pressure differential 8.5psi
    5. pressurization sources
      1. positive displacement compressor
        1. compresses fixed packets of fluid
        2. the velocity of fluid need not be high
        3. gives constant flow rate against variable output pressure
        4. the fluid is directly imparted pressure energy
      2. centrifugal compressor
        1. compresses the fluid in a continuous manner
        2. the velocity of fluid must be high
        3. gives variable flow rate against variable output pressure
        4. the fluid is provided kinetic energy which is converted into pressure energy
      3. engine supercharger
        1. reciprocating engine internal supercharger
      4. engine turbocharger
        1. reciprocating engine, exhaust gas griven
      5. bleed air– most common
        1. air delivered from the compressor section of a gas turbine engine
      6. independent cabin compressors
        1. driven by accessory drive gearing or bleed air (rarely)
        2. positive displacement compressors
          1. reciprocating compressors
          2. vane type compressors
          3. [roots blowers]()
            1. type of positive displacement compressor most suitable for use in most large piston engine aircraft
            2. oil-free delivery of air maintained by bearing contained in separate chambers
            3. rotor shafts can be fitted with oil resistant rubber seals
            4. labyrinth seals permit a small amount of air leakage to ambient
              1. oil is blown back
        3. centrifugal cabin compressors
          1. reciprocating engine driven
          2. turbine engine compressor
    6. cabin pressure regulator
      1. can be part of the outflow valve, but usually remote from the outflow valve and connected to it by external plumbing
      2. altitude sensitive device; aneroid bellows
      3. controls cabin pressure
        1. regulates outflow valve reference pressure
        2. slightly below set cabin pressure
        3. set cabin altitude
        4. isobaric setting
          1. isobaric range
            1. select cabin altitude
            2. cabin altimeter = selected altitude
          2. differential range
            1. preset differential pressure
            2. cabin altimeter > selected altitude
        5. set barometric pressure (outside)
        6. set cabin rates
          1. climb
            1. 500ft/min up
          2. descent
            1. 300ft/min down
          3. increase cabin rates
            1. regulator causes outflow valve to open faster
          4. decrease cabin rates
            1. regulator causes outflow valve to open slower
        7. Manual Controller
          1. used in the event of automatic controller failure
          2. if no adjustment is made after it fails, the cabin altitude will climb/descend with the aircraft altitude
          3. manual controller is a toggle which can adjust cabin altitude
        8. Electric Pressurization
          1. same thing, but does it digitally
          2. cabin pressure, static pressure, and inputs from instruments go into magic box, and it adjusts the outflow valves accordingly
      4. instrumentation
        1. cabin VSI
          1. often instantaneous
        2. cabin altimeter
          1. cabin altitude
          2. cabin differential pressure
      5. Outflow valve
        1. located through the pressure vessel beneath the airplane
        2. usually held fully open on the ground by a landing gear operated switch
          1. prevents pressurization on the ground
        3. the cabin rate of climb or descent is determined by the rate of closing or opening of outflow valve
        4. operation
          1. operates pneumatically in response to the settings on the cockpit pressurization panel that influence the balance between cabin and ambient air pressure
          2. relieves all pressure in excess of the amount for which it is set
          3. 3 modes
            1. unpressurized
              1. cabin altitude changes with aircraft altitude
              2. typically on commercial airplanes, though, the cabin will climb slower than the aircraft
              3. reference pressure is lower than cabin pressure
              4. outflow valve is held open
              5. cabin altitude = aircraft altitude
            2. isobaric
              1. cabin altitude stays constant
              2. this is where it is most of the time
              3. reference chamber reduces to a pressure where the aneroid begins to expand
                1. closes the isobaric metering valve
              4. maintains a constant reference pressure, so cabin altitude stays constant, too
            3. differential
              1. maintains a preset cabin differential pressure
              2. safety feature to make sure the differential pressure doesn't exceed maximum allowed if the plane goes too high
              3. cabin at higher altitude than set on cabin pressure controller
              4. pressure differential between the constant reference pressure and the decreasing atmospheric pressure becomes sufficient to move the diaphragm and open the differential metering valve
              5. reference chamber air flows to atmosphere through the differential metering valve, reducing the reference pressure
              6. the isobaric bellows expands
                1. isobaric metering valve closes completely
              7. reference chamber pressure is now controlled, through the differential metering valve
              8. outflow valve opens or closes as required to meter air from the cabin and maintain a predetermined differential pressure value
      6. Safety valve
        1. usually operates automatically
        2. provides:
          1. pressure relief
            1. prevents over pressurization
          2. vacuum relief
            1. prevents a negative pressure differential
    7. Solenoid valve (dump valve)
      1. dumps cabin air pressure
      2. electrically activated valve, operated by solenoid
      3. spring loaded, normally closed
      4. in case of rapid descent
      5. when the cockpit pressure switch is activated, solenoid opens
        1. decreases reference pressure
        2. outflow valve opens
        3. dumps cabin air to the atmosphere
    8. Negative pressure relief valves
      1. spring loaded to the closed position
      2. opens to prevent cabin altitude from becoming higher than airplane altitude
      3. usually opens at -0.5 to -1.0 psid
    9. Delivery air duct check valve
      1. prevents a sudden loss of pressurization in the event that there is a loss of the pressurization source
    10. Pressurization seals
      1. door seals
        1. some inflate
        2. fixed seal
          1. plug-type doors; under pressurization, door is pushed into the plug and compresses the seal
    11. Air distribution
      1. system of air ducts leading from the pressurization source into and throughout the cabin
      2. cabin air supply duct materials depend on air temp
        1. main ducts for air >200C, eg bleed air ducts
          1. stainless steel
        2. ducts for air <100C
          1. soft aluminum
        3. ducts for conditioned air
          1. plastic (rigid or flexible)
      3. typically air is ducted to and released from ceiling vents
      4. air circulates and flows out floor-level vents
      5. air flows aft through the baggage compartments and under the floor area
      6. exits pressure vessel through outflow valve
      7. the flow of air is nearly imperceptible
      8. ducting is hidden below the cabin floor and behind walls and ceiling panels
      9. valves select:
        1. pressurization air source
        2. ventilating air
        3. temperature trim air
      10. in line fans and jet pumps are used to increase flow in certain areas of the cabin
      11. temperature sensors, overheat switches, and check valves are also common

    2.2.4. Vapor Cycle Systems

    1. Temperature
      1. temperature** is a measure of the average kinetic energy of molecules in a substance
      2. heat** is a measure of total kinetic energy of the molecules in a substance
    2. British Thermal Unit (BTU)– the amount of heat energy required to raise the temperature of one pound of fresh water by 1degF (at max density, 34F)
    3. equalizes from hot to cold
      1. sensible heat** is heat energy absorbed or released that results in a change in temperature (feel)
      2. latent heat of vaporization** is heat energy absorbed or released that results in a change in state (phase change)
    4. R-12 = 71 BTU/lb
    5. toxic
    6. R-134a = 93.3 BTU/lb
    7. water = 970.4 BTU/lb
  • conduction: heat transfer by direct contact
  • convection: heat transfer by fluid
  • radiation: hear transfer by wave motion
  • Refrigerant characteristics
    1. colorless
    2. odorless
    3. non-toxic (reasonably)
    4. non-flammable
    5. stable at high and low temperatures
    6. non-reactive with system components
    7. heavier than air
    8. becomes acidic in contact with moisture
      1. hydrochloric acid
    9. expose R12 to an open flame, you get phosgene gas (poisonous)
    10. eye protection required while servicing along with adequate ventilation
      1. if refrigerant comes into contact with the eye, flood eye with cool water, treat with mineral oil and seek medical attention
  • R-12/CFC-12 refrigerant
    1. chlorofluorocarbon
      1. dichlodifuromethane**
    2. composition
      1. C Cl2 F2
      2. chlorine
      3. flourine
      4. carbon
    3. boiling point -21.7F (29.85C)
    4. generally available in white container
    5. mostly banned by Montreal Protocol
      1. 1 Jan 1996
      2. prohibits the manufacture of CFCs
  • R-134a/HFC-134a refrigerant
    1. ~15% less efficient than R-12
    2. 95% less damaging to the ozone layer than R-12
    3. hydrofluorocarbon
    4. composition
      1. C H2 F C F3
      2. hydrogen
      3. fluorine
      4. charbon
    5. 1,1,1,2-tetraflouroethane
    6. boiling point -15.07F (-26.15C) at sea level
    7. vapor pressure 71psi at standard temp
  • components and functions
    1. evaporator
    2. basically heat exchanger
      1. freon enters the evaporator in a low pressure liquid state
      2. cabin air passes across the evaporator
      3. freon exits in a low pressure vapor state
      4. cabin heat is absorbed by evaporation of the refrigerant
    3. compressor
      1. freon enters in a low pressure vapor state
      2. exits in a high pressure vapor state
      3. increases both temp and pressure
      4. mostly engine-driven, occasionally electric-motor driven
      5. electromagnetic clutch engages the compressor
      6. has its own lubrication
        1. follow manual for checking/servicing compressor lube
        2. has to be compatible with freon and internal seals
    4. condenser
      1. freon enters in a high pressure vapor state
      2. exits in high pressure liquid state
      3. heat transfers from freon to the atmosphere
    5. receiver/drier
      1. freon enters in high pressure liquid state
      2. exits in a high pressure liquid state
      3. removes moisture and contaminants from the freon
        1. uses a desiccant filter
      4. acts as a reservoir for freon
      5. often there is a sight glass; bubbles indicate low freon
    6. expansion valve
      1. freon enters the expansion valve in a high pressure liquid state
      2. exits in a low pressure liquid state
      3. separates high pressure side from low pressure side
      4. meters freon to the evaporator by a capillary tube
        1. contains methyl-chloride gas
  • servicing
    1. field servicing is done when filling an evacuated system with refrigerant , or to add refrigerant to a system
    2. servicing manifold
      1. blue valve and hose
        1. low side servicing
        2. low side gauge
      2. red valve and hose
        1. high side servicing
        2. high side gauge
      3. yellow
        1. to freon/vacuum source
    3. vapor cycle performance check
      1. follow aircraft maintenance manual procedures
      2. set-up:
        1. connect manifold set to high and low sides with valves closed
        2. run aircraft engine
          1. 1250rpm (R-12)
          2. 1500rpm (R134a)
        3. set air conditioning thermostat control for maximum cooling
        4. insert thermometer into evaporator as near the coils as possible
        5. set fan to low or medium
        6. operate for 3-5 mins
      3. normal indications
        1. low side
          1. 20-30psi (R-12)
          2. 20-27psi (R-134a)
        2. high side
          1. 225-300psi (R-12)
          2. 220-230psi (R-134a)
        3. evaporator temp
          1. 40-50F
      4. service hose connectors
        1. cannot be connected to the wrong side
        2. shut-off valves must be located within 12" of the 'vehicle end' of the service hose
          1. in some cases, they are incorporated into the valve itself
        3. isolation service valves
          1. they isolate the compressor in case it needs to be removed
          2. that way, freon can be preserved
    4. purging system/adding Refrigerant
      1. purging and refilling
        1. with system still under vacuum (1 atmosphere, so water will vaporize) and the manifold set attached with both the high side and low side valves closed, connect the refrigerant source to center hose
        2. purge air from charging station lines
        3. open high side manifold set valve and observe low side gauge
          1. very slowly!
          2. low side should come out of vacuum
        4. close high side manifold valve and ensure low side valve is open
        5. operate aircraft engine
          1. get compressor to 1250rpm
        6. set controls to full cooling
        7. slowly open low side manifold valve and add required number of pounds of refrigerant
        8. verify absence of bubbles in sight glass
        9. close low side manifold valve and make sure high side is still closed
        10. close and remove refrigeration source and shut down aircraft engine
        11. cap all lines
      2. servicing a system that has lost all refrigerant
        1. check oil
          1. service as required
            1. special high grade refrigeration oil
        2. evacuate the system
          1. procedure
            1. make sure compressor has enough oil
            2. connect manifold set to service ports (correctly)
            3. remove cap on vacuum discharge port
            4. start the vacuum pump
            5. open low and high side manifold valves slowly
            6. after 5mins, check low-side gauge shows vacuum, high-side 0
            7. after 15mins, should be ~ 25"Hg
            8. continue 30-45mins
            9. close both manifold valves and hold vacuum in system
            10. remove vacuum pump and replace caps on system and manifold set
          2. recovery and recycling
            1. cannot legally discharge refrigerant into the atmosphere
            2. must use a recovery process
          3. vacuum pump (remove 1atmosphere, so all water will boil)
            1. 30-45 mins
          4. removes moisture
          5. leak check
            1. system holds vacuum
        3. add refrigerant (see above)
    5. leak detection
      1. system is not cooling cabin
      2. failure to hold vacuum
      3. leak detection methods
        1. look for oil seepage
        2. dye detected under UV light
        3. soapy water bubbles
          1. min 50psi required
      4. electronic leak detector
        1. SAE J2791 detectors
          1. detectable leakage rates of 0.15oz/yr at a distance of 3/8"
        2. SAE J1627 detectors
          1. detectable leakage rates of 0.5oz/yr at a distance of 1/4"
    6. troubleshooting
      1. damaged evaporator/condenser fins
        1. bent fins, blocked ducting or malfunctioning blower motor will prevent the refrigerant absorbing heat
        2. bent and distorted fins can sometimes be straightened with a fin comb
      2. ice in evaporator
        1. caused by inadequate cabin airflow across evaporator
      3. won't take freon
        1. likely an expansion valve failure (typically fails closed)
      4. inlet and outlet of the expansion valve are the same temp
        1. expansion valve is not metering correctly

    • 2.2.5. Combustion and Exhaust Heaters

    1. Combustion Heaters
      1. general info
        1. constructed of corrosion resistant stainless steel material
          1. resists cracking/deterioration from thermal stress
      2. theory and operation
        1. four basic requirements
          1. ventilating air
            1. air that is heated and ducted into the cabin
            2. from ram air duct
            3. ventilating air fan
              1. cabin heat
              2. anti/deicing
              3. engine pre-heat
            4. ventilating air temp regulated by metering the fuel
              1. electrically cycle fuel valve
              2. continuous ignition
          2. combustion air
            1. air used for combustion
            2. supplied through sure duct
            3. blower for ground ops
            4. Comes into burner section
          3. spark
          4. fuel
        2. Safety
          1. Janitrol working flame heater combustion gas flow
            1. combustion air pressure designed to be lower than ventilating sure pressures
            2. ventilating air will flow into combustion chamber of it's compromised
            3. no carbon monoxide escape
          2. Redundancy
            1. 4 switches monitor it's function
              1. combustion air differential pressure switch
                1. senses differential pressure between combustion inlet air and exhaust gas
                2. senses blockage in combustion air duct, shuts down heater
              2. adjustable duct switch
                1. pilot controlled
                2. thermostat at outlet of ventilating duct controls fuel valve
              3. cycling switch
                1. back up to adjustable duct switch
                2. regulates output temp 200-210°F
                3. no pilot control
              4. overheat safety switch
                1. like a circuit breaker that trips at 300+°F
                2. shuts down entire unit except combustion air blower and ventilation fan
                3. heater fail light will illuminate
                4. ground reset
      3. typical components
        1. fuel solenoid
          1. controlled by
            1. adjustable duct switch
            2. cycling switch
            3. overheat safety switch
          2. allows fuel to fuel nozzle
        2. fuel nozzle
        3. ignition plugs
          1. continuous spark igniter system
        4. combustion air fire valve
          1. damper type butterfly valve
          2. located in the combustion air inlet of some heaters
          3. 2 spring-loaded doors soldered together
          4. permits max airflow through combustion air duct
          5. segments will release to seal the duct when solder melts at 400°F
      4. wiring
        1. Is very confusing
      5. inspection and maintenance
        1. fuel system integrity
          1. filter servicing/replacement
        2. electrical system integrity
        3. combustion chamber integrity
          1. pressure decay test
            1. ensures integrity of combustion section of the heater
              1. AD 2004-21-05
                1. supercedes AD 96-20-07
                2. every 500hrs of operation or 2yrs (whichever comes first)
              2. meant to prevent carbon monoxide and fuel leakage
              3. carbon monoxide is not usually an issue because ventilating air pressure is higher than combustion air pressure
            2. pressurize combustion chamber to 6psig
            3. shut off air and start time 45sec
            4. if pressure decays to <1psig, it is leaking
            5. inspect seals and combustion chamber
    2. Exhaust Heaters
      1. muff style heat exchangers that use exhaust gas can easily cause carbon monoxide poisoning
        1. check the heat shroud for soot
      2. causes of exhaust failure
        1. marking of exhaust system parts
          1. never use lead/carbon-based pencils
          2. carbon will cause heat concentration, carbonization and cracking
          3. use chalk, grease pencil, ink
        2. muffler outer wall failures
          1. especially on turbocharged airplanes
        3. spot weld fails
        4. manifold/stack fails
        5. improperly positioned exhaust clamp
        6. cracking from thermal expansion/contraction and vibration
        7. erosion from corrosive gasses
        8. heat concentration from carbon build up or uneven gas flow
        9. engine after-firing; combustion of unburned fuel in the exhaust system
        10. any exhaust failure is a hazard; may cause
          1. carbon monoxide in the cabin
          2. partial/complete loss of engine power
          3. fire
        11. max rate of exhaust system failures occurs between 100-200hrs of operating time; 50% occur within 400hrs
      3. inspect carefully
        1. check for chafing, cracking etc
        2. remove all shrouds to inspect
      4. replace rather than repair
      5. welding can be troublesome
      6. the assemblies are modular, just replace that bit

    2.2.6. Ice and Rain Control Systems

    1. Ice detection
      1. visual detection
        1. look out the window
        2. ice will begin forming around the windshield/airframe
        3. anywhere the air is being accelerated and cooled below dewpoint temp and/or freezing
        4. types of ice
          1. clear ice
            1. clear and smooth
            2. super cooled water droplets do not freeze immediately
            3. forms protrusions/horns
          2. rime ice
            1. rough and opaque
            2. super cooled water droplets freeze immediately
            3. typically conforms to the shape of the airfoil
          3. mixed ice
            1. combo of both clear and rime ice
          4. frost ice
            1. white, semi-crystalline frost which covers the surface of the airplane
            2. frozen dew
            3. forms in clear air, water droplets freeze on surface
      2. electronic detection
        1. probe extends into the slipstream
          1. vibrates at given frequency (40Hz)
        2. ice builds up on the probe –> frequency decreases
        3. triggers microprocessor, illuminates red warning light
        4. crew should turn on anti-ice system
          1. sometimes it happens automatically
        5. red light goes out
        6. white ice light turns on for visual ice detection
        7. as probe detects ice
          1. microprocessor energizes a heating element in the probe
          2. ice melts, frequency returns to normal
    2. Propeller anti-icing/deicing systems
      1. resistive heating components
        1. rubberized boot is bonded to the inboard leading edge of propeller blades
        2. boot contains electrical resistive heating elements designed to prevent and remove ice formation
        3. system components
          1. electrically heated, resistive element boots
            1. bonded to leading edge of blade
          2. switch
            1. cockpit control for starting and stopping cycle of operation
            2. connects aircraft electrical power to prop deicing unit
          3. timer
            1. energizes boots for a specified time
            2. conserves electrical power
            3. prevent overheating of boots
            4. start sequence
              1. left outboard, left inboard, right outboard, right inboard
          4. ammeter
            1. prop deice system indicator in the panel
          5. wiring
          6. slip ring and brushes
            1. conduct electricity to rotating propeller
            2. slip ring is usually mounted on the back of the propeller hub
            3. brushes mounted to front of engine
      2. prop alcohol anti-icing
        1. not very common
        2. works by lowering the freezing point of the water
          1. anti-freeze (ethylene glycol)
        3. isopropyl alcohol
          1. AN-F-13
          2. MIL-F-5566
        4. fluid is transferred from a stationary nozzle on the engine nose case into a circular u-shaped channel
          1. slinger ring** mounted on rear of the propeller
        5. fluid is transferred through nozzles under centrifugal force to each blade shank
        6. narrow strips of rubber (feed shoes) channel fluid from the blade shank to the blade tip
    3. Electric systems
      1. Pitot heat
      2. Windshield deicing
        1. Electric panel
          1. multi-ply lamination glass or polycarbonate
            1. polycarbonate in the middle
            2. heating element nearest the outside
              1. transparent conductive coating
                1. indium tin oxide
                2. stannic oxide
                3. thermistor bonded into conductive coating
              2. resistive wires (not common)
            3. sandwiched in glass plys
            4. must not cause distortion
            5. heat also improves strength and impact resistance
          2. Non Electro Static Formulation A (NESA) windshields
            1. same thing, but expensive
            2. high performance
            3. prevent ice formation
            4. prevent shattering
            5. laminated tempered glass
              1. layer of stannic oxide
              2. thermistor and Wheatstone Bridge temp regulation
              3. vinyl
          3. troubleshooting
            1. check circuit resistance
            2. arcing
              1. caused by breakdown of conductive coating
            3. bubbles and delamination
        2. sometimes bleed air/exhaust heat is used
      3. Resistive element heaters
        1. heated fluid lines
        2. heated seats
        3. heated panels
    4. Airframe systems
      1. Pneumatic deice boots
        1. components and operation
          1. inflatable boots
            1. wing
            2. horizontal stabilizer
            3. vertical stabilizer
          2. inflation air source
            1. vane pump (wet or dry)
          3. deflation air source
            1. vane pump
            2. ejector pump (turbine aircraft)
          4. valves
            1. distributor
              1. sequences timing of boot inflation/deflation
            2. pressure relief
              1. prevents over-pressurization of the boot
            3. suction relief
              1. regulalates vacuum air
              2. holds boots down
          5. gauges
            1. pressure
              1. should fluctuate
            2. vacuum
              1. should be steady
        2. maintenance
          1. do not drag fuel hoses over boots
          2. keep them clean, no fuel, oil, grease, chemicals, dirt, etc
          3. don't put tools/equipment on boots
          4. repair damage promptly
            1. one patch per tube is okay
          5. cover when stored
          6. keep clean
            1. could use mild soap and water
            2. or boot finish
      2. Electro-mechanical expulsion deicing systems (EMEDS)
        1. capacitive discharge actuator
          1. changes shape from elliptical to circular when energized
        2. flexes the leading edge skin to knock ice loose
          1. .030" skin deflection
          2. acceleration of thousands of Gs
        3. advantages
          1. no fluid to maintain
          2. low power consumption
          3. no runback
          4. no aerodynamic disruption of airfoil
          5. low maintenance
          6. environmentally friendly
      3. Hot air/bleed air
        1. exhaust augmenters
          1. basically a heater muff
          2. main purpose of augmenter is to help cool the engine
            1. hot air is a byproduct
        2. combustion heaters
        3. bleed air
          1. just duct hot air along leading edges
      4. Carb heat
        1. hot air source
        2. loss of rpm indicates icing
        3. can occur over 70F
    5. Alcohol systems
      1. chemical freezing point depressant wing anti-ice and deice system (TKS)
        1. based on freezing point depressant concept
        2. an ethyl glycol-based fluid (TKS fluid) lowers freezing point of water below -76F (-60C)
        3. dispenses from laser-drilled titanium panels mounted on the leading edges of the wing, strut, and stabilizers
        4. traditional slinger ring provides ice protection on the propeller
        5. the TKS fluid mixes with super-cooled water in the cloud, depresses its freezing point and allows the mixture to flow off the aircraft without freezing
    6. Windshield rain removal/repellent
      1. Hydrophobic coating
        1. permanent seal coating on outside of windshield
        2. causes raindrops to bead up and roll off
        3. reduces need for wipers, improves visibility
      2. Windshield wipers
        1. worm and spur gear change rotational energy to linear motion
        2. mostly inadequate
      3. Pneumatic rain/ice removal system
        1. works like a hair dryer
        2. blows hot air over the windshield
        3. blows the rain aways
        4. heats/defrosts windshield
        5. powered by electric blower or bleed air
      4. Chemical rain repellent
        1. transparent film put on windshield with chemicals
        2. basically a temporary hydrophobic coating
        3. only use it in wet conditions!
          1. restricts visibility on dry windshields
          2. if it gets on a dry windshield, don't smear it!
        4. applied by
          1. push button switches
          2. trips a solenoid to apply repellant
          3. metered by a timer
    7. Ground deicing/anti-icing
      1. frost, ice, snow very dangerous on flight surfaces
        1. sandpaper ice can reduce lift by 30%, increase drag 40%
        2. increase stall speed, less predictable/controllable
        3. can also form in wheel, brake, hinges, valves, switches, etc
      2. FAR 121.629, 135.227, 91.209 (Clean Aircraft Concept)
        1. no takeoff with frost, snow, ice on airfoils/control surfaces
        2. pre-takeoff check 5min before
      3. Chemicals
        1. FPD (Freezing Point Depressant)
          1. ground de-icing/anti-icing fluids
          2. MIL-A-8243C
          3. ethylene glycol or propylene glycol-based family
            1. may be a mix of both types of glycol
            2. exact formulas are proprietary
          4. typically applied heated (~180F)
            1. Hold-over Time** estimated time de-icing fluid is effective
          5. begins when final application of fluids starts, and expires when it is no longer effective
          6. timetables are just an estimate

    2.3. Landing Gear

    2.3.1. Inner Tubes

    1. SAE Aerospace Standard AS50141 ( MIL-I-5014)
    2. materials
      1. natural rubber
        1. more durable
        2. worse air retention
        3. worse heat resistance
        4. sometimes marked with yellow line/print
      2. butyl rubber
        1. better air retention
        2. good heat resistance
        3. less durable
        4. sometimes marked with blue line/print
      3. tubes required to operate at 100+psi may have nylon ply reinforcement around inside circumference
    3. care/use
      1. preferred storage
        1. original packaging or wrapped in paper
        2. keep in dark, cool space
        3. may be inflated in storage
          1. no more than 1psi, slightly inflated
      2. always check tube size matches tire size
        1. some tubes stretch to fit by design
        2. valve stem location matches wheel port location
      3. in service growth ~25% over time
        1. best practice is to replace tube and tire together
      4. tube indexing
      5. balancing tube and tire
        1. valve stem or tube balance marker should align with red dot on tire side wall
          1. balance marker is a colored stripe marking a heavy spot of tube
          2. red dot marks light spot of tire
        2. if no balance marker, use valve stem
        3. if no red dot on tire side, align valve stem/balance marker and tire sidewall serial number
    4. inspection
    5. inflate just enough to round out the tube
      1. tube sections and seams
        1. chafing
        2. thinned (heat damaged)
        3. wrinkles
        4. cracks
        5. splits
          1. note that some tires have grooves to allow air to escape from between tire and tube
      2. valve and valve base
        1. stem
        2. core
        3. threads, core and cap
        4. ensure dust cap is installed
      3. slippage
        1. if tire and tube slip relative to each other, valve stem can be sheared off
        2. often there will be slippage lines marked on the wheel to check for this
        3. acceptable creep limits may be established with tubeless tires
          1. creep may indicated that tire is underinflated
          2. hard braking/landing could also cause this
    6. valve cores and tools
      1. Schrader p/n 6035
        1. 0-400psi, 70psi nominal
        2. white silicone plunger, teflon plug
        3. torque 2-5 in-lbs for installation
      2. high pressure valve core is embossed with "H"
      3. valve core tool
        1. deflator
        2. extraction tool
          1. for if head of valve core is broken off
        3. threads to chase threads on valve core
        4. threads to chase threads on dust cap

    2.3.2. Tires

    1. TSO C-62
    2. composition by weight
      1. 50% rubber
      2. 45% fabric (usually nylon)
      3. 5% steel
    3. compared to car tires, aircraft tires have much greater
      1. load ratings
        1. 9650 vs 1598
      2. pressure rating
        1. 200 vs 35
      3. designed deflection
        1. 225 vs 112
      4. load/tire weight
        1. 244 vs 78
    4. markings on tires
      1. tire size designations
      2. see handout
        1. type 1
          1. smooth contour
          2. identified with diameter
        2. type 2
          1. high pressure
          2. identified with diameter and section number
            1. type 3**
          3. low pressure
          4. identified with section width - wheel rim diameter
        3. type 4
          1. extra low pressure
          2. diameter, section width, and wheel rim diameter
        4. type 6
          1. low profile
          2. diameter, section width, wheel rim diameter
            1. type 7**
          3. extra high pressure
          4. tire diameter and section width
            1. type 8**
          5. extra high pressure– low profile
          6. identified by diameter x section width - wheel rim diameter
        5. foreign sizing is more standard
          1. tire OD x section width
          2. in millimeters
      3. 'R' in designation means it is a radial ply
      4. tubeless tires
        1. usually marked 'tubeless'
        2. tubetype tires are usually not marked
      5. sidewall vent marking
        1. white or aluminum dots
        2. tubetype
        3. green paint dots
        4. tubeless
        5. if inner tube leaks, the tire allows air to vent out to prevent damage to sidewall
        6. air getting caught in between the ply can cause blistering
    5. tire plies
      1. bias ply
        1. traditional construction
        2. ply orientation 30-60 degrees to tire rotation
        3. provides flexibility and balanced carcass strength
        4. 1, 2, or 3 bead bundles
          1. beads are hoops of steel wire by the wheel flange
          2. anchor the casing plies
          3. provide a firm mounting surface on the wheel
        5. tread reinforcing plies– protective layers
          1. tire can be recapped as long as carcass plies are intact
      2. radial ply
        1. newer construction
        2. ply orientation 90 degrees relative to tire rotation
        3. provides greater tire strength for high loads with less deformation
        4. single bead bundle
        5. undertread protector
          1. protective layers stabilize tire crown
        6. generally fewer plies compared to bias ply
      3. ply ratings indicate number of cotton plies for equivalent strength
    6. tire treads
      1. ribbed
        1. circumferential ribbing
        2. good clearance for water to escape
        3. most widely used
      2. diamond tread
        1. best for unimproved runways
        2. better traction
        3. older tire design
      3. ribbed center, diamond shoulders
        1. 'all weather' tread
      4. treadless
        1. smooth contour
        2. often used in tundra style tires
        3. high flotation tires
        4. less susceptible to cracking
      5. chine
        1. water deflectors on the edge of tires
        2. deflects water away from engine intakes
    7. care of tires
      1. maintenance
        1. avoid exposure to:
          1. petroleum products
            1. remove oil contamination with denatured alcohol, then soap/water wash
          2. electric motors
          3. fluorescent light (ozone)
          4. sunlight
            1. can cover tires while on aircraft
          5. heat
            1. store racked in cool, dark place
              1. generally standing vertically on racks
            2. guard against heat build-up
              1. short ground rolls, slow taxi, min braknig, proper tire inflation
        2. tire installation
          1. allow for nylon stretch
            1. 5-10% within 24 hrs
          2. tubeless tire diffusion
            1. max 5% loss in 24 period
            2. only after the first 12 hrs after inflation
          3. dual inflation pressure discrepancy
            1. more than 5psi difference, correct condition and note in logbook
          4. inflation
            1. do not** inflate to pressure displayed on tire sidewall, get correct pressure from service manual
          5. inflate >75k lbs aircraft tires with nitrogen
          6. 4% higher pressure: unloaded vs loaded conditions
            1. eg. unloaded pressure 180psi, loaded pressure 187.2psi
            2. (180x1.04)
          7. 1% pressure increase for each 5F temp change
            1. eg. 180psi at 70F = 154.6psi at 0F
      2. maintain proper pressure
        1. check pressure weekly or more
          1. recommended before each flight
        2. new mountings: check pressure for several days
        3. check pressure when tire is cool (2-3 hrs after flight)
        4. maintain tire pressure in accordance with aircraft maintenance manual
    8. inspection
      1. common issues:
        1. nylon flat spotting: roll aircraft
        2. leaks: check with water
        3. tread injury: follow aircraft manufacturers' instructions
        4. cuts exposing or penetrating cord body: remove, repair, recap, or scrap
        5. sidewall or tread bulges: remove, mark area before deflation
      2. tread condition and depth
        1. measure tread depth and compare to skid depth
        2. distance between tread surface of new tire and bottom of the deepest groove
      3. sidewall weather checking: check service manual information
      4. tread reinforcement showing: remove for rebuild
      5. wear through carcass plies: scrap
        1. FOD, mark before deflating

    2.3.3. Wheels

    1. manufacture
      1. TSO C26
      2. aluminum alloy or magnesium alloy
      3. to save weight
        1. cast or forged
          1. 2014-T aluminum alloy forging
          2. AZ81C-T magnesium alloy forging
        2. shot peened for fatigue resistance (sometimes)
      4. O-ring between wheel halves on tubeless
      5. knurled flanges identify wheel for tube-type tire (sometimes)
    2. types
      1. fixed flange drop center
        1. tire bead forced over the rim
        2. generally only used with very small tires
      2. demountable (removable flange)
        1. tire may be removed from wheel without removing the wheel from the axle
        2. loose flange– flange segments secured by locking ring spring clip
        3. detachable flange– flange secured by nuts and bolts
      3. split center (split rim)
        1. most common design today
        2. often secured with tie bolts
    3. components
      1. inboard wheel half
        1. steel reinforced keys or keyways for brakes
        2. bearing
          1. bearing cup (interference fit)
          2. caged bearing (cone)
          3. grease seal, retainers, and snap ring
        3. fusible plugs
          1. melt to deflate tire if wheel is overheated
        4. over-inflation valve
          1. blows out to deflate tire in case of over-inflation
          2. on very large wheels
      2. outboard wheel half
        1. inflation valve or hole for inner-tube valve stem
        2. bearing
          1. bearing cup (interference fit)
          2. caged bearing (cone)
          3. grease seal, retainers, and snap ring
        3. axle cap and retaing ring
        4. anti-skid bracket
      3. hardware
        1. internal wrenching bolts, washers, nuts
          1. high-temp self-locking nut
      4. lubricated o-ring seal between wheel halves for tubeless type
    4. inspection
      1. on the aircraft
        1. light aircraft verify proper tire pressure daily
        2. heavy aircraft very pressure before each flight
          1. tire cool, 2-3 hours after flight
        3. check wheel weight security
        4. brake tangs must align with wheel slots
        5. axle nut torque
          1. too loose, bearing cup spins and scallops
          2. too tight, damaged bearing
      2. off the aircraft
        1. procedures
          1. deflate tire first**
        2. break the bead
          1. don't pry at it! use the right tool
        3. remove and properly store bearings
        4. note wheel weight location
        5. remove tie bolts
        6. clean wheel assembly
          1. use rubbing alcohol
      3. clean and inspect bearings
        1. clean
          1. use Stoddard Solvent or Mineral Spirits
          2. make sure to spin all the bearings
          3. don't use compressed air
        2. inspect
          1. galling
            1. damage from rubbing of mating surfaces, welded together by friction
          2. spalling
            1. chips are broken from the surface of case-hardened material because the softer, inner section was deformed
          3. brinelling
            1. from excessive radial load, rollers are forced into the the race, leaving indents
          4. water stain
            1. black discoloration showing intergranular corrosion
          5. discoloration from overheating
            1. blue marks on bearing rollers show that they were operated dry
          6. rust
            1. can ruin bearing surfaces
        3. bearing cup removal
          1. heat wheel
            1. boiling water for 1 hr
            2. oven 35mins at 225F
          2. tap bearing cup out with fiber drift
        4. bearing cup replacement
          1. reheat wheel
          2. chill bearing cup
            1. -25F to -55F for min of 4hrs
            2. dry ice min of 15 mins
          3. coat cup exterior with zinc chromate primer
