          4. drift cup in with fiber drift
      4. wheel halves
        1. cleaning
          1. wheel halves
          2. mineral spirits
          3. bead seat area
          4. alcohol
        2. inspection
          1. bead seat
            1. eddy current inspection
          2. keys or key slots
            1. dye penetrant, magnetic particle, dimensional
            2. check key attachment screw stake
          3. internal and external surfaces
            1. dye penetrant, dimensional
          4. bolts and other hardware
            1. magnetic particle
          5. fusible plugs
            1. visual, replace all if any are distorted
          6. corrosion
            1. check bead seat for trapped water
            2. remove corrosion per manufacturers' instructions
            3. treat aluminum surfaces with Alodine
            4. treat magnesium surfaces with Dow 19
            5. Finish with two coats zinc chromate primer
              1. except mating surfaces and bolt bosses– one coat only
        3. balance weights
        4. most aircraft wheels >10"dia are statically balanced when built, and may have balance weights attached
          1. always return weights to original location
          2. eg. make sure lines match up between wheel halves
  • reassemble wheel
    1. tubeless
      1. clean bead seat area w/ isopropyl alcohol
      2. usually inboard wheel half first
        1. inspect and lubricate wheel o-ring
      3. install tire on inboard wheel half
      4. index outboard wheel half
      5. lubricate hardware if specified
      6. torque per manufacturers' recommendations
      7. inflate tire to 1/2 static inflation pressure
      8. final tire inflation or adjustment on aircraft
    2. tube type
      1. cleane bead seat area
      2. prepare and position inner tube
      3. prepare and position tire
      4. position brake disk
      5. lube hardware if specified
      6. torque per manufacturers' recommendations
      7. inflate tire to 1/2 static inflation pressure
      8. adjust axle nut torque
      9. final tire inflation or adjustment on aircraft
  • mounting/dismounting
    1. dust inside of tire and outside of tube with talc to prevent sticking
    2. index tube valve stem
    3. assemble wheel correctly; use tire cage for inflation
    4. inflate, deflate, re-inflate to help tube settle into place
  • static balancing
    1. put the wheel assembly on a stand and let in turn freely to find the heavy spot
    2. add balance weights as needed
      1. might also use balance pads inside the tire
    3. required balance weight in ounces should not exceed rim diameter in inches

    • 2.3.4. Gear

    1. non-shock absorbing struts
      1. steel, aluminum, or composite materials transfer load to airframe
      2. spring steel gear– Cessna type
        1. load transfer only
        2. minimal rebound protection
        3. generally not field repairable
        4. serialized Cessna component
        5. check Cessna maintenance manual table of limits for alignment data
        6. high strength heat-treated steel
        7. factor shot peened lower surface increases fatigue life and prevent stress cracking
        8. compressive layer thickness: 0.010-0.020" (Cessna)
        9. inspect painted finish for blisters, chips, nicks, and rust
        10. allowable repair
          1. remove corrosion with a stainless steel brush, by hand only
          2. blend with 400 grit aluminum oxide sand paper (20:1 ratio)
          3. work inboard to outboard; along leg length
          4. damage repair limits (Cessna):
            1. >0.012-0.063" re-shot peen
            2. >0.063" replace gear
          5. within one hour of rework:
            1. epoxy primer or equivalent
            2. polyurethane top coat
          6. no power sanding!
            1. may destroy metal temper
          7. no chemical paint strippers!
            1. may cause hydrogen embrittlement
      3. tubular steel nose gear (Grumman TR2)
        1. load transfer only
        2. minimal rebound protection
        3. sometimes field repairable by welding
          1. types A, B, and C (tubular steel) are generally repairable by welded sleeve methods
            1. originally heat-treated assemblies must be re-heat treated after welding
          2. type D is not repairable
            1. axle stub is made of highly heat-treated nickel ally steel and machined to close tolerances
            2. the oleo portion of the structure is generally heat-treated after welding and perfectly machined to work as a shock absorber– welding would cause distortion
        4. some have bungee shock cord
      4. elastic shock rings (Piper Cub)
        1. bungee cord assembly
        2. 'endless' elastic ring of multiple strand rubber
        3. 400-1160lb test rating
        4. cotton braid cover
          1. color coded for rubber cure date
            1. 2 matching stripes tell last digit of year
            2. 1 color tells quarter (of that year)
        5. remove boot for thorough inspection
        6. beware– safety hazard
        7. oil stained cotton cover– damaged
        8. 'necked' diameter – worn, broken elastic
        9. replacement considered preventive maintenance
      5. shock disc (Mooney M20)
        1. stack of rubber discs in strut absorbs shock
        2. 1.6-1.8 tons compressive force required to install them
    2. oleo shock struts
    3. shimmy damper
      1. self-contained hydraulic unit with piston with orifice for hydraulic fluid to pass through
      2. mostly for nose-wheel aircraft
      3. keeps nose wheel steadier on the ground
      4. one part attached to fixed part of landing gear, other to strut
      5. potential causes of shimmy
        1. engine mount/forging cracks
        2. steering collar play, lateral or vertical
        3. worn hardware
        4. tire wear/imbalance
        5. torque link play
        6. steering rod spherical bearing play
      6. other kinds
        1. Lord fluidless uses friction
        2. vane type
          1. four interconnected chambers
          2. fluid is forced between chambers if shaft turns
        3. steering damper does steering and shimmy control
    4. gear extension
      1. Electrical Retraction/Extension
      2. FAR 23.729
        1. electrical motor (DC split field, series-wound)
          1. series-wound motors give a lot of torque
          2. split field motors are reversible in direction
          3. duty cycle limits operating time to prevent overheating of the motor
          4. an electric motor used to raise and lower a landing gear would most likely be a split field series-wound motor
        2. limit switches (micro switches) start/stop motion
      3. Hydraulic Retraction/Extension
        1. engine driven hydraulic pump
          1. generally multiple pumps for redundancy
        2. electrically driven hydraulic pump
          1. power pack**
        3. a hydraulic system referred that will have all hydraulic power components located in one unit
    5. Pneumatic Retraction/Extension
      1. compressed air
        1. on-board air compressor furnishes air
        2. cylinder or flask holds air
        3. air is expelled overboard after use
    6. Emergency Extension
      1. mechanical
        1. lever, hand crank, pull cord, or free fall
      2. hydraulic
        1. hand pump or accumulator
      3. compressed gas cylinder (blow down)
        1. air or nitrogen
      4. on all aircraft equipped with retractable landing gear, some means must be provided to extent he landing gear if the normal operating mechanism fails
    7. Retraction nomenclature
      1. ground lock
      2. mechanical pin, collar, or clamp to keep gear out while working on it
      3. landing gear safety switch
      4. electrical switch actuated by strut extension and compression
        1. mounted on the shock strut
        2. actuated by scissors
        3. on when plane's weight is on the gear
        4. weight on wheels (WOW) switch
      5. down lock
      6. mechanical lock holds gear in extended position and actuates indicator
      7. up lock
      8. mechanical lock holds gear in retracted position
      9. up and down limit switches
      10. electrical switches that completes the indicating circuit and/or stops electrical motor

    2.3.5. Hydraulics

    2.3.6. Braking systems

    1. purpose
      1. slowing, stopping, holding, steering
    2. classifications
      1. independent
        1. separate from other systems
        2. troubleshooting
          1. spongy brakes
            1. air in the lines
            2. deteriorated hose
          2. brake pedal creeps/fades
            1. leak
            2. could be internal leak in master cylinder
          3. brakes drag or don't release promptly
            1. bad return spring
            2. debris in compensator port
          4. delayed brake response
            1. viscous fluid
          5. excessive pedal travel
            1. lack of fluid
            2. worn linings
          6. brake grabbing
            1. leaking fluid in brake lining
          7. brake chatters/squealing
            1. worn pads
            2. misaligned/warped brake disc
      2. dependent
        1. power control
          1. require a metering device
        2. power boost
          1. uses a master cylinder but main hydraulic pressure assists
    3. actuation
      1. mechanical
      2. hydraulic
      3. pneumatic
    4. development over time
      1. tail skid
      2. shoe and drum
        1. mechanical or hydraulic actuation
        2. single servo or dual servo (reversible)
        3. directino of wheel rotation marked on single servo brakes
        4. single shoe or two shoe construction
        5. twelve hole torque plate permits alignment of actuating cylinder
        6. hydraulic single servo brakes– one piston in the actuating cylinder
        7. duo-servo has two
        8. shoe lining held on by rivets
      3. single spot disc
        1. floating disc (Goodyear style)
          1. not common
          2. problems with discs becoming dislocated or rattling
          3. generally should be replaced when worn to 0.16"
          4. remember to inspect the keyways for damage
          5. linings have holes for heat dissipation
          6. self-adjuster maintains clearance as linings wear
        2. fixed disc (Cleveland style)
          1. calipers float laterally
          2. pretty much all Goodyear brakes have been replaced with Cleveland
          3. torque plate attached to axle and actuator slides along anchor pins
          4. different kinds of linings
            1. metallic
              1. held with pins
            2. organic
              1. held with rivets
            3. some have a wear indicator, like a groove or notch
          5. most linings should be replaced when worn to .1"
      4. dual disc and/or multiple piston
      5. multiple disc (dependent brake systems)
        1. segmented rotor annular piston
        2. expander tube
        3. multiple disc/multiple piston
    5. master cylinders
      1. general
        1. single action reciprocating piston pump
        2. transforms mechanical motion into hydraulic pressure
        3. means of varying pressure for braking
      2. reservoir
        1. separate or integral
      3. compensator port or valve
        1. path for fluid to get behind the piston, maintains correct amount in brake lines
        2. compensates for thermal expansion of fluid
      4. different styles
        1. Warner
        2. Scott
          1. diaphragm instead of piston
        3. Goodyear
        4. Gladden
          1. has a parking brake that can compensate for thermal expansion
        5. Matco
    6. Automatic adjusters
      1. correct for gaps from lining wear
        1. pin pushes piston in further as lining thins
      2. Calibrated with a nut
        1. To much torque; no brakes eventually
        2. Insufficient torque; dragging brakes
      3. can indicate lining wear
    7. Power brakes (dependent braking)
      1. draws from main hydraulic system
      2. Fluid flows to:
        1. hydraulic system pressure manifold
        2. accumulator
        3. power brake control valves
          1. meters fluid to brake with pressure ball check valve
          2. piston pushes ball to cut off flow of fluid between reservoir and brakes
          3. also applies system pressure to the brakes
          4. controlled by pilots
          5. has a return line to system manifold to allow for thermal expansion
          6. tuning fork with feel spring mimics sensation of pressure in brake pedals for the pilot
        4. anti-skid control valve
          1. electrohydraulic device that prevents the pilot from slowing too quickly
        5. debooster
          1. mounted on landing gear shock strut, installed between control valve and actuator
          2. reduces pressure to a safe level for brakes
          3. provides larger volume of fluid from reservoir inside
          4. allows for rapid release of brakes with big spring
          5. piston has narrow bit on top (exposed to system pressure) and is wide at the bottom (pushing on brakes)
          6. ball and rise pin can allow fluid to flow through debooster to replenish it as needed
          7. Lockout debooster
            1. in case of a serious leak at brakes
            2. limited travel, no chance to replenish lost fluid
        6. brakes
      3. check valves and hydraulic fuses protect the system from leaks
      4. orifice check valves prevent brake chatter
    8. Expander tube brake
      1. mostly used on older, bigger aircraft
      2. require lots of fluid
      3. dragging brakes fire hazard
      4. sluggish action on cold weather
      5. Components
        1. spider assembly
        2. expander tube
        3. frames to hold it together
        4. brake blocks
        5. tube shield to keep it in place and protect it from heat
          1. it's made of reinforced natural rubber, do heat is an issue
        6. return spring
          1. semi-elliptical shape
        7. torque bars to stop blocks from turning with the wheel
        8. may have a manual adjuster (preload valve) to trap some fluid in the assembly
          1. clearance between blocks and drum is critical
          2. on others, the tube just expands with wear
      6. Maintenance and inspection
        1. don't actuate unrestrained brake; you'll damage it
        2. radial clearance check with feeler gauge
        3. set clearance on adjuster
        4. tap dents out with hammer and wooden block
        5. remove retractor springs with screwdriver and pliers
        6. inspect for corrosion, broken/distorted parts, burns/charring on the tube
    9. Multiple disc brake
      1. rotors and stators are pressed together to brake
        1. bronze-plated rotors catch in the tangs on the wheel and turn with it
        2. segmented rotors linked together allows for thermal expansion to limit warping
          1. slots with relief holes in the rotor do the same thing
        3. steel stators can only move laterally, they are fixed and don't turn with the wheel
          1. linings are usually riveted to the stators on both sides
      2. Pistons
        1. may have one ring-shaped annular piston to push against the pressure plate
          1. sealed with v-ring seal
        2. nowadays they use multiple pistons with one manifold
      3. often no retraction spring
        1. maybe just in the debooster
      4. torque tube is bolted to axle, rotors and stators are stacked on it
        1. slots/splines on torque tube keep stators fixed
      5. don't set parking brake when it's hot; it could damage it
      6. Inspection items, off aircraft
        1. torque tube and pressure plate
          1. visual, dimensional, magnetic particle inspections
        2. piston housing
          1. visual, dimensional, dye penetrant
          2. pistons, seals, backup rings and insulators
          3. replace seals, etc
        3. inlet and bleeder adapter
        4. bolts and other hardware
          1. visual, dimensional, magnetic particle
        5. rotors and stators
          1. thickness, wear, cracks at relieve slots
          2. check for battering on tangs and slots
          3. loose rivets, curled pads
          4. glazed pads
        6. self-adjusters
          1. visual and magnetic particle inspections
          2. check return spring compression
          3. check pin torque
      7. Carbon brakes
        1. rotors and stators made of carbon
        2. compensating shim can be inserted to allow more wear; otherwise only about half can be used
        3. Advantages
          1. excellent heat sink to absorb energy
          2. dimensionally stable; low coefficient of thermal expansion (CTE)
          3. lightweight
          4. when the discs wear down, you can glue multiple old ones together
          5. 20-50% longer lasting
        4. Disadvantages
          1. much more wear from initial brake application when cold
            1. better to have fewer, long brake applications to prolong life
          2. much more expensive
          3. surrounding components will fail from heat first
    10. Power boosted brakes
      1. cross between independent and dependant brake
      2. for aircraft that land too fast for independent brakes but are too light for power brakes
      3. works like a master cylinder, but can get an assist from system hydraulic pressure like a power brake control valve if you press hard enough
    11. Emergency brakes
      1. they use main system hydraulic pressure that goes to shuttle valves at the brakes; it's stirred in a reservoir in case of emergency
      2. a bottle of compressed gas (air or nitrogen) can push the fluid and shuttle valves open
      3. operated by pilot from cockpit
      4. brakes held in off position with shear wire
    12. Inspection and service
      1. Lining replacement
        1. minor repair
        2. with Cleveland brakes, no need to even jack the plane
        3. cut off safety wire
        4. remove backplate
        5. check lining wear
        6. remove and replace linings from backplate and pressure plate as needed
        7. Clean/inspect backplate and pressure plate, they must be straight
        8. min 2 cracks outside crest per 90deg in rivet head
        9. inspect disc
        10. make sure you have correct hardware
        11. follow correct lining conditioning procedure
        12. occasional hard braking prolongs life of brakes
      2. Bleeding brakes
        1. Use MIL-H-5606
          1. petroleum-based, red hydraulic fluid
        2. Can push fluid down, but better to force it up
        3. bleeder screw usually 1/4" wrench
        4. Top down
          1. Fill brake reservoir
          2. Step on brake pedal
          3. Open bleeder screw to drain into container
          4. Repeat until done
          5. Requires 2 people
        5. Bottom up
          1. Pump fluid into bleeder screw with pressure pot (charge with 10-15psi air)
          2. Allow it to flow to the brake reservoir; no need to overflow it

    2.3.7. Anti-skid systems

    1. Modulates brake pressure to prevent a skid
    2. activated when deceleration >20ft/sec2 or when wheel speed >6mph below aircraft speed
    3. <20mph, it is disabled
    4. Why?
      1. tire friction on wet runway is less than that developed at the brake
      2. pilot can't tell where a skid begins
      3. need to maintain a slip, not a skid
    5. Components
      1. Hydraulic control valve
        1. dumps pressure from brake line back to system manifold
        2. torque motor
          1. permanent magnet and a coil wrapped on flapper valve
          2. spool valve shifts to redirect fluid
      2. Control box
        1. generates electrical signal to valve
        2. translates between speed sensor and control valve
        3. provides touchdown protection
      3. Wheel speed sensor
        1. AC
          1. variable reluctance AC generator
          2. varying frequency proportional to wheel speed
          3. signal change to DC
        2. DC
          1. magnet tachometer generator develops voltage proportional to wheel speed
          2. 0.1V/mph, polarity sponsoring
          3. Rapid decay of the voltage triggers the system
    6. Trivia
      1. electrohydraulic system
      2. armed by cockpit switch
      3. AC or DC wheel speed sensor
      4. turns on just below skid point
      5. warning lamp illuminates if system fails or is off
      6. control valve relieves pressure from the brake
      7. touchdown protection with squat switch, so you can't engage brakes early

    2.3.8. Remote indication

    1. Uses
      1. Landing gear
      2. Cowl flaps
      3. Wing flaps
      4. Fuel quantity
      5. Hydraulic pressure
    2. Types
      1. Selsyn synchro system
        1. transmitter
          1. resistor with rotor, contact arm
          2. DC power
          3. creates electromagnetism
        2. indicator
          1. electromagnetism creates electromagnets
          2. they displace a permanent magnet with indicator needle
      2. Magnesyn system
        1. Like an AC selsyn
        2. more rotation of transmitter means more deflection of indicator
        3. permanent magnet rotor
      3. Autosyn system
        1. AC power
        2. electromagnetic rotor
      4. Stall warning
        1. reed-type
          1. mechanical horn activated by negative air pressure
          2. slot on leading edge
        2. vane-type
          1. tab on leading edge acts as an electrical switch
          2. it flops up and activates warning horn/light
      5. Angle of attack indicator
        1. alpha vane
          1. outside fin is free to rotate, attached to a potentiometer
        2. probe type
          1. slotted probe sucks into the relative wind
          2. inside is split into 2 chambers by rotating panel
          3. differential pressure between chambers rotates indicator

    2.4. Nonmetallic Structures

    2.4.1. Fabric

    1. Overview
      1. Advantages
        1. Low equipment cost
        2. Lightweight compared to metal
        3. Resistant to blunt impact
        4. Easy to repair
        5. Recover permits thorough airframe inspection
      2. Disadvantages
        1. Labor-intensive installation
        2. High cost of materials/supplies
        3. Requires periodic recover
        4. Airframe defects may go unnoticed until recover
      3. Terminology
        1. dressed out
          1. all prepped and ready for finishing
        2. warp
          1. length-wise fibers
        3. fill
          1. cross-wise fibers woven in
        4. bias
          1. 45º angle
        5. pinked edge
          1. edge cut in a zigzag to prevent unraveling
        6. selvage edge
          1. woven edge, doesn't unravel
        7. griege
          1. condition of polyester fabric after production but before being heat-shrunk
        8. cross coat
          1. brushing or spraying where the second coat is applied at 90º to the first coat; together they make one cross coat
      4. Natural fabric and dope covering
        1. History
          1. Early
            1. Cotton or linen
            2. Oils or varnishes used for protection, but didn't provide good tension
          2. 1916
            1. Cellulose nitrate dope
              1. Adheres and tautens well
              2. Smooth and durable
              3. Flammable!
          3. WWII
            1. Cellulose acetate butyrate dope
              1. Less flammable
              2. Doesn't adhere as well
          4. Later
            1. System of two versions of for coatings developed
            2. Aluminum solids added to butyrate coatings to prevent UV deterioration
        2. Organic materials
          1. Performance requirements determined by wing-loading (lbs/in2) and never exceed speed (Vne)
            1. measure the tensile strength
          2. Mercerized aircraft cotton
            1. Grade A cotton
              1. TSO-C15d
              2. Min tensile strength when new: 80lbs/in in warp and fill
                1. legal limit of deterioration is 70% = 56lbs/in
              3. required on aircraft with wing loading of >=9psf or never exceed speed >=160mph
            2. Intermediate grade
              1. TSO-C14b
                1. 65lbs/in** in warp and fill min tensile strength when new
              2. can degrade to 46lbs/in (70%)
            3. for use on aircrfat with wing loading <9psf and never exceed speed of <160mph
          3. Glider cloth
            1. Society Automotive Engineers AMS 3802
            2. min tensile strength when new 50lbs/in in warp and fill
              1. can degrade to 35lbs/in (70%)
            3. for gliders with wing loading of <=8spf, provided never exceed speed is <=135
        3. Aircraft linen
          1. British 7F1
          2. meets min strength requirements of TSO-C15
      5. Natural fabrics had a limited lifespan
        1. Cotton: 6-8 years
        2. Linen: up to 15 years
        3. Comes from flax
    2. Synthetic fabrics/finishes
      1. Polyester fabric
        1. unlimited lifespan if protected from UV light– UV radiation is extremely destructive to polyester fabric
        2. dope additives developed to prevent separation from fabric when dry
        3. modern polyester covering systems developed with proprietary finishing products, marketed as fabric/covering systems
          1. Ceconite, Poly-Fiber, Superflite, Air-Tech, or Stewart System
      2. Fiberglass (Razorback)
        1. treated glass fibers; fire resistant
        2. impervious to moisture, mildew, chemicals, and acids
        3. heavy in comparison with other fabrics
        4. no longer manufactured
        5. had an AD issued against because of faulty rib lacing cord
        6. components
          1. fiberglass fabric 5mil thick
            1. 52" or 72" widths
          2. fiberglass reinforcing tape 7mil thick
          3. fiberglass finishing tape 7mil thick
          4. rib lacing cord
          5. cellulose acetate butyrate dope used to achieve shrinking
      3. STC approval allows recover of many aircraft in Standard category
    3. Sealers, Fillers, and Primers
      1. sealer
        1. surrounds the fibers in the fabric with a protective adhesion and keep out dirt and moisture
        2. first coat applied to polyester fabric after attaching to airframe and heat-shrinking
      2. filler
        1. sprayed on in a number of cross coats after the sealer, as required by manufacturer/STC
        2. contains solids or chemicals that block UV light
      3. primer
        1. sealer and filler combined into one
    4. Covering Substitution Approval
      1. STC approval
        1. just find one that applies
        2. documented as a major alteration with 337 form
      2. Field approval
        1. get a DER to demonstrate that it is a suitable substitute
          1. DER data usually required
        2. major alteration, 337 form
      3. Type Certificate Data Sheet (TCDS) Substitution
        1. if the original TCDS approves the use of different fabrics
        2. this counts as a major repair; still needs a 337 form
      4. remember that an IA has to do a pre-cover inspection and a final inspection
  • Inspection
    1. Fabric strength determination
      1. checked at 100hr and annual inspections
        1. follow Part 43 Appendix D
      2. standard is new fabric breaking strength
      3. natural
        1. 'field testers' sometimes used for organic material
          1. tests puncture resistance, not tensile strength
        2. fabric considered airworthy until it deteriorates to strength <70% of original
          1. grade A: 56lbs/in
          2. intermediate grade: 46lbs/in
          3. glider cloth: 35lbs/in
      4. polyester
        1. UV radiation is the main issue
        2. fabric finish should protect it indefinitely, so the main thing to inspect is the protective coating
        3. use a light test; if you can see light through the fabric, that's bad
          1. use 60W light 1ft from fabric
    2. fabric deterioration:
      1. pollution (sulfur dioxide and exhaust gasses)
      2. mold/mildew
      3. acidic dopes and thinners
        1. dope can become acidic over time
      4. insufficient dope film– UV damage
      5. poor storage conditions
        1. unheated dirt floor hangar
        2. vermin
    3. Pre-cover inspection and prep
      1. thoroughly inspect the whole airframe
      2. remove and save old fabric for reference
      3. check that structure is airworthy
      4. make sure coating is compatible
      5. IA inspection required prior to recover
  • Covering
    1. Methods to cover aircraft
      1. Blanket method
        1. bulk fabric cut to size and attached
        2. large/irregular surfaces built from multiple pieces
      2. Envelope method
        1. pre-sewn envelope or 'sock'
        2. slide the open end over the structure
        3. align the seams
        4. close the end
    2. Fabric seams
      1. plain overlap seam
      2. folded fell seam
      3. modified folded fell seam
      4. french fell seam
        1. strongest type
      5. Reinforcing tape
        1. prevents stitching cord from cutting through the fabric
        2. when covering natural fabrics, also used for rib cross-bracing
        3. width conforms to width of rib capstrip– double width sometimes needed for very wide members
      6. surface tape
        1. covers/protects stitching, etc
        2. anti-tear taped can be added on cotton for fast planes
        3. should cover all seams
        4. may be standard-cut or bias-cut (good for curves)
    3. Mechanical attachment to ribs
      1. rib-stitching
        1. make sure to get proper spacing
        2. faster the airplane, tighter the spacing
        3. tighter spacing in the prop wash area
        4. knots
          1. Modified Seine knot
            1. standard knot for rib-stitching
            2. may be hidden with polyester
          2. starter knot
            1. just a square knot, locked with half hitches
            2. requires 1/2 of normal spacing
          3. splice knot
            1. used to splice on a new length of cord
      2. clips
        1. can be used with metal ribs
        2. Martin clip
        3. Cessna clip
      3. screws
        1. PK screw
        2. tapered
        3. used with washer and reinforcing tape
      4. rivets
        1. blind rivets with big head
    4. Reinforcements
      1. drain grommets
        1. reinforce drain holes
        2. need to prevent moisture from accumulating
        3. generally at trailing edges or low points of structure
        4. for seaplane grommets, open end faces aft
      2. inspection rings
        1. reinforce inspection ports
      3. reinforcing gussets
        1. used at exit points for cables/tubes
        2. anywhere something sticks out
        3. reinforcing patches
          1. on natural fabrics, leather was used
    5. Dope
      1. purposes
        1. makes the structure air tight and water tight
        2. tautens fabric
        3. protects from deterioration
        4. reduces skin friction, less parasitic drag
      2. conditions
        1. ground aircraft
        2. need good ventilation
        3. relative humidity: 20-60%
        4. temp: 65-75F
        5. dope must be correct consistency
        6. allow surfaces to stand 4hrs before applying dope
      3. Service Finish cotton/dope steps:
        1. spray distilled water on fabric and allow to dry
          1. removes folds/creases
        2. 2 coats clear dope
          1. fungicide in first coat; very thin, brush only and allow to dry
          2. wet sand second coat (280-320) grit
          3. attach surface tape with second coat
        3. one coat clear dope
          1. brushed or sprayed and wet sanded
        4. 2 coats aluminized dope
          1. sprayed on and wet sanded after each coat
          2. once dry, use lamp to check it's opaque
          3. applied over tapes, inspection rings, reinforcements
        5. 3 coats pigmented dope (color top coats)
          1. sprayed and wet sanded
        6. min number of coats is that required for a taut, well-filled finish
          1. linen takes more than cotton to fill
      4. types
        1. cellulose nitrate
          1. nitrocellulose, plasticizer, solvents
          2. flows freely, easier to apply
          3. better adhesion, lower cost
          4. highly flammable, contains naptha
          5. less tautening
          6. approved thinner: nitrate dope and lacquer thinner
        2. cellulose acetate butyrate (CAB)
          1. cellulose acetate, plasticizer, solvents
          2. better tautening
            1. careful; excessive tautening can warp structure
          3. more fire resistant
          4. solvents penetrate dried nitrate finish
          5. approved thinner: cellulose acetate butyrate thinner
        3. non-tautening dope
          1. for fabric tautened with heat
          2. dope only seals the fabric
        4. fabric rejuvenator
          1. restores plasticizers to rejuvenate the dope (not the fabric)
      5. storage
        1. max temp 60F
        2. can go up to 4mos at 80F
        3. have a way to confine spills
        4. keep fire protection equipment nearby
        5. careful; dope goes off over time!
      6. problems
        1. blushing
          1. excessive evaporation of solvents in dope
            1. causes
              1. high temp
              2. drafts
              3. wrong thinner (eg acetone)
          2. humidity allows cellulose to come out of suspension and weaken the finish
          3. red finishes commonly turn pink with white blotches
          4. retarder can help
          5. if not too severe, corrected with Butyl alcohol, which retards evaporation
        2. roping
          1. caused by thick dope
            1. too cold
            2. improperly thinned
          2. blisters show up
          3. dope drips to opposite side of wing and congeals to cause a blister
          4. solvents trapped in underlying coats of dope
          5. excessive spraying pressure
          6. dragging brush
        3. bubbles
          1. heavy coat of lacquer applied over an un-dried dope finish
          2. allow the surface to dry 10-12hrs before painting wit hlacquer
          3. remove bubble with dope thinner, let dry, sand and refinish
          4. slack panels (fabric panels lose tension)
            1. caused by high temp on un-doped panels
            2. corrected by spraying
            3. use acetone for cold, butyl alcohol for heat
        4. pin holes
          1. may be due to
            1. surface contamination
            2. improper application
            3. too much thinner
            4. moisture in first coats
            5. high temps
            6. insufficient first coat
        5. brittleness
          1. fabric applied too tightly or old dope lost plasticizers
          2. maybe corrected by rejuvenator
        6. peeling
          1. oil or grease contamination
          2. remove contaminants using acetone
        7. runs
          1. excessive dope thinning
        8. sags
          1. too much material sprayed in one pass
        9. inconsistent coloring
          1. inadequate stirring, pigment not in suspension
        10. orange peel
          1. surface looks like an orange texture
          2. causes
            1. dope too viscous
            2. too high temp
            3. compressed air volume too low
            4. wrong thinner
            5. improperly adjusted spray gun
      7. UV protection
        1. aluminized dope
  • Repairs
    1. Deciding to repair or recover
      1. recovering is a major repair
      2. substitution of fabric is a major alteration
      3. issues that could indicate need for recover:
        1. if the damaged area is large, the remaining fabric may be near its durability limits
        2. overly tight or loose dope-based covers
        3. severely cracked dope coating or fabric with splits
          1. could also indicate that moisture got inside and airframe is compromised
    2. Fabric/finish identification for repair
      1. fabric identification
        1. linen
          1. darker than cotton
          2. same thread count as grade A cotton, but less uniform
        2. polyester
          1. white
          2. variety of thread counts
          3. monofilament; no fabric nap
        3. glass
          1. white, but may be pre-coated blue with dope
          2. 36 threads/in
          3. monofilament; no nap
      2. finish identification methods
        1. burn rate
        2. residue
        3. solvent test
    3. Major repairs
      1. repairs involving substitution of material
      2. involving area > 2 adjacent ribs
      3. replacing fabric on wings, fuselages, stabilizers, and control surfaces
    4. Natural fabric repair
      1. tear
        1. baseball stitch with #8, 4-ply cotton thread
        2. space stitching at least 1/4" apart
        3. stitches reach ~1/4" from the tear
        4. leave the dope on the fabric for sewing
        5. redo pigmented dope after sewing, cover with surface tape
      2. patch
        1. patch inserted in trimmed opening
        2. limit: <16" in any direction
        3. 1/2" hem turned under
        4. baseball stitching with knots at intervals
      3. panel
        1. damage >16"
        2. patch is rib laced to capstrips
      4. doped-in patch
        1. for planes that go <150mph with damage <16"
    5. Poly-Fiber repairs
      1. all glue repairs using Poly-Tak
      2. holes <8" need overlap of 1"
      3. holes >8" need 2" overlap with finishing tape

    • 2.4.2. Wood

    1. get notes from missed day
    2. adhesives
      1. refer to maintenance manual
      2. should conform to one of these:
        1. Military spec (MS)
        2. Aerospace Material Spec (AMS)
        3. Technical Standard Order (TSO)
      3. types
        1. casein
          1. milk protein
          2. obsolete; turns alkali
        2. synthetic resins
          1. plastic resin; obsolete, deteriorates in heat
          2. resorcinol; only adhesive approved for aircraft structures
            1. requires perfect joints, can't fill gaps
            2. 2 part mix
          3. epoxy; acceptable if permitted by manufacturer
          4. phenol-formaldehyde; non-structural
      4. assembly periods
        1. open assembly: period between application of the glue and the time when the pieces are bonded
        2. closed assembly: time after bonding while they can still be repositioned
        3. cure time is largely determined by temperature
      5. gluing guidelines
        1. moisture content should be the same for both pieces of wood
        2. room temp
        3. no grease, paint, dirt
          1. check for contamination with a drop of water
          2. check for wax from waxed paper in shipping
          3. clean with aliphatic naphtha
        4. don't sand soft woods; file or plane
        5. glue it within 8hrs of prep
        6. joints should have a thin, even film with adequate adhesion across the entire surface
      6. glue joints accept shear loads
      7. in destructive testing, the wood should fail before the joint (at least 75%)
      8. pressure methods
        1. clamps
          1. spring
          2. parallel
          3. use caul blocks to evenly distribute the pressure
        2. hydraulic press
        3. aircraft nails (sometimes with nail strip)
        4. screws
          1. for larger pieces that can't be nailed
          2. remove screws afterward and fill in the hole with putty
        5. jigs, weights
        6. staples
    3. finishing
      1. varnish purposes
        1. protect wood
        2. control moisture content
        3. aid in inspection (because it's transparent
      2. use naphtha to clean before finishing
      3. bolt holes should be sealed with varnish before fitting the bolt
      4. 2 coats interior, 3 coats exterior
      5. pay particular attention to the end grain, more susceptible to moisture
    4. inspection
      1. make sure the aircraft is dry first!
      2. external
        1. splits in fabric
        2. warping, oil canning, external condition
        3. plywood attachment to supporting structure
        4. alignment of leading and trailing edges
      3. internal
        1. wood shrinkage
        2. mechanical stress
        3. fungus, decay
      4. check glue joints with feeler gauge and magnifying glass
      5. check screws for rust; indicates water contamination
      6. check for elongated bolt holes
      7. check for compression failure with dye

    2.4.3. Plastics

    1. from Greek plastikos– to mold or form
    2. chemical sources
      1. protein: peanuts, cashews, milk, soybeans
      2. natural: shellac, pitch, amber, rosin
      3. cellulose: cellulose nitrate and butyrate
      4. synthetic: petroleum, glycerol, mica, phenol-formaldehyde
        1. thermosets**
      5. polymer which is irreversibly hardened when cured
      6. final shape is set during manufacturing
      7. many char/blister at 400-500F
      8. eg. phenolic resins, polyurethanes, epoxy and polyester resins
        1. thermoplastics**
      9. may be reshaped by heating
      10. transparent thermoplastics
        1. cellulose acetate
          1. derived from acetic acid treated cellulose
          2. replacement for nitrocellulose plastics
          3. obsolete: yellows with age, inferior strength
            1. smell of vinegar indicate deterioration
          4. cold curving
            1. min radius 70x thickness
            2. min temp 70-80F
        2. acrylic
          1. general info
            1. introduced in 1936
            2. cast and extruded manufacture
            3. monomer that can be polymerized
            4. derivative of natural gas
            5. additives change its composition for different applications
            6. brands:
              1. Plexiglass
              2. Lucite
              3. Perspex
            7. general properties:
              1. breaks in dull-edged pieces
              2. good impact resistance: 6-17x glass
              3. low water absorption
              4. good vibration resistance
              5. may stress crack as low as 1,500psi
              6. tensile strength ~10,500psi
              7. high modulus of elasticity: very stiff
              8. surface hardness similar to brass/copper
              9. non-conductive, but can build up static charge
                1. don't rub with a dry cloth
              10. 50% weight of glass, 43% weight of aluminum
              11. slow burn rate: 1.1"/min for 1/4" thick
              12. high thermal expansion; 10x steel, 3x aluminum
              13. typical heat resistance; 180F max, 200F for short periods
                1. 860F self-ignition
              14. generally becomes thicker/smaller when heat formed
                1. when extruded sheets are heated, max shrinkage in direction of extrusion
            8. sheet types
              1. monolithic sheet
                1. one sheet, usually stretched acrylic for strength
              2. laminated sheet
                1. polyvinyl buly layer for increased shatter resistance
                  1. works like plastic wrap
            9. typical usage
              1. windshield/canopies
                1. ~1/8-1/4" thick
              2. vent windows
              3. landing light covers
              4. compass mounts
              5. OAT reinforcements
              6. SCTs available for sound deadening, elimination of windshield center strip
                1. need to recalculate W+B, thicker windshield
          2. working with acrylics
            1. storage
              1. keep cool and dry, <120F
              2. no exposure to solvents or sunlight
                1. be careful removing a sunshade from windshield/canopy, lift, don't drag
              3. never chip ice off, preferably just let it melt
              4. store with masking in place, in bins, tilted to 10deg
              5. if stacked, <18" high
              6. avoid scratching, don't slide on dirty surfaces
            2. handling/cutting
              1. wear clean cotton gloves during fabrication
              2. NEVER use
                1. aromatic naptha
                2. MEK
                3. gasoline
                4. ammonia
                5. benzene
                6. chlorine
                7. alcohol
                8. acetic acid
                9. lacquer thinner
                10. strippers
              3. work on felt-covered table
              4. mark for trimming directly on the plastic with grease pencil
              5. never use a scribe for marking except for cuts
              6. keep tools sharp
              7. be careful to keep it cool; it is bad at dissipating heat
                1. drill slowly
                2. maybe use water-soluble cutting oils to help with cooling
              8. use appropriate drill bits (with zero rake angle)
              9. make sure to deburr cuts and holes
            3. forming
              1. take masking off
              2. heat
                1. oven; small parts
                2. heat gun; spot heating
                3. mineral oil bath; small patches
                4. infrared lamps; sheets
                5. heating strip; small parts, 90deg bends
              3. never use water/steam
              4. cold curving
                1. min radius 180x thickness
            4. annealing
              1. slow cooling for stress relief
              2. usually done before cementing
              3. better stability, less crazing from contraction
            5. stuck masking removal
              1. heat 1min at 250F
              2. rub masking paper with aliphatic naphtha (NOT aromatic naphtha)
              3. use Mask-Off
              4. always flush with water afterward
            6. cementing
              1. solvent softens plastic, parts fuse: cohesive bond
              2. solvent evaporates and plastics harden again
              3. methods
                1. soak
                  1. most common (according to FAA; actually no one does it)
                  2. dip one piece ~10min until soft
                  3. press against opposite side
                2. glue
                  1. thick, paste-type cement
                  2. allow cushion to form on first surface, and press on second surface
                3. direct application
                  1. paste-type cement applied and parts immediately pushed together
                  2. held in a jig until set
              4. heat treat
                1. disperses cement over larger area, to increase strength
                2. regular acrylic: 122F/48hrs
                3. heat resistant acrylic: 150F/48hrs
            7. cleaning
              1. use manufacturer's recommendation
              2. if not specified, use mild soap and fresh water
              3. clean in shade
              4. flush with fresh water first
              5. careful not to scratch, maybe use fingernail to clean off deposits
              6. dry with soft towel
              7. may use nonabrasive wax on small scratches
            8. damage
              1. crazing: small spiderweb cracks, internal and external
                1. reduces strength
                2. obscures visibility
                3. cannot be fixed
                4. causes
                  1. chemicals
                  2. age
                  3. sunlight
                  4. stress
              2. discoloration
                1. yellowing, caused by age or sun
              3. cracks/chips
                1. impact, inadequate allowance for expansion/contraction, burred holes, over-torqued fasteners
              4. scratches
                1. abrasion, poor shop practice
                2. can be repaired
                  1. scratch remover/cleaner; use sparingly
                  2. cushioned abrasive cloths, like Micro Mesh
                    1. used with water
                    2. sand at right angles to scratch
            9. installation
              1. typically use bushing/spacers between fasteners and acrylic, to allow for expansion
                1. mounting holes larger than bolt by 1/8"
              2. be careful not to over-tighten
                1. tighten to firm fit, then back off a full turn
              3. make sure it's the correct windshield
              4. file/sand rough edges
              5. use approved sealing materials
            10. repair
              1. review manual for damage classification
                1. critical vision/noncritical vision area, degree of damage
                2. damage may be too severe to be repairable
              2. repair as required by aircraft maintenance manual and return to service
                1. most repairs are temporary at best
              3. cemented patches are usually beveled
            11. inspection
              1. can sometimes measure the depth of scratches using the reflection of a ruler
              2. can use a prism to inspect under an edge
              3. thickness measurement
                1. ultrasonic measurement
                2. optical micrometer
                3. fingernail test in cracks
        3. polycarbonate
          1. 1/3 weight of acrylic, 1/6 weight of glass
          2. limited heat formability
          3. more impact-resistant than acrylic
            1. often used for canopies
          4. poor surface hardness
          5. softer and more easily scratched
            1. cannot by sanded or buffed
          6. UV may yellow material
          7. acetone will weaken the material and turn it white
          8. acrylic solvent cements will frost the surface
          9. brands:
            1. Makrolon
            2. Lexan

    2.4.4. Composites

    1. combination of two or more materials with distinctly different properties
      1. this creates a new material with characteristics unlike the originals
      2. materials remain separate and distinct with the composite
      3. useful composites are those with properties that are greater than the sum of its parts
      4. examples
        1. wood (cellulose and lignin)
        2. adobe (mud and straw)
        3. reinforced concrete (rebar and cement)
        4. fiberglass (glass and epoxy)
    2. components:
      1. reinforcements (usually fibrous)
        1. provides most of the tensile strength
        2. must be stored/cared for properly
          1. protect from contaminants
          2. clean work area
          3. sizing/finish required in manufacturing
            1. use different finish based on resin type
          4. don't use reinforcements that got wet
        3. forms:
          1. particle
            1. square, or round, piece of material
            2. eg, glass bubbles (q-cell) are hollow glass spheres
          2. whisker
            1. piece of material that is longer than it is wide
            2. usually single crystals
            3. very strong, used to reinforce ceramics and metals
          3. fiber
            1. single filaments much longer than they are wide
            2. can be made of almost any material
            3. not crystalline
            4. most common base
            5. small than the finest human hair
            6. normally woven
            7. weave styles
              1. plain
              2. satin
                1. go over several threads, under one
              3. mat
        4. types:
          1. fiberglass
            1. made by poltrusion
              1. molten glass forced through tiny platinum bushings
                1. ~5-25microns
                2. S-glass = 9microns
              2. baked at 600F to burn off oil
            2. sizing added for protection during processing to ensure matrix bonding
            3. grouped into ends, roving, and bands; and woven
              1. filaments –> strands –> yarn –> cloth
            4. types
              1. e-glass
                1. electrical, high resitivity
                2. developed as an insulator
                3. most commonly used type of fiberglass
              2. s-glass
                1. high strength
                2. stronger, stiffer, heavier than e-glass
          2. carbon fiber
            1. production
              1. pre-oxidation
                1. oxygen absorbed and hydro gen cyanide released
              2. carbonization
                1. heat in nitrogen atmosphere
                2. cyanogen and sodium cyanide are released
              3. graphitization
                1. heat to high temp to remove hydrogen, nitrogen, and oxygen
            2. properties
              1. black
              2. more expensive
              3. high modulus (stiffness)
              4. low impact resistance
              5. filament diameter: 0.0003"
              6. tensile strength 470-530KSI
              7. stronger than Kevlar in compressive strength
              8. not radar transparent; low coefficient of thermal expansion (CTE)
              9. high coefficient of friction at high temp (ablative)
              10. electrically resistive
              11. more cathodic than aluminum –> galvanic corrosion
                1. requires cres/titanium fasteners
              12. machining dust hazards
              13. resistant to fatigue compared to metal
              14. good for primary structural aircraft components and brake pads
          3. aramid (Kevlar)
            1. short for aromatic polyamide
            2. made from polymer (similar to nylon) in a dry-jet wet spinning process
            3. Kevalr 29: industrial uses, protective clothing
            4. Kevlar 49: higher modulus, mostly used in aerospace reinforcement
            5. properties:
              1. tensile strength 43000psi
              2. modulus: 19 million psi
              3. excellent impact resistance
              4. good vibration and sound damping
              5. fiber size: 0.0005"
              6. poor compressive properties
              7. lighter, stronger than e-glass or aluminum
              8. good dimensional stability, low CTE
              9. transparent to radio waves
              10. nonconductive
            6. disadvantages
              1. field repair sometimes done with fiberglass
                1. does not bond well to itself
              2. absorbs moisture; often needs sealant
              3. UV deteriorates fibers
              4. difficult to cut or drill
      2. matrix material (binding substance)
        1. matrix supports the fibers and bonds them together in the composite material
        2. transfers any applied loads to the fibers
        3. gives compressive and shear strength
        4. keeps the fibers in their position and desired orientation
        5. provides environmental resistance
        6. determines the max service temp
        7. types
          1. polyester**
        8. cheap production
        9. not petroleum resistant
        10. nonstructural
        11. low elongation rates
        12. poor load transfer
        13. synthetic resin with MEKP catalyst
          1. very dangerous
        14. generally non-structural
        15. low elongation value
        16. incompatible with petroleum products
        17. accelerator DMA: decreases reaction time
          1. do not mix with MEKP
        18. high shrinkage during cure; causes warpage
        19. toxic fumes when burned
        20. smells of styrene
          1. vinyl ester**
        21. good strength
        22. improved mechanical properties and corrosion resistance
        23. epoxy modified polyester
          1. like a good polyester mixed with a bad epoxy
        24. less moisture absorption than polyester
        25. stronger, more expensive, less shrinkage than polyester
        26. must be promoted; after that it lasts 3 months
        27. MEKP is catalyst
        28. CoNap (cobalt napthenate) is promoter
        29. DMA accelerator
      3. phenolic resin
        1. phenol-formaldehyde compounds developed in the 1900s
        2. interior aircraft structure
        3. low smoke and fire resistance
        4. used internally on aircraft for nonstructural parts
          1. epoxy**
        5. thermosetting plastics
        6. most widely used resin system
          1. dry materials
          2. prepregs and adhesives
        7. high strength
        8. appropriate modulus
        9. better elongation properties
        10. slow cure
        11. most common prepreg cure temps (250-350F)
        12. better strength and adhesion
        13. good environmental resistance
        14. curing achieved by cross-linking
        15. used as a wet resin, pre-preg, or adhesive
        16. more expensive
        17. when doing the layup, prepare a test sample with the same batch
      4. polyimides
        1. high temp use/cure (550F)
        2. usually not used in liquid form
        3. low expansion
        4. special bleeder bagging and release agents required for high temp cure
      5. polybenzimidazoles (PBI)
        1. tougher than epoxy
        2. usually high temp films
        3. adhesives for core repairs
      6. bismaleimides (BMI)
        1. similar properties to polyimides
        2. stronger than many epoxies
        3. similar processing to epoxy
  • prepreg
    1. mixed, but uncured (stage B)
    2. often vacuum-bagged with heat
    3. stages
      1. A: as mixed
      2. B: most solvent evaporated, partially cross-linked
      3. C: resin is insoluble and infusible
    4. advantages:
      1. guaranteed right ratio
      2. variety of systems available
      3. easier to cut/shape
      4. less mess and odor
      5. safer
    5. disadvantages:
      1. expensive
      2. short shelf life
        1. need to track time out of the freezer
        2. improved by kitting parts
      3. sticks to itself
      4. requires freezing and vacuum bagging
        1. needs to be supported in storage
        2. 0F
      5. thicker laminates require autoclave cure
      6. proper thawing is critical
        1. container must be sealed while it thaws
      7. sensitive to contamination from moisture, dirt, oil
        1. condensation is a contaminate
    6. curing
      1. resin consolidation and cure processes are required to ensure laminates are bonded and that matrix is capable of maintaining placement of the reinforcement
      2. during cure, the thermoset is undergoing extensive chemical and morphological change
        1. may need to gradually heat and cool
      3. some actions can be controlled directly or indirectly and some interact
      4. these actions greatly affect the properties of the cured composite
  • added materials
    1. thixiotropic materials
      1. micro-balloons
        1. control density
        2. good for filler
      2. cab-o-sil
        1. fumed silica
        2. general purpose filler
      3. flocked cotton
        1. more structural
        2. eg. repair around a fastener hole
      4. milled glass fibers
        1. structural
    2. peel ply
      1. put on surface during cure
      2. provides textured surface to bond more fiberglass on
      3. saves on sanding
      4. often polyester
    3. fasteners
      1. fewer need on composite structures
      2. CRES or titanium with carbon fiber
      3. rivets are bad
  • core materials
    1. used in sandwich constructions
    2. makes it stiffer and stronger, not much heavier
    3. balsa
      1. high strength to weight ratio
      2. compressive strength higher than most others
      3. end grain must be perpendicular to facings for best compression
      4. low shear strength
      5. subject to fungus, decay, and termites
    4. foam
      1. lower mechanical properties than honeycomb
      2. isotropically strong
      3. closed cell foams preferred
      4. inherent resiliency provides memory
      5. cut and shaped to fit by various methods
        1. polystyrene (styrofoam)
          1. closed cell, 2lb/ft3 is common
          2. hot wire cut, no poisonous gas
          3. incompatible with polyester, vinyl ester resins, gasoline, or acetone
          4. melts at ~160F
          5. used in Long-Eze, Cozy, Dragonfly
        2. polyurethane (urethane)
          1. various densities
            1. if density is <4psf, it's fragile
          2. more chemically resistant than polystyrene
          3. produces cyanide gas when burned
          4. can sand it with itself
          5. compatible with epoxy or polyester resins
        3. polyvinyl chloride (PVC)
          1. foams are cross-linked, strong
          2. may be hot wire cut
          3. less chemically resistant than urethane
        4. polymethacrylimide (Rohacell)
          1. very good mechanical properties
          2. good for high temps, over 250F
            1. will expand during cure with facings if it goes over 350F
          3. most expensive foam core
          4. only foam that can compete with honeycomb
    5. honeycomb
      1. Aluminum 5052 and cres common materials
      2. stronger and best balance of properties from hexagonal cells
      3. production methods: expansion or corrugation
      4. ribbon direction created by individual sheet stacking
        1. anisotropic– strongest in ribbon direction
      5. structure
        1. hex core: good for flat panels
        2. over-expanded core: simple curvatures
        3. flex core: compound curvatures
  • machining
    1. carbide drills work well, diamond is good, but brittle and expensive
    2. high speed
    3. don't use excessive force
    4. cut by abrasive action, not shearing or shaving
    5. drills
      1. brad point
        1. good for aramid
        2. prevents fuzzing
      2. spade point
        1. good for carbon fiber
        2. allows the dust to escape, to avoid it abrading itself
    6. water jet cutting
      1. used for cutting cured laminates
      2. not great with foams
      3. not used on sandwich constructions
      4. precise cutting, limited debris
      5. you can see tick marks in the remnant from cutting
    7. vacuum bagging
      1. purpose
        1. compacts laminate by suction
        2. controls and extracts excess resin and volatiles
        3. down for for ply consolidation and tool surface contour
          1. 29.92 inHg or 14.7psi at MSL
          2. loss of 1/2psi per 1000ft elevation
        4. very uniform pressure to complicated shapes
        5. most common method used
      2. materials
        1. vacuum bagging film: plastic cover
        2. breather: material to allow air to flow through the bagging part (often polyester)
        3. bleeder: soaks up extra resin during the bagging process (may be same as breather)
        4. peel ply: peeled away after the cure to provide a textured surface for secondary bonding
        5. release film (perforated or non-perforated): prevents laminate sticking to tool or bag
          1. perforated type transfers excess resin away from the laminate surface
        6. sealant tape: creates air-tight seal
      3. cure
        1. room temp cure
          1. chemical reaction of resin and hardener causes the resin to cure
        2. elevated temp cure
          1. used with prepreg resin systems that require higher heat for resin cross-linknig
          2. may be use with some room temp resins as a post cure to raise Tg
          3. may use autoclave or heat press
        3. hot bonder
          1. temp controlled with heat blankets
          2. programming, understanding of control; thermocouples and triggering alarms required
          3. monitoring required
            1. low pressure alarm for if vacuum bag hold blanket in place
            2. otherwise bag integrity verified every 2 min
          4. temp controlled by compraing bonder set points with set points (ramps nad holds) with thermocouples at repair airea
          5. control options
            1. thermocouple single control (not recommended)
            2. average of specified thermocouple set
          6. set up
            1. blanket should overlap by min 2" on each side of repair
            2. maybe use metal sheet contoured to fit beneath blanket for cold spots
            3. be careful with heat blankets
        4. thermocouples
          1. minimum 3 thermocouples to monitor heating cycle
          2. if bonding a pre-cured patch, place theromcouple near the center of the patch
          3. install them min .5" away from the edge of the adhesive line
          4. place flash breaker tape below and above the thermocouple
          5. don't put them under the vacuum port or near power cord
          6. always leave slack in the wire
          7. types
            1. J: iron + and constantan -
            2. K: nickel/chromium + and nickel/aluminum -
  • repair
    1. classification
      1. temporary
        1. meets strength requirements
        2. limited by time or flight cycles
        3. must be removed and replaced at end of repair life
      2. interim
        1. restores required strength to the component
        2. does not restore required durability
        3. requires different inspection and/or inspection method
      3. permanent
        1. restores the required strength and durability to the component
        2. same inspection method and interval as the original component
      4. non-structural repairs are different
        1. depends on severity of damage
        2. superficial scars/scratches
          1. simply paint on a coat of resin
        3. damage not exceeding the first lay or ply
          1. fill with a putty of resin and short glass fibers
        4. deeper damage
          1. replace plys
    2. always remember that orientation matters
      1. symmetry: even or odd number of lamina wit hmirro image at the mid-plane
      2. balance: for every lamina centered at some positive angel, there must exist an identical lamina oriented at a negative angel with the same thickness and material properties
    3. scarf patch helps give sufficient area for bonding, but not good for thick laminates
    4. bolted repair
      1. doubler bolted in place
    5. potted compound repair
      1. inject resinous-like material into a hole in core material to fill it
        1. resin mixed with fillers to make it less dense
      2. only used when the damaged section of core is small: ~1/2" dia
    6. blind rivet repair
      1. metal doubler installed over honeycomb core
      2. held in place with blind rivets
      3. need to have a sealant
  • inspection
    1. delamination
      1. separation (voids) between plies
    2. disbonding
      1. separation of core materials from facing plies
    3. possible causes
      1. hangar rash
      2. sonic vibration
      3. improper flight loads
      4. improper manufacturing technique and punctures
      5. erosion/abrasion
      6. LSP aluminum corrosion (galvanic corrosion due to carbon)
    4. visual inspection
    5. tap test
      1. hollow sound may indicate a fault
    6. x-ray
    7. ultrasound
    8. infrared thermography
    9. dye penetrant (not recommended)
  • advantages
    1. high strength to weight ratio
    2. fiber-to-fiber stress transfer through chemical bonding, matrix
      1. modulus** (stiffness/density) ratio 3.5-5x aluminum (ability to resist bending)
    3. longer life than metals (fatigue resistance)
    4. higher corrosion resistance
    5. tensile strength 4-6x steel/aluminum
    6. greater design flexibility
      1. tailored mechanical properties (materials/fiber orientation)
      2. complex double curvature parts produced in one operation
      3. good stealth properties
    7. bonded construction eliminates joints and fasteners– drag reduction
    8. easily repairable
  • disadvantages
    1. difficult to inspect, especially de-lamination detection
      1. technological advancements will correct this eventually
    2. no long term design database, relatively new technology/methods
    3. cost– materials, consumables, and shelf life
    4. expensive processing equipment
    5. no standard methodology for construction or repair
    6. many materials, processes, and techniques with time-sensitive requirements
    7. limited knowledge/expertise in the field
    8. products often toxic and hazardous
      1. dermatitis
      2. respirable fibers
      3. suspected carcinogens
    9. may require lightning strike protection (adds weight)
  • construction styles:
    1. fiber reinforced plastic
      1. glass, graphite, aramid, etc
      2. resin matrix
    2. bonded construction
      1. material assembly by adhesives (not mechanical attachment)
    3. laminate construction
      1. layered, usually by bonding
      2. examples
        1. pulleys
          1. linen with phenolic resin
        2. propellers
          1. birch strips layered with adhesives
        3. plies of glass cloth oriented for directional strength
      3. superior strength and dimensional stability
      4. more damage tolerant than sandwich construction
      5. tailored material properties
      6. selective strength properties
    4. sandwich construction
      1. core materials sandwiched between facings
      2. core materials:
        1. balsa
        2. aluminum, steel, or fiberglass honeycomb
        3. foam
      3. facing materials:
        1. fiberglass
        2. metal
      4. high strength/weight ratio
      5. greater stiffness than a sheet
      6. better able to withstand sonic vibration
      7. reduced cost– fewer fasteners, parts
  • terminology
    1. selvage edge
    2. warp
      1. most bidirectional fabrics are slightly stronger along the warp
    3. fill
    4. bias
    5. tape vs fabric
      1. tape: unidirectional
      2. fabric: woven, bidirectional
        1. isotropic**: strength in all directions
        2. anisotropic**: stronger in axial direction than in the transverse direction (eg, fibers)

    • 2.4.5. Finishing

    1. Registration marking
      1. Nationality marking
        1. assigned by ICAO
      2. Registration marking
        1. assigned by FAA
          1. FAR part 45 Identification and Registration Marking
            1. No one may operate a US registered aircraft without registration markings
            2. marking must be permanent
            3. it must be clear
            4. no ornamentation
            5. contrasting color to background
            6. exceptions for movie productions, exhibition, antique aircraft, with prior approval
            7. Size
              1. Fixed wing powered: 12” height
              2. Gliders: 3” height
              3. Experimental: 3”, or 2” if it looks like an antique
              4. Width =2/3 height
                1. Except 1, which is 1/6 height
              5. Strike thickness= 1/6 height
              6. Spacing = 1/4 width
          2. FAR part 47 Aircraft Registration
            1. Identification number rules
              1. Max 5 characters after the N
              2. Can’t use I or O
              3. Can be all numbers, up to 2 suffix letters
              4. 0 must be preceded by a different digit
    2. Painting
      1. chemical stripping
        1. corrosion/rust potential for bare metal surfaces
          1. don't touch metal with bare hands; use gloves/cloth
        2. use natural cloths to wipe it, to avoid static charge
        3. REMOVE/MASK COMPONENTS/AREAS HARMED BY STRIPPER
          1. windshield
          2. plastics
          3. composites
          4. wheel pants
          5. tires
          6. pitot tube
          7. antennas
        4. Be very aware of environmental and personal protections
        5. can cause hydrogen embrittlement in some high-strength steels
        6. types
          1. liquid solvent
            1. contains MEK
            2. for small areas
            3. evaporates fast
          2. wax-based methylene chloride
            1. Jasco stripper, aka brown death
            2. wax holds solvent against surface until paint is gone, then seeps under the film to prevent paint from re-sticking; very effective
            3. if you get it on the skin, wash it off immediately! Use running water
            4. wear eye protection!
            5. may cover with a polyethylene drop cloth to slow evaporation
            6. once it's stripped, wash/scrub thoroughly to remove stripper
              1. fayed edges, rivets, fittings may need a solvent to clean out
        7. decal removal
          1. may use a heat gun for plastic decals
          2. use aliphatic naphtha for metal decals
          3. use MEK on vinyl decals
          4. use lacquer thinner on paper decals
        8. alternatives
          1. abrasive blasting
          2. water jets
          3. Boeing flashlamp (used with dry ice)
          4. pyrolysis : burn it away
      2. corrosion removal and cleaning
        1. for aluminum, magnesium, and steel
          1. scotchbrite (maroon)
          2. aluminum wool for aluminum
          3. dichromate solution used on magnesium
          4. don't use caustic cleaners on aluminum
        2. oxydation identification
          1. iron oxide, red
          2. aluminum oxide, white
          3. magnesium oxide, white
          4. copper chloride, green
        3. acetone
          1. good for grease removal
          2. cleans uncured epoxy and polyester resin from tools
        4. tack cloth
          1. wax impregnated cloth, traps dust
          2. wipe lightly, don't rub
        5. Stoddard solvents/mineral spirits
          1. cleaned called on grease and grime
          2. leaves oily residue
        6. turpentine
          1. pine tree product
          2. thins oil-based paints and varnishes
        7. aliphatic naphtha
          1. used to clean rubber, acrylics
          2. to wipe down surfaces just before painting
        8. boiled linseed oil
          1. aka tube oil
          2. used internally on metal tubing; coating protects from corrosion
          3. very flammable, beware heat build-up when in contact with organic material
      3. paint components
        1. pigment
          1. provides the solid content
          2. color and durability
          3. may improve strength and adhesion
          4. shape affects strength
            1. spherical, rod, or plate
          5. size affects hiding ability
        2. binder (film former)
          1. eg, polyurethane, acrylic
          2. holds pigment together
          3. made of resins
          4. makes it adhere to the surface
        3. solvent
          1. transfer agent
          2. reduces binder and transfers paint spray gun
        4. additives
          1. may reflect UV radiation
          2. prevent wrinkling, blushing
          3. gloss
          4. strength
          5. speed curing
      4. Alodine
        1. conversion coating
        2. treatment used to repair an anodized aluminum finish
        3. must rinse off with water
        4. can prevent corrosion and provide surface from primer and paint
        5. iridescent, golden finish
        6. sometimes comes in pen form for scratch touch-up
        7. used with Teledyne Continental Engine cylinder heads
          1. over-temp condition indicated where alodine finish changes to yellow-green
          2. color changes at cylinder hit spots
      5. Dissimilar metal care
        1. use non-porous tapes and barriers between parts
        2. zinc chromate primer is good
        3. Don't use hygroscopic materials
      6. Paint system VOC
        1. traditional finishes
          1. low solid content
          2. high volatile organic compounds (VOC)
        2. modern finishes
          1. high solid content
          2. low VOC
          3. low molecular weight
        3. primer VOC reduced from 650g/L to 350g/ L
        4. top coat VOC reduced from 600g/L to 450g/L
      7. Primers
        1. bonds better to bare surface
        2. types
          1. wash
            1. combo primer and dilute phosphoric
            2. aluminum, magnesium, steel, or fiberglass
            3. very thin application
            4. butyl alcohol retarder added to slow evaporation when spraying
            5. add top coat 30 min-8hrs after application (longer than that and it will glaze)
              1. requires atmospheric moisture**
            6. otherwise, might cause filiform corrosion! (especially under polyurethane paint)
              1. from unconverted phosphoric acid in the wash primer
              2. high solid content in paint traps it in
            7. can supplement with distilled water in the mix
            8. ideally 57% humidity at 70F
            9. if properly hydrated, it will have a glossy, blush-free finish
            10. use hygrometer to find the dew point and calculate necessary hydration
            11. min .09lbs water per lb of air
        3. zinc chromate
          1. yellow or green tinted
          2. allows a little water to penetrate and release chromate ions, inhibiting corrosion
          3. Don't use it with acrylic nitrocellulose lacquers or a wash primer!
          4. can be applied over alodine
          5. thin mist coat application
          6. toxic, carcinogenic– Use protection!
          7. usually reduced with toluene
          8. good corrosion resistance, but not great adhesion or durability
          9. quick drying
          10. longtime industry standard
        4. red oxide
          1. commonly used on steel tubing
          2. reduced with toluene
          3. dried within~1hr, oxidizes in air
        5. grey enamel undercoat
          1. not sandable
          2. high corrosion resistance
          3. good for composites
          4. single component
        6. urethane
          1. 2 part
          2. isocyanate activator
          3. film thickness control is important for shrinkage
          4. good for composites
          5. sandable
        7. epoxy
          1. non-sandable
          2. 2 part catalyzed
          3. not hazardous
          4. safer and more chip resistant than urethane
          5. slow curing
            1. min 5hrs
          6. can be applied over wash primer
          7. paint top coat within 24hrs
        8. plastic
          1. plastic adhesion promoter
          2. used for painting plastic parts
        9. acrylic cellulose nitrate
          1. least common, obsolete
          2. doesn't agree to bare metal
            1. used after wash primer
          3. used under acrylic nitrocellulose top coats
      8. typical systems
        1. nitrocellulose lacquer
          1. solvent evaporation
          2. not very durable
          3. easily repaired
          4. layers
            1. bare metal
            2. acid etch
            3. zinc chromate primer
            4. nitrocellulose lacquer top coat
        2. acrylic lacquer
          1. solvent evaporation
          2. layers
            1. bare metal
            2. wash primer
            3. acrylic lacquer primer
            4. acrylic lacquer top coat
        3. enamels
          1. réduction (oxydation)
          2. layers
            1. bare metal
            2. acid etch
            3. primer
            4. oil-based enamel
        4. epoxy and polyurethane
          1. 2 part catalyzed chemical reaction
          2. layers
            1. bare metal
            2. acid etch
            3. wash primer
            4. polyurethane/epoxy primer
            5. polyurethane/epoxy top coat
    3. Paints
      1. nitrocellulose lacquer
