        1. far drying (evaporation)
        2. easily damaged by gasoline, solvents, scratches
        3. easily repaired, just spray on think coats
        4. thinned for spraying
        5. min 2 coats
        6. sand between coats and buff for max gloss
      2. acrylic lacquer
        1. little more durable
          1. plastic additive add durability
        2. thinned for spraying
        3. applied over wash or epoxy primers
        4. ~30min dry time between coats
      3. acrylic enamel
        1. works by oxidation
          1. exposure to air or heat
        2. reduced with proprietary enamel reducers
          1. enamels use reducers, not thinners
        3. glossy, hard surface
        4. apply by thin mist coat, then wet cross coat
        5. wait 48hrs before painting
      4. epoxy
        1. resin and hardener
        2. similar to polyurethane paints, but no isocyanates
        3. better chip resistance than polyurethane
        4. applied as light coat over primer
        5. may over-coat old wash primer
        6. requires catalyst to cure, not included
      5. polyurethane
        1. state-of-the-art
        2. 2 part resin and hardener
          1. cures by chemical reaction
        3. high gloss
        4. durable and chemically-resistant
        5. very expensive
        6. reduced flashes off quickly, but catalyzed resin remains
        7. ~60% pigment gives deep wet look
          1. slow-flowing, thick
        8. contains isocyanates, be careful!
        9. use within 4-6hrs of catalyzing
        10. call immediately: can will burst from moisture contamination
        11. usually mix 1:1 and give 15-30min induction time before applying
          1. time needed to initiate reaction
          2. generally increases in cold temp or high humidity
          3. improper induction causes blushing and poor color appearance
        12. apply tack coat, allow reduced to flash off, then apply wet cross coat
        13. don't apply too thick, it will crack
        14. finish will 'crawl' for several days
        15. wait 24hrs before putting on making tape
        16. clean equipment right away!
    4. Thinners, reducers, retarders
      1. thinners are for lacquers, like nitrocellulose and acrylic
      2. reducers are for enamels, epoxies, urethane, and polyurethane, like Alkyd, acrylic and polyurethane
      3. retarders slow evaporation or cure rate
      4. all 3 adjust viscosity for spraying
        1. viscosimeter measures time for fluid to flow through orifice by gravity until first break in liquid stream
        2. viscosity is important when spray painting
    5. Other compounds
      1. bituminous paint
        1. acid proof paint
        2. used inside battery boxes and on aluminum float hulls
        3. looks like tar
        4. neutralizing agents
          1. lead acid battery: baking soda
          2. nickel/cadmium battery: boric acid
      2. wing walk compound
        1. can spoil lift, use sparingly
    6. Equipment
      1. paint booth
        1. clean
        2. well lit
        3. ventilated
      2. compressor
        1. should give at least 90psi and 10cfm air volume
        2. high volume/low pressure
          1. portable turbine or direct hook-up types
          2. turbine working pressure output ~5psi
          3. most critical operating characteristic is constant volume of air!
      3. electrostatic painting
        1. voltage difference between material and work surface
        2. surface must support a static charge
        3. material sometime coats backside of surface sprayed
        4. most efficient, but not common
      4. airless spray
        1. low pressure air compressor drive reciprocating airless material pump
        2. adaptable to a wide range of material viscosities
        3. used in high volume contruction and production work, not common on aircraft
        4. finish quality not as good
        5. material pressure range 500-3000psi
        6. material breaks down at orifice nozel into droplets
        7. slow drying solvent (thinners and reducters) usually required
        8. be careful! can inject paint into your skin and poison yourself
      5. spray gun set up
        1. purge line of moisture at air transformer
        2. fill cup with correct viscosity of material and secure to gun
        3. set pressure at regulator of transformer
      6. pattern can be adjusted at material knobs
    7. Problems
      1. poor adhesion
        1. bad prep
        2. wrong primer
      2. blushing
        1. moisture
        2. wrong temp or humidity
      3. pinholes
        1. trapped solvents, air, or moisture
        2. material contamination
      4. runs and sags
        1. too much applied in one coat
        2. too thin paint
      5. orange peel
        1. paint too thick
        2. not uniformly mixed
        3. too little time between coats
      6. fisheye
        1. improper surface cleaning
      7. sanding scratches
        1. mostly on composites
      8. wrinkling
        1. trapped solvents
        2. uneven drying or cure
      9. spray dust
        1. standing too far back
        2. rough finish

    2.4.6. Interiors

    1. Aircraft restraints
      1. Rules
        1. FAR 23.785: seats, berths, litters, safety belts, shoulder harnesses
        2. FAR 91.107 Use of safety belts, shoulder harnesses, and child restraints
          1. Mostly rules for pilots
          2. Pilot must make sure everyone has a seat with a harness and is strapped in
            1. Exceptions for babies and parachuters
        3. FAR 91.521 Shoulder harnesses
          1. TSO C22 **Safety belt standards
      2. Configurations for harnesses
        1. Must be attached to primary structure
          1. usually with doublers
          2. Tubular steel airframe attachments
            1. welded gusset
            2. nicopress sleeve
            3. clamp
        2. Mounting
          1. Seat mounted
          2. Airframe mounted
            1. Side
            2. Ceiling
            3. Floor
            4. Directly rearward
        3. Types
          1. Single diagonal harness
            1. best for longitudinal movement
          2. Double over shoulder harness
            1. works well for longitudinal and lateral movement
          3. inertia reel: belt locks with sharp movement
      3. Replacement of seatbelt is preventative maintenance
    2. Fire resistance certification
      1. normal, utility, and acrobatic category aircraft FAR 23.853
        1. materials must be at least flame resistant
          1. adjacent to firewall: self-extinguishing, must pass test in FAR 23 Appendix F
          2. further: if firewall burns at 2000°F for 15 minutes, they must not ignite

    2.5. Assembly & Rigging

    2.5.1. Background info

    1. Parts of Airplane
      1. cockpit (flight deck)
      2. fuselage
      3. wing
      4. empennage
      5. stabilizers
      6. engine
      7. Flight control surfaces
        1. aileron
        2. rudder
        3. elevator
        4. flap
          1. plain
          2. split
          3. fowler
          4. slotted
        5. spoiler
        6. leading edge slots/slots
        7. speed brake
        8. trim tab
    2. Atmosphere
      1. pressure
      2. density
      3. humidity
    3. Newton's Second Law
      1. F=ma
    4. Bernoulli's Principle

    2.5.2. Aircraft jacking

    1. Don’t be stupid
    2. Follow instructions in the manual

    2.5.3. Small airplane assembly

    1. level the fuselage, see manual
    2. install wings/struts; torque attachment bolts correctly
    3. wing alignment
      1. angle of incidence
        1. cams, eccentric bushings, shims can adjust this slightly
      2. dihedral or anhedral
        1. use dihedral board that has specific taper or a dihedral sling
        2. some aircraft use taut string stretched between wingtips along front spar
          1. determine correct angle by measuring distance between string and wing root fitting
      3. wash in/out
        1. wash in: OB leading tips up
        2. wash out: OB leading tips down
      4. cantilever wings need very little adjustment
      5. strut-braced wings using V-struts have provisions for adjusting everything that way
    4. recheck the fuselage for level
    5. adjust the fittings in the end of the front struts to get correct dihedral
    6. adjust fitting in rear struts for wash in/out
    7. attach and torque horizontal/vertical stabilizers
    8. attach moveable control surfaces
      1. must be free to pivot
      2. no excess looseness
      3. adjust to correct cable tension
      4. check for full travel
    9. attach control actuating mechanisms
    10. Most aircraft have pretty limited adjustability, there's only so much you can do
    11. Rigging specs
      1. refer to the TCDS
        1. inspection programs must include all checks that are needed to determine that the aircraft meets TCDS rigging specs

    2.5.4. Control Systems

    1. cable
      1. types
        1. non flexible
          1. only for straight runs with no pulleys
        2. flexible
        3. extra flexible
          1. eg. 7x19
        4. available in stainless steel and carbon steel
          1. stainless is slightly weaker
        5. smallest cable for primary flight controls: 1/8"
      2. nicopress sleeves
        1. copper sleeves may be compressed onto cable
        2. check with go/no go gauge
        3. may be used to splice cables
          1. 2 sleeves: equal to full original strength
      3. swaged terminals
        1. use swaging tool
        2. check with gauge
        3. several stages to swaging
      4. Proof load test
        1. after cable is complete, load it to 60% of breaking strength
        2. load applied gradually, held for 3 min
        3. mark cable with paint
      5. inspection
        1. wipe full length of cable
        2. wear gloves
        3. move controls and check around pulleys
          1. if there might be corrosion, disconnect cable to check
        4. most cable systems are closed loop
          1. except rudder and trim systems
        5. some trim systems use a return spring
        6. max fairlead deflection: 3deg
          1. like a bushing for a cable to pass through a wall
          2. can be a seal where control cable exits pressurized cabin
          3. pressure seals filled with grease
      6. pulleys
        1. must be carefully aligned and free to rotate
        2. must have guards to keep cable from jumping
        3. good practice to rotate a cable at inspection to distribute wear
        4. check pulley for wear and damage
        5. min 2" distance from turnbuckles
      7. travel adjustment and cable tension
        1. TCDS specify required control surface deflection
        2. maintenance manual gives correct procedures
        3. may use rigging pins to lock system in place
        4. check cable tension with tensiometer
          1. use correct riser for cable size
        5. some large aircraft have automatic tension adjusters to account for distortion with temp, altitude
        6. cable temp should be accounted for when adjusting tension on long runs
          1. check the charts for cable type, thickness, length
        7. springback
          1. controls have a stop on the surface
          2. springback slows the control surface before it overextends the cables
    2. push/pull rods
      1. very common
      2. bell cranks change the direction of travel
      3. only small amount of wear allowed in helicopters
      4. torque tubes apply torsional force

    2.5.5. Biplane Rigging

    1. basically no different
    2. most biplanes have struts between the wings and truss wires
    3. commonly have 2 lower panels attached to the fuselage and a 'flying' center section on top– root of upper wing panels
    4. terminology
      1. stagger
        1. upper wing placed ahead/behind lower wing (positive/negative stagger)
      2. decalage
        1. difference in angle of incidence of the wings
        2. positive: upper wing has greater AOI
        3. negative: lower wing has greater AOI
      3. center section
        1. if upper wing is mounted above the fuselage, this is where it attaches
        2. true to fuselage laterally and longitudinally, no twist
      4. cabane struts
        1. struts between center section and fuselage
      5. interplane struts
        1. struts between wings
    5. follow proper procedures for assembly

    2.5.6. Landing Gear and Wheel Alignment

    1. critical, esp. for tailwheels
    2. aircraft should be level and wheels on grease plates
    3. do it somewhere away from wind, etc
    4. check camber (tire tilt) and toe
      1. positive camber: tire leans out
      2. negative camber: tire leans in
    5. terminology
      1. castor
      2. steering inclination
        1. angle between angle of pivot axis and vertical tire
      3. trail or offset
    6. approach tires from a corner angle

    2.5.7. Helicopters

    1. Autogyro: rotary aircraft with freewheeling main rotor, thrust from prop
    2. Helicopter: main rotor is driven
    3. Components
      1. hull, fuselage
      2. engine
      3. clutch
      4. transmission
      5. main rotor
      6. tail rotor
      7. control systems
    4. Cyclic, collective, pedals
    5. Swashplate
    6. Precession

    2.5.8. Airworthiness

    1. Pilot is ultimately responsible
    2. ELT
      1. On 121.5 MHz, VHF 243 MHz
      2. All planes need one
      3. Now monitored by satellites
        1. GPS being introduced
      4. Satellites
        1. COSPAS
        2. SARSET
        3. some low Earth orbit, some geostationary
      5. Must be registered

    2.6. Hydraulics

    2.6.1. Hydraulic Theory

    1. hydraulics: a science that explains the behavior of liquids
      1. aircraft usage: science that explains the use of confined liquids used to transmit force
      2. etymology
        1. hydro: water
        2. aulus: pipe/tube
    2. random aircraft dangers
      1. turbine plane of rotation
        1. marked with red stripe
      2. turbine intake area
        1. 25 ft
      3. turbine exhaust area
        1. 100ft at idle
        2. 200-350ft at full power
      4. loud noise
        1. 110-130dB; can cause permanent hearing damage
      5. radar exposure
        1. possible eye damage, sterility, cancer
      6. power actuated equipment
        1. electrical, hydraulic, or pneumatic
        2. capable of huge force
        3. damage, injury, or death
      7. 'no step' areas
      8. servicing areas/bays
        1. FOD hazard
        2. keep clean, free of debris
        3. always perform a tool count
    3. servicing reminders
      1. power off unless required by maintenance instructions
      2. ground aircraft, don't smoke w/i 50ft
      3. only use approved cleaning solvents
      4. be careful with compressed air or nitrogen
      5. know locations of fire extinguishers
      6. wear clean clothing, free of contaminants
    4. advantages of hydraulic systems
      1. lighter weight than mechanical systems
      2. easy to install
        1. flexible hose
        2. confined areas, long distance remote control and actuation
      3. simple to inspect
        1. fewer components
        2. just look for leaks
      4. usually self-lubricating and cooling
      5. minimum maintenance requirements
        1. service reservoirs, accumulators, filters
        2. sample fluid
      6. almost 100% efficient
        1. negligible energy loss due to friction
      7. instantaneous motor start, stop, or reverse
    5. threats to hydraulic systems
      1. loss of pressure
        1. leaks (internal or external)
        2. pump failure
      2. fluid contamination
      3. system overheating
        1. can cause fluid to solidify, build up sludge
    6. uses of hydraulic systems
      1. primary flight controls
        1. ailerons, elevators, and rudders
      2. secondary flight controls
        1. flaps, slats, spoilers, speed brakes
      3. braking systems
        1. independent and dependent brakes
        2. emergency brakes
        3. thrust reversers
      4. autopilot servos/yaw damper
      5. starting systems
        1. on-board hydraulic motor/accumulator and/or ground hydraulic carts
      6. utility systems
        1. cargo doors/ramps
        2. turbo-prop propellor and helicopter rotor brakes
      7. specialized systems
        1. windshield wipers
        2. wing fold
        3. lock positioning
        4. radar antenna
        5. aerial refueling probe extension
    7. Hydraulic horsepower
      1. (psi*gpm)/1714=hp

    2.6.2. Hydraulic Fluids

    1. primary purpose: transmit/distribute force
    2. secondary purposes
      1. cooling
      2. lubrication
      3. cleaning (carry contamination to filters)
    3. desirable properties
      1. fire resistant
        1. high flash point
          1. min temp @ which vapors could ignite
        2. high fire point
          1. min temp @ which combustion could be sustained
      2. chemical stability
        1. anti-corrosive
        2. anti-oxidation
        3. anti-erosive
        4. shear stable
      3. lubricity
      4. min viscosity change with temp
        1. low (stable) viscosity
        2. low freezing point
      5. acceptable expansion/contraction with temp
      6. acceptable seal compatibility
      7. low density
      8. super-cleanliness
    4. Fluid properties
      1. Pressure
        1. pressure = density*height
        2. force over an area
        3. Pascal=Newton/meter2
        4. measured with manometer
          1. Hydrostatic Paradox**: pressure exerted by a column of liquid is determined only by the height of the column
        5. it is independent of the volume of the column
    5. Temperature
      1. heat (BTUs, calories) and temp (C, F) are different
    6. Density
      1. weight/volume
        1. water is 62.4lbs/ft3
      2. affected by temp
        1. water is at max density at 4C
      3. specific gravity
        1. ratio of densities
        2. density of a substance/the density of water
        3. for gasses, we use air as the reference
    7. Buoyancy
      1. object displaces its volume worth of fluid when submerged
      2. the weight of the displaced fluid equals the buoyant forces
    8. liquids
      1. have a definite volume
      2. assumes the shape of container
      3. practically incompressible
      4. readily transmit pressure
    9. gasses
      1. no definite volume
      2. assume shape of container
      3. readily transmits pressure
      4. lighter than liquid
      5. compressible
    10. Pascal's Law
      1. pressure in an enclosed container is transmitted equally and undiminished to all parts of the container and acts at right angles to the walls that enclose it
    11. General Gas Law: \[P_1 V_1 T_2 = P_2 V_2 T_2\]
      1. Boyle's Law
        1. when temp is constant
        2. volume is inverse to pressure
      2. Guy-Lussac's Law
        1. when volume is constant
        2. temperature is directly related to pressure
      3. Charles' Law
        1. when pressure is constant
        2. volume is directly related to pressure
    12. viscosity
      1. internal resistance to fluid flow
      2. free-flowing liquids have low viscosity (water)
      3. slow-flowing liquids have high viscosity (tar)
      4. viscosity usually increases at low temps
  • choosing fluid
    1. consult current aircraft maintenance manual for type/quantity of fluid
    2. follow manufacturer's recommendation on brand, type, compatibility, and interchangeability
  • fluid bases
    1. color-coded to help with identification
    2. vegetable
    3. blue
      1. MIL-H-7644
      2. castor oil and alcohol
      3. flushing agent: same fluid, clean and new
      4. cleaning agent: denatured alcohol
      5. compatible seals: natural rubber
      6. not used anymore
        1. highly flammable
        2. chemically unstable
        3. susceptible to dirt/contamination
    4. mineral (petroleum)
    5. red
      1. MIL-H-5606
      2. petroleum (kerosene) distillate with additives
      3. flushing agent: naphtha or Varsol, or same fluid
      4. cleaning agent: Stoddard solvent
      5. compatible seals: Nitrile (Buna-N) or neoprene
      6. has a TCP additive for anti-wear, which can cause paralysis if it gets under your skin
      7. flammable under normal conditions
        1. flash point 82C
        2. fire point 95C
    6. synthetic
      1. phosphate ester
      2. light purple
        1. Skydrol, LD-4, Type 5, HyJet 5, etc
        2. SAE AS1241
        3. phosphate ester with additives
        4. flushing agent: same fluid, clean and new
        5. cleaning agent: SkyKleen, non-halogenated cleaning solvents (no chlorine!)
        6. compatible seals: ethylene propylene (EPR) or butyl rubber
        7. types IV and V most common
        8. Class 1: low density
        9. Class2: standard density
        10. fire resistant
        11. hygroscopic (turns acidic when exposed to moisture)
      3. polyalphaolefin (synthetic hydrocarbon)
      4. red
        1. MIL-PRF-83282D, MIL-PRF-87257
        2. flushing/cleaning agent: see service manual
        3. compatible seals: check manual
        4. military fluid, mostly compatible with 5606 systems
        5. wide operational temp range
        6. fire resistant
    7. magnetorheological (MR) fluid
      1. ferrous elements suspended in the fluid
      2. magnetic field changes behavior from flee-flowing liquid to a semi-solid
    8. other (please ignore)
      1. MIL-H-6083
        1. mostly a preservative fluid
        2. petroleum-based, red
        3. MIL-27601B
          1. colorless
          2. polyalphaolefin

    • 2.6.3. Hydraulic seals

    1. prevent leaking (internal/external), air entrance
    2. nomenclature defined by cross-section
    3. hardware
      1. seals
        1. gaskets
          1. static seals; no relative motion
          2. can be rubber, metal, etc
          3. metal gaskets
            1. crush washers
              1. unbroken surface installed against flange
        2. packings
          1. dynamic seals; relative motion
          2. can be uni-directional or bi-directional
        3. shapes
          1. o-rings are most common seal
            1. sizing
              1. sized by width and ID
              2. standard size (-001-475)
              3. boss size (-901+)
          2. t-ring
            1. comes with built-in back-up rings
            2. piston-type
            3. rod-type
          3. quad-ring (x-ring)
          4. v-ring (chevron) seal
            1. pressure should face open end
            2. there are adapters for 2-way pressure
              1. use with adjustment gland nuts
          5. u-cup
          6. d-ring
            1. o-ring that won't roll
      2. anti-extrusion (back-up) rings
        1. leather (obsolete)
        2. teflon, nylon
        3. scarf joints should fit together after installation
        4. prevents seal from being extruded
        5. generally needed for application >1500psi
      3. wipers and scrapers
        1. felt (spreads fluid along the shaft)
        2. lip to scrape off debris, prevent contamination
    4. standard practice
      1. store seals properly
      2. avoid contamination
      3. only install new seals and backup rings
      4. use only specified seal, and note cure date
      5. don't damage gland surface when removing
      6. immediately discard/destroy old seals
      7. inspect seals before installation
      8. lube seals before installation with same fluid as in the system
      9. use shims to protect seal during installation

    2.6.4. Hydraulic systems

    1. system components
      1. Basic components
        1. reservoir
          1. functions
            1. supplies normal operating needs of system
            2. additional fluid lost to normal leakage
            3. may supply emergency fluid (eg. standpipe)
            4. purges system of air
            5. allows foreign particles to settle out
            6. heat exchanger
            7. expansion space for fluid
            8. allows for different liquid volume based on actuator position
          2. components
            1. tank
            2. internal baffles and fins
            3. de-aeration plate (sort of colander at return)
            4. outlet/return port
            5. integral filter (with bypass valve)
            6. standpipe, staggered ports, or partitioned divider
            7. depressurization valve
            8. bootstrap reservoir pressure port
            9. bootstrap reservoir air bleed/relief valve
            10. hand service port, scupper, finger strainer
            11. pressure fill port, sampling valve
            12. fluid temp indication switch/transmitter
            13. fluid quantity indicator
              1. dipstick
              2. sight gauge
              3. liquidometer (float, mechanical indication)
              4. variable resistor float
              5. capacitance probe
              6. linear variable displacement transformer (LVDT)
              7. synchro (selsyn, autosyn, magnesyn)
              8. piston indicator (in bootstrap reservoirs)
            14. vent, vent filter check valve
            15. drain
          3. pressurization
            1. purpose
              1. prevents pump cavitation
                1. vapor bubbles collapse, implosion
                2. damaging to equipment
              2. also allows reservoir to be lower
            2. methods
              1. regulated bleed air
              2. hydraulic bootstrap (floating piston)
              3. venturi
                1. T
                2. jet pump
                3. aspirator
              4. reservoir vent plumbed to pressurized cabin
            3. alternatives to pressurization
              1. auxiliary electric pump before main pump
              2. power pump impeller (booster pump)
          4. factors influencing level
            1. actuator position
            2. fluid temp
            3. accumulator condition
              1. system pressure present or dumped
              2. flat accumulator (loss of gas charge)
          5. servicing
            1. before servicing
              1. accumulator dumped (no hydraulic system pressure)
              2. landing gear down, speed brakes closed
              3. check accumulator precharge
              4. (for hand fill) release reservoir pressure at dump valve
            2. before engine start
              1. pressurize with air to ~70psi to substitute for bleed air
        2. pump
          1. sources of power
            1. engine (EDP)
              1. requires system relief valve
            2. electric motor (ACMP)
              1. usually controlled by a pressure switch
                1. eg. Bourdon tube, bellows, piston type
            3. bleed air (ADP)
            4. ram air (RAT)
              1. usually for emergencies
              2. a windmill pops out of the plane to power the pump
            5. hydraulic pressure (PTU)
              1. on large planes with multiple separate hydraulic systems
              2. no direct transfer of fluid, but can repressurize a circuit
              3. makes 'barking dog' sound
            6. hand pump (see notes from Reka?)
          2. constant displacement pumps
            1. a pump's purpose is to create flow
            2. delivers the same volume of fluid per revolution of the pump
            3. uniform rate of flow
            4. pump rating
              1. volumetric output at a given pressure (back pressure against the pump)
            5. pumps have positive displacement
            6. system requirements:
              1. unloading device (valve, switch)
              2. emergency relief valve
            7. types:
              1. vane
                1. low pressure (25psi), high volume
                2. good for pneumatic systems
                3. often used for gyros
                4. eccentric, with adjustable-length vanes
                5. suction regulator bleeds ambient air into the line to control the speed of the gyros
                6. usually engine-driven
                7. use swept fittings to reduce turbulence in the lines
                8. wet pumps: lubricated/sealed with engine oil
                  1. wet pumps need an air/oil separator
                  2. splined coupling with spring connects to engine, instead of shear shaft
                  3. regulation required
                  4. most are bi-directional
                  5. check lubrication before final installation
                  6. very long service life
                9. carbon vane pumps are dry
                  1. carbon wears to lubricate the walls
                  2. generally uni-directional; canted vanes
                  3. regulation required
                  4. last ~300-400hrs; fails suddenly, no warning
                  5. delicate, easy to break
                10. vane pump troubleshooting
                  1. dry pump overheats
                    1. dirty filter
                    2. kinked/blocked hoses
                    3. mis-adjusted regulator
                  2. broken vanes or rotor
                    1. pump dropped
                    2. teflon tape used on fittings
                    3. turned prop backwards
                    4. engine cleaning solvent got into pump housing
                    5. case was clamped in vise
                    6. poor pump overhaul, worn case cavity
                  3. too much engine oil in wet pump
                    1. leaky pump mounting flange gasket
              2. external gear
                1. medium pressure (1500psi) and volume
                2. sometimes coupled with electric motors
                3. two gear meshed together
                4. often has a shear shaft for in case it gets stuck
                5. not good with abrasive/thick fluids
                6. features:
                  1. case and cover– end or side ports
                  2. drive and driven gear
                    1. spur
                    2. helical
                    3. herringbone
                  3. bushings
                    1. usually bronze, lubed with hydraulic fluid
                    2. reduces friction on the shaft
                  4. pressure leading port/check valve or internal spring
                  5. case pressure port/check valve
                    1. to flood the port with slight positive pressure
                    2. prevent air from being drawn into the pump
                  6. shear shaft/pin
                    1. tongue drive on older pumps
                  7. seepage drain ports
                    1. mounting flange may have seepage drain to check for poor shaft seal
              3. gerotor
                1. medium pressure (1500psi) and volume
                2. kind of internal gear pump
                3. drive gear mounted inside driven gear
                  1. driven gear has 1 more lobe than the drive gear
                4. lobes capture fluid in pockets inside the pump
                5. common for engine oil pumps; good for more viscous liquids
              4. piston
                1. high pressure (3000psi), low volume
                2. very popular in hydraulic systems
                3. high power to weight ratio
                4. ~90% efficiency
                5. has to turn at very high rpm; ~3750 is typical (1.5x crankshaft)
                6. low inertia, low vibration
                7. styles of piston pump
                  1. axial/cam type
                    1. rotating cam pushes the pistons to different positions
                    2. springs push the pistons back into place, cam follower
                  2. bent axis (Vickers)
                    1. uses a cylinder block and connecting rods instead of cam
                    2. the angle achieves the sequencing/reciprocating of pistons, like the cam
                    3. max stroke angle 30deg to minimize load
                    4. universal link drives cylinder block
                8. ways to increase displacement
                  1. larger pistons
                  2. more pistons
                  3. longer stroke (greater angle)
                9. case pressure controlled by foot valve
                10. caged ball bearing drive– radial and thrust bearing
                11. odd number of pistons to minimize pulsation
                12. case is flooded with fluid
                13. tight internal clearances resist leaks
                14. might also have shear shaft, seepage drains
              5. peristaltic pump
                1. good for biohazards or fluids that need to be isolated
                2. fluid stays in a tube and is squeezed through it
          3. variable displacement pumps
            1. engine, electrical, hydraulic motor, or air driven
              1. advantages of motor-driven pumps
                1. ease of installation
                2. constant speed
                3. can be put anywhere
                4. not dependent on engine operation
                5. centrifugal-type boost pump to minimize cavitation
              2. disadvantages
                1. weight and size
            2. may have multiple stages to help prevent cavitation
              1. eg, impeller at the intake
            3. quick disconnect fitting often provided
            4. ports
              1. dischare
              2. suction
              3. case drain
                1. routed back to the reservoir
                2. internal path for fluid to circulate for cooling
                3. case drain filter can be used for troubleshooting, look for:
                  1. metal debris
                  2. flow
                  3. temp
                  4. condition
            5. valves
              1. possible integral bypass relief valve
              2. depressurization valve (outlet)
                1. to shut off flow
                2. eg, to stem a leak
              3. fire valve (inlet)
                1. can starve the pump of hydraulic fluid
                2. safety feature in case of fire
              4. parallel pump discharge check valves
                1. if there are multiple pumps in the circuit
                2. to prevent a broken pump from turning into a motor
            6. fast response to system demand
              1. unloading valve not required
              2. accumulator not required for pressure surges (but may be installed for other reasons)
            7. principles of operation
              1. demand (effective stroke control)
                1. made by Stratopower
                2. axial piston pump with flow control
                3. pressure compensator (valve inside pump) controls discharge
                  1. modulates flow to control pressure
                    1. responds to system pressure**
                  2. compensator adjustment
                    1. make adjustments with the pump running in the zero flow condition
                4. sections of the pump
                  1. mechanical drive
                    1. nutating plate: holds pistons in position
                  2. fluid displacement
                    1. hollow pistons in sleeves
                  3. pressure control
                    1. pressure control done with compensator spring and 'spider'
                      1. the pistons have holes in them, which can be closed with sleeves
                      2. the position of the sleeves is controlled by the adjusting screw
                  4. bypass
                    1. bypass port acts as case drain
              2. stroke reduction
                1. piston type pumps may have a swashplate to vary output
                2. changes the angle of the cam –> adjusts length of stroke
                3. controlled by a compensator, similar to demand type
                  1. piston exposed to system pressure opposes a spring with adjustment screw
              3. intake starvation
                1. very uncommon
                2. similar to demand pump, but no sleeves
                3. compensator can limit flow from reservoir
            8. performance ratings
              1. based on ability to withstand back pressure
              2. engine driven: rated in gal/min at given rpm
              3. electrical: rate in gal/min at given pressure
          4. centrifugal boost pump
            1. variable displacement
            2. prevent cavitation of the main pump
          5. pump troubleshooting
            1. pressure gauge fluctuates when hydraulic pump is operating
              1. inadequate supply of hydraulic fluid
            2. higher than normal pump temp
              1. excessive internal pump leakage
            3. hydraulic pump chatters during operation
              1. air leak at pump inlet line
            4. hydraulic fluid flowing from seepage drain port
              1. worn pump shaft seal
            5. broken shear shaft
              1. seized pump
              2. unloading valve failure
        3. selector valve
          1. aka directional control valve
          2. types:
            1. hand-operated
              1. three-port selector valve
                1. 1 in from pump, 1 return, 1 to actuator
                2. used to control single-acting actuator
                  1. fluid only moves it in one direction
                  2. has a return spring or some other source of force in the other direction
              2. four-port selector valve
                1. used to control double-acting actuator
                  1. fluid moves it in both directions
                2. port A goes to flat side of piston, port B goes to rod side
                3. 1 in from pump, 1 return, 1 to each side of the actuator
                4. different styles
                  1. globe type
                  2. poppet type
                    1. cam shaft in valve body
                    2. cam lobes bear against poppets
                  3. slide type
                    1. move slide back and forth
                    2. gaskets separate sections of the slide
                    3. can't take as much pressure
                  4. rotary poppet
            2. solenoid operated
              1. relay moves a spool in the selector valve
              2. can be remotely operated
            3. servo selector valve
              1. for cases where precision is necessary
              2. can make actuator stop and hold at a particular position
              3. reed controls actuator
                1. 2 solenoid windings control position of reed
                2. hydraulic fluid is squirted through nozzles at a reed
                3. reed is deflected and can block one nozzle, moving actuator
                4. called a torque motor
        4. actuator
          1. convert fluid pressure to mechanical force
          2. types
            1. linear
              1. double acting
                1. balanced
                2. unbalanced
                  1. different surface area on different sides of the piston
              2. single adding
                1. usually hydraulic force is opposed by a spring or gravity
            2. rotary (motor)
            3. some are cushioned
              1. outflow fluid slowed by restrictor with metering pin
              2. may also have a spring
            4. ball-lock actuator; actuator locked in place, eg, for a landing gear down lock
          3. powered flight controls
            1. historical development
              1. unpowered
              2. boosted
                1. cockpit control moves pilot valve and control surface
                2. no manual reversion required
                3. reversible
              3. 3000psi hydraulics
              4. autopilots
              5. fully powered (with/without reversion in case of failure)
                1. cockpit control only moves pilot valve
                2. q feel or spring feel needed
                  1. actuator that opposes movement of flight control
                  2. faster the aircraft, the stronger the resistance
                  3. helps pilots sense what they're doing
                  4. before, they used springs, bungees
                3. manual reversion/bypass valve or hydraulic redundancy reqired
                4. irreversible
              6. fly by wire
              7. digital FBW
              8. 5000psi hydraulics
              9. power by wire
      2. Other components
        1. filter
          1. filtration ratings
            1. fineness
              1. absolute
                1. largest particle that will pass through the filter
              2. mean
                1. average size of openings
              3. nominal
                1. arbitrary micrometer value
            2. pressure
              1. flow rating vs pressure
                1. gpm drop at specified pressure
              2. differential pressure
              3. collapse pressure
                1. max pressure it can withstand without collapsing
          2. locations
            1. pressure manifold (after pump)
            2. return manifold
            3. case drain in the pump
            4. integral
              1. reservoir
              2. power control unit (PCU) or servo valve last chance
          3. contamination
            1. sources
              1. adverse conditions
              2. component wear
              3. poor maintenance practice
            2. types
              1. organic
              2. metallic and non-metallic solids
              3. foreign fluids
              4. gasses
              5. water
        2. accumulator (see notes from Reka?)
        3. valves
          1. pressure regulator (unloading valve)
            1. unloads the pump
            2. Not needed with variable displacement pumps
            3. could just use relief valve, but that's not great, pump is never unloaded
              1. or, relief valve + power control valve
                1. power control valve needs pilot to divert flow from pump to circuit
                2. poppet pops back out once pressure rises high enough
                3. helps to unload pump, but still not great
                4. generally paired with surge cylinder to dampen surges
            4. Keeps system pressure between 2 limits
              1. Kick in and kick out
              2. Usually ~20% difference between kick in and kick out pressures
            5. Usually works by internally opposing forces
            6. accumulator hold it in kicked out position
            7. adjusted with pressure adjusting screws or shims
            8. has drain to prevent hydrostatic lock
              1. internal or external types
            9. return to service after overhaul is a legal gray area (depends on whether it's 'complex', undefined term)
            10. balanced-pressure type
              1. uses the difference between two surface areas to balance the forces within a range
              2. Vickers, Bendix
            11. signs of failure
              1. slightly higher system pressures
              2. buzzing or chattering relief valve
              3. higher than normal system temp
              4. discolored (oxidized) fluid
            12. troubleshooting
              1. early kick in
                1. leaky check valve
              2. late kick out
                1. leaky bypass spool
              3. chattering check valve
                1. low reservoir level
              4. unloading valve cycles too frequently
                1. low or no accumulator pre-charge
              5. accumulator does not charge with fluid (0 pressure)
                1. broken or missing pilot valve spring
                2. relief valve set too low
              6. will not kick in or kick out
                1. spools or plungers stuck in their bores
          2. relief valve
            1. purposes
              1. emergency safely for failed unloading valve
              2. overload protection
              3. limits output force of hydraulic actuators
            2. installation requires port to pressure and return
            3. does not unload the pump; throttled operation
            4. operating positions:
              1. cracking
                1. adjusted to open at cracking pressure
                2. cracks at ~20% of kick out pressure
                3. this is what the adjustment changes
                4. can be affected by back pressure
              2. full flow
              3. reseat
            5. back pressure minimized by
              1. balanced relief valve design
                1. rod built on top of ball
              2. external drain
              3. return manifold check valves
            6. some are designed to reduce rapid opening and closing pressure
            7. full flow and reseat pressure are typically 10% above and below cracking pressure
              1. thermal relief valves**
            8. for actuators or other elements that are isolated from the system relief valve, in case of thermal expansion in the lines
            9. works like a normal relief valve
            10. cracks at ~40% of kick out pressure
          3. flap overload valve
            1. prevent pilot from lowering the flap at too high an airspeed
            2. diverts hydraulic flow from flap down line back to reservoir
            3. it is sensitive to back pressure
        4. check valves
          1. very simple, 1-way device
          2. different types
            1. ball type
            2. cone type
            3. poppet type
            4. swing type
          3. generally has a spring to oppose flow
          4. orifice check valve
            1. full flow 1 way, restricted flow the other
            2. may have a needle to adjust orifice
            3. can be used to slow actuator movement
          5. metering check valve
            1. like an orifice check valve, to meter fluid
            2. may be used in landing gear or flap systems
            3. a metering pin unseats the ball
          6. bypass check valve
            1. aka manually operated check valve ( MOCV)
            2. used to divert flow away from an accumulator
              1. eg, when using the hand pump
        5. ratchet valve
          1. locks actuator in fixed position by trapping fluid in the actuator
          2. safety device, eg for powered doors
          3. prevents leaking selector valve from causing failure
          4. especially important if selector valve is left ON
        6. hydraulic firewall shutoff
          1. shuts off hydraulic fluid in case of a fire
          2. motor-operated, electrically controlled
          3. could damage pump to run it without fluid coming in
        7. purge valve
          1. for when you first start the pump
          2. allows air to escape for a moment
          3. installed between pressure and return manifolds
          4. normally held in open position by spring, closed by hydraulic pressure
        8. quick disconnect fittings
          1. male and female halves
          2. usually male portion is mounted on device (usually a pump)
          3. female usually mounted in hose
          4. self-sealing when open
          5. may have a ratchet
          6. allows you to disconnect without losing fluid or contaminating the system
          7. several different styles
        9. shuttle valve
          1. usually has 3 ports
            1. normal
            2. emergency
            3. common outlet
          2. allows a component to by powered by 2 different sources
            1. usually normal and emergency systems
          3. used in landing gear extension and brakes
        10. sequence valve
          1. times operation of one event to another
          2. mechanically tripped
            1. plunger pushes up a ball
        11. priority valve
          1. uses hydraulic pressure build up to sequence events
          2. works like a relief valve
          3. will isolate the component if pressure is too low
        12. hydraulic fuse
          1. may be triggered by excess flow or ∆P
          2. protects circuit from leaks
          3. often self-resetting
          4. may be installed on almost any part of the system
        13. cross flow valve
          1. holds some pressure in down side of the actuator, to slow it's movement
          2. prevents actuator cavitation
        14. dump valve
          1. dumps pressure from back side of actuator to allow emergency pressure to move it
          2. must be manually reset to return to normal operation
        15. flow equalizer
          1. synchronizes multiple actuators so they move at the same pace
        16. flow regulator
          1. controls volume of hydraulic fluid passing through
          2. like an automatic variable restrictor
          3. eg, can be used at inlet of hydraulic motor to keep speed constant
          4. metering port inside
        17. irreversible valves
          1. used on helicopters
          2. blocks feedback to collective or cyclic stick
          3. basically check valves
      3. pressure reducer
        1. if only one part of the hydraulic system needs very high pressure, and the other components need lower pressure, this can reduce the pressure
        2. is this like a debooster?
      4. auxiliary pump
        1. often a hand pump
      5. pressure gauge
        1. may be paired with a snubber
          1. dampens pressure surges to protect sensitive devices
          2. pin and plunger assembly inside
          3. prevents oscillation of indicator
  • open-center system
    1. system with a type of selector valve with a pass-through path for fluid to return directly to the reservoir on the 'neutral/off' setting
      1. valves must automatically return to neutral when done
    2. mostly seen on older aircraft with very simple systems
    3. benefits:
      1. good cooling due to continuous circulation
      2. minimizes stress on circuit; no pressure when actuators are not operating
        1. no need for pressure regulating devices
        2. less stress on components
        3. less prone to leakage
      3. less fluid required, smaller reservoir
    4. disadvantages:
      1. only one component can usually be operated at a time
        1. selector valves are in series, so flow is cut off to the downstream ones
      2. slower actuation because you need to build the pressure
  • closed-center system
    1. selector valves arranged in parallel
      1. when they are 'off', fluid cannot flow through
    2. advantages:
      1. can easily operate multiple actuators at once
      2. immediate actuation
    3. disadvantages:
      1. needs pressure regulating devices
      2. harder to control system heat
      3. more component wear and tear
      4. more prone to leakage due to constant pressure
  • power pack
    1. self-contained hydraulic unit
    2. makes it easier to install
    3. outlined by dotted box in schematics

    • 2.6.5. Ground support equipment

    1. hose testing unit
      1. pressure testing
      2. hold pressure ~30sec @ 1.5x system pressure
    2. jacks
    3. maintenance stands
    4. ground pump for testing/servicing
    5. test stand

    2.6.6. High pressure pneumatic systems

    1. advantages
      1. reduced fire hazard
      2. limitless supply of air (with compressor)
      3. lightweight (no liquid, no return lines)
      4. small leakage may not cause rapid pressure drop
      5. not sluggish in cold weather
    2. disadvantages
      1. moisture accumulation– may freeze and cause blockage
      2. not self-lubricating
    3. system components
      1. compressor
        1. often multiple cylinders to compress air in stages
        2. may be driven by engine or motor (electric or hydraulic)
          1. hydraulic motor is preferred; less fire hazard
          2. may need flow regulator
            1. to control speed of hydraulic motor driving compressor
            2. if flow is excessive, the sleeve throttles the fluid
            3. keeps compressor's motor steady
      2. absolute air pressure regulator
        1. mounted at inlet for first stage of compression
        2. may draw from bleed air, the regulator stabilizes the inlet air
        3. may be bellows type
      3. valves
        1. check valve
          1. minimizes back flow
          2. usually flap type
        2. selector valve
          1. no return needed, it's just dumped
        3. restrictors
          1. control flow
      4. moisture separator
        1. may have heat blanket to keep water from freezing
        2. may be supplemented by desiccant cartridge downstream
      5. pneumatic flask
        1. spherical or cylinder
        2. drain tube goes through the flask, top to bottom
        3. port at the top siphons the moisture out
        4. reinforced by wire mesh
    4. often used in emergency systems
      1. inflatable exit slides
      2. back up for hydraulic systems
    5. Soviet planes tend to have pneumatic systems

    3. Powerplant

    3.1. Ignitions

    3.1.1. Internal Combustion Engine Theory

    1. Powerplants** convert potential energy to kinetic energy
  • primary purpose is to create motion from an energy source
  • 2 basic forms of powerplant
    1. engine: converts combustible fuels to mechanical motion
    2. motor: converts electrical power into mechanical motion
  • How do they work?
    1. law of conservation of energy
      1. Application to combustion engine
        1. most internal combustion engines operate by containing the combustion process
        2. combustible fuels have potential energy
        3. when ignited, fuels convert from liquid to gas
          1. they expand
          2. they release heat
      2. energy can be converted into different forms
      3. 2 forms of motion created by powerplants
        1. rotary
        2. linear
        3. Charles' Law**: pressure and temperature of a confined gas are directly proportional
      4. when properly contained, expanding gasses exert force
      5. magnitude of the force applied corresponds with the temperature
  • Types of combustion engines
    1. 4-stroke cycle (aka Otto Cycle)
      1. most certified aircraft recips use this
      2. series of events
        1. intake
          1. intake valve opens as piston approaches TDC
          2. fuel/air charge drawn into cylinder
          3. intake valve closes ~50-75deg of rotation after bottom dead center (BDC)
        2. compression
          1. piston compresses charge so it can burn and expand properly
        3. ignition
          1. just before piston gets to TDC, charge is ignited
          2. it will continue to burn until ~15deg after TDC
          3. it is a controlled burn, not an explosion
        4. power stroke
          1. piston is forced down
          2. connecting rod changes linear motion of piston to rotary motion of crankshaft
        5. exhaust
          1. exhaust valve opens before piston reaches BDC
          2. exhaust valve stays open until shortly after the intake valve opens
            1. this helps with cooling and pushing the exhaust gasses out
          3. piston pushes exhaust gasses out, and cycle starts over
      3. momentum, and other cylinders, drives piston through non-power strokes
      4. usually 2 spark plugs for redundancy and complete combustion
      5. chemical potential energy –> heat + pressure –> linear motion –> rotary motion
    2. 2-stroke cycle
      1. used in ultra-lights, light-sport, and many experimentals
      2. only requires one revolution of the crankshaft for all 5 events
      3. intake and exhaust valves are lower in the wall of the cylinder
      4. not as efficient, because some of the charge is lost out the exhaust port
      5. up stroke: compression, ignition
      6. down stroke: intake, power, and exhaust
      7. on up stroke, piston pulls charge in below it, then it is routed around the piston for compression
    3. diesel
      1. higher compression in the chamber
      2. fuel is sprayed directly into hot, pressurized air
      3. no need for a spark plug
      4. more of an explosion than a burn
      5. diesel/jet A fuel ignites at a much lower temp (300-400F)
      6. can be 4-stroke or 2-stroke
    4. rotary cycle
      1. three-sided rotor in an elliptical housing
      2. completes 3 of the 4 cycles for each revolution
      3. can be single- or multi-rotor
      4. air or water cooled
      5. much less vibration than a recip
      6. banks of them can be stacked to increase power
    5. turbine
      1. spinning compressor pushes air into combustion chamber
      2. fuel is sprayed into combustion chamber (like with diesel)
      3. gasses explode back and power the turbine fan (which drives the compressor)

    • 3.1.2. Principles of Ignition

    1. most engines need some ignition source for combustion (not all)
      1. comes from some heat generating device/system
      2. most common is electric spark
        1. electric spark arcs between 2 electrodes in the cylinder head
      3. in 4-stroke engine, 2 revolutions/power event
        1. sparks need to be timed correctly to every other rotation
        2. needs to happen in different cylinders at different times
    2. ignition needs
      1. type, duration, and source of ignition depends on engine and fuel
      2. sparking must be consistent
      3. needs to occur differently at different times
        1. eg at different rpms– most significant factor for timing
        2. load, fuel/air ratio, intake air density, and temp also affect it
        3. rate of fuel burn stays pretty constant at ~200ft/sec
        4. when combustion is happening quickly, ignition duration needs to be shorter
        5. even when things are operating correctly, partial misfires are common
      4. Timing
        1. factors affecting ignition timing:
          1. temperature
            1. temp: measure of molecular activity
            2. heat: measure of capacity to do work
            3. most fuels ignite at different temps
              1. flash point:** lowest temp at which liquid gives off enough vapors to form an ignitable mixture with air, but not continue to burn
            4. fuels have different heat requirements to sustain combustion
              1. fire point**: lowest temp at which vapors keep burning for min 5sec after ignition source is removed
          2. heat energy produced by ignition source
          3. duration of ignition
        2. Powell's Magic Angle
          1. process of power generation is to compress air and fuel
          2. in recips, the piston does this in the cylinder
          3. in recips, max average pressure should occur at 15deg past TDC for maximum efficiency and power
          4. manufacturers often advance or retard the ignition event to make sure it reaches max pressure at this angle
          5. Powell's magic angle describes the relationship between the rotation force on the crankshaft and the linear force in the cylinder
            1. at TDC, linear force produces no rotary force
            2. at 90deg, max rotary force
            3. ignition should occur before, how much before depends on RPM
              1. eg, at 2500rpm with 5" piston, should be ~40deg before Powell's, or 25deg before TDC
        3. a directly coupled engine (car) will need to vary spark timing to get optimum power at different rpms
          1. fluid coupled engines (aircraft, boat) only produce most of their power in the last few hundred rpm (2400-2700)
        4. Problems:
          1. detonation**
        5. as load, air density, and temp increase, fuel tends to explode, rather than burn
        6. heat and pressure cause the fuel to ignite at the speed of sound in multiple flame fronts
        7. called uncontrolled burning
        8. occurs when pressure and temp in cylinder exceed limits
        9. rapid release of energy and high pressure shock the piston way before TDC and Powell's angle
        10. very bad, things get broken
          1. preignition**
        11. result of alternative ignition source
          1. eg, metal shaving that gets red hot, or feathered valve edges
        12. ignition out of sequence
        13. can be related to detonation, but it's different
          1. can occur simultaneously or independently
    3. air/fuel ratio
      1. running it too lean gets it hot
        1. could cause detonation
        2. more likely to just melt things
      2. running it rich cools the engine
      3. 15lbs air/1lb fuel is stoichiometric ratio, where there is complete combustion of both fuel and air
    4. ignition conditions
      1. engine needs extra sparking power during starting because of low temps and low rpm
        1. spark occurs late to avoid damaging the starter
      2. during off periods, sparking must be disabled, for safety

    3.1.3. Lenz's Law

    1. an induced current always flows in such a direction that its magnetism opposes the motion or the change that induced it
    2. as lines of magnetic force grow outward through a conductor, they induce an EMF in the conductor itself
    3. the induced voltage is always in the opposite direction of current flow
    4. this counter EMF is only present until the current in the conductor reaches a steady value, and the flux lines are no longer expanding
    5. once current is removed from the inductor, the stored energy is released and the inductor attempts to keep the current flowing, once again opposing the change in current

    3.1.4. Faraday's Law

    1. the voltage induced in a coil is a function of the rate of the change of magnetic flux
      1. voltage can be increased in 2 ways
        1. increase the magnet's speed of rotation (not practical)
        2. decrease time over which the flux changes
      2. solution: current interrupting device in the primary circuit
        1. cam and breaker point assembly
        2. causes voltage to go from peak to zero almost instantaneously
        3. when the circuit is opened, the field will collapse immediately –> greater voltage induced

    3.1.5. Magnetos

    1. requirements
      1. must spark at any altitude, attitude, humidity, rpm
      2. including when wet or frozen over
      3. very reliable and predictable
      4. on aircraft, must be self-contained
    2. operation
      1. advantages
        1. completely self-contained, small
        2. engine-driven
        3. can have multiple for redundancy, improved flame propogation
        4. will run even if aircraft alternator/battery fails
      2. disadvantages
        1. they need moderate engine rpm to function optimally
        2. we time them to the median range; they can't really change to adjust to higher or lower rpms
          1. except on start
      3. overview:
        1. usually run in pairs for redundancy and to improve ignition
          1. impact is greater with large bore engines
          2. some are dual magnetos; two distributors in one housing
            1. single central generator
            2. somewhat less redundancy, saves space
        2. engine-driven by accessory gear
        3. slotted mounts allow to fine tune the timing
        4. high voltage leads
          1. each end is connected to one spark plug in each cylinder
          2. each magneto typically feeds all bottom spark plugs on one side, all top spark plugs on the other
      4. basically an AC generator with transformer to step up voltage
        1. electromagnetic induction
          1. when a conductor is moved through a magnetic field, an electromotive force is induced in the conductor
            1. voltage is generated
            2. with a closed circuit, current is induced
          2. can be done in different ways
            1. fixed field: conductor rotating in a magnetic field
            2. rotating field: rotate a magnet by a coil
          3. lines of flux flow through
            1. north pole
            2. pole shoe 1
            3. extension 1
            4. soft iron core (inside coil)
            5. extension 2
            6. pole shoe 2
            7. south pole
        2. transformers
          1. changes a voltage (up or down)
          2. 2 coils, not electrically connected
            1. arranged so that the magnetic field surrounding one coil cuts through the other coil
            2. alternating voltage across one coil creates alternating voltage in other coil by mutual induction
            3. different # of loops in the coils –> different voltages
              1. turn ratio**: primary turns:secondary turns
            4. >1 step down
            5. <1 step up
        3. parts
          1. soft iron core
            1. provides a circuit of low reluctance for flux lines
          2. primary coil
            1. receives electrical energy from the source of applied voltage
          3. secondary coil
            1. receives electrical energy by induction from primary coil
        4. in magnetos, primary and secondary winding are wound around the same soft iron core
          1. primary coil: fewer turns, heavy wire
          2. secondary coil: many turns (~13,000), fine wire
    3. 3 circuits
      1. magnetic circuit
        1. rotating multi-pole permanent magnet
        2. iron core
          1. laminations of very high grade soft iron
          2. sheets of mica in between
        3. pole shoes
      2. primary electrical circuit
        1. primary coil
          1. few turns of heavy copper wire
        2. breaker contact points
          1. made of tungsten (resists wear from arcing)
        3. condenser (capacitor)
          1. prevents burning of breaker contact points
          2. like a shock absorber
          3. condenser is installed in parallel with breaker points
            1. absorbs electricity as the points begin to open to avoid arcing
            2. also facilitates more rapid collapse of the charge in primary circuit
      3. secondary electrical circuit
        1. secondary coil
          1. ~13,000 turns of fine insulated wire
        2. distributor rotor and block
          1. rotor made of non-conductive material
            1. spins to connect secondary coil to different electrodes
            2. turns at 1/2 crankshaft speed
          2. block is also non-conductive, holds terminals and electrodes
        3. leads, spark plugs, etc
          1. ignition lead carries voltage to spark plug
          2. current arcs across electrodes on the spark plug
    4. rotating magnet
      1. full register
        1. breaker points are closed
        2. poles align with pole shoes
          1. greatest density of flux lines
      2. neutral position
        1. poles farthest from pole shoes (~45deg past full register)
        2. all flux lines short circuit; no flow through core
    5. housing
      1. vented to atmosphere to remove gasses from arcing
      2. designed to prevent condensation/moisture from accumulating
        1. could cause shorting at the distributer, called flashover, leads to cylinders misfiring
        2. leaves a conductive carbon trail, so it keeps getting worse over time
  • What's with the breakers?
    1. the primary effect of the coil is its property to oppose any change in current through it
      1. initially, it opposes incoming current
      2. once the lines of flux are no longer expanding, the current is accepted and reaches a steady value
    2. we say that the EMF that initially opposed the current is now stored in the inductor's magnetic field
      1. once current is removed, that stored energy is released
      2. the inductor attempts to keep the current flowing, once again opposing the change in current
        1. Flux density**: density of the lines of magnetic flux
      3. as the magnetic field from the rotating magnet begins to change polarity, the flux density in the magnetic circuit begins to decrease
      4. this allows the stored charge in the inductor (primary coil) to release its stored charge in an attempt to keep the current flowing
      5. the flux from the coil and rotating magnet are additive
    3. E-gap
      1. when the rotating magnet begins changing polarity, the stored magnetic field in the inductor is still releasing its charge
      2. this allows us to maximize the buildup of the field (think Faraday's Law)
      3. keep the field at a high value in one direction until the rotating magnet has time to rotate through the neutral position to a point a few degrees beyond neutral
        1. this few degrees beyond neutral is called e-gap position
        2. e = 'efficiency'
        3. with the magnetic rotor in e-gap position and primary coil holding the magnetic field of the magnetic circuit in the opposite polarity, a very high rate of flux change can be obtained by opening the primary breaker points
        4. opening the breaker points stops the flow of current in the primary circuit and allows the magnetic rotor to quickly reverse the field through the coil core
        5. at this point, if there had been no coil, the magnetic circuit would have already been reversed
        6. the rate of field collapse when the points open is enhanced by the reversed field
        7. this sudden flux reversal produces a high rate of flux change in the core, that cuts across the secondary coil, induces high voltage
        8. enhancing too much e-gap interferes with the buildup of the next field cycle
        9. in a sense the primary circuit and the magnetic circuit are constantly working against each other, and the secondary is auxiliary to them
      4. when flux density is at its highest, just before the field begins to collapse, we use the breaker points to abruptly open the circuit and minimize the time that it takes for the field to collapse

    • 3.1.6. Magneto inspection & repair

    1. Magneto inspection & repair
      1. typically just send them out to repair facility for overhaul
      2. exterior inspection
        1. check security of fasteners, wiring
        2. flange attachment to engine
        3. leaks or obvious damage
        4. check/adjust magneto timing to engine
      3. interior inspection
        1. done during overhaul or at 500hrs
        2. sometimes ADs or SBs call for interior inspection on some models at specific intervals
        3. check:
          1. teeth on distributor rotor
          2. coil
          3. bearings
          4. clearances
        4. replace primary wear items
          1. breaker points
            1. wear in different ways, usually leaves some pitting
            2. if wear is minimal, they may be dressed with a file to flatten peaks
          2. brushes
          3. clean, lube
        5. measure rotor flux for loss of magnetism
        6. ignition harnesses degrade over time
          1. caused by use and heat, contaminants, mechanical damage
          2. typical failure is for spark to escape shielding, grounding the circuit
          3. once spark begins to trail, it leaves carbon trail
          4. leaking harnesses can only be replaced
          5. wires can be bade up, but most common field repair is to replace entire harness
          6. harnesses can be tested
          7. magnetos and harnesses can be pressurized

    3.1.7. Timing the magneto

    1. internally
      1. has to do with how you reassemble it
      2. rotor must be just past neutral (e-gap)
        1. degrees specified by manufacturer
        2. breaker points just begin to open
      3. how to set e-gap
        1. install the cam
          1. some can be indexed infinitely, others have only 1 or 2 positions
        2. align the distributor rotor with the chamfered tooth on the end of the rotating magnet
          1. this gets drive and driven gears lined up correctly
          2. depends on if it's CW or CCW
          3. there are indicator marks on the rotor
        3. set the rotor to the position where the breaker points will open
          1. find neutral position (magnet will naturally tend toward this)
            1. painted tooth should be visible in witness hole
          2. install timing indicator and pointer (under breaker cover)
        4. adjust points to open at e-gap (given in manual)
          1. use timing light
          2. with one screw set, carefully pry apart the breaker points until they just open, and secure the second screw
            1. points are secured with 2 screws, one attachment is slotted
        5. move rotor to max opening between the points
          1. check gap between points with feeler gauge, refer to manual
          2. if it is not in spec, the breaker points should be replaced
            1. this happens if the cam is indexed wrong
        6. auxiliary breaker points can then be adjusted, if needed
    2. externally
      1. distributor rotor must be directed to the correct cylinder
        1. intake and exhaust valves must be closed
        2. piston must be approaching TDC
        3. must have fuel/air charge
      2. magnetos attached to the engine case with adjustable hold down clamps and fasteners
      3. the drive gear must be correctly aligned/timed to the engine gear
      4. magnetos can be disabled by grounding the switch (connected to p-lead)
      5. P-lead (primary lead)
        1. connects primary winding to ignition switch
        2. turn magneto off by grounding in through the p-lead
        3. do this from the cockpit with magneto switch
        4. 'hot' mag is not grounded
          1. if engine keeps firing when switch is off, p-lead is bad
        5. great danger of accidental start if the p-lead fails!
        6. treat every mag like it's hot
        7. made of shielded wire, for obvious reasons
      6. normally, both magnetos must be set to spark simultaneously
        1. Use a magneto synchronizer/timer
          1. green to 1 mag, red to other, black to ground
        2. Must do this while crankshaft and camshaft are in correct position
        3. some mags have split timing (don't fire at the same time)
      7. correct timing means burning charge reaches max pressure at 15deg ATDC
      8. at lower rpm, we want later spark
        1. most mags are set for cruise rpm and fixed there

    3.1.8. Installation and adjustment

    1. procedure overview
      1. remove top or bottom spark plugs
      2. rotate prop so cylinder 1 is at TDC of compression stroke
        1. timing mark on the crankshaft or ring gear aligns with engine case split
        2. dot/hole on front face of starter lines up with timing marks on ring gear
        3. can use a timing indicator/disc
        4. feel pressure on spark plug hole
      3. verify TDC by checking timing marks
      4. move prop back to proper position for ignition (per data plate/manual)
      5. with magneto in e-gap position, install mag onto engine and hand tighten
        1. REMOVE ANY LOCKS before moving anything
      6. attach mag synchronizer and rotate mag to check timing
      7. torque it down, and check it again
      8. install second mag, make sure they're synched
      9. attach p-leads, ignition harness
      10. make sure spark plugs are wired correctly
    2. mags must be inserted with the drive gears correctly meshed to the engine
    3. set the crankshaft so that cylinder 1 is in position for ignition (see data plate/manual)
    4. put magneto on in e-gap position
      1. find chamfered internal tooth in witness hole
      2. or use an alignment pin or rotor lock to keep it at e-gap
        1. make sure to remove it!!
    5. use a buzz box (magneto timer) to fine tune the setting
      1. twist magneto on the accessory case to adjust timing
    6. get both magnetos synchronized
    7. check accuracy after torquing with prop
    8. make sure p-leads are properly attached
    9. attach spark plug leads to correct locations

    3.1.9. Auxiliary ignition

    1. starting devices
      1. magneto operation relies on crankshaft rotation
      2. not set up well for low rpm; can be difficult to start aircraft
        1. combustion occurs to early and the engine kicks back and fails to start
        2. we can improve it by delaying the ignition timing until the piston is very close to TDC on the compression stroke
      3. ways to improve starting:
        1. impulse coupling**: delay the magneto ~30deg, then let it catch up quickly
      4. use a spring loaded device to hold the magnet in place until the piston reaches TDC
      5. it has flyweights that catch stop pins at low rpm
        1. stops the magneto for ~20-30deg
      6. retards timing and speeds up shaft rotation during start
      7. when rpm increases, the flyweights retract and magneto operates normally
      8. hard to see an impulse coupling on a magneto
        1. on a magneto with an impulse coupling, the shaft that coumes out to the drive paws (rotor shaft) is a little bit longer
          1. sometimes you can't tell because of an adapter
        2. you can tell if the impulse coupling is there by listening
      9. disadvantages
        1. they have a problem with the new generation of starters and light weight high current wiring
          1. some newer starters now spin the engine faster than the coupling is designed to work
          2. the counterweights retract right away
        2. the counterweights can become magnetized and not engage the stop pins
        3. possibility of engine kick-back
        4. congealed oil or sludge on the counterweights during cold weather may prevent them from moving
      10. typically installed on left mag; can disable right mag during start
    2. booster coil and trailing finger: second distributor electrode that distributes the late spark
      1. used on larger engine magnetos
      2. connected to a separate booster coil
        1. small induction coil
        2. provides a shower of sparks to the spark plugs until the magneto fires properly
        3. used mostly on large radial engines
        4. circuit is independent of the magneto circuit
        5. battery voltage is applied when the magneto switch is in the start position, or automatically when starter is engaged
        6. the main electrode carries the normally timed magneto output, and auxiliary electrode carries the booster coil output
        7. breaker points are normally closed, held by a spring
        8. when magnetic field is strong enough, it pulls the points open
          1. then field collapses, generates voltage for a spark
    3. starting vibrator
      1. provides a rapid firing battery-powered spark
      2. will only work when the starter circuit is on, the retard points are open and the main points are closed
      3. power comes from starter circuit
        1. after aircraft starts, power to starter is cut off
      4. not high voltage; just gets sent to retard breaker points in magneto
      5. can be electromechanical or solid state
      6. electromechanical
        1. when the starter circuit closes, the meshing relay closes to power vibrator
        2. this energizes the relay coil; generates a magnetic filed that pulls the normally open contact points closed
          1. this sends pulses of DC current to the magneto with retarded breakers
        3. vibrator coil is also energized, creating a magnetic field
          1. once it is strong enough, it will pull the normally closed point open
          2. this collapses the magnetic field, and the spring pulls the points closed again
        4. this cycle repeats many times/sec, makes a buzzing sound
      7. solid state
