        1. does the same thing as the electromechanical one, but everything is done using electrical components
        2. doesn't vibrate
    4. battery powered ignition
      1. basically just a magneto, but the battery is the power source
      2. switch sends battery current to a primary coil
      3. needs to be internally timed like a magneto
      4. transformer steps up the 12V to 24kV!
      5. after the initial spark surge, it will continue conducting current at as lower voltage until the magnetic field is drained from the coil
      6. works well at all rpm ranges

    3.1.10. Ignition system check

    1. mag drop check
      1. part of the run-up checklist
      2. drop should be even for both magnetos
      3. if there is no drop, you probably have a hot mag; p-lead is grounded
      4. also tests ignition leads and spark plugs
    2. ignition switch check
      1. makes sure that all magneto ground leads are grounded
      2. how to
        1. set engine speed to lowest possible rpm
        2. momentarily turn ignition switch to off position
        3. engine should completely quit firing
        4. after drop of 200-300rpm, return to both position a quick as possible
          1. if you're too slow, the engine could backfire
      3. if the engine keeps going, there's at least one hot mag; check the p-leads

    3.1.11. Ignition harness and leads

    1. Spark plug firing order
      1. Established by crankshaft and camshaft
      2. #1 on distributor goes to #1 cylinder (after that it varies)
      3. designed to reduce vibration, stress
      4. Same sequence on Lycoming and Continental, but they label the cylinders differently (stupid)
        1. Lycoming starts in front, Continental in rear
        2. They both have odds on right, evens on left
      5. Radial engines
        1. #1 cylinder at top, opposite sump
        2. On twin banks, odds on rear bank, evens on front
        3. Cylinders numbered clockwise from pilot seat (in direction of prop rotation
        4. 5, 7, or 9 cylinder engines fire is sequentially, then evens
    2. High voltage distribution
      1. most common type, with step-up inducer coil in the magneto
      2. high current carried through leads to spark plugs
      3. wires and spark plugs are positively mechanically attached
        1. not clipped or snapped on
        2. screwed on and torqued
        3. ensures shielding is grounded, to prevent EMI on radios
      4. harness wires
        1. Bendix used braided core
        2. Slick used single coiled wire
        3. sometimes a glass filament impregnated with carbon
        4. silicon rubber insulation
        5. woven copper shielding, usually tinned to prevent oxidation
        6. attachments at each end
          1. nut
          2. insulating sleeve
          3. contact spring
      5. disadvantages
        1. Lots of EMI
        2. at high altitude (~20k), it's difficult to keep this high voltage in the center conductor
          1. in low density, sparks jump from distributor finger to housing or there's an electrical breakdown in the harness
    3. Low voltage distribution
      1. secondary coil moved to cylinder
      2. separate coils for each spark plug
      3. transformer sits on top of spark plug
      4. only used at very high altitudes
      5. often pressurized
      6. mag has primary coil, feeds to another primary coil at the plug

    3.1.12. Spark plugs

    1. purposes
      1. starts ignition
      2. can regulate cylinder temp a little
    2. sections
      1. shell
        1. usually coated with zinc or tin to inhibit corrosion
        2. grounded to engine case
        3. usually multiple ground electrodes, to spread out wear and extend life of the plug
        4. all sections fused together at the tip
      2. insulator
        1. ceramic
        2. keeps current from jumping to shell
        3. connector and ceramic should be clean and sterile
      3. core
        1. center electrode
        2. may have a small resistor
          1. reduces current in secondary
          2. prevent rapid deterioration from capacitance after firing
          3. we want high voltage, but not high current (basically arc welding)
          4. 'R' stamped on it indicates it has a resistor
    3. can be insulated or non-insulated
      1. insulated = shielded; outer shielding grounded to protect from EMI
      2. top and bottom fused together over center section
    4. typical designs
      1. Sizes
        1. ¾" -20 thread "all weather design"
          1. use ⅞" socket
          2. large cable diameter
          3. good shielding
          4. double environmental shield
        2. ⅝" -24 thread
          1. use ¾" socket
          2. more compact
          3. cheaper
          4. slightly less weather resistant
          5. used at lower altitudes
      2. cores
        1. copper core with steel ground
          1. massive electrodes
        2. silver core with iridium ground
          1. fine wire electrodes
    5. reach
      1. how deep spark plug can be threaded into cylinder
      2. plug must match cylinder
      3. short reach: 14mm
      4. long reach: 18mm
      5. different lengths allow for
        1. cylinder head thickness
        2. proper immersion depth in combustion chamber
        3. control of spark plug temp and heat range
    6. Removal
      1. Don't use a wrench, can damage fins
        1. can use wrench on harness nut
      2. pull out terminal sleeve in a straight line
        1. avoid damaging wire, sleeve, or insulator
        2. connector well must be sterile; don't let anything touch it
        3. if you drag the spring, it will leave a conductive track, carbon track will build up
      3. some harnesses have an extra smaller nut, to prevent twisting the wires
        1. just hold it in place with a wrench
    7. Temp
      1. 800-1630ºF range for proper operation
      2. to hot, pre-ignition
      3. too low, lead fouling
      4. ceramic and core fit tighter in "cold" spark plugs
        1. more ceramic contact with shell means a colder plug
        2. "hot" plugs dissipate heat slower
    8. Part №
      1. Champion
        1. eg RHM40E
          1. R: resistor
          2. shielding type
            1. H: ¾
            2. E: ⅝
            3. blank: unshielded
          3. bore x reach
            1. M: 18mm x ½"
            2. L: 14mm x ½"
            3. B: 18mm x 13/16"
            4. J: 14mm x ⅜"
            5. U: 18mm x ⅛"
          4. heat range
            1. higher number, hotter range
          5. electrode design
            1. E: 2 prong massive
            2. W: fine wire iridium

    9. Inspection
      1. spark plugs give good clues as to the state of the combustion chamber
        1. they get dirty when they're run cold
        2. they erode more quickly when run hot
        3. can tell if it's running too lean or rich
      2. troubleshooting
        1. carbon fouling
          1. black soot
          2. plug is operating too cold
          3. too rich
          4. excessive idling
          5. idle mixture set too rich
          6. weak ignition lead
        2. oil fouling
          1. wet, black grease on firing end
          2. oil is conductive, will cause misfiring
          3. caused by oil seeping past piston rings, long periods of inoperation
          4. mostly seen on lower plugs, common on radial engines
          5. may be cleared up by cycling the engine, if mild
          6. may indicate faulty piston rings, damaged piston, or worn valve guides
        3. fuel fouling
          1. caused by excessive fuel-air mixture or over-priming
        4. lead fouling
          1. even, fluffy coating, light tan to brown color
          2. solid chucks of lead trapped
          3. will eventually fill firing end
          4. may short the electrodes
        5. combustion fouling
          1. comes from never cleaning the plugs
        6. silica fouling
          1. from sand, dirt in injection system
        7. cracked insulator
          1. dropped plug
          2. If you drop it once, drop it twice (in the trash)
        8. connector well flashover
          1. from roughly removing/installing ignition lead
          2. dragging the cigarette leaves a carbon trail, causes carbon tracking and flashover
      3. set the gap
        1. check it with rod-type feeler gauge (go/no go gauge)
        2. use special clamp tool
        3. never adjust the gap with the feeler gauge between electrodes
        4. avoid over-gapping, it will almost always damage the plug
        5. check that the electrodes are still serviceable with erosion gauge
    10. Installation
      1. put anti-seize on 2nd and 3rd threads, NOT on electrodes or in connector well
      2. proper torque
        1. 360-420in-lbs on Lycoming
        2. 300-360in-lbs on others
        3. use spark plug socket
      3. use new gasket (don't tell Lyle)
      4. for unshielded plugs, clip lead on
        1. mostly older planes
        2. may require safety wire

    3.1.13. Turbine igniters

    1. turbine ignition overview
      1. gas turbine ignition system converts electrical energy to discharge– sparks/arcs
        1. high heat, voltage
        2. located in combustion chamber of turbine
      2. compressed air in the turbine is very hot; just spray fuel into it
        1. once combustion process begins, igniters are no longer necessary; continuous combustion
        2. some systems also have circuits to allow continuous operation of igniters
        3. can also sometimes monitor flame pressure and automatically turn on ignition if there is a drop in the compressor discharge pressure
      3. on/off control generally integrated into the aircraft system
        1. computerized controls handle the entire starting sequence, including ignition
        2. pilot control is available, but not really used
    2. types
      1. earlier types worked similar to shower of sparks system
      2. high-energy capacitor-discharge
        1. used on most jets
          1. preferred because of the high voltage and high heat intensity of the spark
          2. works well, even at altitude
        2. less susceptible to fouling than spark plugs
          1. self-cleaning, sparks can clean the gap
          2. heat burns off any deposits
          3. not used continuously, only on take-off and landing)
        3. components
          1. on/off control
          2. ignition exciter (1)
            1. dual system, fires 2 igniter plugs
            2. 24V DC circuit
              1. can also be AC, but needs full wave rectifier
            3. radio noise filter prevents noise voltage from being induced into the aircraft electrical system
            4. 2 cams
              1. powered by DC motor
              2. multi-lobe cam allows pulses of energy in, then it's stepped up, rectified, and stored in a massive capacitor
              3. single lobe cam discharges capacitor, allows flow through triggering transformer (steps up voltage), then ionizes gap at spark igniter
              4. bleeder resistor bleeds off any extra charge to ground
            5. some contain radioactive cesium, be very careful and don't open them
              1. often soldered shut for safety
          3. leads (2)
          4. igniters (2)
            1. 2 per engine, in different locations
            2. much higher voltage than in spark plugs, must work at much higher altitudes and lower temps
            3. looks like a spark plug, center electrode generally recessed
            4. many different styles, depends on engine
            5. types
              1. constrained gap
                1. used in some gas turbine engines
                2. operates at cooler temps
                3. does not project into the combustion chamber liner
                4. spark doesn't stay close to the plug, it arcs beyond the face of the combustion chamber liner
              2. shunted gap
                1. used in low voltage systems
                2. creates really high intensity spark
                3. uses a semi-conductor material near the gap that gets so hot that it ionizes the air and creates a path of least resistance
            6. handling igniter plugs
              1. be careful! don't get electrocuted
              2. to remove
                1. disconnect transformer input lead
                2. wait recommended length of time (~5min) to let capacitor bleed down
                3. disconnect igniter lead and ground the center electrode to engine
                4. then, remove igniter plug
            7. inspection
              1. check ceramic lead terminal for signs of
                1. arcing
                2. cracks
                3. carbon tracking
              2. grommet seal
                1. no flashover
                2. no carbon tracking
              3. leads
                1. should stay flexible
                2. no arcing through the insulation
                3. no kinks or fraying
              4. check the whole system for security
                1. firm mounting
                2. no shorts or arcing
                3. no loose connection

    3.2. Powerplant Electrical

    3.2.1. Battery charging and specific gravity project

    1. Overview

      Project: discharge and recharge a lead acid battery Measure and document the voltage and specific gravity of each cell as the battery is charging

      • using voltmeter and hydrometer

      Then, plot it on a graph showing specific gravity, voltage, and state of charge

      • make sure it's clear which cell is which
    2. Hydrometer

      determines specific gravity of a liquid; compares density to water specific gravity: ratio of mass of a solid or liquid to the mas of an equal volume of water works like a turkey baster small, sealed glass tube of water, weighted at the end to keep it upright, goes into the baster part inside the narrow stem is a scale from 1.1 to 1.3

      1. How to use it

        suck up just enough of the electrolyte to float the tube inside the syringe the tube will float higher in denser liquid read the scale at the surface level

        1. remove battery vent cap
        2. insert hydrometer into the first cell to be checked
        3. gently squeeze the bulb to expel the air and slowly release to draw in enough electrolyte to float the hydrometer within the syringe (but not too much, make sure the tube is not bumping on the upper edge)
        4. record the specific gravity reading for that cell
        5. replace the electrolyte
        6. replace the cap
        7. continue the process with the remaining cells
      2. Determining the state of charge

        indicated by the density of the electrolyte when the lead acid battery is fully charged, most of the sulfuric acid is in the solution in a new, fully-charged battery, the electrolyte is ~30% acid and 70% water by volume and should by 1.3 times heavier than water during discharge the sulfuric acid enters the plates, the solution becomes less dense, and its specific gravity drops below 1.3 any reading 1.275-1.300 is good any reading 1.240-1.275 is fair any reading 1.200-1.240 is bad

      3. Precautions

        temperature may affect readings and must be taken into consideration 70F-90F, no correction needed always take reading before adding water, not after this gives time for the water to mix well with the electrolyte, so you don't draw up an overly diluted sample by accident specific gravity of a cell is a reliable measure of state of charge only so long as nothing has been added to the electrolyte except small amounts of distilled water to replace that lost to evaporation be very careful with the sulfuric acid!!! it burns clothes and skin wash any exposed area thoroughly with water and apply baking soda wear splash-proof goggles and protective clothing like gloves, apron, and boots SDS are on hand

    3. Charging the battery

      recharge at max 3amps (make sure it's <1/2 charged to start) measure and document voltage and specific gravity every hour do not short across battery terminals battery generates hydrogen and oxygen while charging; make sure to prevent ignition! no open flames, sparks, or other sources of ignition leave caps on while charging before disconnecting or reconnecting a battery to the charger, always turn the power off first

    4. Project requirements
      • should take 4-5 weeks
      • at the end of each class, flush the hydrometer out and leave it filled with water this makes it last longer
      • after the batter has been completely charged, perform a batter load test
      • complete batter discharge/charging worksheet and answer the questions
      • produce a graph of static voltage and specific gravity over the period of discharging and charging static voltage requires disconnecting from the charger the batter and letting it sit for a few minutes
    5. Measurements

    3.2.2. Electrical power sources/storage

    1. batteries
      1. overview

        container of 1 or more cells in which chemical energy is converted into electricity and used as a power source cathode and anode placed a specific distance apart and are chemically connected with an electrolyte (similar to galvanic corrosion) chemical reaction results in free electrons which can produce voltage/current batteries always produce direct current

      2. types
        1. primary cell

          not rechargable alkaline batteries eg. AA batteries no fluid inside (aka dry cell batteries) high internal resistance, irreversible chemical reaction cheap and easy to use

        2. secondary cell

          rechargable eg. lead acid, nickel cadmium, lithium ion usually identified by the material used for plates high internal resistance most common types:

          • lead-acid
          • nickel-cadmium (NiCad)

          most GA aircraft use lead-acid batteries most commercial/corporate aircraft use NiCad batteries batteries do not power the aircraft in flight; that's the alternator/generator batteries are used for:

          • ground power
          • emergency power
          • operating the starter
          • stabilizing the generator system
          1. lead acid batteries
            1. chemical reaction

              similar to automotive batteries plates become lead sulfate, electrolyte becomes water anode, cathode, and electrolyte inside the battery

              • anode is porous lead (Pb)
              • cathode is lead oxide (PbO2)
              • electrolyte is mix of sulfuric acid (H2SO4) and water

              reaction equation: Pb + PbO2 + 2 H2SO4 = 2 PbSO4 + 2 H2O

              • lead can become lead sulfate if it loses a hydrogen by sending 2 electrons through the circuit and bonds with other chemicals
              • the lead oxide can become lead sulfate if it gains the 2 electrons and hydrogen
              • the electrolyte loses the sulfuric acid and gains 2 waters
            2. cell construction

              a cell has many plates to get enough current output cells are linked in series, to get enough voltage they are connected by plate straps/cell links there are separators between plates to prevent shorts

              a typical battery has 6 cells, each cell produces ~2.1V lead acid battery cells cannot be removed

              space is left below the plates to allow for lead sulfate sediment accumulation if the sediment touches the plate, it can short out the cell

              vent plugs allow gasses to escape when upright, but prevent leakage when inverted (lead weight inside the cap) should be ~0.125" gap between electrolyte surface and bottom of the vent well

            3. battery life

              if the battery goes too low, it may not recharge generally, they will last ~5yrs specific gravity of 0.050 or more between cells means its near the end of its life battery life cycle: number of complete charge/discharge cycles a battery can perform before normal charge capacity is <80% of initial capacity varies 500-1300 cycles can be shortened:

              • over-discharging causes excess sulfation
              • too-rapid charging or discharging causes overheating –> shedding active material
              • shorting of plates, internal discharge

              eventually, even fully charged, the battery cannot supply enough power for starting

            4. battery ratings

              voltage is determined by the number of cells connecting batteries in parallel increases amp-hour capacity connecting them in series increases the voltage amp-hour rating

              • measurement of battery capacity
              • determined by multiplying current flow by time for discharge
              • function of the plate and electrolyte materials

              cold cranking amps

              • amps it can deliver at 0F for 30secs while maintaining a voltage of at least 7.72V
              • battery can only put out so much at a time (because of internal resistance)
              • this gets worse as it is discharging and as the battery gets older
              • it is a function of plate surface area

              a load tester is used to see max output performance

              • used with battery at full charge
              • full charge = 12.68V after resting (static)
              • specific gravity 1.275 or higher
              • should stay in green voltage range for 10secs
            5. servicing
              1. check state of charge

                by checking specific gravity of the electrolyte with hydrometer density of fluid compared to that of distilled water hydrometer is a glass tube with a sealed float containing a scale and water accurate between 70F-90F; otherwise you have to adjust the reading fully charged, should be about 1 part sulfuric acid to 2 parts water

                • specific gravity = 1.275-1.300
                • it will have a very low freezing point
                • cell voltage will get up to 2.113V
                • max static charge should be ~12.68V
              2. check electrolyte level

                should be 1/8" below vent well; don't overfill it should cover the plates can be adjusted by adding distilled water take specific gravity reading before adding any water! do not add electrolyte! if you spill electrolyte, neutralize with baking soda and water, rinse the area with fresh water

              3. charge the battery

                if the charging voltage is >14.2V, the electrolyte will produce oxygen and hydrogen gasses hydrogen escapes from the cell –> water loss always charge in a well-ventilated area

                • constant voltage charging voltage slightly above open-circuit voltage is placed across the terminals if it has low voltage, the charge will put a lot of current through it as it charges, its voltage goes up and the current through the battery goes down when it's fully charged, only enough current flows to compensate for loss to its internal resistance
                  • voltage regulation the generator of an aircraft provides constant voltage to charge the battery the generator is regulated to have slightly higher voltage than the battery it charges the battery at a rate limited by the current limiter or the max ouput of the generator the max current flows until the battery voltage reaches that limited by the voltage regulator the charging current then drops until the battery is fully charged
                • constant current charging standard lead acid batteries should by charged slowly, with 1-2A, never exceeding 14.2V general rule: use no more than 7% of the amp-hour rating of the battery this is rarely done, which tends to kill batteries faster overcharging makes the cells flake apart, lead sulfate collects at the bottom
                • pulse/burp charging aka desulfators high voltage/current pulses break up plate sulfation it can take weeks to restore plates, but can extend their life
              4. keep it clean

                dirty battery can conduct current through grease and dirt battery and battery box should be serviced/cleaned every 100hrs/annually

              5. battery boxes

                battery boxes must be in areas approved by the manufacturer must be suitably vented battery must be secured in its box so it won't vibrate or chafe may use rubber or wooden spacers used as shims corrosion is a primary concern; any corrosion should be completely removed and touched up with acid-resistant paint remove any corrosion on cables and terminals, then apply dielectric grease fumes from the battery are corrosive, so boxes have vents to remove the fumes the battery box may have ram air routed through it to remove any fumes and carry them to a sump jar with sodium bicarb

            6. failures

              most commonly plates fail when they short or sulfate

              • they typically fail in 1 cell first
              • usually shorts internally
              • electrolyte will look black and nasty
              • cell will boil in charge and discharge

              plates or posts can break posts can corrode Dangers:

              • pressure failure they can explode
              • flash explosion they release hydrogen and oxygen; they can combust
              • getting shocked
              • shorting
              • hot conductor, fire
            7. disposal

              batteries contain hazardous material; don't put them in the garbage! most are recyclable must be taken to a Hazardous Waste disposal facility

            8. new batteries

              batteries shipped in a wet state must be maintained in a state of charge, even before use so, often they are shipped dry charged with the electrolyte separate to activate a dry charged battery:

              • remove seals from each cell
              • fill each cell to the correct level with the included electrolyte
              • ready for immediate use, but it is recommended to give it a freshening charge first
              • allow it to sit ~1hr, check the electrolyte level in each cell
              • charge at the rate specified by the manufacturer until the specific gravity remains constant in three consecutive checks at 1hr intervals
              • get rid of any extra electrolyte, don't save it dilute it a lot, and dispose of properly
              1. installation

                always disconnect the ground first and install it last write the date of installation on the battery don't short the terminals!

          2. valve-regulated lead-acid batteries (VRLA)

            similar to lead acid batteries, except all electrolyte is absorbed in glass-mat separators with no free electrolyte aka sealed batteries when charging, oxygen combines chemically with the lead to form lead sulfate and water this oxygen recombinations suppresses the generation of hydrogen overall, there is no water loss during charging, so no provisions are made for water replenishment battery cells havea pressure relief safety valve to vent if it's overcharged may have quick disconnect for easy installation and removal attaches the battery leads in the aircraft to a receptacle mounted on the side of the battery prevents accidental shorting when working with it

          3. nickel cadmium batteries
            1. usually interchangeable with lead acid type batteries

              when replacing a lead acid battery with a nicad, the battery compartment must be clean, dry, and free of all traces of acid must be washed out and neutralized with ammonia or boric acid solution, and allowed to dry completely must be painted with an alkali resistant varnish the pad in the battery sump jar should be saturated with a 3% (by weight) solution of boric acid and water before connecting it to the system

              the chemistry of lead acid and nicad batteries is incompatible one electrolyte will contaminate the other shops should have two separate servicing areas for the different types of battery maintain separate: charging systems cleaning facilities installation tools

            2. overview

              able to charge and discharge at high rates without the voltage drop associated with lead acid batteries much more expensive than lead acid operating temps -65F to 165F most commonly used on turboprop, turbojet aircraft they consist of individual, interchangeable cells and may be serviced individually can be assembled into 12V or 24V configurations, connected by cell-links 9 or 10 cells –> 12V 19 or 20 cells –> 24V cell links must be properly torqued, or else they can get overheated and show burn marks cells are housed in epoxy-coated steel cases

            3. construction

              cathode is nickel hydroxide anode is cadmium hydroxide separators are made of nylon and cellophane electrolyte is a 30% (by weight) solution of potassium hydroxide (KOH) and distilled water the electrolyte is only a conductor, does not participate in the chemical process therefore, specific gravity readings are pretty useless; will remain constant around 1.24-1.30 at 80F KOH is a strong base (alkali) it is dangerous, will damage skin, eyes, and clothing if spilled, neutralize it with boric acid and water, or with vinegar

            4. discharging

              when a load is connected across the battery electrons leave the negative plate and entre the positive plate throuh the conductor and load oxygen is driven from the positive plate and is recovered by the negative plate as it discharges, the electrolyte is absorbed by the plates and the level drops in the cell so, only top them off with water when they are completely charged!

            5. charging

              oxygen is driven from the negative plate, leaving just cadmium the positive plate accepts the oxygen and becomes more oxidized charging continues until all of the oxygen is removed from the negative plate near the end of the charge, it will gas when the water in the electrolyte is decomposed hydrogen gas is released, causing a loss of some water in the electrolyte at full charge, the electrolyte level is at its highest point when it is fully charged, it gets hot and the electrolyte bubbles and spews out the top throug the cell vent this leaves a deposit of potassium carbonate, a white powder

            6. condition of charge

              nicad batteries maintain a constant voltage almost up to the point of complete discharge the electrolyte does not participate in the chemical processes so, there is no simple way to determine the condition of charge of the battery the only way you know how much charge it has, is if you know how much you put into it

            7. thermal runaway

              nicad batteries have a very low internal resistance (IR) this is why they can charge/discharge so quickly also, voltage and resistance vary inversely with temperature so, with an increase in cell temperature, voltage and IR will decrease rise in cell temperature could be:

              • rapid discharge from high ambient temperatures in a poorly ventilated installation
              • if the cellophane breaks down in the separator material, allowing oxygen to unite with cadmium this reaction can generate enough heat to dangerously decrease the IR

              typically, aircraft have a fault protection system that monitors battery condition turbine starters need a lot of energy to start if the aircraft is having difficulty starting the battery can get too hot the center cells in particular get hot in a hung start, wait a bit for it to cool off with lowered resistance, the cells accept all the current coming from the generator, exacerbating the problem cells can break down, catch fire, explode

            8. servicing
              1. inspection in the airplane

                check batteries ~ every 50hrs batteries should be carefully monitored check that the battery box is free of contamination ventilation system clear and operating jar in the sump saturated with boric acid solution look for spilled electrolyte loose connectors, signs of arcing check/adjust electrolyte (in the shop)

              2. inspection in the shop

                wash battery:

                • check vent caps to prevent water entering the cels and flush the top of the battery with tap water
                • remove any deposits with non-metallic bristle brush
                • air dry completely

                check the leakage current

                • any conduction between the cell terminal and the case can cause it to self-discharge
                • check for current, not voltage
                • set meter to highest scale
                • place positive lead on positive terminal and negative lead on battery case
                • decrease scale until you detect current
                • any current >100mA is too much, battery should be disassembled and cleaned

                charge battery

                • use a constant current charger or special charger/analyzer as recommended by manufacturer
                • if you use a constant voltage charger, make sure to follow manufacturer instructions it is fully charged when the current tapers off and stabilizes
                • the constant current method is really preferred; slower but more effective

                adjust the electrolyte

                • wait until it's fully charged, when electrolyte is at the highest level make sure to adjust the level immediately after charging
                • adjust the electrolyte to the recommended level (~1/4" above top of plates)
                • add only distilled water, no potassium hydroxide only water is lost, so only water should be replaced

                discharge battery

                • only way to know the state of the battery is to discharge it and measure its amp-hr capacity
                • battery is connected to a load bank and discharged at its one or two hour rate until it has an average voltage of 1.0V/cell
                • record the time that this takes if <70% of the time specified by the manufacturer, the battery capacity has decreased and it needs equalization equalization: rebalancing the cells

                recharge battery

                • after rest period, charge the battery
                • monitor voltage and temperature of each cell to identify any faulty ones

                final check

                • check torque on cell links
                • check electrolyte level
                • check leakage current between cells and case
                • update the records any time it is serviced, make a record entry of the battery condition this allows trend tracking, to see problems before they get out of hand should contain:
                  • condition of case and cells
                  • tightness/condition of cell links
                  • electrolyte level and water added to each cell
                  • voltage of each cell at end of charge
                  • mention any hot spots or conditions to note on later inspections
    2. capacitors

    3.2.3. DC Review

    1. Math
      1. Numbers
        • whole numbers: integers from 0 up
        • integers: include negatives
        • fractions: ratio between integers, portion of a number numerator, vinculum, denominator to add/subtract fractions, they need a common denominator multiplying/dividing is simpler
        • decimal numbering system
        • scientific notation
        • metric system milli, micro, nano, pico
        • engineering notation
      2. Algebra
        • order of operations parentheses exponents and roots multiplication/division addition/subtraction left to right
        • rounding vs truncating
        • algebraic transposition basically just using variables
        • algebraic matrix grid system to organize data
    2. Structure of matter

      matter: anything with mass and volume states of matter:

      • solid
      • liquid
      • gas
      • plasma

      element: substance that cannot be broken down further by chemical means atom: smallest particle that will retain the properties of the element neutrons and protons in the nucleus, electrons in the cloud molecule: 2 or more atoms joined together compound: molecule that contains at least 2 different elements

      forces within an atom:

      • electrostatic force polarized force between electrons and protons holds atoms together and allows them to form bonds (?)
      • magnetic force
      • gravitational force

      Electrons

      • exist in shells around the nucleus
      • valence electrons form chemical bonds
      • allows current to run through conductors
      • valence electrons = 2n2 n is the shell level
    3. Conductors, Semiconductors, Insulators
      • number of electrons in outer shell determines its conductivity
        • conductors (eg copper) have very few valence electrons
        • semiconductors (eg silicon) have a half full valence shell
        • insulators (eg argon) have a full valence shell
      • they often combine to achieve a complete valence shell
      1. Conductors

        they share their valence electrons atoms with few, loosely bound valence electrons give them up or transfer them easily gold, copper, silver have free electrons when they bond together with themselves, they make good conductors

      2. Semiconductors

        don't always combine with themselves sometimes will share their free electrons with other elements (covalent bonding)

        • this may affect the conductivity of the resultant element

        by mixing certain elements we can makea semiconductor that can vary its conductivity silicon, germanium most widely used semiconductors

      3. Insulators

        inert atoms with full valence shells glass, ceramic, plastic, noble gasses

    4. Electromagnetic Forces
      • north/south polarity
      • only exists when electrons are moving in coordinated current
        • some materials can retain magnetic properties without current
        • different in this way from electrostatic force
      • magnetic field surrounds any permanent magnet or moving magnetic charge
      • magnetic polarity established by direction of force lines in an object
        • flows from north to south end
      • running current generates a magnetic field, and magnetic field can generate current
        • iron is really good at this
      • Electromotive Force
        • electrons can by moved by friction, magnetism, or a chemical reaction
        • electromotive force (EF) pushes them
        • this movement of electrons is called current (I) measured in amps
        • electrical charge measured in coulombs
          • 1A = 1C/sec
          • 1C = 6.24x1018
      • Voltage
        • difference in electrostatic potential between two points
        • measured in volts
        • can exist without current flowing
      • Work/energy
        • 1 joule = 1 Newton-meter (N-m)
        • 1 Volt = 1 joule/coulomb
      • Resistance
        • resistors convert electric energy to heat
        • all matter has some resistance
        • measured in ohms
        • inverse relation to wire diameter, direct relation to wire length
      • Ohm's Law and Power
        • V=IR
        • P=IV
          • power is work over time
          • electrical units are Watts
          • mechanical units are HP or joules/sec
    5. Circuits
      • all circuits must have
        • electromotive source
        • one or more conductors
        • load
      • may also have a controller
      • AC/DC
      • circuit types:
        • series
        • parallel
        • combined
      • Kirchoff's Law
        • Current: current is additive in parallel branches, must add to total
        • Voltage: voltage is additive in series, must add to total
      • Measuring with a multimeter
        • voltage can be measured with circuit open or closed measure it in parallel
        • current must be measured with closed circuit measure it in series
        • resistance must be measured with open circuit measure it removed from the circuit
        • continuity is basically resistance checks to make sure the circuit is not open
    6. Electromagnetic Induction and Generator Theory
      • overview
        • when a conductor is moved through a magnetic field, EMF is generated in the conductor
        • when it is in a complete circuit, it induces current
      • induction
        • different ways to accomplish induction
          • fixed field: rotating conductor in magnetic field, or
          • rotating field: rotating magnetic field near a conductive coil
          • both generate voltage and, in a circuit, current
        • armature is the conductor where current is developed
      • Left Hand Rule for Generators the polarity of voltage depends on the direction in which the conductor is moved and the position of the north and south poles of the magnetic field
        • index finger: magnetic lines of flux from north to south
        • thumb: direction of conductor movement
        • middle finger: direction of electron current flow
      • Generating current
        • a coil rotating in a field generates an alternating current/voltage
      • output strength depends on three factors:
        1. speed of coil rotation since it is geared to the engine, it's not practical to keep increasing rpm
        2. number of turns of the coil this is set at manufacture; not adjustable
        3. strength of magnetic field (number of lines of flux) this is the only factor that we can play with we can do this using an electromagnet (an iron core in a coil) when we run DC current through the coil, we generate a field
      • Left Hand Rule for Electromagnets wrap fingers around coil in direction of electron flow, thumb indicates direction of magnetic flux lines
      • generators will produce AC
        • if you want DC, you need to rectify it
        • Methods of Rectification
          • Commutation attachment at the end of the armature that switches back and forth to keep current flowing in one direction used with fixed field generator positions DC generator brushes so that commutator segments change brushes at the same time the armature current changes direction always 2 commutator bars for each coil segments are positioned to maximize output produces a ripple DC output to reduce the ripple, add more coils (and a capacitor) full wave rectification
          • diode bridge rectifier (solid state) use diodes to make sure current only flows one way used with rotating field generators full wave rectifier diode like a check valve, turned on and off by applying a voltage across it
            • forward biased: acts as conductor
            • reverse biased: acts as insulator
        • AC vs DC
          • major advantage of AC is that current and voltage can be increased/decreased by transformer
            • this means you can decrease current to save on weight with thinner wires
          • an inverter can be used to turn DC to AC

    3.2.4. Using a Multimeter

    1. Circuit analysis and troubleshooting
      • troubleshooting requires:
        • understanding circuit operation
        • know how to use the equipment
        • recognize what you're seeing
      • multimeter measures
        • voltage
        • resistance
        • current
        • continuity
        • may have other functions
          • temperature
          • capacitance
          • Hertz
          • diode checkers
      • styles of multimeter
        • switched manually switch between ranges to get most accurate reading err on the side of too high a range, work your way down
        • auto-ranging switches between ranges automatically for best reading
      • meter leads
        • red: connected to voltage/resistance or amp port positive connection
        • black: always connected to common/ground negative connection
        • probes handles
        • tips connection point at the end of the probe
      • what to check for
        • open circuit
          • may be opened with a switch
          • fuse burnt out
          • broken wire
          • other component failure
        • continuity
        • short circuit
          • high current flow
          • things burn out
        • voltage
          • 2 types of current, AC/DC
            • when reading AC, output is converted to DC equivalent (Effective Value or Root Mean Square RMS) this is <max value multiply max value by 0.707 to find effective value
              • How to Calculate RMS Vpp = voltage peak-to-peak instantaneous voltage: voltage at single point in the cycle Vi = sine of the angle x peak voltage Vrms = square root of the mean of the squares of the instantaneous voltages you can get a close guess using the Vi from equally spaced points along the curve to do it right, just use calculus
          • differential measurement across a component
          • ground referenced measurement always touch black probe to ground quick and easy way to troubleshoot a circuit allows you to find voltage of a specific point
        • resistance
          • disconnect component first
        • current
          • first, disconnect power
          • insert ammeter in series and turn power back on
          • select highest current setting and work down
          • almost never used for troubleshooting
        • diodes
          • they have polarity anode: positive lead cathode: negative lead bar on the diode indicates the cathode check them both ways for opens and shorts
          • forward vs. reverse bias forward bias: when it allows current to flow; conductor reverse bias: when it prevents current from flowing; insulator
          • LEDs
            • anode (positive) lead is longer may also have a bump
      • how to find issues
        • usually with a voltmeter and system power on
        • also, ohmmeter, power off, component removed from circuit
        • typically, don't use the ammeter function unnecessary and time-consuming can cause undue wear on components

    3.2.5. Schematics and Components

    1. schematics and diagrams
      1. primary depicts circuit in simple formats that shows relationships between all parts/circuits typically, wires are solid lines, and parts are drawn with symbols or boxes may put a certain type of info in one location
        • grounding points
        • connector/pin diagrams
      2. flow chart does not depict circuits shows a flow of diagnostic decision making usually have data about testing results
      3. logic circuits designed to show logic flow decision-making tool shows how the circuit processes decisions/information
    2. electrical components
      • resistors
        • multitap resistor option of 2 different resistances
        • variable resistor
      • capacitors
        • fixed capacitor value doesn't change
        • polarized capacitor has + or - on one side
        • variable capacitor
      • switches
      • circuit breaker
        • pull out (can be used as a switch)
      • battery
      • AC power source sine wave in a circle
      • antennas
        • aerial
        • dipole
        • loop
      • ground
        • chassis ground
        • earth/general ground
        • static ground
      • coils
        • inductor
          • center-tapped inductor
        • transformer
          • air core
          • iron core
          • variable
      • solid state devices
        • diode arrow is anode, line is cathode forward bias: current flows when positive is anode and negative is cathode
          • LEDs shown with arrows (light rays) coming off
          • zener in 1-way up to a point, then goes both ways
          • photodiode has arrows coming in
          • bidirectional like a 2 way zener diode
        • transistors like a diode, but with an extra region in it (P or N)
          • Bipolar transistors
            • PNP emitter (with an arrow, inputs electrons), collector (output), and base (controller) arrow going in use a smaller current to regulate a larger current if it's an N region and goes negative, current will flow if its a P region and goes positive, current will folw
            • NPN arrow going out
          • Junction Field Effect Transistors (JFETs) source, drain, and gate may be p-type or n-type gate works pretty much the same as above less sensitive to voltage and lower noise output slower, can't handle as much current voltage-controlled current device
    3. logic gates
      • and/nand
      • or/nor
      • not
      • ex-or/ex-nor

    3.2.6. Wiring and Connectors

    1. Electrical Wiring Interconnection System (EWIS)
      • FAA guidance to address electrical system design and maintenance issues
      • rule-making specific to transport aircraft, but best practices apply to all aircraft
      • electrical connectors are designed to meet milspec these are approved for use in all certificated aircraft
    2. Wiring
      1. definitions
        • wire: single, solid conductor or stranded conductor covered with insulating material
          • types
            • solid, generally not used in aircraft
            • stranded, good for flexibility resists vibration and fatigue
        • cable: 2 or more separately insulated conductors in the same jacket
          • types
            • shielded wire for EMI protection
            • twisted pair 2 wires twisted together in single jacket done to cancel out cross talk and EMI from external sources when an interfering signal is applied equally to both sides of the twisted pair, the interference is neutralized
            • Radio Frequency cable, aka coax cable RG58, RG400 similar to a shielded cable don't bend or kink! designed to conduct radio frequencies from antenna/receiver
        • American wire gauge (AWG) aka Brown and Sharpe est 1857 standardized diameters of round, solid, nonferrous, electrically conducting wire cross-sectional area of each gauge is an important factor for determining its current-carrying capacity larger number = smaller wire
      2. wire selection

        use wire selection chart

        • must be sized to have sufficient mechanical strength may be premature failure from vibration, flexing, connect/disconnect, termination for wires <#20, be extra careful use additional clamps and group with min 3 other wires cannot use a wire with <19 strands may be tin, silver, or nickel to reduce corrosion ensure insulation can withstand anticipated heat exposure
        • within allowable (IR) voltage drop
        • can safely carry circuit current
        • should be able to carry more current than the circuit breaker or fuse
      3. wire identification
        • all wire used on aircraft must have its type identification printed along its length
          • placed at the end of each wire and at 15" max intervals
          • wires <3" don't need identified
          • wires 3"-7" should be identified in the center
          • can be printed on a heat shrink sleeve
      4. insulation
        • PVC (polyvinyl chloride)
          • introduced in 1950s
          • flammable
            • produces thick, toxic smoke
            • if doused with water, it creates hydrochloric acid
          • susceptible to
            • chafing (it's soft)
            • aging (gets brittle)
          • dangerous and bad
        • Teflon
          • introduced in 1969
          • tended to split longitudinally because of the manufacturing process
          • susceptible to cold flow (creeping of conductor)
          • caused the Apollo 1 fire
          • banned by major manufacturers in 1983
        • ETFE (ethylene-tetraflouroethylene fluoropolymer) aka Tefzel general purpose wire meets mil specs strong, flexible good high/low temperature properties fair cold flow properties soft at rated temperature (150C)
        • TKT Teflon/Kapton/Teflon arc-track resistant abrasion resistant excellent insulation protection high heat tolerance resists smoking when burning meets mil specs and passes Part 25 standards
      5. mounting and routing
        • minimum bend radius of wire groups or bundles = 10x the OD of the largest wire/cable
        • where wires break out at terminations or to reverse directions in a bundle, the radius may be 3x the OD of the wire/cable if suitably supported
        • thermocouple wire should be done in accordance with manufacturer's recommendation don't cut it!!!
        • coax cables bend at a radius of at least 6x the cable OD
        • wiring should be routed above fluid lines and kept away from fluids, fire hazards, or contamination
        • slack is important
          • too much slack can lead to damage, allows the cable to contact structure or other equipment
          • too little slack can put pre-load on cable, can damage bundle, clamps
          • sufficient slack should be left between the last clamp and the termination or electrical equipment to prevent strain at the terminal and to minimize adverse effects of shock-mounted equipment
          • max 1/2" deflection between clamps with normal hand pressure
        • service loop
          • meant to make maintenance easier
          • extra wiring to allow for easy installation of equipment in the panel
          • components should slide out far enough for you to hook it up in the back
            • usually 3-6 inches
        • clamps
          • should be compatible with environment
            • temperature
            • fluids
            • UV light
            • mechanical loads
          • wires should be adequately supported
            • max interval 24"
            • should be snug without pinching
            • wires should not move when lightly pulled axially
              • except for RF cables, they should move freely, must not be crushed
          • clamps should be installed with the hardware downward
          • protect grommets from hydraulic fluid or oil
          • don't use tape or tubing in lieu of standoffs
        • problems
          • vibration, chafing tie wraps, ties, insulation cracking
          • moisture degradation corrosion, insulation brittle
          • maintenance rough handling, metal shavings, and debris
        • never add an electrical load without doing a complete circuit load analysis
        • splices in bundles should be staggered to avoid increasing its size too much
        • wire lacing
          • used to secure wire groups or bundles to provide ease of maintenance, inspection, and installation
          • straps/zip ties meeting mil specs may be used where the temperature is <120C
            • may not be used in SWAMP areas
              • wheel wells
              • wing flaps or folds
              • high vibration areas they will wear through engine mounts!
              • anywhere exposed to UV lights unless they are special straps resistant to UV
          • spot tie with clove hitch and square knot
      6. wire terminals
        • tensile strength
          • terminals are attached to the endes of electrical wires to facilitate connection of the wires to terminal strips or items of equipment
          • tensile strength of the wire-to-terminal joint should be at least equivalent to the tensile strength of the wire itself
        • resistance of the wire-to-terminal joint should be negligible
        • selecting terminals
          • wire size and insulation diameter
          • application environment
          • insulation material compatibility
          • stud size
            • lug hole size should match stud diameter
          • conductor material compatibility
          • current rating
        • max 4 lugs or 3 lugs and a bus bar per stud
        • tightening terminal connections should not deform lugs
      7. circuit protection
        • breaker/fuse must always open before any component downstream can overheat and generate smoke or fire
        • breakers/fuses are designed to protect the wire, not components
        • located as close to the bus as possible
        • protective devices must be installed in all circuits except:
          • main circuits of starter motors used during starting only
          • circuits in which no hazard is presented by their omission
        • a protective device for a circuit essential to flight safety may not be used to protect any other circuit
        • each resettable protective device (in which the tripping mechanism cannot be overridden by the operating control) must be designed so that
          • a manual operation is required to restore service after tripping
          • if an overload or circuit fault exists, the device will open the circuit regardless of the position of the operating control
        • if it is needed for safe flight, it must be easily accessible in flight to be reset or replaced
        • for fuses replaceable in flight, you must have 1 spare of each rating or 50% spare fuses or each rating, whichever is greater, accessible to pilot
        1. fuses
          • strip of metal usually made of tin alloy and bismuth
          • it melts to open the circuit; not resettable
          • types of fuse holders:
            • plug-in in-line holders used for small and low capacity fuses
            • clip used for heavy, high capacity fuses
        2. current limiter
          • similar to a fuse
          • made of copper
          • once it opens, it needs to be replaced
            • will withstand considerable overload for a short period of time
          • used in heavy current circuits ~30A or more
          • used primarily to sectionalize an aircraft circuit or bus
          • found in starter circuits
        3. resettable circuit breakers
          • can be used as a switch
          • trip-free design (opens regardless of operating control position)
          • should be exercised regularly to keep contacts clean
    3. Connectors
      • many different types
        • common brands (among many)
          • cannon
          • molex
          • AMP
        • generally either male/female pin pairings, or contact pads with spring pins
        • crimp or solder type
      • a good connection:
        • provides continuity with little resistance
          • pins typically copper, maybe gold-plated
        • is mechanically sound and unlikely to break the wire with relative motion
        • prevents corrosion of wire and connector components
        • should be easily disconnected, but only on purpose
      • best practices
        • energize the most protected side (female side)
        • use crimp-type pins
        • most will have a special tool for inserting and removing pins
        • don't rely on the wires for a good mechanical connection
        • use screw plates at the back end for strain relief on wires
        • always use the right tool for the job
        • test new connections with a pull and an ohmmeter
        • re-verify connector pin-outs before applying power
      • common problems
        • bent pins
        • wire pulled out of pin (recessed pin)
        • corrosion
        • misalignment of pins
        • pin crimped onto installation
        • wrong hole
        • pin crimped onto shield

    3.2.7. Instrumentation

    1. what is it?
      • instruments required to measure/indicate the functioning of the powerplant
      • generally installed on the instrument panel
      • includes:
        • manifold pressure
        • rpm
        • engine temperature
        • oil temperature
        • carburetor air temperature (CAT)
        • fuel/air ratio
        • fuel quantity/flow
        • amps
        • oil pressure
        • EGT
        • CHT
      • types
        • mechanical
        • electrical
        • electronic
    2. why do we care?
      • data is provided to:
        • pilots in real time
        • maintenance personel (data logging)
        • warning systems
        • flight management and other automated systems
      • data about
        • position
        • pressure
        • motion (typically rotary)
        • temperature
        • quantity, rate or flow
      • range markings
        • based on engine operating limits from aircraft TCDS
        • colors
          • red radial line beyond which a dangerous operating condition exists arc indicates dangerous operating range because of propeller resonance triangle, dot, or diamond mark on an engine instrument indicates max limit for high transients, like starting
          • yellow arc covers a given range of operation, indicates caution acceptable, but for a limited amount of time
          • green arc shows normal, safe range of operation
          • white slippage mark
    3. basic instruments
      1. tachometer

        on a recip, it shows crankshaft rpm calibrated in 100s of rpms green arc indicates permissible rpm range red line shows max rpm for takeoff; anything more is an overspeed condition used to indicate aircraft total time; only accurate at cruise speed (normally stamped on instrument case) on turbines, they use percent rpm indicators each rotating assembly in the engine has its own rpm indicator 100% position is the highest rpm the engine can do helicopters use a dual tachometer; speed of engine and speed of main rotor when clutch for the main rotor is fully engaged, the two needles are 'married', lined up

      2. oil pressure indicator

        reading taken from pressure side of oil pump if you don't see oil pressure, shut it down immediately oscillation of the pointer may indicate air in the lines

      3. fuel pressure indicator

        calibrated in psi typically, pressure is sensed at the carburetor or fuel injection unit inlet of each engine on small recips, this also functions as fuel flowmeter, because flow is proportional to pressure

      4. fuel flowmeter

        measures amount of fuel delivered to the engine usually in gallons/hr, on larger aircraft it is lbs/hr on a continuous-flow fuel injection system is actually a pressure gauge that measures pressure drop across the injector nozzles

      5. oil temperature indicator

        shows temperature of oil as it enters the engine if it's too hot, it won't cool the engine well enough

      6. exhaust gas temperature gauge EGT

        used to indicate the fuel/air ratio being burned in the cylinders modern systems measure EGT as the gasses leave the cylinders gives an indirect indication of mixture being burned on a recip, used to set ideal fuel/air mixture setting for max efficiency

      7. cylinder head temperature gauge CHT

        usually 1 thermocouple lead and indicator for each engine sensor is put on the hottest cylinder thermocouple is used as a spark plug gasket

      8. manifold pressure gauge MAP

        measures absolute pressure in the induction system of a recip usually measured in "Hg red line indicates max manifold pressure during takeoff

      9. carburetor air temperature gauge CAT

        measured at carburetor entrance used as indication of ice formation in the induction system if the engine backfires, there will be a momentary rise in the gauge induction system fire will show up here as continuous increase in heat

    4. how it works
      1. remote position indicating system
        • often impractical or impossible to have gauges directly connected to where things are being sensed
        • so, sensors are connected to the gauge in the cockpit
          • small motors in the instrument housing used to position pointers
          • electrical signal sent through wires very reliable nad accurate powered by aircraft electrical system
      2. synchro-type remote indicating instruments
        • transmitter and receiver connected with wires
        • 'synchro' means the indicator in the cockpit is going to move the exact same amount as the transmitter
        • common in position indicating systems
          • flaps
          • landing gear
          • autopilot
          • radar
          • fluid pressure
        • uses the relationship between electricity and magnetism
          • electricity can create directional magnetic fields
          • those fields can interact with magnets or other fields we use this to deflect indicators
        1. types
          • DC
            • variable resistor sends signal
            • Selsyn
              • transmitter, indicator, and connecting wires transmitter often connected to moving unit by mechanical linkage
              • how it works
                • transmitter is a circular resistance winding (like in a potentiometer) with a rotatable contact arm
                • the ends of the arm are brushes touching the winding in two places
                • the shaft of the arm is attached to the unit being measured
                • there are 3 points in the winding which connect to 3 electromagnets; as the resistance varies to each point, the magnetic force at the other end varies
                • the pointer is attached to a permanent magnet and deflects to indicate the position
          • AC
            • AC system, uses induction instead of resistance
            • Magnesyn
              • permanent magnet rotor attached to unit being measured sits inside a coil with iron core
              • the magnet induces a voltage in the coil
              • there are 4 points where current can go off the coil
            • autosyn
              • basically the same as above
              • only difference is they use electromagnets instead of permanent magnets
      3. pressure sensing
        • Bourdon tube less sensitive used for higher pressure applications (hydraulic, engine oil) senses pressure, but can be used to measure temperature
        • diaphragm more sensitive; used to measure low or negative pressures eg, altimeters, manifold pressure measures the difference between two pressures
        1. pressure switch
          • used to trigger warning device of unsafe condition
          • fuel or oil pressure, etc
          • diaphragm closes a switch to activate warning circuit
        2. pressure transmitter
          • converts pressure to electrical signal
      4. rotational rate sensing
        • tachometer mostly found in recips
        • types
          • centrifugal balances between coil spring and flyweights (like a propeller governor) as flyweights push out (with centrifugal force), the spring is compressed the spring shifts a shaft connected to a pinion gear, which deflects the pointer on the gauge
          • magnetic drag cup tach cable drives a permanent magnet which rotates to produce a current in the copper or aluminum 'drag cup' around it drag cup is attached to the pointer (it has a calibrated hairspring to pull it toward zero) the eddy current in the drag cup creates its own magnetic field, which interacts with the rotating magnet's flux field this causes the drag cup to rotate, deflecting the pointer strength of the field is proportional to the speed of rotation
          • electric tach basically a simple generator driven by the engine sends current to a motor that drives a magnetic drag cup tach frequency output of the generator is directly proportional to the engine rpm when generator is used for the tachometer it's called a tach/gen
      5. temperature sensing
        • physical characteristics of materials change with temperature in a consistent way
          • expansion/contraction varies and is unique to each material
          • this means we can measure temperature indirectly
        • types
          • bimetallic thermometer
            • 2 different metal strips bonded together in curl
            • connected to an indicator needle
            • often used to measure OAT
          • Bourdon tube
            • connect it to a bulb of methyl chloride
            • used to measure oil temperature
          • thermistor
            • temperature sensitive resistor
            • usually, a metal's resistance increases with temperature
          • thermocouple
            • circuit of 2 unlike metals joined at 2 junctions one measured, one reference
            • differential pressure between the two generates a small voltage
            • voltage is proportional to temperature of the measured junction
            • used to measure high temperatures (CHT, EGT)
            • leads are designed for specific installations and cannot be altered
            • gasket or bayonet
    5. replacing instruments
      • when to replace
        • cracked glass
        • if it won't zero out
        • pointer is loose on the shaft
        • fogged up (probably a vent problem)
      • when not to replace
        • red line missing
        • chipped case paint
        • loose mounting screws
        • leaking at the line B nut

    3.2.8. Final Review

    • thermocouples may not be altered
      • hot and cold junctions
    • engine instrumentation
      • bourdon tubes
      • EGT used to set the best mixture setting for fuel efficiency
      • fuel flowmeter actually measures pressure
      • oil temperature gauge measures it as it enters the engine
      • exhaust gas analyser indicates fuel/air ratio being burned in the cylinders
    • derated switches help obtain reasonable switch efficiency and service life
      • used in DC motor circuits
      • different de-rating factors
    • series-wound DC motor
      • high starting torque
      • used on starters
    • growler test
      • testing for a shorted armature
      • you put the armature in a vibrating box
    • DC motors
      • series
        • draws higher current on starting
      • shunt (parallel)
      • compound
      • major components
    • AC motors
      • 3 phase induction
      • capacitor start
    • polish commutators and slip rings with 000 sandpaper; other abrasives can cause shorting
    • starter circuit have current limiters, not fuses/breakers
    • most aircraft AC is 400Hz
    • counter EMF vs applied EMF
    • replace starter brushes at 1/2 original length
    • magnetic braking on an electrical motor
    • induction coil iron cores laminated to reduce eddy currents
    • galvanometer uses d'Arsonval movement

    3.3. Reciprocating engines

    3.3.1. Overhaul procedure includes:

    • researching the engine
    • researching required tools and how they operate
    • researching overhaul procedures
    • researching overhaul specifications
    • researching any other pertinent information

    3.3.2. Intro

    1. Engines vs Motors
      • powerplants create motion from energy engines: convert combustible fuel motors: convert electrical power
      • 2 basic forms of motion rotary reciprocating
    2. Engines overview

      most internal combustion engines contain/confine the combustion process eg. in a reciprocating engine's cylinder or in the combustion chamber of a turbine

      1. Law of the Conservation of Energy
        • total energy of an isolated system remains constant
        • energy isn't created or destroyed, just transformed stored chemical energy (in fuel) converts to heat energy, which pushes the piston to convert it to kinetic energy
      2. First Law of Thermodynamics
        • temperature has a direct relationship to pressure in a contained gas
      3. 3 forms of thrust generation
        1. rotational power output is most common wheels on the ground linear force exerted against the ground propellers in water or air airfoil exerts linear thrusting motion against fluid linear force produces linear output
        2. linear fluid motion trap heated gasses in an envelope and use the pressure differential to generate thrust (hot air balloon) create linear fluid motion in a fluid to generate thrust (rocket) expanding gasses are contained and directed in one direction no need for moving parts for thrust (but do need it for directional control) space rockets must supply their own combustion components, fuel and oxygen can generate lots of thrust over a short period
        3. nuclear steam engine convert liquid to gas with heat use it to power steam engines pro: lots of power over a very long time cons: cost, weight, size, and hazardous materials
      4. Constant flow cycle (turbine engines)

        produces linear fluid flow with axial flow multi-stage pump compressors combustion is contained in the combustion chamber by pressure from front and back, plus walls of the engine very good power-to-weight ratio and can run for a long time, very reliable output device is rotating, but thrust exertion is similar to a rocket (so, sort of a mix?)

      5. Reciprocating engines

        contain expanding gasses for a short period force of expansion pushes on piston to create linear motion connecting rods and crankshaft convert this into rotary motion

        1. pros:
          • pretty lightweight
          • pretty fuel efficient
          • relatively low maintenance costs
          • wide range of power output options (0.1hp-12,000hp)
          • can rotate in either direction
          • unlimited torque options
          • rpm from 10-20,000
          • higher output power in narrow or wide rpm range
          • reliable with proper maintenance
          • durable
          • adaptable to various applications
    3. Basic parts of reciprocating engines
      • cylinder
      • piston rings seal combustion chamber
      • connecting rod wrist pin holds it to the crankshaft
      • crankshaft
      • crankcase
    4. Things to remember
      • Otto cycle intake compression power exhaust
      • Powell's Magic Angle = 15deg past TDC max force should be applied here to allow for max piston force converted to rotary force
      • can have 2 stroke or 4 stroke engines 2 stroke rarely used for outputs >100hp

    3.3.3. Basic Operation

    1. 2 stroke cycle

      most are spark-ignited, low compression, gasoline engines there are a few rare examples of high compression ignited, 2 stroke diesel engines may be used in ultra-light, light sport, or experimental aircraft

      1. cycle events

        requires only 1 up and 1 down stroke

        • down for power and exhaust
        • up for intake and compression intake happens below the piston and is routed around through transfer port to combustion chamber it as piston goes down reed valve allows fuel/air mixture into the crankcase, acts as a flapper check valve piston sucks fuel charge in and then pushes it up
    2. 4 stroke cycle

      invented by Nicolaus August Otto 4 stroke, 5 event, you know the drill

      1. events:
        1. intake intake valve closes 50-75deg past BDC
        2. compression
        3. ignition occurs ~25deg before TDC (except at low rpms, eg starting) because it takes some time to burn
        4. power you want max pressure at 15deg past TDC; Powell's Magic Angle
        5. exhaust exhaust valve opens just before BDC intake valve opens before exhaust valve closes, to help scavenge out exhaust gasses and cool the cylinder
      2. additional parts
        • intake and exhaust valve assemblies
        • camshaft
        • lifters/tappets
        • rockers
        • push rods
      3. timing

        system is positively timed to the crankshaft it drives a camshaft or a camring with a timing chain/belt or an idler gear camshaft rotates at 1/2 crankshaft speed it is vital that the valves, ignition, and pistons operate in a specific timed sequence

        1. detonation

          as load, air density, and temp increase, fuel charge may explode, rather than a controlled burn fuel ignites at the speed of sound in multiple flame fronts shocks the piston, causes damage to the engine occurs way before TDC and Powell's Angle

        2. preignition

          caused by alternative ignition source

          • glowing carbon flakes
          • feathered valve edges

          may go hand in hand with detonation gets very hot!

      4. ventilation

        ventilated by poppet valves (intake and exhaust) intake occurs BTDC to ABDC exhaust occurs BBDC to ATDC this overlap of ~75deg increases ventilation without sacrificing power during TDC and BDC, piston does little to increase crankshaft force, so it is used for ventilation instead

      5. cycle sections

        cycle comprises 720deg of crankshaft rotation

        • ventilation uses 440deg of the cycle
        • compression uses 100-120deg
        • power uses 100-120deg
        • combustion/pressure build up uses 40-60deg
    3. Diesel cycle

      no spark plugs; high compression ignites fuel diesel fuel has a lower flash point: 300F-400F however, less volatile than gasoline, safer to store fuel is sprayed straight into hot air compression ratio >13:1 has later timing, faster burn (more like an explosion) tend to be heavier, not common in aviation can be 2 or 4 stroke fuel has 15% more energy than gasoline

    3.3.4. Engine Designs

    recips come in many forms can have 1 cylinder or many may be rigged to operate with a horizontal or vertical crankshaft

    1. Inline
      1. V-type
      2. U-type

        2 rows of cylinders along the top

      3. 2 rows opposed

        most GA engines are opposed with 4-8 cylinders

      4. W-type

        3 rows along the top sort of like a radial engine, but only the top half

    2. Radial

      1 or more rows of radials stacked largest ever had 36 cylinders could have as little as 3 cylinders 1 master rod, plus articulating rods held in place with knuckle pins

    3. Rotary engines

      have a fixed crankshaft with spinning engine case

      • no air metering throttle
      • fuel is just off/on takeoff and cruise at full power, landing at no power
    4. Wankel cycle (rotary cycle)

      best know for use in Mazda RX series cars light, compact, high power-to-weight ratio found on some experimental aircraft uses eccentric rotary design 3 sided blob rotor spins

      • one face pushing from combustion
      • one exhausting
      • one intaking

      rotors can be stacked together each bank adds about 30-40hp can be air or water cooled very high rpm, generally requires geared reduction for prop

      Szorenyi Rotary Engine different type of rotary engine

      • 4 chambers
      • oblong shaped stator and bit in the center
      • rhombus rotor, chambers change size throughout cycle
    5. Brayton cycle

      aka constant flow cycle turbines excellent power-to-weight ratio moderate fuel efficiency very reliable

    6. Uncommon types

      X-shape– not common Y-shape– also very rare flat engines; just opposed engines, but very flat multiple crankshafts geared together the cylinder is between the crankshafts, pushing pistons both ways

    7. Common manufacturers
      • Allison
      • Bombardier/Rotax 2 and four cycle recips 40-100hp 2 or 4 cylinder
      • Fairchild/Ranger older
      • Franklin
      • Jacobs small to medium radial engines
      • Kinner Airplane and Motors older stuff
      • Porsche mostly on experimentals
      • Pratt & Whitney large radials Wasps also turboshafts and turbines
      • Teledyne Continental 2,4,6 cylinder engines flat opposed 40-435hph also medium radials
      • Textron Lycoming 4,6,8 cylinder engines flat opposed also 9 cylinder radial
      • Warner small radials older
      • Wright large radials Cyclones
      • Wytwornia-Sprzetu large radials used on cropdusters also owns Franklin
    8. Numbering systems

      PREFIX + CID + SUFFIX prefixes

      • F = fan cooled
      • L = left hand rotation (from back)
      • A = aerobatic oil system
      • I = fuel injected
      • G = geared
      • S = supercharged
      • V = vertically mounted (for helicopter)
      • H = horizontally mounted (for helicopter)
      • T = turbo supercharged

      cylinder arrangement prefixes

      • O = opposed
      • R = radial
      • I = inverted
      • V = V type
      • W = W-type

      CID some early engines were numbered by horsepower Types of Horsepower:
