      • indicated horsepower horsepower developped in the cylinders however, due to inefficiencies, the prop never receives full indicated hp
      • actual horsepower delivered to the propeller same as shaft horsepower

      newer systems use cubic inch displacement (CID) rounded to nearest multiple of 5 piston displacement: volume displaced by piston when it moves from BDC to TDC engine CID: piston displacement x number of pistons

      suffixes sub-model designations represent engine modifications, eg

      • engine case
      • nose/output section
      • accessory section
      • counterweights
      • magneto configuration

    3.3.5. Overhaul and Cleaning

    1. pre-overhaul
      • review logbook to get a sense of the engine's condition
        • read it carefully, pay attention
        • notice time between overhauls (TBO)
          • determined by manufacturer
          • can be updated or changed
          • reasonably accurate, not required for private operations
      • if appliances, etc, don't match TCDS, find the documentation/approval for it
      • gather all other pertinent overhaul data
        • most current manufacturer's overhaul manual
          • accessories have their own overhaul manuals
          • issued with TCDS approval
          • periodically updated
          • contains:
            • general description
            • disassembly procedures
            • cleaning procedures
            • inspection and table of limits
              • provides min/max and service limits
              • clearances
                • types of fit:
                  • L = loose large clearance, >.004"
                  • free fit moderate fit for shaft speed >600prm .002"-.004"
                  • medium fit slow shaft speeds <600rmp .0005"-.002"
                  • snug fit zero tolerance, assembled by hand
                  • push fit requires some pressure to insert -.0001" to -.0002"
                  • T = tight/interference fit negative tolerance, heating and cooling with light pressure -.0005" to -.001"
                  • force fit hot/chilled, medium pressure to assemble -.002" to -.006"
                  • shrink fit for steel only hot/chilled, high pressure must be destroyed to disassemble <-.007
            • reassembly and testing
        • most current manufacturer's parts catalog for that engine
          • illustrated parts catalog (IPC)
          • illustrated parts breakdown (IPB)
          • list assemblies by:
            • numbers
            • name
            • illustration
            • next assembly
            • availability of over/undersized parts
            • number required for engine
            • watch for usable on codes
        • historical parts manual
        • manufacturer's service bulletins
          • service letters
          • service instructions
          • any other pertinent info from manufacturer
          • available by subscription, usually available for free on website
          • contains recommended procedures/changes, plus related information
          • 'alert', 'mandatory', or 'emergency' service bulletin is for unsafe condition
          • not technically required without an AD
        • ADs make sure to narrow your search to your specific model/serial number read the AD to determine applicability may not operate a product that doesn't comply with an AD
        • Advisory Circulars (ACs) guidance or clarification for compliance with airworthiness regulations they don't change any regulatory requirement
        • FARs privileges and limitations min requirements
    2. overhaul
      1. disassembly
        • remove engine from aircraft
          • remove all lubricants and degrease it
        • remove all accessories
        • mount on stand
      2. cleaning
      3. inspection (replace/repair as needed)
      4. assembly
      5. final inspection
      6. block run test
    3. cleaning
      • primary cleaners
        • vapor/heat degreasers
          • solvent boils and the part is exposed to the cleaning vapor
          • refrigerator coils contain the solvent and dry the parts
        • ultrasonic
          • vibrating liquid causes cavitation of microscopic bubbles to clean parts
        • non-heated chemicals
        • submersion tanks
      • secondary cleaners
        • steam cleaning
          • moisture can cause corrosion
        • bead blasting
      • only use non-metallic brushes/scrubbers
    4. inspection
      • visual inspection
        • magnifying glass, mirrors
        • good lighting
        • always determine underlying cause of damage
      • magnetic particle inspection
        • magnetic polarization and magnetic dust accumulation
        • ~800-1200amps/inch of diameter
          • make sure contacts are good to avoid arcing!
        • can only be done with ferrous parts
        • magnetization can by circular or longitudinal
          • circular: current runs from end to end
          • longitudinal: current applied with high flux ring
        • dust is iron oxide red, black, green2 or florescent
          • suspended in solvent, usually kerosene
        • check 2 ways, you can miss something that is parallel to lines of flux
        • there are portable, dry sets
        • don't forget to demagnetize the part!
          • gradually remove it from alternating current field
      • dye penetrant inspection
        • ink dyes, zyglo
        • steps
          • clean
          • dye, let it sit
          • wipe off the dye
          • add the developer
        • zyglo works the same, but you need a UV light
      • alignment fixtures, jigs, surface plates, go/no go gauges
      • check dimensions, log everything
      • x-ray
        • gamma rays with photo plate
        • flouroscope: gamma ray with projection screen
      • ultrasonic: sound generator and pickup transducer
      • eddy current: magnetic flux distortions

    3.3.6. Cases and Crankshafts

    1. crankcases
      • usually made from aluminum alloys
      • horizontally opposed engines split the case vertically, along centerline
      • radials split in rings around the crankshaft
        • nose section
        • power section
        • fuel induction section
        • accessory section
      • there may be a front section for
        • gear reduction
        • torque systems
        • propeller governors
      • both types have power section, accessory case
      • radials may have a blower section for superchargers
      • crankcase functions
        • physically support engine components
        • contain lubricants
        • dissipate heat
      1. inspecting crankcase
        • visual
          • look at studs, nut flange areas, mounting flats, interior casting lines
          • abrasion, handling damage, and fretting
        • dye/flourescent penetrant inspection
          • sometimes scratches can be blasted or burnished out
          • flash grinding marks can fool you
          • some cracks can be welded, major repair
        • alignment check
          • case halves can twist, bow, or warp
          • use surface plates and straight edge to check alignment
            • may need to remove studs
        • dimensional checks
          • bearing bores
            • measure with bearing race installed and case halves mated to spec
            • check on several axis
            • damage here calls for a new case
            • may use plasti-gauge, but it's not precise or accurate
          • studs
            • installed with interference fit: .001" clearance
            • often removing it breaks it
            • replace studs when:
              • loose
              • bent
              • bad threads
              • cracked/broken
              • exposed to high temperature (eg. exhaust)
        • main and thrust bearing journals
          • plain/friction bearings
          • should not touch crankshaft; oil film separates them
            • requires oil pressure to work
          • no tolerance for surface imperfections
          • check for any sign of wear, spun bearing, etc
          • transmits oil to crankshaft rod bearings
          • must have some bearing locating device (like a tang)
          • bearings are made of steel insert with a Babbit (lead alloy) bonded surface
            • lip/flange allows it to accept thrust loads
            • must be able to withstand high axial and radial loads
      2. repairing crankcase
        • cases are match mated parts; halves cannot be interchanged internal and external machining is done with the case assembled alignment pins/dowels
        • line boring bores in machined parts that are not collinear can cause misalignment, vibration, or failure cases may be line bored/honed to repair warpage misaligned bores causes uneven bearing wear and undue stresses line boring results in a round concentric bore ensures proper crush fit for the main bearings –> proper oil pressure, improved engine life
          • main/thrust bearing saddles
            • may be machined for oversize bearings
        • throughbolts and case studs
          • fasten cylinders to cylinder decks nad clamp the halves together
            • use hold-down plates
        • stud removal
          • if broken, drill out with reverse-threaded pilot drill (make sure it's center)
          • use tapered fluted stud extractor to remove it
          • it helps to tap it with a hammer as you go
          • or, weld a washer ond nut on to turn it into a bolt
          • if all else fails, Electrical Discharge Machining (EDM) burns out the stud material sulfate of ammonia with low current slowly eats it out
        • stud/hole repair
          • threads can be repaired ith thread files or thread chaser
          • helicoil can be used in soft metals
        • stud installation
          • inspect the stud before installation
          • make sure the hole is clean
          • lubricate if necessary per manufacturer's specs
          • bolts stretch when torqued, don't reuse them
          • install by torque value or stud/deck height
          • don't use teflon tape or loctite
          • types of studs
            • anchored
            • free-through
          • make sure to tighten in proper sequence
          • torque plates should be installed any time a cylinder is removed
          • proper torque also helps seat the bearings in the saddles with proper crush fit
        • case sealing
          • on a Lycoming, use silk thread and specified sealants
            • thread passes on inside of bolts
            • don't use much sealant
          • don't use gluing/hardening type sealants
    2. crankshafts
      • types
        • flanged
        • splined
        • tapered
      1. manufacturing
        • mostly hollow for strength, weight, heat dissipation, and lubrication
        • usually heavier than car crankshafts, for extra momentum, strength
        • forged and machined from chomoly, chrome/nickel, or vanadium, steels
        • journal areas may be case-hardened by nitriding or carburizing
          • these may not be straightened; too brittle
          • improves strength and wear resistance
          • depth of ~.060"
          • carburization bone, ash, charcoal composition for source of carbon heat the part to 1300F soak it in the carbon
          • nitriding ammonia gas heated to 900F iron nitrides form on surface
        • on radials, the shaft may be in 2 pieces, with 1 piece connecting rods
      2. inpsection/repair
        • disassemble into separate parts
          • balance weights, hardware
          • gear drives
          • seals
          • oil tubes (as needed)
          • sludge chambers
          • nose plugs
        • clean by any primary or secondary method except blasting
          • remove all cleaning agents, including water!
          • lightly oil it immediately
        • inspection includes
          • visual
            • major damage, dents, gouging, scoring, fractures
            • flange/shaft threads
            • temperature markings
            • abrasion/fretting wear
            • internal pitting
            • ADs
          • magnaflux (MPI)
            • cracks are usually radial
            • should check longitudinally, too
            • degaussing is extremely critical
            • follow manufacturer's specifications
          • alignment
            • flange warp/bend– front to rear, axial runout
            • shaft bend/twist– side to side, radial runout
            • measure main bearing runout from all bearing journals
            • main bearing runout
          • dimensional
            • all bearing surfaces
              • measure on multiple axes
              • measure at different stations on each bearing surface
            • look for diameter, taper, out of round conditions
            • most journals can be ground to undersize dimensions just use oversize bearings on it -.003, -.010, -.020 (after that, you have to replace it) this can correct runout
          • surface finish
            • rod bearing surfaces will wear on TDC/BCD axis
              • motion stops/no hydrodynamic lubrication
          • crankshaft torsional absorbers
            • they are mated sets, by type certificate harmonic mode number in the notes section
            • held in by pins
            • pins held in by plates with snap rings with split facing crankshaft
          • most likely points of failure
            • stress points, risers
            • fillets
            • grooves
            • holes
            • possible manufacturing defects inclusions, non-homogeneous material
          • failure modes
            • most common in the mid length position
            • tensile failure
              • work hardened, improper heat treatment
              • torsional overload
            • shear failure
              • localized stress riser
              • improper fillet grinding
              • grinding without proper heat control causes 'heat checking'
            • other
              • high cylinder pressure can cause failure around crankpin to main journal creates high crankshaft deflections high brake mean effective pressure (BMEP) turbocharging, supercharging
              • longitudinal forces on crankshaft from thrust pulses 3-bladed propellers give impression of smoothness, but can mask high frequency vibration can cause resonance detrimental to crankshaft
        • reassembly
          • some oil tubes may be replaced in plugged or damaged
          • all dampener hardware should be replaced
          • install Welsh plugs (nose section) may call for light sealant
          • dampeners must by installed in correct location don't assume they were previously installed correcty retaining pins and hardware may differ end to end or side to side
          • driven gear should be interference heat fit make sure timing marks are correct
          • nose seal may be fit prior to case assembly must have correct lip spring avoid old seal groove
      3. parts
        • propeller flange, output attachment
          • types:
            • tapered uses a square keyway old
            • splined often seen on radials may be cut volute, or square
            • flanged 6 or 8 hole patterns 3/8"-1/2" bolts may have lightening holes, alignment pins/bushings cracks, fractures, pitting in this area is cause for rejection threads may be cleaned, flange bushings replaced
        • oil passageways
        • crank pins (connecting rod journals)
        • main/thrust bearing journals
        • crank web (cheek)
        • may have counterweights/dynamic balancers or dampeners
        • sludge chambers

    3.3.7. Connecting Rods

    • convert linear motion to rotary motion
    • opposed engines use 1 rod/cylinder
    • they are split around crankshaft and bolted together
    • radials have 1 master rod per bank articulating or link rods have attachment pins at both ends master rod is usually 1 piece, or may be split master rod travels in a circle, articulating rods travel in elliptical path
    • v engines have fork and blade type connecting rods both rods are connected to the same crank pinsthis causes uneven power pulse, loping at lower rpms 1 blade rod, 1 fork rod
    • can be I or H shaped (cross-section of the arm)
    • piston end is one piece with oilite (bronze) bushing splash fed oiling
    • generally forged or cast steel
    • may be shot peened or heat treated
    1. cleaning
      • uses primary or secondary methods scrubbing and solvents
      • NO bead blasting; surface finish is critical protective coating may affect surface hardening
      • lightly oil after cleaning
    2. inspection
      • visual
        • no nicks or scratches (do not mark)
        • no fretting
        • no abrasion
        • usually a balanced set per engine
        • for split journals, they are mated, don't put it together backwards must be installed in engine directionally due to bearing shell antispin tabs check for this on disassembly
        • look for high temperature discoloration
      • mangnaflux
        • cracks at journal bolt flares
        • across web flanges
        • journal bolts are mandatory replacement items
        • check each rod part separately
      • alignment
        • check for twist/bow between journal and pin hole
        • check for bend in rod
        • check that journal cap to rod mating flats are flush
      • dimensional
        • measure pin bushing and rod journals
          • measure journal with old rod cap bolts tightened to specs
          • measure several axes and locations in the ID
        • look for taper, out of round, and proper dimensions
        • check for sides of rod stretching or compressing
    3. repair
      • replace piston pin bushing (oilite bronze) interference fit, reamed to size
      • renew journal bearings
        • bearing insert will be slightly oversized; pinch fit
      • new connecting rod cap bolts, nuts, washers, and locking devices (every time)
      • bearing inserts should have lock tabs that fit into cap and rod cutouts a few are opposite end on same side, most are opposite end on same end (direction of rotation pushes against tab flat, making the rod directional

      Lycoming Service Instruction 1458G for correct torquing/intall of connecting rods

    3.3.8. Pistons and Rings

    1. Overview
      • usually machined from aluminum alloy forgings
        • grooves machined into outside for piston rings
      • may have cooling fins on the inside
      • fit into cylinder with clearance of .003"-.008" compression rings and oil film seal between piston and cylinder oil control rings provide lubrication flow path to cylinder walls may also have oil scraper ring at the bottom
    2. Anatomy of a piston
      1. heads
        • styles
          • flat
          • convex/domed
          • concave
          • truncated (beveled edges)
          • may have recesses to avoid interference with the valves
        • crown: top surface of the piston subjected to enormous heat
      2. body
        • styles
          • trunk simple cylinder may or may not have recessed skirt
          • slipper some material is cut away to save on weight not used for high-powered engines because they don't have adequate strength and wear resistance
        • ring grooves and lands
        • skirt
          • keeps the piston aligned in the cylinder bore
          • may have cooling fins
        • wrist pin boss gives extra support to the wrist pin
        • sides may be sort of oval-shaped for thermal relief larger diameter perpendicular to piston pin difference of .010"-.012" keeps piston from cocking or slapping when cold
        • tapered pistons may be tapered or chamfered at the top called a choked piston also allows for thermal expansion
        • interior is cleaned of cast flashing by grinding
        • interior, skirt, and bosses may be shot-peened
        • some manufacturers may indicate the propeller side of the piston with a stamp
      3. pins
        • connect piston to connecting rod
        • aka wrist pin
        • tube machined from nickel steel alloy forging
        • case-hardened, ground, and polished
        • hollow for reduced weight, cooling, lubrication, and strength
        • types
          • stationary fixed to the piston
          • semi-rigid fixed to the rod
          • full floating not fixed; most common
        • modern engines use a 'full floating' type free to rotate relative to both piston and connecting rod bearing easy to install and remove
          • must be held in place to prevent it rubbing against the cylinder walls there is a soft aluminum plug on the ends
        • must be replaced at every overhaul
    3. Cleaning
      • soak and scrub with non-abrasive materials
        • mineral spirits
        • phenolic/plastic scrapers for the tops
      • you can blast it with organic materials
      • remove all carbon, but oil staining is okay
      • clean ring grooves with custom plastic scraper
        • lexan, acrylic works well
        • 70-90deg angle works best
        • could also use broken rings, but be careful this could damage the groove, causing stress risers
    4. Inspection
      • visual look for damage check for distortion or wear make sure oil control relief holes are clear
      • dye penetrant check ring lands, pin bosses, inside and outside
      • dimensional check
    5. Rings
      • 'lands' are raised parts under a ring groove; just the not-a-groove
      • compression rings prevent gasses from escaping past the piston gasses slip behind the ring, pushing it out and reinforcing the seal number of rings depends on engine design and application also help with heat transfer from combustion process cross-section is usually rectangular or wedge shaped with a tapered face ends may be butted, stepped, or scarfed

      • oil control ring in the groove right below the compression rings 1 or more rings per piston regulate the thickness of the oil film on the cylinder walls various styles

        • slotted may have a spring or a corrugated piece in the middle
        • full bevel
        • pointed
        • chamfered
        • OD chamfers/bevels face combustion side

        wipe excess oil off the wall returns the oil to thte sump through return holes drilled in the ring groove you can't have chrome rings on a chrome cylinder

      • oil scraper ring installed in the groove at the bottom of the skirt may be installed to wipe up or down, depends on cylinder position and the engine series (top or bottom on radial engine) returns surplus oil to the crankcase
      • end gaps should be staggered

    3.3.9. Cylinders

    1. components
      • made to be identical for ease of manufacturing/assembly
      • valve assemblies, parts and hardware usually provided
      1. forged steel barrel:
        • Chromoly 4130 or 4140
        • screws into the head barrel is tapered and threaded at the top head is heated, barrel is cooled
        • hardened
          • nitriding
          • through-hardening (heat treatment)
          • chrome plating chrome cylinders don't need honed, they crack naturally
          • NiC3 plating
        • after hardening, the walls will be too smooth, then honed with cross hatching
          • good ring seating
          • good oil wetting
        • barrel may be tapered (choked) at the top accounts for thermal expansion also allows for engine wear (occurs more at the top because the piston changes directions there) 'barrel step' may develop, must be ground out reduces torsional loads on the top compression ring
        • barrels are primarily cooled by oil they also have cooling fins fins may have heat-sensitive coating that changes color when overheated
      2. base flange and skirt
        • critical area for inspection
          • flat, undamaged, no thermal scarring
          • fretting/fracture of case bolts may indicated looseness, distortion, trapped debris
        • narrow vs wide deck
          • wide deck uses regular style nuts thicker flange
          • narrow deck uses hold down plates aka banana plates internal wrench type cylinder base nuts
          • early Lycoming engines used narrow decks changed around 1964
          • not interchangeable
          • engine serial number should indicate which it is if it ends with 'A' or 'E', it is wide deck
        • skirt
          • aligns cylinder in the case assists with piston alignment
          • extended skirts reduce oil consumption and prevent hydraulic lock on inverted/radial engines sticks into the crankcase to prevent oil from flowing into the cylinder aka 'full skirt'
      3. cylinder head
        • usually die-cast aluminum alloy
          • good for weight, strength, and thermal conductivity
          • some may be forged
          • added onto it:
            • bronze or brass bushings and guides and various metals for the valve seals
            • intake and exhaust ports
            • after it is cast/forged, it is machined and the spark plug bushings, valve guides, rocker arm bushings, and valve seats are installed
        • chamber is usually semispherical for strength and to improve scavenging of exhaust gasses
        • chamber styles
          • flat has parallel valves highest compression
          • domed has angled valves strongest kind
          • peaked has angled valves
        • provides most of the convection cooling
          • needs lots of cooling fins
          • especially on hot areas (eg exhaust port)

          • very important to take good care of the fins damaged fins can cause overheating should be inspected regularly, check for

            • broken
            • bent
            • cracked

            may be reprofiled, within limits set by manufacturer

    2. color codes
      1. Lycoming
        • For cylinders:
          • between push rods or band around base of the barrel
          • gray/unpainted: standard steel barrels
          • orange stripe: chrome-plated barrels
          • blue stripe: nitrided barrels
          • green stripe: steel cylinder oversized 0.010"
          • yellow stripe: steel cylinder oversized 0.020"
          • double orange: cermicrome (obsolete)
        • For spark plugs:
          • fin area between spark plug and rocker box
          • gray/unpainted: short-reach spark plugs
          • yellow: long-reach spark plugs
      2. Continental
        • For cylinders:
          • fin tip area between spark plug and barrel or band at the bottom
          • gray/unpainted: plan steel or nitrided
          • orange: chrome
          • 2 silver stripes: NiC3, nickel carbide hardened
          • green: oversize 0.010"
          • (if it is Pratt & Whitney radial, gray may mean oversize 0.015")
          • yellow: oversize 0.020"
        • For spark plugs:
          • fin area between spark plugs and rocker box
          • gray/unpainted: short reach spark plugs
          • yellow: long reach spark plugs
    3. repair/reconditioning
      1. hardening
        • chrome doesn't require honing, naturally forms random cracks
        • nitriding
        • cermicrome (obsolete)
        • NiC3 nickel carbide (trademarked by Continental)
      2. glazed cylinders
        • composition of caramelized hydrocarbon fuels and lubricants building up on the walls of the cylinder
        • can be corrected by honing
        • must be removed to allow for proper sealing
        • it is bad when it happens on a cylinder before seating the rings if glazing occurs before any high spots are removed they will let oil slip past, rather than grind off
        • causes high oil consumption, high CHT
      3. honing
        • barrel surface cross hatched 35-45deg angle pattern
        • any scoring too deep to be fixed by honing, it will need to be ground out
        • can be done with a hand drill attachment
        • no welding!
      4. reconditioning
        • valve systems need reconditioned or replaced
        • replace all high temperature hardware (eg exhaust studs)
        • do a thorough cleaning and examination for any damage
        • spark plug holes must be repaired
      5. breaking it in
        • rings and cylinder walls must be micro-machined against each other to provide a gas tight seal and a wetable surface
        • needs grooves to hold oil, but small enough peaks to hold the oil film for rings to ride on
        • basically, use the rings to smooth out and grind down the newly honed walls makes sure that the cylinder and piston are perfectly matched to each other
        • chrome cylinders may take longer to break in
        • cermichrome break in quickly, because the material is porous
        • watch for cylinder temperature to drop and oil consumption to drop and level out; then it's broken in
        • keep cylinder pressure high enough to press rings hard against walls get the engine to high power (75% or more) and keep it there until it is broken in steps:
          1. run 1-3 minutes and inspect for leaks
          2. 20-40 min high load, low rpm run, watch temperature and pressure
          3. de-cowl, inspect immediately
          4. re-cowl, take off quickly and fly for 1-3 hrs use rich mixture, watch the CHT
          5. keep it >75% power and vary rpm load every few minutes don't take it easy! but don't let it overheat, either
        • the problem is, this high pressure isn't good for the plain bearings before they've seated in usually not a huge deal, if they're installed correctly
        • more metal particles in the oil after break in
        • probably don't use any oil additives

    3.3.10. Bearings

    1. friction
      • static friction force required to start a body moving proportional to the weight of the body
      • kinetic friction force required to keep a body sliding on a surface at a constant speed always less than static friction
      • rolling friction requires much less force
    2. bearings
      • any surface which supports or is supported by another surface
      • must be strong enough to withstand the pressure imposed on it
      • should permit one surface to move with minimum friction/wear
      • parts need to be held in close tolerances to allow for efficient, quiet operation and freedom of motion
      1. loads
        • radial force outwards eg, bearing on a rotating shaft
        • thrust axial force bearings may be tapered
        • combination
      2. types
        • plain
          • used for crankshaft, cam ring, connecting rods, and accessory drive shaft bearings
          • usually made of nonferrous metals, like silver, bronze, aluminum, alloys of copper, tin, or lead
            • oilile bearings smaller bearings to support shafts in the accesory section
          • require continuous lubrication
        • roller
          • straight or tapered
            • tapered bearings can take axial and radial loads
          • needle bearings are a variation of roller bearings, with thinner rollers
          • made of chromium steel
            • hard, strong, and corrosion-resistant
            • adaptable for heat-treated forgings
          • chrome-vanadium steel
            • 18% vanadium, 1% chromium
            • strong, tough, resistant to wear/fatigue
            • SAE 6195, used for ball and roller bearings
        • ball
          • accept radial and thrust loads
          • used for shaft bearings and some rocker arm bearings
          • deep groove ball bearings
            • used in radials to transmit propeller thrust and radial loads to the engine nose section
            • race dimensions are close to those of the balls in it
            • supports higher loads

    3.3.11. Valve Systems

    1. hydraulic lifters/tappets
      • link between camshaft and push rod
      • converts rotational motion to linear
      • older engines used solid valve lifters
        • had to be manually adjusted to set the valve clearance between rocker arm tip and valve stem tip
        • susceptible to uneven variation between cylinders
        • knocks before getting warmed up
      • modern ones automatically self-adjust using engine oil pressure
        • valve clearance (aka 'lash') maintained at zero
        • maintains a constant fuel/air mixture and engine volumetric efficiency
      1. lifter body
        • head types
          • barrel head flat on top
          • mushroom head slightly rounded
        • center is slightly offset from the cam path, causing the tappet to rotate this evens out the surface wear cam lobe may also be slightly tapered to help with this hardened rocker pad on the valve cap may also be offset may incorporate a valve rotator cap between valve and rocker
        • has one or more inner barrels and springs
          • these are charged with engine oil pressure when the valve is relaxed
          • bleed oil goes out through the push rod when the valve is actuated
        • valve clearance is set without any oil in the lifter set the lifter in the middle of its operating range on assembly
        • tappets can be reused, but should be replaced when the camshaft is
        • they need lots of lubrication lubricated by splash oil with a variable dynamic, high pressure load wiping load on the tappet head; lots of friction
      2. problems
        • tappets can get clogged/stuck causes severe top end clatter
        • head wear
          • not turning
          • spalling bits breaking off
          • pitting
          • galling surfaces friction welded together
          • scoring caused by contamination or careless assembly/disassembly
    2. camshafts
      • cast steel with plain bearings and lobes
      • some lobes do double duty
      • any scoring/spalling is cause for rejection
      • on Lycomings, they can corrode because they are not lubricated when the engine is off
        • can be reground a little, as long as it is within specs
      • driven by accesory gears, half crankshaft speed
      • determines valve duration and overlap, and firing order
      • radials use camrings
        • one camring per cylinder bank
        • 2-5 lobes per camring
    3. valves
      • parts
        • top
        • keeper groove at the top, by the tip
        • stem may be filled with sodium
        • guide bronze, aluminum and bronze, or steel
        • neck
        • head
        • face on the side with the stem
        • back the side facing into the cylinder
        • seat made of Stellite (range of cobalt-chromium alloys designed for wear resistance) alloys may also contain tungsten or molybdenum and small amount of carbon
        • margin the thickness of the valve where its ground
      • opened by rocker arm, closed by valve spring
      • springs
        • may have multiple springs to avoid harmonic resonance; valve float
        • springs held in place with caps and split retainers
        • painted end may go down
      • problems
        • carbon build up can cause stuck valves
        • valve guides wear needs to be pressed in and reamed to fit
        • corrosion from high sulfur fuels
        • burned from high cylinder temperatures
        • feathering when the margin is <1/32" leads to pre-ignition
        • stretching neck radius gets stretched beyond limits check the diameter of the valve stem with micrometer
        • warpage causes loss of compression
      • grinding
        • intake: 30deg
        • exhaust: 45deg
        • remove as little as possible
    4. rockers
      • simple first class lever
      • cup on one end for the push rod
      • hardened curved pad for the valve tip
      • pivot is oil lubricated bronze bushing should be replaced during overhaul new ones are pressed in and reamed to fit
      • rocker pin, similar to piston pin may be held in place with clips or tabs in the rocker cover lubricated by oil passing through the push rod
      • some rockers have adjusters to set valve clearance (uncommon)
    5. push rods
      • long, thin, hollow tube with hardened spherical cap
      • can wear or loosen and chafe the tube shorter
      • may come in slightly different lengths to establish initial setting for valve clearance shorter tubes have line(s) at the end
      • sealed in a push rod tube allows oil return path from rocker area
      • tinned steel, aluminum, or plastic
      • sealed with hoses or o-rings and clips
    6. valve seats
      • ground to within 1deg of valve stem tip may be ground flat and chamfered, too
      • seat must have higher angle than valve, so they meet at the outer rim
      • cut the main angle first, then inner and outer rings intake: 75deg and 15deg exhaust: 15deg
      • outer edge should be at least 1/32" from the margin, so it can seat without the edge of the valve gouging into the seat

    3.3.12. Gear Reduction

    1. overview
      • when propeller tips approach speed of sound, they loose efficiency
      • reduction gears trade speed for power engine rpm stays high, propeller rpm stays low
      • blade tip speed calculated in distance travelled per minute
      • gear reduction is ratio of engine rpm to propeller rpm
    2. types
      • spur gear
        • two flat gears with different diameters
        • big, heavy, and reverse propeller direction
        • can also be a bell/ring gear over the drive gear maintains propeller direction ratios must be significantly different even heavier
      • planetary gear
        • heavier and more expensive
        • more tooth area engagement
        • external type has 2 sun gears, with the planetary gears around both
      • beveled planetary gear
        • like external planetary gears
        • both sun gears can be the same size, or slightly different
        • one is fixed, the other is driven gear; ring gear rotates as drive gear
    3. maintenance
      • gears installed with some backlash for thermal expansion
        • may be set with a dial indicator or torque wrench
      • beveled assemblies may be assembled with preload
      • inspection
        • magnaflux
        • dye penetrant
        • wear pattern analysis
      • chipped teeth or cracks may call for replacement of entire gear set
      • larger assemblies may need balancing
      • inspect splines and keyways for fretting, rounded corners, thermal damage don't mistake heat treatment for damage
      • inspect all axial and radial bearings for wear/damage
      • replace as recommended by manufacturer

    3.3.13. Engine Timing Systems

    1. function
      • valve timing
      • ignition timing
    2. how to
      • always do the timing with the #1 cylinder at TDC of compression stroke
      • then, time camshaft to crankshaft with idler gear
        • usually just align the index marks on accessory gears and the crankshaft
        • crank gear may have a dimple or O on one tooth
        • idler gear usually has 2 teeth marked; the marked tooth on the crank gear goes in between them
        • same thing with cam to idler gear and the other idler gear
        • radial camrings use a similar marking scheme for each gear in the drive circuit
        • valve lead is degrees a valve opens before TDC or BDC valve lag is degrees a valve stays open after TDC or BDC
        • retarded valve timing causes very low power advanced valve timing can cause after-firing
        • commonly, valves open more quickly than they close these sharp ramps can cause cam wear and valve train pounding
      • next, time magnetos to crankshaft
        • review from ignitions class
        • not technically required for an annual, but it's a good idea service bulletins say to do it
        • achieve max pressure at Powell's Magic Angle: 15deg ATC
        • on aircraft, the spark is set for cruise rpm
        • timing reference marks on starter ring gear, propeller flange, or alternator drive pulley maybe on propeller reduction drive gear; need to remove a plug to open a witness hole and check
        • magneto should be internally timed to e-gap (a few degrees past neutral) before doing any of this
    3. cylinder firing order
      • make sure to run ignition leads to the correct cylinders don't just match cylinder numbers to the numbers on the distributor
      • firing order starts with 1, and is set to minimize vibration and stress
      • cylinder numbering
        • odd numbers on right
        • Lycoming: start from front (1324)
        • Continental: start from back (1423)
        • radials start at the top, count clockwise from pilot's perspective (direction of propeller rotation) with 2 banks, odds to the rear, evens to the front evens fire around in opposite direction of odds

    3.4. Propellers

    3.4.1. Regulations

    1. FAR 65.81

      A&P can perform/supervise maintenance, preventative maintenance, and alterations– EXCEPT for major repairs/alterations to propellers (or instrument work) definitions found in Part 43 App. A

    2. FAR 65.87

      A&P can return a powerplant or propeller to service after performing, supervising, or inspecting it EXCEPT major repairs/alterations to propellers can perform a 100hr inspection on engines and props and approve/return to service

    3. FAR 65.91

      IA can inspect and return to service an aircraft or any related part or appliance after major repair/alteration INCLUDES propellers can also perform annual inspection or perform/supervise a progressive inspection

    4. Prop speed/pitch limits

      FAR 23.33

      • fixed pitch propellers
        • during takeoff and climb, limit engine rpm to max takeoff rpm
        • during closed throttle glide at Vne, the propeller may not raise rpm >110% max rpm
      • controllable pitch propellers (not constant speed)
        • low and high pitch settings provisions same as fixed pitch prop range
      • controllable pitch (constant speed)
        • governor operational: prop must limit engine speed to max takeoff rpm
        • governor non-operational: prop at low pitch, mean must be provided to limit max rpm to 103%
    5. Prop markings

      FAR 45.11

      • required propeller markings– blades or hubs manufactured under a production or type certificate
        • identification by means of plate, stamping, engraving, etching, or other approved method on non-critical surface

      FAR 45.13

      • manufacture's name
      • model designation
      • serial number
      • production certificate number
    6. Feathering requirements

      only if feathering is enabled FAR 23.1153

      • feathering control needed for each propeller
      • prevent accidental feathering
      • must be able to unfeather in flight

      FAR 23.1027

      • if oil is used, there should be a supply reserved for feathering
      • must prevent sludge affecting feathering operatio

    3.4.2. Terminology

    it's just an airfoil (usually turns CCW from front)

    • leading edge
    • trailing edge
    • blade back (cambered side) we look at it like the pilot looks at it
    • blade face (flat side)
    • shank
    • tip
    • hub

    3.4.3. Types

    1. configuration on the aircraft
      1. tractor

        mounted on the front, pulls the aircraft low induced stress, rotates in undisturbed air downsides:

        • you have to look through it
        • makes the aircraft sort of nose-heavy
      2. pusher

        mounted at the rear and pushes the aircraft better visibility subject to damage from debris often mounted up high especially good for watercraft

      3. both; tractor and pusher

        eg. Cessna 337 Skymaster messes with the tail, must be split cooling can be an issue

      4. contra-rotating

        aka coaxial propeller 2 propellers on the same shaft rotate in different directions planetary gear transmission tangential airflow normally wasted is directed through rear propeller, increasing efficiency

      5. un-ducted fan (UDF)

        aka propfan turbofan thingies turbine-like blades sticking out of the engine nacelle counterrotating

    2. pitch types
      1. fixed pitch

        all one piece, pitch is set

        • increased engine power –> higher rotational speed, thrust, and drag
        • absorbs limited amount of excess engine power as aircraft speed increases
        • possible engine overspeed damage in a dive without pilot input

        durable, simple, relatively cheap and lightweight usually wood or metal may be a climb prop or a cruise prop; chosen for aircraft's principal purpose

        1. fixed pitch wood props

          metal leading edge and tipping fabric trailing edge laminated wooden blade metal hub assembly

          • parts:
            • hub
            • shank
            • laminated wood blade
            • metal tipping
            • fabric sheathing
            • tip
          • advantages:
            • better thrust to weight ratio
            • dampens vibration better
            • easy to inspect
          • disadvantages:
            • dirt/rain erosion
            • easily affected by environmental changes
            • requires more routine maintenance
            • typical bolt re-torque every 25hrs or seasonally
            • decay
          • usually made of laminated birch or maple
            • 5-9 laminations, each 3/4" thick
            • birch: good for vibration
            • maple: good for erosion
            • stronger
            • resists bending stresses, warping, moisture
            • fewer possibilities of hidden flaws
          • manufacturing
            • wood air dried and then kiln dried
            • wood selected for:
              • grain slope
              • specific gravity
              • moisture content
              • freedom from defects
            • cut laminations to a pattern
            • surfaced, sized, checked for balance
            • laminate is glued into a blank using phenolic resin
              • held under pressure 24-36hrs
              • aged in conditioning room
            • blank roughed in a jig, hung for one week
            • shaped and profiled in a carving machine
            • final carving done by hand
              • use protractors, templates to ensure contours/angles
            • bore and fit the hub
            • final sanding
            • dope on the fabric covering 12-15in from tip
              • strengthens tip
              • prevents abrasion and longitudinal splits
            • varnished
            • add metal tipping
              • .020" brass, Monel, or stainless steel
              • attached with rivets or screws
              • solder the heads and sand it smooth
            • drill small holes at the tip for moisture drainage
            • more varnish
            • stamp pertinent data
              • serial number
              • blade angle/station
              • max rpm
              • hp rating of engine
          • parameters
            • max thickness typically 30% afte on chord
            • max blade angle variation generally 1/4"
            • clean with soap and water, rinse
            • reject propeller if warped beyond clearance
            • store horizontally
              • minimize water damage
            • correct vertical imbalance with
              • washers
              • metal plate attached to hub
            • correct horizontal imbalance by:
              • solder at screw heads
              • adding varnish
          • types of mounting shafts
            • splined
              • need to inspect spline for wear with go/no go gauge
                • check front/rear cones for wear and conformity
            • tapered
            • flanged
        2. fixed pitch metal props

          forged all the way through, one piece older ones of forged steel now they mostly use forged aluminum alloy

          1. manufacturing
            • machine/hand ground and finished
            • final pitch set by twisting blades
            • balance checked and adjusted
              • horizontal: remove metal from the tips or lead wool inserted in hub boss holes
              • vertical: remove metal at boss, leading/trailing edges, or add balance weights to boss side
            • anodized to prevent corrosion and painted
          2. pros:
            • more efficient
            • better cooling than wood
          3. cons:
            • more expensive to buy, but more cost effective because they last
            • more prone to vibration
      2. ground adjustable props

        clamps at the shanks let you adjust the blade angle by hand used in many primary trainers split high-strength steel hub secured with clamps or bolts something to hold the blade hub securely, endpiece on the blade you have to make very small adjustments and make sure it's definitely good!

      3. automatic pitch

        the ends of the blades can change on their own changes in response to engine rpm and flight attitude

        • high rpm/low airspeed –> low blade angle
        • moderate power/high airspeed –> higher blade angle

        uses ATF and CTF with counterweights

        • counterweights added to augment ATF for high pitch angle
        • CTF allowed to act on propeller for low pitch angle

        not common anymore

      4. 2 position controllable pitch

        engine oil pressure extends a piston, overcoming counterweights and decreasing pitch when valve opens, oil drains from the cylinder, counterweights increase pitch controlled from the cockpit oil valve on the nose case ports oil to and from propeller

      5. electric controllable pitch
        • electric motor controlled from the cockpit
        • you can set the pitch, but there is no governor to maintain rpm
      6. constant speed propellers

        either oil-to-increase pitch or oil-to-decrease pitch propeller governor must be matched to the system governor maintains desired engine rpm usually has pilot-controlled pitch change feature (electrical or mechanical)

        1. full-feathering propeller features

          pilot operated– a feature or option as part of a constant-speed propeller system governor controlled blades rotated to a high pitch (~90deg) for when the engine is not running reduces exposed front area, propeller is less likely to windmill often requires an external accumulator

        2. reverse-pitch propeller features

          lets airplane reverse typically on the inboard engine of a 4 engine aircraft can be used for braking usually locked out by gear squat switch blade angle from -5deg to -24deg reduced cooling, potential for FOD

        3. Constant Speed Theory
          • fixed forces
            • CTF
            • counterweights may be counterweighted, or not
            • springs
              • may have single or double acting actuator
              • if single acting, may need a spring
            • air/nitrogen charge
          • variable force from governor
          • change the angle
            • use oil pressure from the governor
            • can also use some of the other, fixed forces
          1. oil to increase

            common in GA aircraft simplest, lightest, most affordable always on non-feathering propellers governor uses boosted oil pressure to increase pitch natural CTF decreases pitch, usually assisted by a spring generally not counterweighted

          2. oil to decrease

            used on feathering propellers counterweights fitted to each blade which increase pitch (with centrifugal force) ATF, spring can assist the counterweights may be used on aerobatic aircraft to protect the engine from overspeeding may also have double-acting actuator, with oil to increase and decrease

    3. tips
      • square
        • sturdier than round
      • round
      • scimitar decreases tip disturbances and induced drag
      • q-tip propeller bent tips act like winglets reduce the vortices more efficient, less noise

      blade cuffs

    3.4.4. Theory

    prop moves through the air at an angle basically generates lift, but forward

    1. Blades

      blades are twisted to account for the fact that the tip travels faster than the shank angle of attack is less near the tip blade angle– basically angle of incidence effectively, the relative wind can change the angle of attack AoA decreases as forward velocity increases climbing/diving changes it, too blade speed (rpm) pitch: related to both speed and angle how many inches the prop moves forward per rotation Turbine blades are different, but similar idea

      1. Blade Element Theory

        Dryewiechi Theory: each element must operate at its own best angle of attack to create thrust at its best design speed twist decreases from root to tip thrust is developed as a resulf of Newton's Third Law velocities vary greatly between blade stations

    2. Propeller Efficiency
      1. Pitch and slip

        geometric pitch - effective pitch = slip geometric pitch describes the ideal pitch, effective pitch is the actual pitch given that it is moving through air (effective pitch is lower) (tangent of blade angle at 75% blade station) (2pi) (blade radius at 75% blade station) = geometric pitch geometric pitch * efficiency = effective pitch

      2. Torque and power

        power is always lost to inefficiencies (heat, friction, vector changes, etc) prop efficiency = (thrust hp/torque hp)

        Prony brake can be used to measure torque

        brake horsepower is what is delivered to the propeller

        Mean Effective Pressure (MEP)

        • BMEP: brake mean effective pressure derived from brake torque
        • IBEP: indicated mean effective pressure calculated from pressure in the cylinder this is not what you will get in the real world
        • FMEP: friction mean effective pressure IBEP - BMEP MEP lost to inefficiencies
    3. Forces on a propeller
      • thrust bending forces tend to bend blades forward (like coning on a helicopter) can sort of oppose centrifugal force
      • centrifugal forces greatest force on the prop tensile stresses tries to throw blades out of the hub can be up to 7500 times weight of the blade
      • torsional stesses caused by ATF: aerodynamic twisting force tries to increase blade angleh center of pressure is forward of axis of rotation CTF: centrifugal twisting force tries to decrease blade angle much greater than ATF can be used to help decrease blade angle in variable pitch props
      • side loading
      • torque bending force the reason you always need right rudder
    4. Left-turning tendencies
      • Reaction to propeller torque propeller goes clockwise, airplane wants to turn counterclockwise this make the airplane roll
      • Propeller factor (P-factor) if airplane has a high AoA, the blades will generate more thrust on the downstroke and not much on the upstroke this makes the airplane yaw on a twin engine, the critical engine is the one that, when lost, exacerbates the p-factor problem much better to lose the other engine (usually the left), especially if it feathers this is why we have counterrotating propellers on twin engines, but that's expensive
    5. Beta

      any angle below that required for idle reverse pitch falls within the beta range

    3.4.5. In the cockpit

    1. Regs

      This is the old Part 23, but old aircraft still need to conform to it

      • FAR 23.777 Cockpit Control controls must be identified: power, prop, mixture cockpit location specified: console or overhead control order specified: power, prop, mixture
      • FAR 23.779 Motion and Effect of Control power (throttle): forward to increase thrust propeller: forward to increase rpm (and decrease pitch) mixture: forward for rich
      • FAR 23.781 Cockpit Control Knob Shape controls should be easy to distinguish by feel
      • FAR 23.925 Propeller Clearances
        • ground clearance tricycle gear 7", gear deflected tailwheel 9", gear deflected seaplanes 18", because of swells and spray
        • structural clearance 1" radial clearance from aircraft 1/2" longitudinal clearance with feathered propelleror propeller in most critical pitch position
    2. Controls

      generally, keep the prop ahead of the throttle

      • to increase, advance propeller, then advance throttle
      • to decrease, retard throttle, then retard propeller
    3. Instruments
      1. tachometer

        indicates propeller rpm

        • red radial line max permissible rpm (engine at full power, with aircraft flying ot best rate of climb) different from the static rpm, which is stationary (not specified for constant speed propellers)
        • green arc bottom end: min recommended rpm for continuous operation except in restricted ranges upper end: max rpm for continuous operation
        • yellow arc extends from max rpm for continuous operation to the max allowed rpm
        • red arc restricted range of operation avoided except to pass through as quickly as possible (resonance of engine/prop issues)
      2. manifold absolute pressure (MAP) sensor

        commonly used with constant speed propellers to assist with setting the correct amount of engine power during climb and cruise read in inches of mercury ("Hg) FAR 23.1305: required on altitude engines or engines equipped with a controllable propeller

        manifold pressure:

        • at rest, not running, MAP sensor is just a barometer
        • at idle, the throttle is mostly closed; air being drawn in by the pistons, through restricted throttle, creates a low pressure in the manifold (10-13"Hg)
        • as throttle opens, more air is allowed in and the pressure rises toward atmospheric pressure (~20"Hg)
        • at full throttle, MAP will be very close to atmospheric pressure (25-28"Hg)

    3.4.6. Governor

    1. functions
      1. boost oil pressure
      2. set propeller rpm
      3. automatically maintains rpm with variations in engine load
    2. parts
      • flyweights
      • speeder spring
      • pilot valve/spool
      • gear pump
      • pilot control
    3. how it works
      • gear pump boosts engine oil pressure a lot
      • pilot sets tension in speeder spring
      • speeder spring and flyweights (driven by centrifugal force, engine rpm) push on the pilot spool spool seeks to balance the two forces
      • pilot spool can direct boosted oil to the actuator(s) or away
      • pistons move the propeller blades

    3.4.7. Propeller manufacturers

    1. McCauley
      • non-counterweight type
        • common on Cessna
        • forged aluminum blades on a steel hub
        • designed for flanged crankshafts
        • parts
          • piston and cylinder
          • hollow piston rod, conveys oil from crankshaft
          • blade actuating link
          • blade actuating pin
          • low pitch return boost spring
        • oil filled hubs
        • ways to hold the blades
          • retention nut
          • threadless retainers
      • feathering
        • has counterweights on the outside
        • single acting
        • oil-to-decrease
        • counterweights and spring to increase pitch
        • rotating latches engaged to prevent feathering on the ground
        • rotating latches disengaged during in-flight engine shut-down to permit feather
    2. Hartzell
      • non-feathering
        • can be oil to increase or decrease
          • oil to increase doesn't have counterweights (CTF to decrease)
        • compact or steel hub
      • feathering
        • single acting
        • oil to decrease pitch
        • counterweights and springs to increase pitch and feather
        • feathering spring aids in feathering
        • unfeather provided by engine restart, feather position for blades 80-85deg
      • reversing
      • steel or aluminum (compact) hub
        • steel generally for feathering props, mechanisms are exposed
        • aluminum ones are smaller, basically it's all contained in the hub itself
      • numbering system
        • HC = constant speed
        • describes blades
        • feathering
        • counterweights
        • reversing
        • which flanges it can mount onto
    3. Sensenich
      • all fixed-pitch
      • mostly wooden
    4. Hamilton-Standard

      see it on the big radial engine in the hangar

      1. counterweighted two-position
        • engine oil pressure (not boosted)
        • either high or low, no in between
          • 2 or 3 position oil selector valve
        • has counterweights, thrown out by centrifugal force to increase pitch
        • oil to decrease
        1. Assembly groups
        2. blades
          • forged heat-treated aluminum alloy
          • steel thrust washers installed at the butt
          • semi-circular thrust bearing installed between washers
          • bushing shrunk fit in blade, bushing notches engage counterweight bracket for base angle setting
          • almuimnum plug has stud for bracket
        3. hub
          • spider aligns the blades
            • arms greased from barrel fitting
          • 2 barrels come together like a clamshell
            • phenolic barrel support to keep it from touching the spider
          • micarta spacers (pre-plastic material)
          • front cone seat 30deg, rear cone seat 15deg
            • front cone takes most of the load
            • front is steel, rear is bronze
        4. cylinder and return spring
          • cylinder moves, piston stays attached to propeller, screwed to engine shaft
            • piston acts as propeller retaining nut
            • aluminum cylinder, steel lining
            • bearing shaft rides in counterweight cam track
            • cylinder head threaded to cylinder
          • can be constant speed if you replace the valve with a governor
          • set base angle (highest pitch) with notches in the large circular end with a pin
          • counterweights attached to cylinder
          • cam slides on back of counterweight, stops can be adjusted to set range of travel
      2. counterweighted constant speed
        • basically the same as above, but paired with governor
        • governor boosts to ~180psi
        • range of either 15 or 20 degrees (as opposed to 10deg for 2-speed)
          • springs used on the 20deg version
            • springs counterwound to prevent surging
        • counterweight caps made of steel (as opposed to aluminum on 2-speed)
        • usually 3 blades, could be 2
        • can be balanced with washers or lead wool
      3. troubleshooting
        • propeller gets stuck in high pitch
          • broken oil valve
        • sluggish pitch change
          • counterweight bearings not lubricated
          • incorrect counterweight caps
          • counterweight bearing race installed upside down
        • oil leakage between piston and cylinder
          • faulty inboard piston seal
        • vibration and eccentric cylinder action
          • wear, cylinder cocked
      4. Hydromatic (23E50 single-acting feathering)
        • pressurized oil from three sources
          • engine oil, 80-100lbs applied to dome, outboard side of piston
          • governor oil, 180-200lbs applied to inboard side of piston oil-to-increase
          • auxiliary oil, 400-600lbs (for feathering and unfeathering) separate electric pump
        • piston is connected to propeller blades by a cam assembly
        1. hub and blade assembly
          • blades fit over arms of spider
          • barrel halves come together and hold everything together
          • forged aluminum blades flanges, bearings, packings at the shank bronze blade bushing holds it to the spider gear segment connects to piston assembly
          • distributor valve (or shaft extension) runs oil from engine and governor to both sides of piston it only moves during unfeathering
        2. dome assembly
          • rotating cam
          • fixed cam moves back and forth, but doesn't rotate
          • piston
          • dome shell

    3.4.8. Reduction Gearing

    • allows engine to run at higher rpm, increased power
      • wider blade chords
      • more blades
      • longer blades
    • less vibration from air compressibility
    • high tip speeds are inefficent; more slip with longer blades
    • types:
      • external/internal spur and pinion
        • driven gear and drive gear
        • external
          • needs a strong nose case due to precessive forces
          • propeller offset from crankshaft
          • propeller will turn opposite direction of crankshaft
          • quill shaft (shaft with gear in the middle, bearings on the ends) can help with torsional vibration
        • internal
          • big gear has internal teeth, pinion rides around inside it
          • takes up less space
          • both turn the same direction
      • planetary
        • sun gear, ring gear, and planet gears
        • more stable, but also heavier, more complicated
      • bevel planetary
        • bevelling increases surface area of gear faces
        • looks like a combo of the other two types

    3.4.9. Propeller Syncing

    • what?
      • for twin engines
      • fine tunes the governor/propeller speeds between the engines on multi-engines
      • similar to tuning an instrument
      • holds rpm w/i 3
    • why?
      • can cause vibration this really annoys pilots
    • how?
      • synchronizing match rpms between propellers magnetic proximity sensors (pulse counters) in the governor of each line set one as primary for reference control box takes pulse count and sends signal to control actuator actuator has a motor on a jackscrew to move arm at the top of the governor when they are in sync, motor shuts itself off +/-50rpm range (leave this synchronizer off when making power adjustments)
      • synchrophasing keep the propellers oriented in the same position they should be synchronized first helps to compensate for any vibration don't use it for takeoff/landing or whenever adjusting power Type 1 is manual, Type 2 is automatic sensors sense blade angle, send the info to the control box control box uses trimming coils to momentarily change the flyweight angle in the secondary governor

    3.4.10. Propeller Blade Tracking

    • stick attached to the wing chock wheels ignition off remove spark plug from each cylinder
    • surface table shoot for within 1/16"
    • problems: bent blade bent flange mis-torqued mounting bolts
    • correct with shims

    3.4.11. Propeller Balancing

    • engine roughness may indicate propeller imbalance
    • may be corrected by fixing tracking problems or weight distribution
    • correctable (possibly in the field) Newtons per revolution balance and track correction
    • uncorrectable (possibly in factory/repair station) Newtons per revolution with harmonic aerodynamic imbalance, worn/loose parts
    • static balance balance propeller on knife edge arbor and check it horizontal and vertical horizontal checks balance to tip vertical check balance in the hub may add solder to tip to change the weight balance washers put on bolts may use lead wool in hollow bolts can use suspension balancing, like with a tire

    • dynamic balancing propeller may be balanced alone, but have a vibration problem when mounted on the engine add sensors:

      • accelerometer in inches/second reading should be <0.1ips
      • tracker to count rpms

      check it at cruise rpm

    3.4.12. Installation

    1. tapered

      has a keyway to locate the propeller

      • 2 versions retaining nut with lock-nut safety retaining nut with snap ring helps with removal
      1. steps to install
        • clean everything well hub interior and shaft, remove any rust use fine sandpaper, emery cloth or ScotchBrite
        • coat shaft with Prussian blue transfer medium to check for a good fit check for transfer of the blue to the hub interior; min 70% no one actually does this, but it is a good idea
        • install hub and front plate on the propeller and torque the mounting nuts
        • install the key into the slot
        • add anti-sieze
        • put the propeller on
        • install the hub nut and torque it get the holes for the clevis pin lined up
        • install the snap ring over the hub nut
        • add the clevis pin with a cotter pin
    2. splined

      master spline is wider, for locating the propeller splines can wear, check with the go/no go gauge

      1. cones
        • uses front and rear cones for centering
        • steel front cone 30deg taper 2 halves are a matched set, replace them together assembled around the flanges of the retaining nut takes the thrust load
        • split bronze cone 15deg taper 1 piece
        • make sure the cones are seated properly! check with Prussian blue if the rear cone is touching the back of the propeller if not, sand down the apex of the rear cone, to allow the propeller to come further back to seat if the front cone is not making good contact, move propeller forward by installing rear cone spacers
        • installation is similar to with a tapered shaft
    3. flanged
      • 4"-8" diameter
      • mounting provisions
        • bolt holes bolts go through propeller hub and flange, to the nuts on the back
        • doweled flange holes in flange line up with dowels from the propeller hub small holes for indexing pins
        • threaded insert flange inserts press fit into the flange counterbores on propeller hub no need for nuts
      1. installation
        • make sure everything is clean and undamaged flange and propeller mounting surface check for run-out if flange could be bent inspect hardware
        • install and lube flange or shaft seal
        • check for indexing; if none, then have blades at 10:00 and 4:00 coming up on compression stroke or, 1 bolt hole ahead of the TDC mark on the flange
        • seat propeller against the flange; no gaps!
        • tighten the bolts, torque in a star pattern
        • safety with .042" safety wire
        • re-torque frequently for wooden propellers

    3.4.13. Composite blades

    1. pros
      • more flexible
      • easily repaired
      • better damage tolerance
      • better vibration dampening
      • easier to design
      • lighter
        • longer/more blades for less weight
        • smaller counterweights
        • less stress on the hub
    2. construction
      1. materials
        • fibrous material in a plastic resin aluminum shanks bonded to composite blades
        • glass fiber
        • carbon fiber
        • Kevlar
        • boron
      2. characteristics
        • strength and stiffness determined by type, diameter, and orientation of fibers
        • epoxy resin matrix supports, protects, and holds fibers
        • foam is used ot support the face and cambered surfaces, resists skin distortion
        • static discharge/lightning protection may be embedded in the skin
        • spar designs vary by manufacturer
        • nickel steel leading edge erosion shield protects from
          • water/rain
          • gravel and grit
          • birds
          • etc
    3. manufacturers
      • Hartzell
        • shear web type spar design
      • MT
      • Dowty-Rotol
        • shear web type spar design
      • Hoffmann
      • Hamilton Sundstrand
        • uses aluminum spar
    4. inspection
      • coin tap test
      • blade cuff damage
      • damage to leading edge shield
      • look for any signs of debonding, note damage limits

    3.4.14. Inspection and Repair

    • determine if it's a major repair/alteration: those are not allowed!
      • check FAR 43 Appendix A
      • err on the side of caution
    • refer to AC 43.13, ch 8 for info on propeller repairs
    1. inspection
      • cracks
      • bruises
      • scars
      • warping
      • glue failure
      • separated laminations
      • broken sections
      • finish defects
      • inspect the bolts, replace if needed
      • elongated bolt holes
      • burned finish/wood
    2. minor repairs
      • on wood propellers, touch up with varnish where it's worn coat the hub bore and bolt holes with moisture preventative, like varnish track and balance it
      • remember to retorque wooden propeller bolts every 50hrs
      • small cracks and cuts parallel to the grain can be repaired by working glue into the crack or cut
      • dents or scars can be repaired with filler composed of glue and clean, fine sawdaust
      • remove all loose and foreign matter before repairing

    3.4.15. Turboprops

    • turbine engine with reduction box and propeller
    • may have single, fixed shaft or a free turbine, split shaft
    1. fixed shaft
      • propeller driven through reduction gear assembly
      • very narrow range of available speeds
      • good control in beta range (below idle) and reverse thrust
    2. ranges
      • alpha normal operating range
      • beta pitched for below idle usually positive degrees includes reverse thrust mostly for ground operation propeller directly controlled by power lever (not governor)
    3. characteristics
      • high propulsion at low airspeeds
      • lifts heavy loads off short runways
      • best efficiency 0.6-0.8Mach range
      • best for low altitude flights (as opposed to turbines, better at high altitude)
      • more complicated and heavier than a turbojet
      • low specific fuel consumption higher efficiency at higher speeds
      • large diameter propellers; long landing gear
      • efficient in reverse thrust
    4. reduction gearing
      • turbine speeds typically ~30,000rpm, propeller ~2,000rpm
      • torquemeter senses pressure on the stationary part of the reduction assembly
    5. PT-6
      • single acting propeller control feathering and reversing
      • 2 governors
        • propeller governor
          • uses a beta valve assembly to control beta range
          • uses bleed air from FCU to control Ng overspeed
            • orifice opens to bleed CDP off FCU
        • main overspeed governor
          • protects the engine in case propeller governor failure
          • set to dump governor oil in case of overspeed
      • controls
        • condition lever
          • cut off shut off all fuel
          • low idle min Ng rmp at 50%
          • high idle min rmp at 80%
        • power level
          • alpha range
          • beta range
        • propeller lever
          • sets governor speeder spring
          • propeller governor controls speed of power turbine by changing blade pitch
            • oil-to-decrease
            • single-acting control, with spring and counterweights
          • when pulled all the way back, goes to feather
    6. Garret TPE 331
      • single acting propeller
      • can feather and reverse
      • fixed shaft turbine
      • oil-to-decrease
      • oil supplied to piston by beta tube
      • accessories
        • propeller governor
        • underspeed governor
        • propeller pitch control unit around beta tube
        • feathering valve
      • controls
        • power lever controls fuel in alpha range controls blade angle in beta range has flight idle/ground position
        • condition lever controls governor in alpha range controls underspeed governor in beta range to maintain fuel flow shutoff position at bottom of travel
      • autofeather
        • armed during takeoff and landing (power lever >90%)
        • senses engine torque
          • <17%, it is disarmed on the good engine
          • <10%, it dumps oil from failed engine's governor
        • feathering spring and counterweights feather the propeller

    3.5. Turbines

    3.5.1. History of turbines

    1. Ancient Greece

      first steam engine

    2. 1600-1900

      inventors toyed with steam boiler turbine systems reciprocating engine turbine driven supercharger was developed

    3. 20s and 30s

      Whittle, Heinkel, Campini, and GE were deveolping the tech

    4. late 30s, early 40s

      first turbine flights

    5. first turbojet aircraft

      ME262 fighter made in Germany in WWII two 1000lb thrust engines 400+mph 1600 were built and flown

    6. first US turbine jet

      GE made the first one then, Westinghouse was contracted to build a new 'all American' version

    7. mid 40s

      more companies joined in:

      • Detroit Diesel Allison
      • Garrett Ai Research (now Honeywell)
      • Boeing
      • Teledyne CAE (Continental Aviation)
      • Avco/Textron Lycoming
      • Pratt & Whitney
      • Solar Turbines
      • Westinghouse
      • Wright Bros
      • General Electric
    8. Current manufacturers
      • GE
      • Pratt & Whitney
      • Rolls-Royce Aero
      • Honeywell
      • Williams International
      • BMW Roll-Royce
      • CFM
      • IAE

    3.5.2. Basic theory of operation

    1. overview

      lots of high velocity gasses expelled backwards from engine resultant thrust moves aircraft rocket is a simple version of this; expanding gasses in confined space released in one direction

    2. compression

      a true turbine engine has a high volume air pump which draws air in the air is compressed and accelerated fuel is added and it ignites

    3. combustion

      compressor packs several tons of air into the combustion chamber every minute (works as a series of fans) fuel is forced into the combustion chamber by the nozzle as a mist spark provides ignition and mixture burns similar process to a blowtorch creates very hot gasses

    4. turbine

      gas temp increases, causing gas expansion forces this spins a turbine wheel the turbine bootstraps the compressor

    5. exhaust

      gasses eject out the back, causing thrust seems sort of like a perpetual motion machine, but energy needs to be added with fuel

    6. efficiency

      some energy is spent driving the turbine, the rest generates thrust significant losses to friction and heat (70-80%)

    3.5.3. Basic Laws

    1. Thermodynamics

      the study of heat energy

      1. energy

        the capacity to do work

        • potential
        • kinetic

        energy is conserved

      2. heat vs temp

        they are independent of each other temperature: state of molecular activity heat: transfer of energy heat can be measured in BTUs or calories heat can be transferred through conduction, convection, or radiation

    2. Newton
      1. inertia

        a body at rest will remain at rest, and a body in motion will remain in motion unless acted upon by an outside force

      2. F=ma
      3. action –> reaction

        for every action, there is an equal and opposite reaction

    3. General Gas Law
      1. Charles

        given constant pressure, a gas's temperature is directly proportional to its volume it is a direct ratio

      2. Gay-Lussac

        given constant volume, a gas's temperature is directly proportional to its pressure direct ratio

      3. Boyle

        given constant temperature, a gas's volume is inversely proportional to its pressure direct ratio

    4. Avogadro

      any gas molecule will occupy the same volume at equal temperature and pressure even though the mass of different molecules is different therefore, displacing massive molecules with less massive ones will lower density (given constant pressure and temperature)

    5. Pascal

      pressure of a confined fluid is equal and undiminished at every point force is exerted at right angles to the interior of the confinement, regardless of shape

    6. Venturi

      fluid flowing through a constriction will decrease pressure and temperature and increase velocity can be used to measure mass of flow on a known venturi with differential pressure sensor if flow or density increases, the pressure differential will increase ram air from the engine inlet can increase both density and flow

    3.5.4. Ducting systems

    1. general
