      in any fluid ducting system, flow results from a pressure differential; fluid flow high to low pressure tapered closed ducting systems will affect pressure, temperature, and velocity engine inlet and outlet pressures must be different for flow to exist

    2. types
      1. Converging

        pressure and temperature decrease, and velocity increases

      2. Diverging

        pressure and temperature increase, and velocity decreases

      3. Combined
    3. in turbines

      engine rotor stage = 1 rotor/stator combo each stage uses ducting (converging, diverging, or both) to modify airflow, pressure, temperature, and velocity

      Compression stage: converging duct, rotors draw air in and increase velocity compressor is a series of diverging ducts within a large converging duct each rotor stage increases velocity across the blades each stator vane trades velocity for pressure and directs air to the next rotor stage then, flow diverges and velocity increases for combustion (when obviously the temperature will increase a lot)

      Combustion stage temperature increases, raising pressure

      Turbine stage: diverging duct trade all that pressure for velocity across the turbine blades and exhaust the gasses it is important that the gas moves out quickly to avoid stalling

      Afterburner: converging to increase velocity only if needed high velocity to exert thrust against ambient air

    3.5.5. Thrust vs rpm v hp

    1. units

      thrust

      • measured in lbs or kN
        • 1 lb = 4.448 Newtons
      • measure of force

      power

      • measured in shaft horsepower
      • measure of work over time
    2. application

      inlet ram can affect density, which changes the amount of work the engine does at different velocities and air pressures, the turbine engine will perform differently

      on reciprocating engines, hp and rpm are basically linked on turbine engines, the engine speed (%rpm), air density, and speed all affect the engine's power output

    3. %rpm

      thrust/throttle settings are usually expressed in terms of %rpm

      • rpm operating ranges vary widely between different engines red line = 100%rpm rated thrust may be over 100%rpm
      • thrust has a non-linear relationship with rpm idle speed can be 60% of rpm, with 75% power at 92%rpm related to the 'cascade effect'

      %rpm vs %thrust will be similar between different engines

    4. thrust output

      you can measure thrust output by comparing inlet and outlet pressure EPR: engine pressure ratio may vary widely between different engines

    3.5.6. Safety

    1. high velocity air

      inlet and outlet 25ft in front in 180deg arc at idle, air from the rear is going 120mph or more even 50ft away at full power, it will exceed 700mph at 50ft

    2. high temperature

      outlet can by 300-1000deg within 50ft stay out of the 50deg arc about the centerline to the rear of the aircraft

    3. noise

      all around high danger areas at 45deg from the centerline on inlet or outlet side both can permanently damage hearing noise damage accrues chronically ear protection is necessary, always, even if it doesn't seem too loud

    4. FOD

      inlet and sides common inlet accidents involve objects being drawn in from around the cowl sides including water, rocks, tools, birds, and people don't leave anything loose nearby can also include internal engine parts coming out the rear; stay away! engine cowls often have red lines to indicate hazard zones

    3.5.7. Common designs

    1. Centrifugal flow

      1 and 2 stage (single or double flow) air moves from center out to the rim of the rotor impeller and diffuser (same principle as rotor and stator in axial flow) flow can by parallel for higher volume, or double-staged for higher pressure each stage of centrifugal compressor produces much greater compression than a stage of axial flow but, with axial flow you can stack more stages together

      pros:

      • easier to build
      • cheaper
      • smaller units can be easily mass produced
      • simpler maintenance
      • newer ceramic designs can compete with axial flow

      cons:

      • large frontal area
      • bigger units are very heavy
      • hard to design for large power outputs
    2. Axial flow

      air flows axially through it

      pros:

      • smaller frontal area
      • greater overall compression ratio (can be over 46:1)
      • higher overall power output
      • higher bypass ratios
      • very efficient

      cons:

      • more expensive
      • complex to manufacture
      • higher cost to overhaul
      • very sensitive to FOD
      • sensitive to unusual operation parameters
    3. multi-stage engines

      typically, axial flow engines have multiple stages

    4. reverse flow

      some versions will have the flow reverse through the diffuser and combustion sections often associated with centrifugal compressors it shortens the length of the engine, like it's folded up common on helicopters and GPUs, other small craft

      pros:

      • compact design
      • lightweight
      • takes advantage of centrifugal compressor flow paths

      cons:

      • robs some kinetic energy from the flow with direction changes
      • limited overall power output (inefficient at high power settings)
    5. low bypass– turbojets

      this was an early design, not common most or all of the air flows through the combustion/turbine chambers gas returns to compressor after combustion stage

      pros:

      • very high thrust velocity
      • good for supersonic flight
      • small frontal area
      • maximizes use of ram air at high velocities
      • high thrust to weight ratio

      cons:

      • inefficient at low speeds and altitudes
      • fuel inefficient
      • long take-offs
      • very noisy
    6. medium bypass

      quieter and more fuel efficient than low bypass also higher thrust ratings and better performance over a wide range of altitudes

    7. high bypass– ducted turbofans

      newer design to save fuel and reduce noise pretty standard on airliners anything with bypass ratio greater than 3:1 (usually higher)

      • pros:
        • can be >12:1
        • good low-mid speed power
        • specific fuel consumption and weight moderate
        • good short takeoff characteristics
        • very high power ~185,000lbs of thrust
      • cons:
        • large frontal area
          • vulnerable to FOD
        • can't exceed mach 1
    8. geared output– turboprops

      common for propeller-driven applications, ground power, and ground transportation has a gear-reduction box for the propeller

      1. turboprops
        • pros:
          • best specific fuel consumption
          • great low speed power/thrust
          • great reverse thrust
        • cons:
          • max speed ~300kts
          • need large ground clearance for large propeller
          • complicated, expensive, heavy gear boxes
        • often has split turbines
          • one for gas production
          • one for geared power production
      2. turboshaft

        generally helicopters

    9. split spool rotors

      • different rotor speeds in different turbine sections to maximize efficiency

        • on turbojets, it is just to maximize airflow
        • on turbofans, N1 spool is power turbine, N2 is gas producing turbine
        • turboshaft or turboprop will use N1 for shaft power, N2 for gas producing
        • split rotor spoll shafts connect compressors and turbines via tubular shaft inside a larger shaft
          • inner spool connects front compressor to rear turbine; N1 (low pressure)
          • outer spool connects rear compressor and front turbine; N2 (high pressure)
        • in turboshaft/prop, the gas producer drive the compressor; Ng
          • power turbine drives geared shaft output to rotor blades or propeller; Np

    3.5.8. Subsystems

    1. inlet construction & configuration
      • typical inlet design is determined by aircraft mission profile
      • primary goals
        • max inlet pressure from inlet airstream
        • min turbulence in all conditions/attitudes
      • factors in this are
        • airspeed and attitude
        • ducting friction
        • weather
        • aircraft maneuverability
        • location of engine on aircraft
      • inlet surface/shape is critical to performance
        • surface imperfections can cause serious drop in performance
        • friction/turbulence can cause loss of inlet pressure
        • long ducts, sharp turns, poor repairs, etc are bad
      • other concerns:
        • anti-icing
        • noise
        • structural support
        • FOD protection
      • some inlet designs
        • most common design is single entrance, short duct
        • some have blow-in doors that open during high power slow flight
          • during high speed flight, internal ram air pressure closes them
        • divided duct
          • used mostly on military aircraft with mid/aft mounted engines
          • half on each side of the fuselage (?)
        • compressor center cone/spike is part of the inlet (like a spinner)
          • shapes airflow around the engine core
          • subsonic ducts are a diverging duct up to the point of the compressor spike then, it becomes a cylindrical converging duct
          • subsonic spikes are short and stubby supersonic spikes are longer and may be movable, fore and aft you can slow supersonic air to subsonic speeds:
            • variable ramps to cause oblique and normal shock waves slow down airflow
            • converging duct to reduce to mach 1, then diverging duct to further reduce speed and increase pressure
              • usually use a variable geometry duct
              • this give max efficiency in subsonic, transonic, and supersonic flight
        • back in the day, they used screens to protect aluminum compressor blades
          • stronger blades made those obsolete except for helos and GPUs
          • helicopters usually use a centrifugal flow particle separator instead of a screen
        • inlet may be anywhere on the airframe; below, above, behind engine inlet
          • ducts direct air from inlet to compressor
      • preferrably, inlet will receive airflow separated from aircraft airstream
        • may have flow plate, or by suspended away from the airframe and attached by a pylon
      • every 1% loss of efficiency at inlet = ~1.5% loss at outlet
      • maintenance
        • follow manufacturer's recommendations
        • inspect for FOD
        • check all systems and moving parts
          • blow-in doors
          • anti-ice systems
          • variable inlet components
    2. compressor section
      • centrifugal compressor
        • parts
          • impeller
            • drives air out, speeds it up
            • may be balanced by removing weight
            • machined from cast titanium, high nickel steel, or ceramic castings
            • may have reversed exducer lip to decrease shockwave from air exiting into the diffuser ducts
          • diffuser
            • slows air down, converts velocity to pressure
            • also is a high pressure barrier wall to downstream combustion gas
            • set of guide vanes radiating out in a spiral from impeller
            • they feed into a common collector scroll or duct to feed air to combustion chamber
            • highest pressure area in the engine
            • air may be bled off here to prevent compressor surge or other functions
            • usually cast aluminum
              • some stainless steel parts in high-wear areas
            • may also support front inlet of the combustor
              • mounting for fuel nozzles, fuel manifolds, igniters, and inspection ports
          • compressor manifold
            • diverts airflow from diffuser to combustion chamber
        • one or two stages (can't really do more)
          • each centrifugal stage produces much greater compression than an axial stage
            • each stage compression is 6:1 or 7:1
          • may be combined with axial stages
      • axial compressor
        • air pressure and temperature rise continually, velocity doesn't really rise
        • each rotor/stator pair is one stage of compression
          • stator section includes external compressor housing, seals, and stator blades
            • may be a stack of discs, or partial circumference shells
          • rotors are stacked discs with blades attached to central hubs
            • blade attach designs:
              • fir tree
              • dove tail
              • these allow for heat conduction, thermal expansion, and reduced vibration
            • blades are cast, forged, or machined
              • forging better for strength, casting better for detail
        • blade inspection/repair:
          • chord and span dimensional checks
          • NDT inspections for internal failures, occlusions, scratches
          • damages blending to save blades and vanes from rejection
          • shingling blades distorted to point that shroud support wings overlap
          • different areas of the blade have different tolerances for repair, refer to manual
          • basic inspections done in the field, but a repair station will take care of most damage
        • rotor is an airflow, it increases velocity
        • engines may have containment rings to deflect any bits that go flying off in a safer direction
        • stator is a divergent duct, decreases velocity to increase pressure
        • cascade effect pressure and temperature continue to rise at each stage
        • modern engines can exceed 40:1 compression ratio
        • at each stage, blades and vanes are placed closer together
        • modern engines use longer, stronger blades to reduce number of stages
        • problems:
          • FOD: anything that goes in causes serious problems airflow can degrade even from normal blade/vane wear
          • compressor stalls/surges result of uneven progressive pressure ratio pressure ratios should increase progressively from stage to stage
          • choking stalls low rpm causes lower compression at the rear of compressor at lower compression ratios, the volume of air at the rear of the compressor is high and airflow through the engine is low low airflow through the engine will decrease unless engine is accelerated in low flow, the front stages of compression lose efficiency low compression ratio –> low rotor speed :( happens due to low air density (hot day, high altitude) may also be caused by disrputed airflow (damage, FOD, wear)
          • acceleration stalls too rapid increase of airflow –> increase AoA of airfoil compressor blades stall, compression is lost in extreme cases, combustion gasses flow forward
        • ways to regulate progressive compressor ratios
          • lower (de-rate) AoA on front stages
          • bleed air control valve on middle or rear stage temporarily lowers internal pressure
          • split compressor into 2 rotors to allow some variable float in pressure ratio
          • VIGV (variable inlet guide vanes) or VGV (variable guide vanes) to change the AoA over blades
          • variable area exhaust nozzle to unload compressor
          • VSV (variable stator vane) rotates/pivots to change the relative AoA
    3. combustion section
      1. burner section (liner)
        • functions
          • add energy to air with fuel
          • contain combustion
          • discharge hot gasses to turbine
            • cool air and hot gasses must be well mixed to consistent temperatures
            • must shape airflow going into turbine nozzle guide vanes (NGVs)
        • fabrication
          • fabricated from high nickel stainless steel alloys: Monel or Hastelloy
          • must withstand temperatures >3000F
          • perforated to allow airflow over/through liner surfaces
          • may be coated with heat-reflective, abrasion-resistant ceramic
          • convergent duct between burner and turbine inlet to maximize velocity
        • fuel nozzles installed in the diffuser end of burners, near igniters
          • provide shaped and atomized fuel mist in a fine conical or annual pattern
        • shapes cooling airstream to prevent metal from burning
          • reflects radiant heat back into the hot airflow
        • must complete all combustion before reachig the outlet
        • types:
          • can
          • annular
          • can annular
        • maintenance
          • inspect for heat damage
            • hot streaks
            • cracks
            • burn holes
            • surface distortion
            • carbon deposits
          • on-wing inspection done with a boroscope
      2. nozzle guide vanes
        • direct hot combustion gasses to first stage turbine
        • subsequent stage vanes are numbered and called nozzle vanes
        • airflow here is at or near speed of sound
        • this is the hottest section of the engine
        • inspection:
          • burn through: poorly atomized fuel
          • sulfidation: chemical corrosion
        • manufacture
          • cast or forged with internal passages and pin holes (gill holes) for cooling
            • disburses cooling air as a shield along the surface of vanes
              • transpiration cooling
              • can allow for temperature increase from 1800F to 2500F
              • may also be used for turbine blades
            • commonly coated with ceramic to resist heat, corrosion, and erosion
    4. turbine section
      • function
        • extract energy from combustion airflow and convert it to kinetic energy
        • can be up to 375hp each blade
        • 75% of power drives compressor, the rest moves the aircraft
      1. blades
        • impulse
          • looks like a crescent
          • air moves in it, swish
          • equal inlet and outlet area
            • velocity remains pretty steady
            • temperature drops
          • airflow pushes in blade cup
        • reaction
          • looks more like an airfoil
          • loss of velocity and temperature to impulse and reaction forces
          • kinetic energy from impact force and Bernoulli's principle
        • reaction-impulse
          • impulse root, reaction tip
          • most common because tip speeds are greater
          • they are longer
          • maintain air velocity and pressure along span of blade
          • extra lift from reaction section also counteracts centrifugal spin of expanding air outwards
          • maximizes speed wthin a narrow rpm range
            • this is why turbines produce most of their power in the last 10% of their rpm range
        • similar construction to nozzle guide vanes
          • may use transpiration cooling
        • attached by fir tree or dove tail mounts
        • may have shrouded tips
          • better sealing with knife edge air seals
          • thinner interlocking blades with less distortion
          • non-shrouded tips (squealer tips) have thin edges that cut themselves into a close seal with the outer case
        • as blades age, they creep
          • get longer from centrifugal force and heat
          • outer seal is meant to wear off to accomodate this
        • blade and guide vane can sulfidate
      2. turbine
        • commonly have a containment ring in case a piece goes flying off
        • maintenance includes
          • visual inspection
          • NDT
          • blade measurement
          • if you replace a blade, rebalance it and/or use a new blade with same mass and moment
          • hub/disk inspection is critical
            • cracking around blade attachment slots
            • heat scoring
            • distortion
            • visual, eddy current, FPI, x-ray, and MPI inspections
    5. thrust reversers and exhaust sections
      1. exhaust sections
        • functions of exhaust system:
          • shape air flow, pressure, and velocity to maximize thrust and reduce noise
            • noise suppression is becoming increasingly important
            • mix hot stream gasses with cold stream air
            • hot stream gas diffusers mix cooling air into exhaust gasses
            • acoustical liners
            • supersonic aircraft use a diverging duct to further increase supersonic flow
              • usually a variable nozzle to allow for exhaust transistion to supersonic velocity
        • may use converging duct – pure jet
        • may use straight or diverging duct – turbofan or turboprop
      2. thrust reversers
        • used for dynamic braking
        • can generate up to 1/2 full thrust in reverse thrust
        • types:
          • Cascade
            • has a translating sleeve
            • looks like the nacelle splits in two
            • blocks flow out the back and redirects it
          • Clam shell
            • looks like a mouth on the back of the engine that closes
            • like a sail that catches the exhaust gasses
        • turboprop engines may also allow for reverse thrust
          • reverse thrust is called 'beta range' of the throttle motion
          • most enabled by squat switch
            • some can produce beta thrust in flight, to slow down
          • propeller is pitched to reverse thrust
    6. augmentation
      1. afterburner
        • extended exhaust section with fuel nozzles
        • provides rocket action in the exhaust section
        • must be long enough for fuel to to burn completely at subsonic airspeed
        • exhaust then has 2-3x as much gas to propel out the rear of exhaust nozzle
      2. injection
        • some may inject water to increase exhaust mass density
        • not used in commercial aviation
        • may include vectored thrust to increase agility
    7. seals, shafts, bearings, and housings
      1. bearings
        • support rotating shaft
        • maximum performance for minimum weight
        • loads up to 1500lbs on each roller/ball
        • turbine bearings are usually ball or roller bearings
          • tapered/needle bearings not used; they fall apart
        • bearing cages silver oxide plated for corrosion resistance in high temperature areas
        • balls/rollers ride on oil film, only a few microns thick
        • bearing debris may be found by oil analysis or chip detector
        • possible defects:
          • slivers hair-like slivers or magnetic material indicates wearing interference on new or rebuilt engines
          • flakes found in filters or chip detector paper thin, shiny chips may indicate bearing spalling
          • chunks found in filters or chip detector 3 dimensional particles may indicate bearing or race is failing
          • debris usually found in filters or chip detector fuzz-like particles normal, found during day-to-day operations
        • visual inspection
          • often use lab microscope
          • check for:
            • general condition
            • discoloration and overheating
            • spalling; flaking metal
            • skidding and scoring
            • ball and roller concentricity
            • ball/roller surface finish
      2. seals
        • types
          • air to air
            • stops air from bypassing compressors or turbines
            • usually incorporated into the outer edge of a rotor blade shroud
            • knife edge that wears into a honeycomb seal ring
            • seal ring surface also called 'abradable liner' creates tight seal between blade tip and seal ring, even with blade creep a kind of 'labyrinth seal'
            • labyrinth seals allow some slight leakage
              • they create a series of knife edges that don't quite make contact with adjoining surface
          • air to oil
            • keeps oil in the lubrication system
            • types:
              • carbon seal to stell seal plate race
                • smooth carbon surface rubs against polished, hardened steel race
                • similar to a plain bearing
                • carbon powder mixes with oil film to lubricate and create a seal

              • pressurized air/oil these can be non-rubbing labyrinth seals or rubbing contact seals

                • prossurized air/oil seals uses labyrinth seals and bleed air to keep oil in an inner chamber or bearing compartment

                full contact seals are for critical seals that cannot tolerate any leakage

          • brush
            • hybrid air to air/air to oil
            • like a labyrinth seal, but lightly contacts the rotor or shaft
            • densely packed very fine wire filaments
            • wires can flex in contact with rotor shaft
          • mechanical
            • fluids and gasses
            • surface seals, lip seals, or conical tube seals
              • surface seal– crush seals uses a pre-formed internal lip or ridge seals by direct contact may be ceramic, metal, synthetic rubber
              • lip seal or Garlock seal retains fluids around turning shafts pre-formed synthetic lip seal surface rides on shaft surface normally uses spring tension behind the lip to hold it against the shaft
              • conical tube seal used with compression nut, retainer, and fluid fittings seals fluid lines (fuel and oil) used to repair damaged AN fittings?
            • maintenance is critical
              • can by damaged by dropping
              • orientation may be critical
              • should be lubricated when installed
              • avoid dirt contamination
        • inspection
          • visual and dimensional
          • flexible seals are consumable; must always be replaced
          • rubber seals will harden/leak with age
            • may attract abrasive dirt
            • wear or grooving may occur on rotating shafts
    8. gear boxes
      1. function
        • reduce high rotor shaft speeds for driven accessories
        • for turboshaft and turboprop power outputs for a propeller or rotor system that requires much lower shaft speed
      2. types
        • spur
          • internal
          • external
          • beveled gear
        • planetary
          • internal
          • external
          • beveled gear
      3. maintenance
        • general inspection critical areas for NDT inspection MPI, dye penetrant, wear pattern analysis inspect all splines and keyways for fretting, rounded corners, thermal damage inspect all axial and radial bearings for wear/damage
        • oil servicing/analysis
        • chip detection magnetic detector in the oil sump chipped teeth or cracks may call for replacement of entire gear set; some are matched sets
        • life limited parts
        • harmonic sensors
      4. overview
        • should be assembled with some backlash for thermal expansion set with a dial indicator
          • may be the opposite on beveled assemblies; preloaded set with a torque wrench
        • bigger gear and ring assemblies may require dynamic balancing
        • planetary gear repair is a major repair
        • some systems use a quill shaft, a long shaft that allows flexing to help with vibration
    9. torque monitoring systems
      1. Regs
        • required on all torque producting engines
          • turboprop
          • turboshaft
        • one of the primary aircraft engine performance instruments in the cockpit
          • equivalent to the tach on a reciprocating engine
        • must be working and calibrated
      2. how it works
        • receives input from torque sensing unit
        • displays as
          • torque oil pressure (psi or ft-lbs)
          • torque percent
          • horsepower
      3. types
        • quill shaft, or phase shift uses a flexible inner shaft and an outer reference tube they are connected at only one end; indicator magnets, or proximity sensors, are attached at the other
        • hydro-mechanical 'balanced oil piston' connected to power output shaft senses the force of the propeller resisting the acceleration of the engine fixed gear is allowed to twist slightly with torque 2 types, both using hydraulic pressure:
          • pistons
          • ball detents
    10. starter systems
      1. function
        • to get the engine spinning fast enough to get continuous combustion
        • can provide pneumatic pressure electrical energy, hydraulic pressure
      2. types
        • pneumatic heats rapidly air impingement systems start from compressed air supplied on the ground low pressure (30-50psi) but high volume called 'huffer cart' may start one engine like this, then use that engine to start the other one; cross bleed air
        • electric
          • heats rapidly
        • electric
        • small APU very common, use a ground power cart or a small turbine installed on the aircraft
          • hydraulic
          • eg Chinook helicopters
          • use accumulator to start an APU
          • hydraulic pump on APU powers a starter motor on the engine
        • jet fuel starter
          • eg Harrier, F-16
          • electric starter starts a small turbine in the JFS
          • JFS linked by clutch to the main engine
        • cartridge start or multi-breach starter
          • obsolete since the 80s
          • smaller turbines
          • mostly military
          • cordite cartridge fuel was unstable and aged poorly
        • isopropyl nitrate starter
      3. how to start
        • may include pre-warm igniter, should make snapping noise
        • spool to 8-18%rpm with ignition ON
        • turn on fuel
        • hold starter and wait for engine to accelerate
        • release starter at 40-50% engine rpm
        • shut off fuel and motor (starter only– no fuel or ignition) engine if hot or hung start
        • can take 40-60secs
    11. fuel and control
      1. purpose
        • deliver fuel to burner
          • correct amount for all operating ranges
          • correct state fine droplets in a cone spray
      2. fuel
        • jet fuel vs avgas more viscous ignites at lower temperature absorbs water easily 3x the BTUS less volatile
        • metering fuel is the only way to control power output of a turbine engine on reciprocating engines, we meter the airflow to control power
        • turbines are slow to respond to a change in power/rpm large masses (spool mass and air mass) cause rotors to respond slowly
      3. fuel control mechanism
        • must be able to deliver correct amount without over- or under-fueling over fueling could cause overtemp or stall under fueling could cause a hung start, rpm isn't high enough to produce acceleration power must be consistent in acceleration, deceleration, or steady power
        • needs plenty of filtration can be down to 5 microns
        • may use fuel to cool oil and internal working of accessories this also heats the fuel
        • fuel may be used to lubricate engine accessories
        • may use dump valve control for engine shut down and to drain fuel manifold when fuel inlet solenoid shuts off, the dump valve solenoid opens dumps rail fuel back into the system
        • metered fuel based on:
          • throttle position
          • inlet temperature
          • inlet pressure
        1. types
          1. mechanical

            basically obsolete

          2. electrical
          3. electronic– FADEC

            lots of sensors

      4. fuel injection
        • fuel must be atomized consistently at all speeds must be in a conical spray
        • combustion must be complete before gasses reach the turbine
        • nozzles
          • simplex on or off
          • duplex primary and secondary flows has 2 feeds from the fuel control; may use flow divider in the manifold or in the fuel control unit
          • pressure controls fuel volume
          • shuts off fuel flow when engine is turned off
          • may have 'chopper' to create turbulence; helps atomize the fuel in larger nozzles
      5. fuel pump
        • types
          • in-tank boost pump
          • engine driven pump
          • mechanical fuel control pump
        • systems will have distribution manifolds (rails) made of stainless steel tubing and flex hoses
      6. system components
        • pressurized tank with boost pump
        • low pressure fuel filter
        • shut off valve
        • engine driven fuel pump
        • main filter
        • pressure regulator
        • cooler/heater
        • control/throttle
        • manifolds, flow dividers
        • fuel nozzles
        • dumps, drains, return lines
    12. control systems and sensors
      • monitor and control systems (mainly fuel system) also ignition, emergency, thrust reversing, and afterburner systems
      • basic units
        • input sensors
          • temperature at different stages, over time
          • pressure
          • throttle position
          • fuel flow
          • ambient conditions
        • controllers
        • output actuators
          • fuel flow
          • engine monitoring data and faults
          • ignition starting
          • accessory controls

      • now, generally Full Authority Digital Electronic Control (FADEC) unit

        • controls:
          • EPR, engine pressure ratio
          • PLA, power level angle, throttle position
          • N1, low pressure rotor speed
          • N2, high pressure rotor speed
          • P2 and T2, inlet pressure and temperature
          • T12, fan inlet temperature
          • Ps13, fan discharge pressure
          • P25, HPC inlet pressure
          • EGT, exhaust gas temperature

        not serviceable by mechanics; line replaceable unit! early models had hydro-mechanical controls some electrical systems were developed others:

        • electronic engine control (EEC)
        • monitoring engine control (MEC)
        • primary monitoring control (PMC)
    13. lubrication systems
      1. oil
        • only use synthetic turbine oil try not to mix brands always check the specs eg BP 2380
        • viscosity must be consistent and low, even when cold or very hot
        • must be able to retain lubricating/cooling qualities at very high temperatures regular mineral oil breaks down a 470F
        • some types
          • MIL-O-6081 mineral based 300F
          • MIL-L-7808 synthetic ester 400F type 1
          • MIL-PRF-23699 synthetic ester 450F type 2
          • Aeroshell 560 or Mobil 254 synthetic ester type 3
        • generally, the different types should be cross-compatible, but don't risk it
        • synthetic oils
          • will get thicker, darker with age
          • has ~4yrs shelf life; rotate stocks
          • may attack some paint finishes; careful of any spills
      2. purpose
        • primary: prevent bearing/accessory damage from friction
        • cooling
        • anti-ice
        • corrosion control
        • hydraulic power (sometimes)
      3. subsystems
        • pressure
          • flows from tank to main pump by gravity
          • pump delivers oil to bearings and accessory drive
            • must pass through filter first
          • fuel/oil heat exchanger cools down hot oil has a built-in bypass valve
          • flows through last-chance strainers to spray nozzle
          • pressurized, cool, clean oil sprays into bearing compartments onto the bearings
        • scavenge
          • collects oil from bearing compartments and accessory gearbox to return to oil tank
          • gear type scavenge pump sends oil through return lines
            • may be integral with the main pressure pump
          • oil passes through air/oil separator in the tank
          • this is called a 'hot tank' system
        • breather
          • prevents excess air pressure in bearing compartments
          • makes sure oil flows freely without air getting into the system
          • there is controlled air leakage into oil system (to maintain labyrinth seals); breathe air
          • breathe air returns to main accessory gear box and goes through air/oil separator
          • oil drains to the bottom of the gearbox and returns to tank
          • air vents through gearbox breather port
      4. components
        • external
          • tank
          • pump
          • filter
          • pressure regulating valve
          • fuel-oil cooler
          • magnetic chip detector
        • internal
          • de-aerator/separator in oil tank
          • bypass valve in the filter may have differential pressure sensor to indicate at flight deck when bypass valve opens
          • scavenge pumps in gearbox and bearing compartments
          • strainers and nozzles in flow path to bearings
      5. on turbines
        • dry sump lubrication oil tank is separate from the engine held in a reservoir until needed
        • main and auxiliary pumps provide flow through the system
        • sumps and scavenge pumps return oil to reservoir
        • fuel or bypass air heat exchangers may be used to cool the oil
        • turbine oil may be used to lubricate the starter and generator drives
        • maintenance
          • ail analysis
          • filter change
          • oil change
          • regular servicing
        • many systems are pressurized; be careful to depressurize before opening
    14. ignition systems
      1. types
        • high tension
        • low tension
      2. function
        • continuous sparking to ensure fast, reliable starting
        • turns off once combustion gets going
        • re-lights for
          • precipitation and ice
          • in flight shut downs (IFSD)
    15. cooling and anti-ice

    3.5.9. Turbine operation

    1. safety
    2. starting/running/shut-down sequence
    3. failure modes

    3.5.10. Maintenance operations

    1. cleaning
    2. basic repairs
    3. filters
    4. lubricants
    5. adjustments
      • fuel control adjustments are being phased out
      • 'trimming' was done to set EPR/EGT scheduling to a specific rpm scale
        • this was done by adjusting link rod lengths and trimming to exhaust nozzles
      • newer engines use DEEC and FADEC to do this automatically
        • controls may be tweaked during initial set up
        • any other adjustments are made automatically
    6. GSE– stands, transportation, and storage
      1. GSE
        • all manufactures produce GSE specific for their engines
          • stands
          • installation rigs
          • shipping containers
          • fixtures for aircraft with engine removed
        • never use unapproved equipment
      2. Storage
        • important to prevent contamination in engine, fittings, tubes, etc
          • install plugs, baffles, stored with dehydrants, lubricants
          • cover completely when transporting
          • maybe store engine in a sealed, dry nitrogen pressurized metal container container may have observation port to check humidity indicator
        • prevent corrosion

    3.5.11. Troubleshooting

    1. lubrication
      • possible problems:
        • restricted flow
          • eg hoses can get kinked or peel/deteriorate inside
        • leakage
        • failed bypass device
        • inaccurate instruments
      • oil problems lead to inadequate cooling and lubrication
        • can lead to clogging and varnish buildup
      • improper servicing can cause overfilling, loose caps, etc
      • do visual inspection, pressure testing, disassembly for further inspection as necessary
    2. fuel
      • burn marks: fuel nozzle problems
        • remove nozzle and test flow
      • check nozzles and distribution manifolds for clogging and heat related problems
      • delivery problems
        • leaks
        • restrictions
        • worn out pumps
        • failed pump drives
        • failed bypass devices or valves
      • electrical power problems
        • loose or dirty connectors
        • broken wires, solder joints
        • failed components (eg controllers or actuators)
      • use standard electrical troubleshooting techniques
    3. ignition
      • very simple system; not much to check
      • only 4 parts, mostly just remove and replace as needed
        • igniter
        • lead
        • exciter
        • power source
      • weak or no spark check power/ground to igniter driver check igniter for damage and function
    4. FADEC
      • lots of potential problems
      • general guidelines
        • test all inputs
        • test all outputs and actuators
        • test all power and ground circuits
        • if all else is good and problem persists, then replace the controller
        • look at FADEC errors to help diagnosis
    5. engine performance check
      • trimming– idle speed and max thrust adjustment during performance testing
      • verify rated thrust
      • check in case of
        • engine change
        • engine module change
        • fuel control change
        • suspected performance loss
      • adjustments include
        • idle adjustment
        • max % rpm
        • max EPR
      • acceleration check check that time from idle to full power is within spec
      • throttle spring-back and cushion check throttle should hit fuel control internal stop before reaching full travel 'cushion' is power lever distance between full and takeoff power
      • part power trim check done to save fuel, reduce noise, and prolong engine life steps
        1. mechanically stop power lever at full power during trimming
        2. adjust to max continuous thrust rating
        3. after trimming, remove mechanical stops from power lever and check take-off power
      • types of trimming procedures
        • EPR rated crew uses engine pressure ratio gauge to set power
        • speed rated crew uses tach to check fan spee to set engine power/thrust
        • never 'up trim' an engine to compensate for inlet duct loss, bleed air loss, or accessory drive loss could cause it to go over limits of temperature, rpm, and thrust

    3.5.12. Specific systems

    1. APU
    2. turboprop
    3. turbofan/jet
    4. Variable Vane Systems
      • used in big, high compression engines or ones that tend to have compressor surging during acceleration/deceleration at lower speeds
      • it automatically varies the shape of the compressor gas path
        • excludes unwanted air from the front compressor stages
        • maintains ratio between compressor rpm and airflow
        • at low rpm, the stator vanes close partially
        • as speed increases, the stators open more
        • this meters air, sets the correct AoA for the blades, and slows the air down
      • vanes stay closed-ish until 65% rotor rpm
      • they open fully around 95% rotor rpm
      • controlled by FCU, EEC, or FADEC
        • senses N1, N2, EGT, TIT, and CIT
        • driven by hydraulic actuators, usually driven by fuel pressure
        • usually driven by fuel pressure, or electro-mechanically
        • linkage between vanes and actuators called 'unison rings' or 'vane synchronizer rings and vane leveling arms'
          • may also be used for thrust augmentation and vectoring
      • inspection
        • check for leveling arm pins
        • look at the spot welds on the synchronizing ring
        • rubbing wear
        • broken pins
    5. Turbine Active Clearance Control
      • maintains blade clearance on large turbines
      • thermostatically controlled by FADEC, controls fresh cooling air vented to the case surface
      • cool air shrinks the case diameter, closing the gap at the blade tips
